Pet Sematary

Chapter 1

Louis Creed, who had lost his father at three and never knew a grandfather, didn't expect to find a father figure in his middle age, but that's what happened… though he called this man a friend, as a grown man must when he finds the man who should have been his father later in life. He met this man the evening he, his wife, and their two children moved into the big white house in Ludlow. Winston Churchill moved in with them. Church was his daughter Eileen’s cat.

The university search committee had been slow, the hunt for a house near the university had been stressful, and by the time they neared the house—all the landmarks were right… like bad omens before a disaster, Louis thought grimly—they were all tired, tense, and on edge. Gage was teething and fussy. He wouldn't sleep, no matter how much Rachel sang. She offered him her breast even though it wasn't his usual time. Gage knew his feeding schedule as well as she did—maybe better—and bit her with his new teeth. Rachel, still unsure about moving to Maine from Chicago, where she'd lived her whole life, burst into tears. Eileen joined her. In the back of the station wagon, Church paced restlessly, as he had for the three days it took to drive from Chicago. His yowling from the carrier had been bad, but his pacing after they let him loose in the car had been almost as upsetting.

Louis felt like crying. A wild idea came to him: he'd suggest they go back to Bangor for food while waiting for the moving van, and when his three loved ones got out, he'd speed away without looking back, the car burning gas. He'd drive south to Orlando, Florida, get a job at Disney World as a medic under a new name. Before hitting the highway, he'd dump the damn cat, too.

Then they rounded the last curve, and there was the house he'd only seen before. He'd flown out to see each of the seven they'd picked from photos after he got the job at the University of Maine, and this was the one he'd chosen: a big old New England house (newly sided and insulated; heating costs were high but not unreasonable), three big rooms downstairs, four up, a long shed for more rooms later—all surrounded by a big lawn, green even in the August heat.

Beyond the house was a large field for the children to play in, and beyond the field were woods that seemed endless. The property bordered state land, the realtor had said, and there would be no development soon. The local Native American tribe had claimed nearly eight thousand acres and the legal battle might last for years.

Rachel stopped crying. She sat up. “Is that—”

“That’s it,” Louis said. He felt uneasy—no, scared. He felt terrified. He had committed a big part of their lives for this; it wouldn’t be paid off until Eileen was seventeen.

He swallowed.

“What do you think?”

“I think it’s beautiful,” Rachel said, and that was a huge weight off his chest—and his mind felt clearer. He saw she wasn't joking; it was in how she looked at it as they turned into the paved driveway that curved around to the shed in back, her eyes taking in the empty windows, her mind already thinking about things like curtains and oilcloth for the cupboards, and who knew what else.

“Daddy?” Ellie said from the back seat. She had stopped crying, too. Even Gage had stopped fussing. Louis enjoyed the quiet.

“What is it, love?”

Her eyes, brown under dark blond hair in the rearview mirror, also looked over the house, the lawn, the roof of another house in the distance to the left, and the big field stretching up to the woods.

“Is this home?”

“It’s going to be, honey,” he said.

“Hooray!” she shouted, almost making his ear ring. And Louis, who could sometimes get very annoyed with Ellie, decided he didn’t care if he ever saw Disney World in Orlando.

He parked in front of the shed and turned off the car’s engine.

The engine ticked. In the silence, which felt very big after Chicago and the busy State Street and the Loop, a bird sang sweetly in the late afternoon.

“Home,” Rachel said softly, still looking at the house.

“Home,” Gage said, happy on her lap.

Louis and Rachel looked at each other. In the rearview mirror, Eileen’s eyes got wider.

“Did you—”

“Did he—”

“Was that—”

They all spoke at once, then all laughed together. Gage didn’t care; he just kept sucking his thumb. He had been saying “Ma” for almost a month and had tried to say something that might have been “Daaa” or just what Louis wanted to hear.

But this, maybe by chance, was a real word. Home.

Louis took Gage from his wife’s lap and hugged him.

That was how they came to Ludlow.

Chapter 2

In Louis Creed’s memory, that moment always seemed magical—partly because it might have been, but mostly because the rest of the evening was chaotic. In the next three hours, there was no peace or magic to be found.

Louis had carefully put the house keys (he was a neat and orderly man) in a small manila envelope labeled “Ludlow House—keys received June 29.” He had placed the keys in the Fairlane’s glove compartment. He was certain of it. Now they were missing.

While he searched, becoming more annoyed, Rachel lifted Gage onto her hip and followed Eileen to the tree in the field. He was checking under the seats for the third time when his daughter screamed and then cried.

“Louis!” Rachel called. “She cut herself!”

Eileen had fallen from the tire swing and hit her knee on a rock. The cut wasn't deep, but she was screaming as if she had lost a leg, Louis thought (a little unkindly). He glanced at the house across the road, where a light was on in the living room.

“Okay, Ellie,” he said. “That’s enough. The people over there will think someone is being murdered.”

“But it hurrrrts!”

Louis fought his anger and silently returned to the car. The keys were gone, but the first-aid kit was still in the glove compartment. He got it and came back. When Ellie saw it, she screamed even louder.

“No! Not the stingy stuff! I don’t want the stingy stuff, Daddy! No—”

“Eileen, it’s just Mercurochrome, and it doesn’t sting—”

“Be a big girl,” Rachel said. “It’s just—”

“No-no-no-no-no—”

“You want to stop that or your butt will sting,” Louis said.

“She’s tired, Lou,” Rachel said quietly.

“Yeah, I know the feeling. Hold her leg out.”

Rachel put Gage down and held Eileen’s leg, which Louis painted with Mercurochrome even though she wailed louder and louder.

“Someone just came out on the porch of that house across the street,” Rachel said. She picked Gage up. He had started to crawl away through the grass.

“Great,” Louis muttered.

“Lou, she’s—”

“Tired, I know.” He capped the Mercurochrome and looked sadly at his daughter. “There. And it really didn’t hurt a bit. Admit it, Ellie.

“It does! It does hurt! It hurrrr—”

He wanted to slap her, so he grabbed his leg hard.

“Did you find the keys?” Rachel asked.

“Not yet,” Louis said, snapping the first-aid kit closed and getting up. “I’ll—”

Gage began to scream. He wasn't just fussing or crying, but really screaming, squirming in Rachel’s arms.

“What’s wrong with him?” Rachel cried, pushing him almost blindly at Louis. It was, he thought, one of the good things about marrying a doctor—you could shove the kid at your husband whenever the kid seemed to be dying. “Louis! What’s—”

The baby was grabbing wildly at his neck, screaming. Louis flipped him over and saw an angry white bump rising on the side of Gage’s neck. And there was also something on the strap of his jumper, something fuzzy, squirming weakly.

Eileen, who had quieted down, began to scream again, “Bee! Bee! BEEEEEE!” She jumped back, tripped over the same rock sticking out that she had already fallen on, sat down hard, and began to cry again in pain, surprise, and fear.

I’m going crazy, Louis thought, amazed. Wheeeeee!

“Do something, Louis! Can’t you do something?”

“Got to get the stinger out,” a voice behind them said slowly. “That’s the way. Get the stinger out and put some baking soda on it. The swelling will go down.” But the voice had such a strong Down East accent that for a moment Louis’s tired, confused mind couldn’t understand the words: Got t’get the stinga out ’n put some bakin soda on’t. ’T ’l! go daown.

He turned and saw an old man of perhaps seventy—a strong and healthy seventy—standing there on the grass. He wore overalls over a blue work shirt that showed his thick, wrinkled neck. His face was sunburned, and he was smoking an unfiltered cigarette. As Louis looked at him, the old man pinched the cigarette out between his thumb and forefinger and put it in his pocket neatly. He held out his hands and smiled crookedly… a smile Louis liked right away—and he didn’t usually like people quickly.

“Not trying to tell you what to do, Doc,” he said. And that was how Louis met Judson Crandall, the man who should have been his father.

Chapter 3

He had watched them arrive from across the street and had come over to see if he could help when it seemed they were "in a bit of a jam," as he said.

While Louis held the baby on his shoulder, Crandall stepped close, looked at the swelling on Gage’s neck, and reached out with one blocky, twisted hand. Rachel started to protest—his hand looked terribly clumsy and almost as big as Gage’s head—but before she could say anything, the old man’s fingers had made a single sure movement, as skillful and quick as the fingers of a man shuffling cards or making coins disappear. And the stinger lay in his palm.

“Big one,” he said. “Not the biggest, but good enough to show off, I guess.” Louis burst out laughing.

Crandall looked at him with that crooked smile and said, “Yep, a good one, isn't it?”

“What did he say, Mommy?” Eileen asked, and then Rachel burst out laughing too. Of course it was terribly rude, but somehow it was okay. Crandall pulled out a pack of Chesterfield Kings, stuck one in the wrinkled corner of his mouth, nodded at them kindly as they laughed—even Gage was giggling now, despite the swelling from the bee sting—and lit a wooden match with his thumbnail. The old have their tricks, Louis thought. Small ones, but some of them are good.

He stopped laughing and held out the hand that wasn’t holding Gage’s bottom—Gage’s definitely wet bottom. “I’m happy to meet you, Mr.—”

“Jud Crandall,” he said and shook his hand. “You’re the doctor, I guess.”

“Yes. Louis Creed. This is my wife Rachel, my daughter Ellie, and the kid with the bee sting is Gage.”

“Nice to meet all of you.”

I didn’t mean to laugh… that is, we didn’t mean to laugh… it’s just that we’re… a little tired.

That—how little he said about it—made him giggle again. He felt completely worn out.

Crandall nodded. “Of course you are,” he said, which sounded like: Co’s you aaa. He looked at Rachel. “Why don’t you take your little boy and your daughter to the house for a bit, Missus Creed? We can put some baking soda on a cloth and cool it down a little. My wife would like to say hello too. She doesn’t get out much. Her arthritis has been bad the last two or three years.”

Rachel looked at Louis, who nodded.

“That would be very nice of you, Mr. Crandall.”

“Oh, just call me Jud,” he said.

There was a sudden loud honk, a motor slowing, and then the big blue moving van was turning—heavily—into the driveway.

“Oh Christ, and I don’t know where the keys are,” Louis said.

“That’s okay,” Crandall said. “I have a set. Mr. and Mrs. Cleveland… the ones who lived here before you—gave me a set, oh, it must have been fourteen, fifteen years ago. They lived here a long time. Joan Cleveland was my wife’s best friend. She died two years ago. Bill went to that senior apartment complex over in Orrington. I’ll bring them back over. They’re yours now, anyway.”

“You’re very kind, Mr. Crandall,” Rachel said.

“Not at all,” he said. “Looking forward to having kids around again.” Except that the sound of this, as strange to their Midwestern ears as a foreign language, was yowwuns “You just want to watch them around the road, Mrs. Creed. Lots of big trucks on that road.”

Now there was the sound of slamming doors as the moving men hopped out of the cab and came toward them.

Ellie had wandered away a little, and now she said, “Daddy what’s this?”

Louis, who had started to meet the moving men, glanced back. At the edge of the field, where the lawn stopped and high summer grass took over, a path about four feet wide had been cut, smooth and short. It wound up the hill, curved through a low stand of bushes and a small group of birches, and out of sight.

“Looks like a path of some kind,” Louis said.

“Oh, yes,” Crandall said, smiling. “Tell you about it sometime, missy. You want to come over and we’ll fix your baby brother up?”

“Sure,” Ellie said and then added with a certain hopefulness “Does baking soda sting?”

Chapter 4

Crandall brought back the keys, but Louis had already found his. There was a space at the top of the glove compartment, and the small envelope had fallen down into the wiring. He pulled it out and let the movers in. Crandall gave him the extra set. They were on an old, dull keychain. Louis thanked him and put them in his pocket without thinking, watching the movers carry in boxes and dressers and all the things they had gathered over the ten years of their marriage. Seeing them like this, out of their usual places, made them seem smaller. Just a bunch of stuff in boxes, he thought, and suddenly he felt sad and down—he figured he was feeling what people called homesickness.

“Uprooted and transplanted,” Crandall said, suddenly beside him, and Louis jumped a little.

“You sound like you know the feeling,” he said.

“No, not really.” Crandall lit a cigarette—pop! went the match, flaring brightly in the early evening shadows. “My dad built that house across the street. Brought his wife there, and she got pregnant there, and that child was me, born in 1900.”

“That makes you—”

“Eighty-three,” Crandall said, and Louis was a little relieved that he didn’t add young at heart, a phrase he really hated.

“You look a lot younger than that.”

Crandall shrugged. “Anyway, I’ve always lived there. I joined up when we fought in World War I, but the closest I got to Europe was Bayonne, New Jersey. Nasty place. Even in 1917 it was a nasty place. I was happy to come back here. Got married to my Norma, worked on the railroad, and here we still are. But I’ve seen a lot of life right here in Ludlow. I really have.”

The moving men stopped by the shed entrance, holding the box spring that went under the big double bed he and Rachel shared. “Where do you want this, Mr. Creed?”

“Upstairs… just a minute, I’ll show you.” He started toward them, then paused for a moment and glanced back at Crandall.

“You go on,” Crandall said, smiling. “I’ll see how things are going with everyone. Send them back over and get out of your way. But moving in makes you real thirsty. I usually sit on my porch around nine and have a couple of beers. When it’s warm, I like to watch the night come. Sometimes Norma joins me. Come over, if you want.”

“Well, maybe I will,” Louis said, not planning to at all. Next thing would be an informal (and free) checkup of Norma’s arthritis on the porch. He liked Crandall, liked his crooked smile, his casual way of talking, his Yankee accent, which wasn’t harsh but so soft it was almost a drawl. A good man, Louis thought, but doctors quickly became wary of people. It was too bad, but sooner or later even your best friends wanted medical advice. And with old people, it never ended. “But don’t wait for me, or stay up—we’ve had a terrible day.”

“Just so long as you know you don’t need an invitation,” Crandall said—and there was something in his crooked smile that made Louis feel that Crandall knew exactly what Louis was thinking.

He watched the old guy for a moment before joining the movers. Crandall walked straight and easily, like a man of sixty instead of over eighty. Louis felt that first small pull of affection.

Chapter 5

By nine o’clock the movers were gone. Ellie and Gage, both exhausted, were sleeping in their new rooms, Gage in his crib, Ellie on a mattress on the floor surrounded by a small mountain of boxes—her billions of Crayolas, whole, broken, and dull; her Sesame Street posters; her picture books; her clothes; heaven knew what else. And of course Church was with her, also sleeping and growling rustily in the back of his throat. That rusty growl seemed the closest the big tom could come to purring.

Rachel had walked around the house restlessly with Gage in her arms earlier, questioning the places where Louis had told the movers to leave things, getting them to rearrange, change, or restack. Louis had not lost their check; it was still in his breast pocket, along with the five ten-dollar bills he had set aside for a tip. When the van was finally emptied, he handed both the check and the cash over, nodded at their thanks, signed the receipt, and stood on the porch, watching them head back to their big truck. He figured they would probably stop in Bangor and have a few beers to settle the dust. A couple of beers would be good right now. That made him think of Jud Crandall again.

He and Rachel sat at the kitchen table, and he saw the circles under her eyes. “You,” he said, “go to bed.”

“Doctor’s orders?” she asked, smiling a little.

“Yeah.”

“Okay,” she said, standing. “I’m beat. And Gage is likely to be up in the night. You coming?”

He hesitated. “I don’t think so, just yet. That old guy across the street—”

“Road. You call it a road, out in the country. Or if you’re Judson Crandall, I guess you call it a rud.”

“Okay, across the rud. He invited me over for a beer. I think I’m going to do that. I’m tired, but I’m too wired to sleep.”

Rachel smiled. “You’ll get Norma Crandall to tell you everything, even what kind of mattress she sleeps on.”

Louis laughed, thinking how funny—funny and scary—it was that wives could read their husbands’ minds after a while.

“He was here when we needed him,” he said. “I can do him a favor, I guess.”

“Barter system?”

He shrugged, not wanting to explain that he had quickly grown fond of Crandall. “How’s his wife?”

“Very sweet,” Rachel said. “Gage sat on her lap. I was surprised because he’s had a hard day, and you know he doesn’t like new people, especially when he's tired. And she had a dolly she let Eileen play with.”

“How bad would you say her arthritis is?”

“Quite bad.”

“In a wheelchair?”

“No… but she walks very slowly, and her fingers… ” Rachel held her own slim fingers up and hooked them into claws to show him. Louis nodded. “Anyway, don’t be late, Lou. Strange houses give me the creeps.”

"It won't feel strange for long," Louis said, and kissed her.

Chapter 6

Louis came back later feeling small. No one asked him to examine Norma Crandall; when he crossed the street (remember, he told himself, smiling), the lady had already gone to bed. Jud was a dim shape behind the screens of the enclosed porch. There was the nice squeak of a rocker on old linoleum. Louis knocked on the screen door, which rattled softly against its frame. Crandall’s cigarette glowed like a big, friendly firefly in the summer darkness. From a radio, low, came the sound of a Red Sox game, and it all gave Louis Creed a strange feeling of coming home.

“Doc,” Crandall said. “I thought that was you.”

“Hope you meant what you said about the beer,” Louis said, coming in.

“Oh, I never lie about beer,” Crandall said. “A man who lies about beer makes enemies. Sit down, Doc. I put a couple extra on ice, just in case.”

The porch was long and narrow, with rattan chairs and sofas. Louis sank into one and was surprised at how comfortable it was. At his left was a tin pail filled with ice cubes and a few cans of Black Label. He took one.

“Thank you,” he said and opened it. The first two swallows felt like a blessing.

“You’re welcome,” Crandall said. “I hope you’ll be happy here, Doc.”

“Amen,” Louis said.

“Hey! If you want crackers or something, I could get some. I have some cheese that’s just about ready.”

“A piece of what?”

“Rat cheese.” Crandall sounded a little amused.

“Thanks, but just the beer for me.”

“Well then, we’ll just let it go.” Crandall belched, sounding happy.

“Your wife in bed?” Louis asked, wondering why he was talking like this.

“Yep. Sometimes she stays up. Sometimes she doesn’t.”

“Her arthritis is very painful, right?”

“You ever see a case that wasn’t?” Crandall asked.

Louis shook his head.

“I guess it’s okay,” Crandall said. “She doesn’t complain much. She’s a good old girl, my Norma.” His voice showed a deep and real love. Out on Route 15, a tanker truck rumbled by, so big and long that Louis couldn’t see his house across the road for a moment. The word ORINCO was written on the side, just visible in the fading light.

“One hell of a big truck,” Louis said.

“Orinco’s near Orrington,” Crandall said. “Chemical fertilizer plant. They come and go, all right. And the oil tankers, and the dump trucks, and the people who go to work in Bangor or Brewer and come home at night.” He shook his head. “That’s the one thing about Ludlow I don’t like anymore. That damn road. No peace from it. They go all day and all night. Wake Norma up sometimes. Hell, wake me up sometimes, and I sleep like a goddam log.”

Louis, who thought this strange Maine landscape almost eerily quiet after the constant roar of Chicago, just nodded.

“One day soon the Arabs will stop the oil, and they’ll be able to grow African violets right on the road line,” Crandall said.

“You might be right.” Louis tilted his can back and was surprised to find it empty.

Crandall laughed. “Just grab yourself another, Doc.”

Louis hesitated and then said, “All right, but just one more. I have to get back.”

“Sure you do. Not moving easily, huh?”

“It is,” Louis agreed, and then for a time they were silent. The silence was comfortable, as if they had known each other for a long time. This was a feeling Louis had read about in books, but never felt until now. He felt ashamed of his earlier thoughts about free medical advice.

On the road a semi roared by, its running lights twinkling like stars.

“That’s one mean road, all right,” Crandall repeated thoughtfully, almost vaguely, and then turned to Louis. There was a strange little smile on his wrinkled mouth. He stuck a Chesterfield cigarette into one corner of the smile and lit a match with his thumbnail. “You remember the path there that your little girl mentioned?”

For a moment Louis didn’t; Ellie had mentioned a lot of things before finally falling asleep for the night. Then he did remember. That wide, mowed path going up through the small group of trees and over the hill.

“Yes, I do. You promised to tell her about it sometime.”

“I did, and I will,” Crandall said. “That path goes up into the woods about a mile and a half. The local kids around Route 15 and Middle Drive keep it nice because they use it. Kids come and go… there’s a lot more moving around than there used to be when I was a boy; then you picked a place and stayed there. But they seem to tell each other, and every spring a bunch of them mows that path. They keep it nice all summer long. Not all of the adults in town know it’s there—a lot of them do, of course, but not all—but all of the kids do. I’d bet on it.”

“Know what’s there?”

“The pet cemetery,” Crandall said.

“Pet cemetery,” Louis repeated, puzzled.

“It’s not as strange as it probably sounds,” Crandall said, smoking and rocking. “It’s the road. It kills a lot of animals, that road does. Dogs and cats, mostly, but that’s not all. One of those big Orinco trucks ran over the pet raccoon the Ryder children used to have. That was back—Christ, must have been in ‘73, maybe earlier. Before the state made keeping a raccoon or even a skunk that couldn’t spray illegal, anyway.”

“Why did they do that?”

“Rabies,” Crandall said. “A lot of rabies in Maine now. There was a big old St. Bernard dog that got rabies down south a couple of years ago and killed four people. That was a terrible thing. The dog hadn’t had his shots. If those people had made sure that dog had its shots, it never would have happened. But a raccoon or a skunk, you can vaccinate it twice a year and it still doesn’t always work. But that raccoon the Ryder boys had, that was what the old-timers used to call a ‘sweet raccoon.’ It would waddle right up to you—gosh, he was fat!—and lick your face like a dog. Their dad even paid a vet to neuter him and take out his claws. That must have cost him a fortune!”

Ryder, he worked for IBM in Bangor. They moved to Colorado five years ago… or maybe six. Funny to think of those two almost old enough to drive. Were they still sad about that raccoon? I guess they were. Matty Ryder cried so much his mom got worried and wanted to take him to the doctor. I guess he’s over it now, but they never forget. When a good animal gets killed on the road, a kid never forgets.

Louis’s mind turned to Ellie as he had last seen her tonight, fast asleep with Church purring softly on the foot of the bed.

“My daughter’s got a cat,” he said. “Winston Churchill. We call him Church for short.”

“Do they climb when he walks?”

“I’m sorry?” Louis had no idea what he was talking about.

“Does he still have his balls or has he been fixed?”

“No,” Louis said. “No, he hasn’t been fixed.”

In fact there had been some trouble over that back in Chicago. Rachel had wanted to get Church spayed, had even made the appointment with the vet. Louis canceled it. Even now he wasn’t really sure why. It wasn’t as simple or as stupid as thinking his masculinity was linked to his daughter’s tomcat, or even his annoyance at the idea that Church would have to be neutered so the fat housewife next door wouldn’t have to bother securing her plastic garbage cans—those things were part of it, but mostly it was a vague but strong feeling that it would ruin something in Church that he himself valued—that it would take away the defiant look in the cat’s green eyes. Finally he had pointed out to Rachel that they were moving to the country, and it shouldn’t be a problem. Now here was Judson Crandall, pointing out that part of country living in Ludlow involved dealing with Route 15, and asked him if the cat was fixed. Try a little irony, Dr. Creed—it’s good for your blood.

“I’d get him fixed,” Crandall said, crushing his cigarette between his thumb and forefinger. “A fixed cat doesn’t tend to wander as much. But if he’s always crossing back and forth, his luck will run out, and he’ll end up there with the Ryder kids’ raccoon and little Timmy Dessler’s cocker spaniel and Mrs. Bradleigh’s parakeet. Not that the parakeet got run over in the road, you understand. It just died one day.”

“I’ll think about it,” Louis said.

“You do that,” Crandall said and stood up. “How about that beer? I think I’ll have a bit of that old Mr. Rat after all.”

“Beer’s gone,” Louis said, also standing, “and I should get going, too. Big day tomorrow.”

“Starting at the university?”

Louis nodded. “The kids don’t come back for two weeks, but I should know what I’m doing by then, don’t you think?”

“Yeah, if you don’t know where the pills are, I guess you’ll have trouble.” Crandall offered his hand and Louis shook it, remembering again that old bones hurt easily. “Come over any evening,” he said. “I want you to meet my Norma. I think she’d like you.”

“I will,” Louis said. “Nice to meet you, Jud.”

“Same here. You’ll settle in. You might even stay a while.”

“I hope we do.”

Louis walked down the uneven path to the side of the road and had to wait while another truck, followed by five cars headed toward Bucksport, went by. Then, raising his hand in a quick wave, he crossed the street (road, he reminded himself again) and went into his new house.

It was quiet, with the sounds of sleep. Ellie didn’t seem to have moved, and Gage was still in his crib, sleeping like Gage always did, spread out on his back, a bottle nearby. Louis stopped there, looking at his son, his heart suddenly filled with such strong love for the boy that it felt almost risky. He thought part of it was just missing all the familiar Chicago places and faces that were now gone, wiped away so well by the distance that they might never have existed. People move around a lot more than they used to… you used to pick a place and stay there. There was some truth to that.

He went to his son, and because no one was watching, not even Rachel, he kissed his fingers and then gently touched them to Gage’s cheek through the crib bars.

Gage made a soft noise and turned onto his side.

“Sleep well, baby,” Louis said.

He quietly undressed and got into his side of the bed, which was just two single mattresses together on the floor for now. He felt the day’s stress start to fade. Rachel didn’t move. Unpacked boxes loomed like ghosts in the room.

Just before falling asleep, Louis propped himself up on his elbow and looked out the window. Their room faced the front, and he could see the Crandall’s house across the road. It was too dark to make out shapes—he could have on a moonlit night—but he saw the glow of a cigarette over there. Still awake, he thought. He might be up for a while. Old people don’t sleep well. Maybe they’re standing guard.

Against what?

Louis was thinking about that as he fell asleep. He dreamed he was in Disney World, driving a bright white van with a red cross on the side. Gage was next to him, and in the dream Gage was at least ten years old. Church was on the dashboard of the white van, looking at Louis with his bright green eyes, and out on Main Street by the 1890s train station, Mickey Mouse was shaking hands with the children around him, his big white cartoon gloves swallowing their small, trusting hands.

Chapter 7

The next two weeks were busy for the family. Little by little, Louis began to settle into his new job. He wondered what it would be like when ten thousand students arrived, many using drugs and alcohol, some with diseases, some worried about grades or homesick, a dozen or so—mostly girls—with eating disorders. He knew that when they all arrived on campus, it would be a different story. As Louis started to understand his job as head of University Medical Services, Rachel began to understand the house.

Gage was busy falling and bumping as he got used to his new surroundings. His sleep schedule was off for a while, but by the middle of their second week in Ludlow, he was sleeping through the night again. Only Ellie, about to start kindergarten in a new place, seemed constantly excited and on edge. She would suddenly giggle for a long time, become almost depressed, or throw tantrums over nothing. Rachel said Ellie would be fine once she realized school wasn't as scary as she imagined. Louis agreed. Most of the time, Ellie was her usual sweet self.

Louis started having a beer or two with Jud Crandall in the evenings. Around the time Gage began sleeping through the night, Louis began bringing over a six-pack every other night or so. He met Norma Crandall, a kind woman with rheumatoid arthritis—a terrible disease that ruins the later years of otherwise healthy people. But she had a positive attitude and refused to give in to the pain. Louis thought she might have another five to seven good years ahead of her, even if they weren't very comfortable.

Against his usual habits, he examined her himself and reviewed the prescriptions her doctor had given her, finding them to be correct. He was disappointed that he couldn't do or suggest anything else for her. Her doctor had things under control as much as possible, unless there was a sudden breakthrough, which was unlikely. He knew you had to accept things, or you would end up losing your mind.

Rachel liked Norma, and they became friends by sharing recipes, like boys trading baseball cards. Norma started with her apple pie recipe, and Rachel shared her beef stroganoff recipe. Norma liked both of the Creed children, especially Ellie, who she said would be "a real beauty." Later that night, Louis told Rachel that at least Norma hadn't said Ellie was going to grow into a troublemaker. Rachel laughed so hard she farted, and then they both laughed so long and loud that they woke up Gage in the next room.

The first day of kindergarten arrived. Louis, feeling confident in his job at the infirmary and the medical facilities, took the day off. (Besides, the infirmary was empty; the last patient, a summer student who had broken her leg, had been discharged a week before.) He stood on the lawn with Rachel and Gage as the school bus turned onto their street and stopped in front of their house. The doors opened, and the noise of children filled the air.

Ellie looked back at them, as if wondering if she could still avoid going. Perhaps seeing their expressions, she realized it was too late and that everything after this day was inevitable, like Norma Crandall's worsening arthritis. She turned and got on the bus. The doors closed, and the bus drove away. Rachel started crying.

"Don't," Louis said. He wasn't crying, but he was close. "It's only half a day."

"Half a day is bad enough," Rachel said, scolding him as she cried harder. Louis held her, and Gage put his arms around both of them. When Rachel cried, Gage usually cried too. But not this time. He has us to himself, Louis thought, and he knows it.

They waited with some worry for Ellie to return, drinking too much coffee and guessing how it was going for her. Louis went into the back room that would be his study and fiddled around, moving papers without really doing anything. Rachel started lunch way too early.

When the phone rang at ten fifteen, Rachel rushed to it and answered with a quick “Hello?” before it rang again. Louis stood in the doorway between his office and the kitchen, convinced it was Ellie’s teacher saying she couldn’t handle it; the school system had found her too difficult and was sending her back. But it was just Norma Crandall, saying Jud had picked the last of the corn and they could have a dozen ears if they wanted. Louis went over with a shopping bag and told Jud he should have let him help pick it.

“Most of it ain’t worth much anyway,” Jud said.

“Please don’t talk like that while I’m here,” Norma said. She came out on the porch with iced tea on an old Coca-Cola tray.

“Sorry, my Love.”

“He’s not sorry at all,” Norma said to Louis and sat down with a wince.

“Saw Ellie get on the bus,” Jud said, lighting a Chesterfield.

“She’ll be fine,” Norma said. “They usually are.”

Almost, Louis thought sadly.

But Ellie was fine. She came home at noon, smiling and happy, her blue first-day-of-school dress swaying around her scraped legs (with a new scrape on one knee to see), holding a picture of what looked like two kids or maybe two tall cranes, one shoe untied, one ribbon gone from her hair, shouting, “We sang ‘Old MacDonald’! Mommy! Daddy! We sang ‘Old MacDonald’! The same one as at Carstairs Street School!”

Rachel glanced over at Louis, who was sitting in the window seat with Gage on his lap. The baby was almost asleep. There was something sad in Rachel’s glance, and although she looked away quickly, Louis felt a moment of terrible panic. We’re really going to get old, he thought. It’s really true. No one’s going to make an exception for us. She’s on her way… and so are we.

Ellie ran over to him, trying to show him her picture, her new scrape, and tell him about “Old MacDonald” and Mrs. Berryman all at the same time. Church was twining in and out between her legs, purring loudly, and Ellie was somehow, almost miraculously, not tripping over him.

“Shh,” Louis said and kissed her. Gage had gone to sleep, unmindful of all the excitement. “Just let me put the baby to bed and then I’ll listen to everything.”

He took Gage up the stairs, walking through hot slanting September sunshine, and as he reached the landing, such a premonition of horror and darkness struck him that he stopped—stopped cold—and looked around in surprise, wondering what could possibly have come over him. He held the baby tighter, almost clutching him, and Gage stirred uncomfortably. Louis’s arms and back had broken out in great rashes of gooseflesh.

What’s wrong? he wondered, confused and frightened. His heart was racing; his scalp felt cool and suddenly too small to cover his skull; he could feel the surge of adrenaline behind his eyes. Human eyes really did bug out when fear was extreme, he knew; they did not just widen but actually bulged as blood pressure climbed and the fluid pressure in the head increased. What the hell is it? Ghosts? Christ, it really feels as if something just brushed by me in this hallway, something I almost saw.

Downstairs the screen door whacked against its frame.

Louis Creed jumped, almost screamed, and then laughed. It was simply one of those psychological cold pockets people Sometimes passed through—no more, no less. A momentary lapse. They happened; that was all. What had Scrooge said to the ghost of Jacob Marley? You may be no more than an underdone bit of potato. There’s more gravy than grave to you. And that was more correct—physically and mentally—than Charles Dickens had probably known. There were no ghosts, at least not in his experience. He had pronounced two dozen people dead in his career and had never once felt the passage of a soul.

He took Gage into his room and laid him in his crib. As he pulled the blanket up over his son, though, a shudder twisted up his back, and he thought suddenly of his Uncle Carl’s “showroom.” No new cars there, no televisions with all the modern features, no dishwashers with glass fronts so you could watch the magical sudsing action. Only boxes with their lids up, a carefully hidden spotlight over each. His father’s brother was an undertaker.

Good God, what gave you the horrors? Let it go! Dump it!

He kissed his son and went down to listen to Ellie tell about her first day at the big kid’s school.

Chapter 8

That Saturday, after Ellie finished her first week of school and before the college students returned, Jud Crandall crossed the road and walked over to the Creed family on their lawn. Ellie had stopped her bike and was drinking iced tea. Gage crawled in the grass, looking at bugs, maybe even eating some; Gage wasn't picky about where he got his protein.

“Jud,” Louis said, standing up. “Let me get a chair.”

“No need.” Jud wore jeans, an open work shirt, and green boots. He looked at Ellie. “Still want to see where that path goes, Ellie?”

“Yes!” Ellie said, jumping up. Her eyes sparkled. “George Buck at school said it was the pet cemetery, and I told Mommy, but she said to wait for you because you knew where it was.”

“I do,” Jud said. “If your folks say it’s okay, we’ll walk up there. You’ll need boots, though. The ground is a bit wet in places.”

Ellie hurried into the house.

Jud watched her with fond amusement. “Maybe you’d like to come too, Louis.”

“I would,” Louis said. He looked at Rachel. “Want to come, honey?”

“What about Gage? I thought it was a mile.”

“I’ll put him in the Gerrypack.”

Rachel laughed. “Okay… but it’s your back, mister.”

They started off ten minutes later, all of them but Gage wearing boots. Gage was sitting up in the Gerrypack and looking at everything over Louis’s shoulder, wide-eyed. Ellie ran ahead constantly, chasing butterflies and picking flowers.

The grass in the back field was almost waist high, and now there was goldenrod, that late-summer sign that tells on autumn every year. But there was no autumn in the air today; today the sun was still all August, even though August was almost two weeks gone. By the time they had reached the top of the first hill, walking in a line along the cut path, there were big patches of sweat under Louis’s arms.

Jud paused. At first Louis thought it might be because the old man was out of breath—then he saw the view that had opened up behind them.

“Pretty up here,” Jud said, putting a piece of timothy grass between his teeth. Louis thought he had just heard the perfect Yankee understatement.

“It’s gorgeous,” Rachel breathed and then turned to Louis, almost accusingly. “How come you didn’t tell me about this?”

“Because I didn’t know it was here,” Louis said, and was a little ashamed. They were still on their own property; he had just never found time to climb the hill in back of the house until today.

Ellie had been a good way ahead. Now she came back also looking with honest wonder. Church padded at her heels.

The hill was not a high one, but it didn’t need to be. To the east, thick woods blocked any view, but looking west, the land sloped away in a golden and sleepy late summer dream. Everything was still, hazy, silent. There was not even an Orinco tanker on the highway to break the quiet.

It was the river valley they were looking at, the Penobscot, where loggers once floated logs from the northeast to Bangor and Derry. They were south of Bangor and a bit north of Derry. The river flowed wide and peacefully, as if in a deep dream. Louis could see Hampden and Winterport on the other side, and he thought he could trace Route 15, a black line running by the river, almost all the way to Bucksport. They looked over the river, its thick border of trees, the roads, the fields. The spire of the North Ludlow Baptist Church stuck out from the old elms, and to the right, he saw Ellie’s sturdy brick school.

White clouds moved slowly toward a faded denim horizon. Everywhere were the late-summer fields, worn out, dormant but not dead, a striking tan color.

“Gorgeous is the right word,” Louis said.

“They used to call it Prospect Hill,” Jud said. He put a cigarette in his mouth but didn’t light it. “Some still do, but younger people have moved in, and it’s mostly forgotten. Not many people come up here. It doesn’t seem like you can see much because the hill isn’t high. But you can see—” He gestured and stopped talking.

“You can see everything,” Rachel said softly, in awe. She turned to Louis. “Honey, do we own this?”

Before Louis could answer, Jud said: “It’s part of the property, yes.”

Which wasn’t quite the same thing, Louis thought.

It was cooler in the woods, maybe eight or ten degrees cooler. The path, still wide and marked with flowers in pots or coffee cans (mostly wilted), was now covered with dry pine needles. They had walked about a quarter of a mile, downhill, when Jud called Ellie back.

“This is a good walk for a little girl,” Jud said kindly, “but promise your mom and dad that if you come here, you’ll stay on the path.”

“I promise,” Ellie said quickly. “Why?”

He glanced at Louis, who had stopped to rest. Carrying Gage, even in the shade of the pines and spruces, was hard work. “Do you know where you are?” Jud asked Louis.

Louis thought about possible answers but rejected them: Ludlow, North Ludlow, behind my house, between Route 15 and Middle Drive. He shook his head.

Jud pointed back with his thumb. “Town's that way,” he said. “This way, nothing but woods for fifty miles or more. They call it the North Ludlow Woods, but it touches a bit of Orrington, then goes to Rockford. Ends up on those state lands I told you about, the ones the Indians want back. It sounds funny that your nice little house on the main road, with its phone, lights, and cable TV, is on the edge of a wilderness, but it is.” He looked back at Ellie. “Just don’t go wandering around in these woods, Ellie. You might lose the path, and who knows where you'd end up.”

“I won’t, Mr. Crandall.” Ellie seemed impressed, even awed, but not scared, Louis saw. Rachel, however, looked at Jud uneasily, and Louis felt a little uneasy himself. He figured it was a city person's fear of the woods. Louis hadn’t held a compass since Boy Scouts, twenty years before, and his memories of finding your way by the North Star or which side of trees moss grew on were vague, like his memories of tying certain knots.

Jud looked them over and smiled. “We haven’t lost anyone in these woods since 1934,” he said. “At least, nobody local. The last one was Will Jeppson—no great loss. Except for Stanny Bouchard, Will was the biggest drunk around here.”

“You said nobody local,” Rachel said, not quite casually, and Louis could almost read her mind: We’re not local. At least, not yet.

Jud paused and nodded. “We lose a tourist every two or three years because they think you can't get lost right off the main road. But we've never lost one for good, missus. Don’t worry.”

“Are there moose?” Rachel asked, sounding worried, and Louis smiled. If Rachel wanted to worry, she would.

“Well, you might see a moose,” Jud said, “but it won’t bother you, Rachel. During mating season they get a little grumpy, but usually they just look. The only people they chase when they're not mating are people from Massachusetts. I don’t know why, but it’s true.” Louis wasn't sure if the man was joking; Jud looked completely serious. “I’ve seen it many times. Some guy from Saugus or Milton or Weston up a tree, yelling about a herd of moose, all of them as big as a motorhome. Maybe moose can smell Massachusetts on a person. Or maybe it’s just all those new clothes from L. L. Bean’s they smell—I don’t know. I wish a college student would write a paper about it, but I guess none ever will.”

“What’s rutting time?” Ellie asked.

“Never mind,” Rachel said. “I don’t want you up here unless you’re with a grown-up, Ellie.” Rachel moved a step closer to Louis.

Jud looked sad. “I didn’t want to scare you, Rachel—you or your daughter. No need to be scared in these woods. This is a good path; it gets a little buggy in the spring and it’s a little muddy all the time—except for ‘55, which was the driest summer I can remember—but hell, there isn’t even any poison ivy or poison oak, which there is at the back of the schoolyard, and you want to stay away from it, Ellie, if you don’t want to spend three weeks of your life taking starch baths.”

Ellie covered her mouth and giggled.

“It’s a safe path,” Jud said earnestly to Rachel, who still didn’t look convinced. “Why, I bet even Gage could follow it, and the town kids come up here a lot, I already told you that. They keep it nice. Nobody tells them to; they just do it. I wouldn’t want to spoil that for Ellie.” He bent over her and winked. “It’s like many other things in life, Ellie. You keep on the path and all’s well. You get off it and the next thing you know you’re lost if you’re not lucky. And then someone has to send out a search party.”

They walked on. Louis began to get a dull cramp of pain in his back, from the baby carrier. Every now and then Gage would grab a double handful of his hair and tug hard or give a cheerful kick to Louis’s kidneys. Late mosquitoes buzzed around his face and neck, making their eyes water.

The path curved down, bending in and out between very old fir trees, and then cut widely through a prickly, tangled patch of undergrowth. The walking was muddy here, and Louis’s boots squelched in mud and some standing water. At one point they stepped over a marshy spot using a pair of good-sized clumps of grass as stepping stones. That was the worst of it. They started to climb again and the trees took over again. Gage seemed to have magically put on ten pounds, and the day had, with some similar magic, warmed up ten degrees. Sweat poured down Louis’s face.

“How you doing, hon?” Rachel asked. “Want me to carry him for a while?”

“No, I’m fine,” he said, and it was true, although his heart was beating fast in his chest. He was more used to telling people to exercise than he was to doing it.

Jud was walking with Ellie by his side; her lemon-yellow slacks and red blouse were bright splashes of color in the shady brown-green gloom.

“Lou, does he really know where he’s going, do you think?” Rachel asked in a low, slightly worried tone.

“Sure,” Louis said.

Jud called back cheerfully, not turning around: “Almost there… you doing okay, Louis?”

Louis thought, the man is over eighty, but he doesn’t seem tired at all.

“I’m fine,” he answered a little sharply. He would have said the same even if he were having a heart attack, he thought. He smiled, adjusted his backpack, and kept walking.

They reached the top of the second hill, and then the path went through some tall bushes and thick undergrowth. It got narrower, and then Louis saw Ellie and Jud go under an arch made of old, weathered boards. The words PET SEMATARY were written on them in faded black paint, barely readable.

He and Rachel smiled at each other and walked under the arch, automatically reaching for each other’s hands, as if they were getting married.

For the second time that morning, Louis was filled with surprise and awe.

There were no pine needles here. Instead, there was a circle of cut grass, about forty feet across. It was surrounded by thick bushes on three sides and a pile of fallen trees on the fourth, a messy heap that looked both scary and hazardous. Louis thought that anyone trying to get through or over it should wear protective gear. The clearing was full of markers, clearly made by children using whatever they could find—pieces of wood, scrap metal, and so on. Yet, against the background of low bushes and thin trees struggling for light, the fact that they were handmade, and that people had created this place, somehow made their simple design stand out. The surrounding forest gave the place a strange sense of importance, a charm that was not religious but more ancient and wild.

“It’s lovely,” Rachel said, but she didn’t sound like she meant it.

“Wow!” Ellie shouted.

Louis took Gage off his shoulder and set him down to crawl. Louis felt relief in his back.

Ellie ran from one marker to the next, excitedly pointing them out. Louis followed her while Rachel watched the baby. Jud sat down, legs crossed, leaning against a rock, and smoked.

Louis noticed the place seemed more than just orderly; the memorials were in rough circles.

SMUCKY THE CAT, one wooden marker said. The writing was childish but neat. HE WAS OBEDIANT. And below: 1971-1974. A little further around, a piece of slate had a name in faded red paint: BIFFER. And below that, a rhyme: BIFFER, BIFFER. A HELLUVA SNIFFER / UNTIL HE DIED HE MADE US RICHER.

“Biffer was the Desslers’ cocker spaniel,” Jud said. He had scraped a spot in the dirt with his heel and was tapping his ashes into it. “Got run over by a dumpster last year. Ain’t that some poem?”

“It is,” Louis said.

Some graves had flowers, some new, most old, many decayed. Most of the writing Louis tried to read had faded. Others had no mark, and Louis guessed they were written with chalk or crayon.

“Mom!” Ellie shouted. “Here’s a goldfish! Come see!”

“I’m okay,” Rachel said, and Louis looked at her. She stood alone, outside the circle, looking uneasy. Louis thought: She’s upset even here. She was never comfortable around death (not that anyone is), probably because of her sister. Rachel’s sister died young, and it left a scar Louis knew not to touch. Her name was Zelda, and she died from spinal meningitis. Her sickness was long, painful, and ugly, and Rachel was young then. If she wanted to forget, he thought it was okay.

Louis winked at her, and Rachel smiled at him.

Louis looked up. They were in a natural opening in the woods. He guessed that explained why the grass was so healthy; the sun could reach it. Still, it would have taken watering and care. That meant carrying cans of water up here or maybe using Indian pumps, even heavier than Gage in his Gerrypack, carried on small backs. He thought again that it was strange for children to have kept it up for so long. He remembered his own childhood interests, and seeing Ellie's, they usually burned out quickly—fast… hot… and quick to die.

Moving further in, the pet graves were older; fewer and fewer of the inscriptions could be read, but those that could gave a rough timeline going back in time. Here was TRIXIE. KILLED ON THE HIGHWAY SEPT 15, 1968. In the same circle was a wide flat board planted deep in the ground. Freezing and thawing had warped it and tilted it to one side, but Louis could still make out IN MEMORY OF MARTA OUR PET RABBIT DIED MARCH 1 1965. A row further in was: GEN. PATTON (OUR! GOOD! DOG! the inscription emphasized), who had died in 1958; and POLYNESIA (who would have been a parrot, if Louis remembered Doctor Doolittle correctly), who had squawked her last “Polly want a cracker” in the summer of 1953. There was nothing readable in the next two rows, and then, still far from the center, carved roughly on a piece of sandstone, was HANNAH THE BEST DOG THAT EVER LIVED 1929-1939. Sandstone was relatively soft, so the inscription was now just a faint outline, but Louis found it hard to imagine how long some child must have spent carving those nine words on the stone. The dedication of love and grief seemed amazing to him; this was something parents didn't even do for their own parents or for their children if they died young.

“Wow, this really goes back a long time,” he said to Jud, who had wandered over to join him.

Jud nodded. “Come here, Louis. I want to show you something.”

They walked to a row only three back from the center. Here the circular pattern, which seemed almost random in the outer rows, was very clear. Jud stopped before a small piece of slate that had fallen over. Kneeling carefully, the old man stood it up again.

“There used to be words here,” Jud said. “I carved them myself, but they've worn away now. I buried my first dog here. Spot. He died of old age in 1914, the year the Great War started.”

Struck by the thought that this graveyard was older than many graveyards for people, Louis walked toward the center and looked at several of the markers. None of them were readable, and most had been almost taken back by the forest floor. The grass had almost completely grown over one, and when he stood it back up, there was a small tearing sound from the earth. Blind beetles scurried over the area he had uncovered. He felt a small chill and thought, Boot Hill for animals. I’m not sure I really like it.

“How far back do these go?”

“Gee, I don’t know,” Jud said, putting his hands deep in his pockets. “The place was here when Spot died, of course. I had a lot of friends in those days. They helped me dig the hole for Spot. Digging here isn’t easy, either—the ground’s really stony, you know, hard to dig. And I helped them sometimes.” He pointed here and there with a rough finger. “That there was Pete LaVasseur’s dog, if I remember right, and there’s three of Albion Groatley’s barn cats buried right in a row there.

“Old Man Fritchie kept racing pigeons. Me and Al Groatley and Carl Hannah buried one of them that a dog got. He’s right there.” He paused thoughtfully. “I’m the last one left from that group, you know. All dead now, my friends. All gone.”

Louis said nothing, just stood looking at the pet graves with his hands in his pockets.

“Ground’s stony,” Jud repeated. “Couldn’t plant anything here but corpses anyway, I guess.”

Across the way, Gage began to cry softly, and Rachel brought him over, holding him on her hip. “He’s hungry,” she said. “I think we should go back, Lou. Please, okay?” her eyes asked.

“Sure,” he said. He put the Gerrypack on his shoulder again and turned around so Rachel could put Gage in. “Ellie! Hey Ellie, where are you?”

“There she is,” Rachel said and pointed toward the fallen trees. Ellie was climbing as if the fallen trees were like the monkey bars at school.

“Oh, honey, you want to come down from there!” Jud called out, worried. “If you put your foot in the wrong hole and those old trees move, you’ll break your ankle.”

Ellie jumped down. “Ow!” she cried and came toward them, rubbing her hip. The skin wasn’t broken, but a stiff dead branch had torn her pants.

“You see what I mean,” Jud said, tousling her hair. “Fallen trees like this, even someone who knows the woods well won’t try to climb over it if he can go around. Trees that all fall down in a pile get mean. They’ll bite you if they can.”

“Really?” Ellie asked.

“Really. They’re piled up like straws, you see. And if you were to step on the right one, they might all come down in an avalanche.”

Ellie looked at Louis. “Is that true, Daddy?”

“I think so, hon.”

“Yuck!” She looked back at the fallen trees and yelled: “You tore my pants, you stupid trees!”

All three of the adults laughed. The fallen trees did not. It just sat there, getting bleached by the sun as it had for years. To Louis, it looked like the bones of some ancient monster, killed by a truly good and noble knight, maybe. A dragon’s bones, left here in a huge pile of stones.

It struck him even then that the fallen trees were too well-placed, standing as they did between the pet cemetery and the deep woods beyond, woods that Jud Crandall sometimes called “the Indian woods.” Its randomness seemed too deliberate, too perfect to be natural. It—

Then Gage grabbed his ear and twisted it, laughing, and Louis forgot about the fallen trees in the woods beyond the pet cemetery. It was time to go home.

Chapter 9

Ellie came to him the next day, looking worried. Louis was working on a model in his study. This one was a 1917 Rolls-Royce Silver Ghost—680 pieces, over 50 moving parts. It was almost done, and he could almost see the chauffeur in uniform, like the coachmen of the past, sitting proudly behind the wheel.

He had been obsessed with models since he was ten. He started with a World War I Spad that his Uncle Carl had bought him, worked through most of the Revell airplanes, and moved on to bigger and better things as a teen and in his twenties. There was a time when he made boats in bottles, and another when he made war machines, and even one when he built guns so real it was hard to believe they wouldn’t fire—Colts and Winchesters and Lugers, even a Buntline Special. For the last five years or so, he had been making big cruise ships. A model of the Lusitania and one of the Titanic were on his shelves at his university office, and the Andrea Doria, finished just before they left Chicago, was currently displayed on the mantel in their living room. Now he had moved on to classic cars, and if his past was any indication, he figured he would want to do something new in four or five years. Rachel saw this, his only real hobby, with a wife's patience that held, he thought, some contempt; even after ten years of marriage she probably thought he would outgrow it. Maybe some of this came from her father, who still thought, just as he had when Louis and Rachel got married, that he had gotten a jerk for a son-in-law.

Maybe, he thought, Rachel is right. Maybe I’ll just wake up one morning at thirty-seven, put all these models in the attic, and start hang gliding.

Meanwhile Ellie looked serious.

Far away, in the clear air, he could hear the perfect sound of church bells calling people to worship on a Sunday morning.

“Hi, Dad,” she said.

“Hello, pumpkin. What’s up?”

“Oh, nothing,” she said, but her face said otherwise; her face said that plenty was up, and—none of it was good, thank you very much. Her hair was freshly washed and hung loose to her shoulders. In this light it was still more blond than the brown it was becoming. She was wearing a dress, and Louis realized that his daughter almost always wore a dress on Sundays, even though they didn’t go to church. “What are you building?”

Carefully gluing on a mudguard, he told her. “Look at this,” he said, carefully handing her a hubcap. “See those linked R’s? That’s a nice detail, huh? If we fly back to Chicago for Thanksgiving and we get on an L-1011, look out at the jet engines and you’ll see those same R’s.”

“Hubcap, so what?” She handed it back.

“Please,” he said. “If you have a Rolls-Royce, you call it a wheel covering. If you’re rich enough to own a Rolls, you can show off a little. When I make my second million, I’m going to buy one. Rolls-Royce Corniche. Then when Gage gets carsick, he can throw up into real leather.” And by the way, Ellie, what’s wrong? But it didn’t work like that with Ellie. You didn’t ask things directly. She was careful about sharing too much. It was something Louis admired.

“Are we rich, Daddy?”

“No,” he said, “but we won’t starve.”

“Michael Burns at school says all doctors are rich.”

“Well, you tell Michael Burns at school that many doctors get rich, but it takes twenty years… and you don’t get rich running a university clinic. You get rich as a specialist. A gynecologist or an orthopedist or a neurologist. They get rich faster. For general doctors like me, it takes longer.”

“Then why don’t you be a specialist, Daddy?”

Louis thought of his models again and how he had suddenly not wanted to build warplanes anymore, how he had also gotten tired of Tiger tanks and gun emplacements, how he had come to believe (almost suddenly, it seemed looking back) that building boats in bottles was pretty silly; and then he thought of what it would be like to spend your whole life checking children’s feet for hammertoe or putting on those thin Latex gloves so you could feel along a woman’s vaginal canal with one trained finger, feeling for bumps or sores.

“I just wouldn’t like it,” he said.

Church came into the office, stopped, looked at the situation with his bright green eyes. He jumped silently onto the windowsill and seemed to fall asleep.

Ellie glanced at him and frowned, which Louis thought was very strange. Usually, Ellie looked at Church with so much love it was almost sickening. She began to walk around the office, looking at the models, and said in a voice that was almost casual, “Wow, there were a lot of graves in the Pet Sematary, weren’t there?”

This is the heart of the matter, Louis thought, but he didn't look up. After checking his instructions, he started putting the carriage lamps on the Rolls.

“There were,” he said. “More than a hundred, I’d say.”

“Daddy, why don’t pets live as long as people?”

“Well, some animals do live about as long,” he said, “and some live much longer. Elephants live a very long time, and some sea turtles are so old that people don’t really know how old they are… or maybe they do, and they just can’t believe it.”

Ellie quickly dismissed that. “Elephants and sea turtles aren’t pets. Pets don’t live very long at all. Michael Burns says that every year a dog lives, it’s like nine of our years.”

“Seven,” Louis corrected without thinking. “I know what you mean, honey, and there’s some truth to it. A dog who lives to be twelve is an old dog. It’s because of something called metabolism, which seems to tell time. It does other things too—some people can eat a lot and stay thin because of their metabolism, like your mother. Other people—like me—can’t eat as much without getting fat. Our metabolisms are different, that’s all. But mostly, metabolism seems to act like a body clock for living things. Dogs have a fast metabolism. Human beings have a much slower metabolism. Most of us live to be about seventy-two. And believe me, seventy-two years is a very long time.”

Because Ellie looked really worried, he hoped he sounded more sincere than he felt. He was thirty-five, and it seemed like those years had passed as quickly as a draft under a door. “Sea turtles, on the other hand, have an even slower metabo—”

“What about cats?” Ellie asked, looking at Church again.

“Well, cats live about as long as dogs,” he said, “mostly.” This was a lie, and he knew it. Cats lived dangerous lives and often died violent deaths, usually just out of sight. Here was Church, dozing in the sun (or pretending to), Church who slept peacefully on his daughter’s bed every night, Church who had been so cute as a kitten, all tangled up in a ball of string. And yet Louis had seen him stalk a bird with a broken wing, his green eyes sparkling with curiosity and—yes, Louis would have sworn it—cold delight. He rarely killed what he stalked, but there had been one exception—a large rat, probably caught in the alley between their apartment building and the next. Church had really finished that rat off. It had been so bloody that Rachel, who was six months pregnant with Gage, had had to run to the bathroom and throw up. Dangerous lives, violent deaths. A dog would catch them and rip them open instead of just chasing them like the silly, easily fooled dogs in cartoons, or another tomcat would get them, or poisoned bait, or a car. Cats were like gangsters in the animal world, living outside the law and often dying there. Many of them never grew old by the fire.

But those were maybe not things to tell your five-year-old daughter, who was just beginning to understand what death meant.

“I mean,” he said, “Church is only three now, and you’re five. He could still be alive when you’re fifteen, in high school. And that’s a long time from now.”

“It doesn’t seem long to me,” Ellie said, her voice now shaking. “Not long at all.”

Louis stopped pretending to work on his model and asked her to come over. She sat on his lap, and he was again struck by how beautiful she was, made even more so by her being upset. She had dark skin, almost Middle Eastern looking. Tony Benton, a doctor he had worked with in Chicago, used to call her the Indian Princess.

“Honey,” he said, “if I could, I’d let Church live to be a hundred. But I don’t make the rules.”

“Who does?” she asked, and then, with great disdain: “God, I guess.”

Louis stopped himself from laughing. It was too serious.

“God or Someone,” he said. “Things just stop working—that’s all I know. Nothing is certain, babe.”

“I don’t want Church to be like all those dead pets!” she cried, suddenly tearful and angry. “I don’t want Church to ever die! He’s my cat! He’s not God’s cat! Let God have His own cat! Let God have all the old cats He wants, and kill them all! Church is mine!”

There were footsteps in the kitchen, and Rachel looked in, surprised. Ellie was now crying against Louis’s chest. The horror had been spoken; it was out in the open; it had been described and could be faced. Now, even if it couldn’t be changed, it could at least be cried about.

“Ellie,” he said, rocking her, “Ellie, Ellie, Church isn’t dead; he’s right over there, sleeping.”

“But he could be,” she wept. “He could be, any time.”

He held her and rocked her, believing, rightly or wrongly, that Ellie cried because death couldn't be stopped, that it didn't care about arguments or a little girl’s tears; that she cried because it was so cruel and unpredictable; and that she cried because people could turn symbols into ideas that were either good and noble or horribly scary. If all those animals had died and been buried, then Church could die

(any time!)

and be buried; and if that could happen to Church, it could happen to her mother, her father, her baby brother. To herself. Death was just an idea; the Pet Sematary was real. The rough markers held truths that even a child’s hands could feel.

It would be easy to lie now, like he had about how long cats live. But she would remember a lie later and maybe add it to the list children make about their parents. His own mother had told him a lie, a small one about women finding babies in the grass when they wanted them, and even though it wasn't a big deal, Louis had never forgiven his mother for telling it—or himself for believing it.

“Honey,” he said, “it happens. It’s a part of life.”

“It’s a bad part!” she cried. “It’s a really bad part!”

There was no answer for this. She wept. Eventually she would stop crying. It was the first thing she had to do to accept a truth that wouldn't go away.

He held his daughter and listened to church bells on Sunday morning, floating across the September fields; and it was some time after she had stopped crying that he realized she had fallen asleep, like Church.

He put her up in her bed and then came downstairs to the kitchen, where Rachel was beating cake batter too hard. He mentioned his surprise that Ellie should just conk out like that in the middle of the morning; it wasn’t like her.

“No,” Rachel said, setting the bowl down on the counter with a decisive thump. “It isn’t, but I think she was awake most of last night. I heard her tossing around, and Church cried to go out around three. He only does that when she’s restless.”

“Why would she…?”

“Oh, you know why!” Rachel said, angrily. “That damned pet cemetery is why! It really upset her, Lou. It was the first cemetery of any kind for her, and it just… upset her. I don’t think I’ll write your friend Jud Crandall any thank-you notes for that little hike.”

All at once he’s my friend, Louis thought, amused and troubled at the same time.

“Rachel—”

“And I don’t want her going up there again.”

“Rachel, what Jud said about the path is true.”

“It’s not the path and you know it,” Rachel said. She picked up the bowl again and began beating the cake batter even faster. “it’s that damned place. It’s unhealthy. Kids going up there and tending the graves, keeping the path… really sick is what it is. Whatever problems the kids in this town have got, I don’t want Ellie to catch it.”

Louis stared at her, puzzled. He strongly suspected that one of the things which had kept their marriage together when it seemed as if each year brought the news that two or three of their friends’ marriages had fallen apart was their respect for the unknown—the half-understood but never spoken idea that maybe, when you got right down to the heart of things, there was no such thing as marriage, no such thing as union, that each person stood alone and ultimately defied reason. That was the mystery. And no matter how well you thought you knew your partner, you occasionally ran into blank walls or fell into pits. And sometimes (rarely, thank God) you ran into a full-fledged area of alien strangeness, something like the clear-air turbulence that can buffet an airliner for no reason at all. An attitude or belief which you had never suspected, one so peculiar (at least to you) that it seemed nearly insane. And then you trod lightly, if you valued your marriage and your peace of mind; you tried to remember that anger at such a discovery was for fools who really believed it was possible for one mind to know another.

“Honey, it’s just a pet cemetery,” he said.

“The way she was crying in there,” Rachel said, pointing toward his office with a batter-covered spoon, “do you think it’s just a pet cemetery to her? It’s going to hurt her, Lou. No. She’s not going up there anymore. It’s not the path, it’s the place. She already thinks Church is going to die.”

For a moment, Louis felt like he was talking to Ellie. As if she were wearing stilts, one of her mother’s dresses, and a very good Rachel mask. Even her face was the same—serious and a bit sad on the surface, but hurt underneath.

He struggled, because suddenly the problem felt big to him, not something to ignore because of that mystery… or that loneliness. He struggled because it seemed like she was missing something huge, and you couldn’t miss it unless you were closing your eyes on purpose.

“Rachel,” he said, “Church is going to die.”

She stared at him, angry. “That’s not the point,” she said, speaking slowly and clearly, like she was talking to a child. “Church isn’t going to die today, or tomorrow—”

“I tried to tell her that—”

“Or the day after that, or probably for years—”

“Honey, we can’t know for sure—”

“Of course, we can!” she shouted. “We take good care of him, he’s not going to die, no one is going to die around here, so why do you want to upset a little girl about something she won’t understand until she’s older?”

“Rachel, listen.”

But Rachel wasn’t going to listen. She was furious. “It’s hard enough to deal with a death—a pet or a friend or family—when it happens, without making it into a… a goddam tourist spot… a F-F-Forest Lawn for a-animals…” Tears ran down her face.

“Rachel,” he said and tried to touch her shoulders. She quickly and sharply shook him off.

“Forget it,” she said. “You don’t understand at all.”

He sighed. “I feel like I fell into a secret hole and into a huge blender,” he said, hoping she’d smile. She didn’t; her eyes were fixed on his, dark and blazing. She was enraged, he realized; not just mad, but totally enraged. “Rachel,” he said suddenly, not sure what he’d say until it came out, “how did you sleep last night?”

“Oh please,” she said dismissively, turning away—but he saw a flash of pain in her eyes. “That’s really smart. Really smart. You never change, Louis. When things go wrong, blame Rachel, right? Rachel’s just being overly emotional.”

“That’s not right.”

“No?” She took the cake batter to the counter by the stove and set it down with a bang. She started greasing a cake pan, her lips tightly pressed together.

He said calmly, “It’s okay for a child to learn about death, Rachel. Actually, it’s important. Ellie’s reaction—her crying—seemed normal to me. It—”

“Oh, it sounded normal,” Rachel said, turning to face him again. “It sounded very normal to hear her crying her heart out over her perfectly healthy cat—”

“Stop it,” he said. “You’re not making any sense.”

“I don’t want to talk about it anymore.”

“Yes, but we’re going to,” he said, now angry too. “You had your say—now let me have mine?”

“She’s not going up there anymore. And as far as I’m concerned, that’s the end of it.”

“Ellie has known where babies come from since last year,” Louis said carefully. “We got her the Myers book and talked to her about it, remember? We both agreed that children should know where they come from.”

“That has nothing to do with—”

“It does, though!” he said harshly. “When I was talking to her in my office, about Church, I started thinking about my mother and how she told me that old story about babies coming from cabbage leaves when I asked her where women got babies. I’ve never forgotten that lie. I don’t think children ever forget the lies their parents tell them.”

“Where babies come from has nothing to do with a goddamn pet cemetery!” Rachel shouted at him, and her eyes told him Talk about the similarities all night and all day, if you want to, Louis; talk until you’re blue in the face, but I won’t accept it.

Still, he tried.

“She knows about babies; that place up in the woods just made her want to know something about what happens at the other end of life. It’s perfectly natural. In fact, I think it’s the most natural thing i—”

“Will you stop saying that!” she screamed suddenly—really screamed and Louis recoiled, startled. His elbow hit the open bag of flour on the counter. It fell off the edge and hit the floor, splitting open. Flour puffed up in a dry white cloud.

“Oh fuck,” he said sadly.

In an upstairs room, Gage began to cry.

“That’s nice,” she said, also crying now. “You woke the baby up too. Thanks for a nice, quiet, stress-free Sunday morning.”

She started past him and Louis put a hand on her arm. “Let me ask you something,” he said, “because I know that anything—literally anything—can happen to people. As a doctor I know that. Do you want to be the one to explain to her what happened if her cat gets sick or cancer—cats get cancer a lot, you know—or if he gets run over in that road? Do you want to be the one, Rachel?”

“Let me go,” she almost hissed. The anger in her voice was weaker than the hurt and confused fear in her eyes—I don’t want to talk about this, Louis, and you can’t make me, that look said. “Let me go, I want to get Gage before he falls out of his cr—”

“Because maybe you should be the one,” he said. “You can tell her we don’t talk about it, nice people don’t talk about it, they just bury it—oops! but don’t say ‘buried,’ you’ll upset her.”

“I hate you!” Rachel sobbed and pulled away from him.

Then he was of course sorry, and it was of course too late.

“Rachel—”

She pushed by him roughly, crying harder. “Leave me alone. You’ve done enough.” She paused in the kitchen doorway, turning toward him, the tears streaming down her cheeks. “I don’t want this discussed in front of Ellie anymore, Lou. I mean it. There’s nothing natural about death. Nothing. You, as a doctor, should know that.”

She whirled and was gone, leaving Louis in the empty kitchen, which still echoed with their voices. At last he went to the pantry to get the broom. As he swept, he thought about the last thing she had said and how huge this disagreement was, one they hadn't seen for so long. Because, as a doctor, he knew that death was, except maybe for childbirth, the most natural thing in the world. Taxes weren't a sure thing; human fights weren't; society's problems weren't; good and bad times weren't. In the end, there was only time passing, and the gravestones, which became worn and unreadable over time. Even sea turtles and the giant sequoias had to give out someday.

“Zelda,” he said aloud. “Christ, that must have been bad for her.”

The question was, should he just ignore it or should he try to fix it?

He tipped the dustpan over the trash can, and flour slid out with a soft puff, covering the thrown-out cartons and empty cans.

Chapter 10

“Hope Ellie didn’t take it too hard,” Jud Crandall said. Louis thought, not for the first time, that the man had an odd—and uncomfortable—way of knowing exactly what was bothering him.

He, Jud, and Norma Crandall sat on the Crandalls’ porch in the cool evening, drinking iced tea instead of beer. On Route 15, traffic was heavy with people going home after the weekend. Louis figured they knew this might be the last good weekend of the summer. Tomorrow, he'd be back to work at the University of Maine infirmary. Students had been arriving all day yesterday and today, filling apartments in Orono and dorms on campus, making their beds, seeing old friends, and probably complaining about early classes and cafeteria food. Rachel had been cold to him all day—no, freezing was more like it—and he knew that when he went home tonight, she'd already be in bed, Gage sleeping with her, the two of them so far over on her side that the baby might fall off. His side of the bed would have grown to three quarters of the bed, all of it looking like a big, empty desert.

“I said I hoped—”

“Sorry,” Louis said. “Just thinking. She was a little upset, yeah. How did you know?”

“Seen it happen plenty of times,” Jud said. Jud gently took his wife’s hand and grinned at her. “Haven’t we, dear?”

“Lots and lots,” Norma Crandall said. “We like the children.”

“Sometimes that pet cemetery is the first time they really face death,” Jud said. “They see people die on TV, but they know it’s not real, like those old Westerns they used to show at the movies on Saturday afternoons. On TV and in those movies, they just hold their stomachs or chests and fall over. That place up on the hill seems a lot more real to most of them than all those movies and TV shows, you know.”

Louis nodded, thinking: Tell my wife that, why don’t you?

“Some kids aren’t affected at all, or at least it doesn’t show, but I’d guess most of them kind of… kind of take it home with them to think about later, like all the other stuff they collect. Most of them are fine. But some… you remember the little Holloway boy, Norma?”

She nodded. Ice chattered softly in the glass she held. Her glasses hung on her chest, and the headlights of a passing car lit up the chain for a moment. “He had such nightmares,” she said. “Dreams about dead bodies coming out of the ground and all sorts of things. Then his dog died—ate some poisoned bait, that’s all anyone in town could figure, wasn’t it, Jud?”

“Poisoned bait,” Jud said, nodding. “That’s what most people thought, yes. That was 1925. Billy Holloway was maybe ten then. Went on to become a state senator. Ran for the U.S. House of Representatives later, but he lost. That was just before Korea.”
