“He and some of his friends had a funeral for the dog,” Norma remembered. “It was just a mutt, but he loved it a lot. I remember his parents were a little against the burying, because of the bad dreams and all, but it went off fine. Two of the bigger boys made a coffin, didn’t they, Jud?”

Jud nodded and finished his iced tea. “Dean and Dana Hall,” he said. “Them and that other kid Billy hung out with—I can’t remember his first name, but I’m sure he was one of the Bowie kids. You remember the Bowies that used to live up on Middle Drive in the old Brochette house, Norma?”

“Yes!” Norma said, as excited as if it had happened yesterday… and perhaps in her mind, it seemed that way. “It was a Bowie! Alan or Burt—”

“Or maybe it was Kendall,” Jud agreed. “Anyways, I remember they had a pretty good argument about who was going to be pallbearers. The dog wasn’t very big, so there was only room for two. The Hall boys said they should be the ones since they made the coffin, and also because they were twins—sort of a matching pair, you see. Billy said they didn’t know Bowser—that was the dog—well enough to be the pallbearers. ‘My dad says only close friends get to be pallbearers,’ was his argument, ‘not just any carpenter.’ Jud and Norma both laughed at this, and Louis grinned.

“They were just about ready to fight over it when Mandy Holloway, Billy’s sister, brought out the fourth volume of the Encyclopedia Britannica,” Jud said. “Her dad, Stephen Holloway, was the only doctor around here in those days, Louis, and they were the only family in Ludlow that could afford a set of encyclopedias.”

“They were also the first to have electric lights,” Norma broke in.

“Anyway,” Jud resumed, “Mandy came out all flustered, head up and acting important, all of eight years old, petticoats flying, that big book in her arms. Billy and the Bowie kid—I think it must have been Kendall, the one who crashed and burned up in Pensacola where they were training fighter pilots in early 1942—they were getting ready to take on the Hall twins over the privilege of carrying that poor old poisoned mutt up to the graveyard.”

Louis started giggling. Soon he was laughing out loud. He could feel the day's tension—old feelings left from the bitter argument with Rachel—beginning to loosen.

“So she says, ‘Wait! Wait! Look at this!’ And they all stop and look. And damn if she isn’t—”

“Jud,” Norma said, warningly.

“Sorry, dear; I get carried away telling stories, you know that.”

“I guess you do,” she said.

“And darned if she doesn’t have that book open to FUNERALS, and there’s a picture of Queen Victoria getting her last goodbye, and there are about forty—eleven people on each side of her coffin, some sweating and struggling to lift the thing, some just standing around in their funeral coats and fancy collars like they were waiting for the race to start. And Mandy says, ‘When it’s a ceremonial funeral of state, you can have as many as you want! The book says so!’ ”

“That solved it?” Louis asked.

“That did the trick. They ended up with about twenty kids, and damn if they didn’t look just like the picture Mandy had found, except maybe for the ruffles and tall hats. Mandy took charge, she did. Got them lined up and gave each of them a wildflower—a dandelion or a lady’s slipper or a daisy—and off they went. By golly, I always thought the country missed out when Mandy Holloway never got voted to the U.S. Congress.” He laughed and shook his head. “Anyway, that was the end of Billy’s bad dreams about the Pet Sematary. He mourned his dog and finished mourning and moved on. Which is what we all do, I guess.”

Louis thought again of Rachel’s near-hysteria.

“Your Ellie will get over it,” Norma said and shifted position. “You must be thinking that death is all we talk about around here, Louis. Jud and I are getting older, but I hope neither of us has gotten too morbid yet—”

“No, of course not, don’t be silly,” Louis said.

“—But it’s not a bad idea to know something about it. These days… I don’t know… no one wants to talk about it or think about it, it seems. They took it off TV because they thought it might hurt the children somehow… hurt their minds… and people want closed coffins so they don’t have to look at the body or say goodbye… it just seems like people want to forget it.”

“And at the same time they brought in cable TV with all those movies showing people”—Jud looked at Norma and cleared his throat—”showing people doing what people usually do in private,” he finished. “Funny how things change from one generation to the next, isn’t it?”

“Yes,” Louis said. “I suppose it is.”

“Well, we come from a different time,” Jud said, sounding almost apologetic “We knew death better. We saw the flu after the Great War, and mothers dying in childbirth, and children dying from infections and fevers that doctors seem to fix with ease these days. When me and Norma were young, if you got cancer, that was a death sentence. No radiation treatments back in the 1920s! Two wars, murders, suicides…”

He fell silent for a moment.

“We knew it as a friend and as an enemy,” he said finally. “My brother Pete died from a burst appendix in 1912, back when Taft was President. Pete was just fourteen, and he could hit a baseball farther than any kid in town. Back then, you didn’t need a college course to study death, or whatever they call it. Back then it came into the house and said hello, sometimes it ate with you, and sometimes you felt it bite you.”

This time Norma didn’t correct him; instead she nodded silently.

Louis stood up, stretched. “I have to go,” he said. “Big day tomorrow.”

“Yes, the fun begins for you tomorrow, doesn’t it?” Jud said, also standing. Jud saw Norma was also trying to get up and gave her a hand. She rose with a grimace.

“Bad tonight, is it?” Louis asked.

“Not so bad,” she said.

“Put some heat on it when you go to bed.”

“I will,” Norma said. “I always do. And Louis… don’t worry about Ellie. She’ll be too busy making new friends this fall to worry much about that old place. Maybe someday they’ll all go up and repaint some of the signs, or pull weeds, or plant flowers. Sometimes they do, when they feel like it. And she’ll feel better about it. She’ll start to get used to it.”

Not if my wife has anything to say about it.

“Come over tomorrow night and tell me how it went at the college, if you get the chance,” Jud said. “I’ll beat you at cribbage.”

“Well, maybe I’ll get you drunk first,” Louis said. “Shut you out completely.”

“Doc,” Jud said with great sincerity, “the day I get shut out at cribbage is the day I’d let a quack like you treat me.”

He left on their laughter and crossed the road to his own house in the late-summer dark.

Rachel was sleeping with the baby, curled up on her side of the bed in a fetal, protective position. He supposed she would get over it—there had been other arguments and times of coldness in their marriage, but this one was surely the worst of them all. He felt sad and angry and unhappy all at the same time, wanting to make things right but not sure how, not even sure that he should be the first to try. It was all so pointless—just a puff of wind somehow blown up to hurricane size by a trick of the mind. Other fights and arguments, yes, sure, but only a few as bitter as the one over Ellie’s tears and questions. He supposed it didn’t take many blows like that before the marriage was damaged… and then one day, instead of reading about it in a note from a friend (“Well, I suppose I should tell you before you hear it from someone else, Lou; Maggie and I are splitting…”) or in the newspaper, it was you.

He undressed to his shorts quietly and set the alarm for 6 A.M. Then he showered, washed his hair, shaved, and crunched up a Rolaid before brushing his teeth—Norma’s iced tea had given him acid indigestion. Or maybe it was coming home and seeing Rachel way over on her side of the bed. Territory is what defines everything else, hadn’t he read that in some college history class?

Everything done, the evening neatly finished, he went to bed… but couldn’t sleep. Something else bothered him. The last two days replayed in his mind as he listened to Rachel and Gage breathing almost together. GEN. PATTON… HANNAH THE BEST DOG THAT EVER LIVED… MARIA OUR PET RABIT… Ellie, angry. I don’t want Church to ever be dead!… He’s not God’s cat! Let God have His own cat! Rachel, just as angry. You as a doctor should know… Norma Crandall saying It just seems like people want to forget it… And Jud, his voice very sure, very certain, a voice from another time: Sometimes it ate with you and sometimes you felt it bite you.

And that voice mixed with his mother’s, who had lied to Louis Creed about sex at four but told him the truth about death at twelve, when his cousin Ruthie died in a stupid car accident. She was crushed in her father’s car by a kid who found the keys in a Public Works Department payloader and decided to take it for a ride and then couldn’t stop it. The kid had only minor cuts; his Uncle Carl’s Fairlane was destroyed. She can’t be dead, he said to his mother’s plain statement. He heard the words, but he didn’t understand them. What do you mean, she’s dead? What are you talking about? And then: Who’s going to bury her? Because although Ruthie’s father, Louis’s uncle, was an undertaker, he couldn’t imagine that Uncle Carl would do it. Confused and afraid, he focused on this question. It was a puzzle, like who cuts the town barber’s hair.

I guess Donny Donahue will do it, his mother said. Her eyes were red; she looked tired. His mother looked worn out. He’s your uncle’s best friend in the business. Oh, but Louis… Sweet little Ruthie… I can’t stand to think she suffered… pray with me, will you, Louis? Pray with me for Ruthie. I need you to help me.

So they knelt in the kitchen, he and his mother, and they prayed, and the praying made it real to him; if his mother was praying for Ruthie Creed’s soul, then her body was gone. He imagined Ruthie coming to his thirteenth birthday party with her decaying eyeballs hanging on her cheeks and blue mold growing in her red hair, and this image caused not just horror but a terrible love.

He cried out in the worst mental pain of his life, “She can’t be dead! MOMMA, SHE CAN’T BE DEAD—I LOVE HER!”

And his mother’s reply, her voice flat but full of images: dead fields under a November sky, scattered rose petals brown and turning up at the edges, empty pools covered with algae, rot, decay, dust:

She is, my darling. I’m sorry, but she is. Ruthie is gone.

Louis shuddered, thinking, Dead is dead—what else do you need?

Suddenly Louis knew what he had forgotten to do, why he was still awake on this night before his new job, thinking about old griefs.

He got up, headed for the stairs, and then went to Ellie’s room. She was sleeping peacefully, mouth open, wearing her blue baby-doll pajamas that she had outgrown. My God, Ellie, he thought, you’re growing fast. Church lay between her legs, also asleep. Sorry for the joke.

Downstairs, a bulletin board hung by the phone, covered with messages, notes, and bills. At the top, Rachel had written in neat caps: THINGS TO PUT OFF AS LONG AS POSSIBLE. Louis found the phone book, looked up a number, and wrote it on a blank note. Below it, he wrote: Quentin L. Jolander, D.V.M.—call about Church—if Jolander doesn’t neuter animals, he will recommend someone.

He looked at the note, wondering if it was time, but knowing it was. Something real had to come from all this bad feeling, and he had decided sometime between this morning and tonight—without even knowing it—that he didn’t want Church crossing the road anymore, if he could prevent it.

His old feelings about it returned, the idea that neutering would make the cat less, turn him into a fat old tomcat too soon, happy just to sleep on the radiator until fed. He didn’t want Church like that. He liked Church as he was, lean and tough.

Outside in the dark, a big truck rumbled by on Route 15, and that settled it. He pinned up the note and went to bed.

Chapter 11

The next morning at breakfast, Ellie saw the new memo on the bulletin board and asked him what it meant.

“It means he’s going to have a very small operation,” Louis said. “He’ll probably have to stay at the vet’s for one night afterward. And when he comes home, he’ll stay in our yard and not want to wander around so much.”

“Or cross the road?” Ellie asked.

She may be only five, Louis thought, but she’s certainly sharp. “Or cross the road,” he agreed.

“Yay!” Ellie said, and that was the end of the subject.

Louis, who had been ready for a difficult and maybe even frantic argument about Church being out of the house for even one night, was surprised by how easily she had agreed. And he realized how worried she must have been. Maybe Rachel wasn't completely wrong about how the Pet Sematary had affected her.

Rachel herself, who was feeding Gage his breakfast egg, gave him a grateful approving look, and Louis felt something loosen in his chest. The look told him that the tension was over; this particular conflict had ended. Forever, he hoped.

Later, after the big yellow school bus had taken Ellie away for the morning, Rachel came to him, put her arms around his neck, and kissed his mouth gently. “You were very sweet to do that,” she said, “and I’m sorry I was so awful.”

Louis returned her kiss, feeling a little uncomfortable nonetheless. It occurred to him that the I’m sorry I was so awful statement, while not exactly common, wasn't exactly something he’d never heard before either. It usually came after Rachel had gotten her way.

Gage, meanwhile, had waddled over to the front door and was looking out the bottom window at the empty road. “Bus,” he said, tugging casually at his droopy diaper. “Ellie-bus.”

“He’s growing up fast,” Louis said.

Rachel nodded. “Too fast for me, I think.”

“Wait until he’s out of diapers,” Louis said. “Then he can stop.”

She laughed, and everything was fine between them again—completely fine. She stepped back, adjusted his tie a little, and looked him up and down carefully.

“Do I look okay, Sarge?” he asked.

“You look very nice.”

“Yeah, I know. But do I look like a heart surgeon? A two-hundred-thousand-dollar-a-year man?”

“No, just old Lou Creed,” she said and giggled. “The rock-and-roll animal.”

Louis glanced at his watch. “The rock-and-roll animal has got to get going,” he said.

“Are you nervous?”

“Yeah, a little.”

“Don’t be,” she said. “It’s sixty-seven thousand dollars a year for putting on Ace bandages, prescribing for the flu and for hangovers, giving girls the pill—”

“Don’t forget the crab-and-louse lotion,” Louis said, smiling again. One of the things that had surprised him on his first tour of the infirmary had been the supplies of Quell, which seemed huge to him—more suited to an army base infirmary than one on a middle-sized university campus.

Miss Charlton, the head nurse, smiled cynically. “Off-campus apartments around here are pretty bad. You’ll see.”

He guessed he would.

“Have a good day,” she said and kissed him again, lingeringly. But when she pulled away, she was playfully stern. “And for Christ’s sake remember that you’re an administrator, not an intern or a second-year resident!”

“Yes, Doctor,” Louis said humbly, and they both laughed again. For a moment he thought of asking: Was it Zelda, babe? Is that what’s bothering you? Is that the problem? Zelda and how she died? But he wasn’t going to ask her that, not now. As a doctor he knew a lot of things, and while the fact that death was just as natural as childbirth might be the greatest, the fact that you don’t mess with a wound that has finally started to heal was also important.

So instead of asking, he only kissed her again and went out.

It was a good start, a good day. Maine was putting on a late-summer show, the sky was blue and cloudless, the temperature at a perfect seventy-two degrees. Driving to the end of the driveway and checking for traffic, Louis thought that so far he hadn’t seen any of the fall colors that were supposed to be so amazing. But he could wait.

He steered the Honda Civic they had bought as a second car toward the university and let it roll. Rachel would call the vet this morning, they would get Church neutered, and that would end this whole Pet Sematary nonsense (it was funny how that misspelling got into your head and began to seem right) and death fears. There was no need to be thinking about death on a beautiful September morning like this one.

Louis turned on the radio and dialed until he found the Ramones singing “Rockaway Beach.” He turned it up and sang along—not well but with hearty enjoyment.

Chapter 12

The first thing he noticed turning into the university grounds was how suddenly and dramatically the traffic increased. There were cars, bikes, and lots of joggers. He had to stop suddenly to avoid two joggers coming from Dunn Hall. Louis braked hard, tightening his seatbelt, and honked. He was always annoyed at how joggers (and bicyclists) seemed to think they had no responsibilities once they started moving. They were, after all, exercising. One of them flipped Louis off without looking. Louis sighed and drove on.

The second thing was that the ambulance was missing from its spot in the small infirmary parking lot, which worried him. The infirmary could handle almost any short-term illness or accident. It had three well-equipped exam rooms off the main lobby and two wards with fifteen beds each. But there was no operating room. For serious accidents, the ambulance rushed people to Eastern Maine Medical Center. Steve Masterton, the physician’s assistant who had shown Louis around, had proudly shown Louis the log from the past two years. There had only been thirty-eight ambulance trips in that time, which was good considering the student population was over ten thousand and the total university population was almost seventeen thousand.

And here he was, on his first day, and the ambulance was gone.

He parked in the spot with the freshly painted sign RESERVED FOR DR. CREED and hurried inside.

He found Charlton, a fit, graying woman of about fifty, in the first exam room, taking the temperature of a girl in jeans and a halter top. The girl had a bad sunburn, Louis saw, with peeling skin.

“Good morning, Joan,” he said. “Where’s the ambulance?”

“Oh, we had a real tragedy,” Charlton said, taking the thermometer from the student’s mouth and reading it. “Steve Masterton came in this morning at seven and found a big puddle under the engine and front wheels. The radiator broke. They took it away.”

“Great,” Louis said, relieved. At least it wasn’t out on a call, which he had feared. “When do we get it back?”

Joan Charlton laughed. “Knowing the University Motor Pool,” she said, “it’ll be back around December fifteenth, wrapped in Christmas ribbon.” She glanced at the student. “You have a slight fever,” she said. “Take two aspirins and stay out of bars and dark alleys.”

The girl got down. She gave Louis a quick look and then went out.

“Our first customer of the new semester,” Charlton said unhappily. She began to shake the thermometer down with quick snaps.

“You don’t seem too happy about it.”

“I know the type,” she said. “Oh, we get the other type too—athletes who keep playing with bone chips and tendonitis and everything else because they don’t want to be benched, they have to be macho men, not let the team down, even if they’re risking pro careers later on. Then you’ve got little Miss Half-Degree of Fever—” She jerked her head toward the window, where Louis could see the girl with the peeling sunburn walking toward the Gannett-Cumberland-Androscoggin dorms. In the examining room the girl had seemed like someone who didn't feel well but was trying not to show it. Now she was walking quickly, her hips swinging prettily, noticing and being noticed.

“Your basic college hypochondriac.” Charlton dropped the thermometer into a sterilizer. “We’ll see her two dozen times this year. Her visits will be more frequent before each round of midterms. A week or so before finals, she’ll be sure she has either mono or pneumonia. Bronchitis is the backup. She’ll get out of four or five tests—the ones where the instructors are weak, to use their word—and get easier makeups. They always get sicker if they know the midterm or final is going to be a multiple-choice test rather than an essay exam.”

“My, aren’t we cynical this morning,” Louis said. He was, in fact, a little surprised.

She winked at him, which made him grin. “I don’t worry about it. Doctor. Neither should you.”

“Where’s Stephen now?”

“In your office, answering mail and trying to understand the latest load of bureaucratic nonsense from Blue Cross-Blue Shield,” she said.

Louis went in. Despite Charlton’s cynicism, he felt comfortably at work.

Looking back, Louis would think—when he could bear to think about it at all—that the nightmare really began when they brought the dying boy, Victor Pascow, into the infirmary around ten that morning.

Until then, things were quiet. At nine, half an hour after Louis arrived, the two student volunteers who would be working the nine-to-three shift came in. Louis gave them each a doughnut and a cup of coffee and talked to them for about fifteen minutes, explaining their jobs, and more importantly, what they shouldn't do. Then Charlton took over. As she led them out of Louis’s office, Louis heard her ask: “Either of you allergic to shit or puke? You’ll see a lot of both here.”

“Oh God,” Louis murmured and covered his eyes. But he was smiling. A tough old woman like Charlton was not always a bad thing.

Louis began filling out the long insurance forms, which were a complete list of drugs and medical equipment (“Every year,” Steve Masterton said, annoyed. “Every goddam year the same thing. Why don’t you write down Complete heart transplant facility, worth about eight million dollars, Louis? That’ll fool them!”), and he was really focused, barely thinking about how good a cup of coffee would be, when Masterton screamed from the waiting room: “Louis! Hey, Louis, get out here! We got a mess!”

The panic in Masterton’s voice made Louis move fast. He jumped out of his chair almost as if he had been expecting this. A scream, as sharp as broken glass, came from where Masterton was shouting. It was followed by a slap and Charlton saying, “Stop that or get the hell out of here! Stop it right now!”

Louis ran into the waiting room and first noticed the blood—there was a lot of blood. One of the student volunteers was sobbing. The other, very pale, had her hands covering her mouth, pulling her lips into a wide, disgusted grin. Masterton was kneeling, trying to hold the head of the boy lying on the floor.

Steve looked up at Louis, his eyes wide and scared. He tried to speak, but nothing came out.

People were gathering at the Student Medical Center’s big glass doors, looking in, their hands around their faces to block the light. Louis suddenly thought of something strange: sitting in the living room as a young kid with his mother in the morning before she went to work, watching TV. Watching the old “Today” show, with Dave Garroway. People were outside, staring at Dave and Frank Blair and J. Fred Muggs. He looked around and saw other people standing at the windows. He couldn’t do anything about the doors, but—

“Shut the drapes,” he snapped at the student volunteer who had screamed.

When she didn’t move right away, Charlton slapped her butt. “Do it, girl!”

The candy-striper sprang into action. Instantly, green drapes were pulled shut over the windows. Charlton and Steve Masterton moved quickly between the boy on the floor and the doors, blocking the view as well as they could.

“Hard stretcher, Doctor?” Charlton asked.

“Get it if we need it,” Louis said, kneeling beside Masterton. “I haven’t even looked at him yet.”

“Come on,” Charlton said to the girl who had closed the drapes. She was pulling the corners of her mouth with her fists again, making that humorless, screaming grin. She looked at Charlton and moaned, “Oh, ag,”

“Yeah, oh, ag is right. Come on.” She gave the girl a hard tug and got her moving, her red and white striped skirt swishing against her legs.

Louis bent over his first patient at the University of Maine at Orono.

He was a young man, about twenty years old, and it took Louis less than three seconds to make the only important diagnosis: The young man was going to die. Half of his head was crushed. His neck was broken. One collarbone stuck out from his swollen and twisted right shoulder. From his head, blood and a yellow, oozing fluid slowly seeped into the carpet. Louis could see the man’s brain, whitish-gray and pulsing through a shattered section of skull. It was like looking through a broken window. The hole was about five centimeters wide; he could almost have delivered a baby from his skull, like Zeus delivering from his forehead. It was unbelievable that he was still alive. Suddenly, he heard Jud Crandall saying sometimes you could feel it bite you. And his mother: dead is dead. He felt a crazy urge to laugh. Dead was dead, all right. That’s for sure.

“Call for the ambulance,” he snapped at Masterton. “We—”

“Louis, the ambulance is—”

“Oh Christ,” Louis said, slapping his own forehead. He turned to Charlton. “Joan, what do you do in a case like this? Call Campus Security or the EMMC?”

Joan looked flustered and upset—something Louis rarely saw. But her voice was calm as she replied, “Doctor, I don’t know. We’ve never had anything like this happen while I’ve been at the Medical Center.”

Louis thought quickly. “Call the campus police. We can’t wait for EMMC to send an ambulance. If needed, they can take him to Bangor in a fire engine. At least it has a siren and lights. Go do it, Joan.”

She left, but not before he saw her deeply sympathetic glance and understood it. This young man, deeply tanned and muscular—maybe from a summer working on a road crew, painting houses, or teaching tennis—and now wearing only red gym shorts with white trim, was going to die no matter what. He would be just as dead even if their ambulance had been parked outside, engine running, when he arrived.

Incredibly, the dying man was moving. His eyes fluttered and opened. Blue eyes, the whites streaked with blood. They stared blankly around, seeing nothing. He tried to move his head, and Louis pressed down to stop him, remembering the broken neck. The head injury didn’t mean he couldn’t feel pain.

The hole in his head, oh God, the hole in his head.

“What happened to him?” he asked Steve, knowing it was a stupid and pointless question. A bystander’s question. But the hole in the man’s head confirmed his role; he was just a bystander. “Did the police bring him?”

“Some students brought him in a blanket. I don’t know what happened.”

He had to think about what to do next. That was his responsibility too. “Go find them,” Louis said. “Take them around to the other door. I want them nearby, but I don’t want them to see any more of this than they already have.”

Masterton, looking relieved to leave the room, went to the door and opened it, letting in a mix of excited, curious, confused voices. Louis could also hear a police siren. Campus Security was here. Louis felt a miserable sense of relief.

The dying man was gurgling. He tried to speak. Louis heard sounds—at least, they sounded like sounds—but the words were slurred and unclear.

Louis leaned over him and said, “You’re going to be all right, fella.” He thought of Rachel and Ellie as he said it, and his stomach gave a great, unpleasant lurch. He put a hand over his mouth and held back a burp.

“Caaa,” the young man said. “Gaaaaaa—”

Louis looked around and saw that he was alone for a moment with the dying man. He could hear Joan Charlton yelling at the candy-stripers that the hard stretcher was in the supply closet off Room Two. Louis doubted if they knew Room Two from anything; it was, after all, their first day on the job. They had gotten a terrible introduction to the world of medicine. The green carpet was now soaked a muddy purple in a growing circle around the young man’s ruined head; the leaking of brain fluid had, thankfully, stopped.

“In the Pet Sematary,” the young man croaked… and he began to grin. This grin was remarkably like the joyless, wild grin of the candy-striper who had closed the drapes.

Louis stared down at him, at first not believing what he had heard. Then Louis thought he must be hearing things. He made some more of those sounds and my mind made them into something clear, matched the sounds to my own experience. But that was not what had happened, and a moment later he had to realize it. A dizzying, crazy terror hit him and his skin began to crawl, seeming to actually move up and down his arms and along his belly in waves… but even then he just didn’t want to believe it. Yes, the words had been on the bloody lips of the man on the carpet as well as in Louis’s ears, but that only meant he was seeing and hearing things.

“What did you say?” he whispered.

And this time, as clear as the words of a talking parrot or a crow whose tongue had been cut, the words were easy to understand: “It’s not the real cemetery.” The eyes were empty, not seeing, edged with blood: the mouth grinning the wide grin of a dead fish.

Horror washed through Louis, grabbing his warm heart in its cold hands, squeezing. It made him smaller and smaller, until he felt like running from this bloody, twisted, talking head on the floor of the infirmary waiting room. He was a man with no strong religious beliefs, not interested in the strange or supernatural. He was not ready for this… whatever it was.

Fighting the urge to run with everything in him, he made himself lean even closer. “What did you say?” he asked again.

The grin. That was bad.

“The soil of a man’s heart is harder, Louis,” the dying man whispered. “A man grows what he can… and takes care of it.”

Louis, he thought, hearing nothing with his conscious mind after his own name. Oh my God he called me by my name.

“Who are you?” Louis asked in a trembling, weak voice. “Who are you?”

“Indian bring my fish.”

“How did you know my—”

“Stay away, us. Know—”

“You—”

“Caa,” the young man said, and now Louis thought he could smell death on his breath, internal injuries, lost rhythm, failure, ruin.

“What?” A crazy urge came to shake him.

“Gaaaaaaaa—”

The young man in the red gym shorts began to tremble all over. Suddenly he seemed to freeze, every muscle tight. His eyes briefly lost their blank look and seemed to find Louis’s eyes. Then everything released at once. There was a terrible smell. Louis thought he had to speak again. Then the eyes went back to their blank expression… and began to cloud over. The man was dead.

Louis sat back, barely noticing that all his clothes were sticking to him; he was soaked with sweat. Darkness bloomed, gently covering his eyes, and the world began to spin unpleasantly. Realizing what was happening, he turned away from the dead man, put his head down between his knees, and pressed his left thumb and forefinger nails into his gums hard enough to draw blood.

After a moment the world began to clear again.

Chapter 13

Then the room filled with people, like actors waiting for their lines. This made Louis feel even more unreal and lost. He had studied these feelings in psychology but never felt them himself, and their strength scared him. He guessed it was how someone would feel if someone slipped LSD into their drink.

Like a play just for me, he thought. The room is cleared so the dying Sibyl can give me a cryptic prophecy, and then everyone comes back as soon as he’s dead.

The candy-stripers clumsily brought in the hard stretcher used for spinal injuries. Joan Charlton followed, saying the campus police were coming. A young man had been hit by a car while jogging. Louis thought of the joggers he'd seen that morning and his stomach churned.

Behind Charlton came Steve Masterton with two Campus Security cops. “Louis, the people who brought Pascow in are…” He stopped and said sharply, “Louis, are you all right?”

“I’m okay,” he said and got up. He felt faint again, then it passed. He reached out. “Pascow is his name?”

One of the campus cops said, “Victor Pascow, according to the girl he was jogging with.”

Louis glanced at his watch and subtracted two minutes. From the room where Masterton had put the people who brought Pascow in, he heard a girl sobbing loudly. Welcome back to school, he thought. Have a nice semester. “Mr. Pascow died at 10:09 A.M.,” he said.

One of the cops wiped his mouth with the back of his hand.

Masterton said again, “Louis, are you really okay? You look terrible.”

Louis opened his mouth to answer, and one of the candy-stripers suddenly dropped her end of the hard stretcher and ran out, vomiting down the front of her pinafore. A phone began to ring. The girl who had been sobbing now began to scream the dead man’s name—”Vic! Vic! Vic!”—over and over. Chaos. Confusion. One of the cops was asking Charlton if they could have a blanket to cover him up, and Charlton was saying she didn’t know if she had the right to get one, and Louis found himself thinking of a line from Maurice Sendak: “Let the wild rumpus start!”

Those awful giggles rose in his throat again, and somehow he managed to hold them back. Had this Pascow really said the words Pet Sematary? Had this Pascow really spoken his name? Those were the things that were throwing him off balance, the things that had sent him spinning out of control. But already his mind seemed to be wrapping those few moments in a protective film—shaping, changing, disconnecting. Surely he had said something else (if he had indeed spoken at all), and in the shock and sad emotion of the moment, Louis had misunderstood it. More likely, Pascow had only moved his lips, as he had at first thought.

Louis looked for himself, for that part of himself that had caused the administration to give him this job over the other fifty-three applicants for the position. There was no one in charge here, no progress; the room was full of people milling around.

“Steve, go give that girl a sedative,” he said, and just saying the words made him feel better. It was as if he were in a rocketship taking off now, moving away from a tiny moon. Said moon being, of course, that irrational moment when Pascow had spoken. Louis had been hired to take charge; he was going to do it.

“Joan. Give the cop a blanket.”

“Doctor, we haven’t taken inventory—”

“Give it to him anyway. Then check on that candy-striper.” He looked at the other girl, who still held her end of the hard stretcher. She was staring at Pascow’s remains with a kind of hypnotized fascination. “Candy-striper!” Louis said sharply, and her eyes snapped away from the body.

“W-W-Wh—”

“What’s the other girl’s name?”

“W-Who?”

“The one who threw up,” he said, his voice hard.

“Juh-Juh-Judy. Judy DeLessio.”

“Your name?”

“Carla.” The girl sounded a bit calmer now.

“Carla, go check on Judy. And get that blanket. There’s a stack of them in the small supply closet near Examining Room One. Go on, all of you. Let’s try to look like we know what we’re doing.”

They started moving. Soon, the screaming in the other room died down. The phone, which had stopped, started ringing again. Louis pressed the hold button without answering it.

The older campus cop looked more composed, and Louis spoke to him. “Who do we need to tell? Can you give me a list?”

The cop nodded and said, “We haven’t had one of these in six years. This is a terrible way to start the semester.”

“It is,” Louis said. He picked up the phone and pressed the hold button again.

“Hello? Who is—” an excited voice started, but Louis interrupted. He began to make his calls.

Chapter 14

Things didn't slow down until almost four that afternoon, after Louis and Richard Irving, the head of Campus Security, spoke to the press. The young man, Victor Pascow, had been jogging with two friends, one of them his fiancée. A car driven by Tremont Withers, twenty-three, of Haven, Maine, had come up the road from the Lengyll Women’s Gymnasium toward the center of campus very fast. Withers’s car had hit Pascow and driven him head-first into a tree. Pascow had been brought to the infirmary in a blanket by his friends and two people who were passing by. He had died minutes later. Withers was being held, facing charges of reckless driving, driving under the influence, and vehicular manslaughter.

The editor of the campus newspaper asked if he could say that Pascow had died of head injuries. Louis, thinking of that broken window through which the brain itself could be seen, said he would rather let the Penobscot County coroner announce the cause of death. The editor then asked if the four young people who had brought Pascow to the infirmary in the blanket might have accidentally caused his death.

“No,” Louis replied. “Not at all. Sadly, Mr. Pascow was, in my opinion, fatally wounded when he was hit.”

There were other questions—a few—but that answer really ended the press conference. Now Louis sat in his office (Steve Masterton had gone home an hour before, right after the press conference—to see himself on the evening news, Louis suspected) trying to pick up the pieces of the day—or maybe he was just trying to hide what had happened, to put a thin layer of normalcy over it. He and Charlton were going over the cards in the “front file”—those students who were struggling through their college years despite some disability. There were twenty-three diabetics in the front file, fifteen epileptics, fourteen paraplegics, and others: students with leukemia, students with cerebral palsy and muscular dystrophy, blind students, two mute students, and one case of sickle-cell anemia, which Louis had never even seen.

Perhaps the worst moment of the afternoon had come just after Steve left. Charlton came in and put a pink memo slip on Louis’s desk. Bangor Carpet will be here at 9:00 tomorrow, it read.

“Carpet?” he had asked.

“It will have to be replaced,” she said apologetically. “There’s no way the stain’s going to come out, Doctor.”

Of course not. At that point Louis had gone into the dispensary and taken a Tuinal—what his first med school roommate had called Tooners. “Hop on the Toonerville Trolley, Louis,” he’d say, “and I’ll put on some Credence.” More often than not, Louis had declined the ride on the fabled Toonerville, and that was maybe just as well; his roomie had flunked out halfway through his third semester and had ridden the Toonerville Trolley all the way to Vietnam as a medical corpsman. Louis sometimes pictured him over there, stoned, listening to Credence do “Run Through the Jungle.”

But he needed something. If he was going to have to see that pink slip about the carpet on his note-minder board every time he glanced up from the front file spread out in front of them, he needed something.

He was doing okay when Mrs. Baillings, the night nurse, stuck her head in and said, “Your wife, Dr. Creed. Line one.”

Louis looked at his watch and saw it was almost five-thirty; he had planned to leave an hour and a half ago.

“Okay, Nancy. Thanks.”

He picked up the phone and hit line one. “Hi, honey. Just leaving—”

“Louis, are you okay?”

“Yeah, fine”

“I heard about it on the news. Lou, I’m so sorry.” She stopped for a moment. “It was on the radio news. They had you on, answering a question. You sounded fine.”

“Did I? Good.”

“Are you sure you’re okay?”

“Yes Rachel. I’m fine.”

“Come home,” she said.

“Yes,” he said. The idea of home appealed to him.

Chapter 15

She met him at the door, and he was stunned. She was wearing the net bra he liked, see-through panties, and nothing else.

“You look amazing,” he said. “Where are the kids?”

“Missy Dandridge took them. We’re alone until eight-thirty… which is two and a half hours. Let’s not waste it.”

She pressed close to him. He smelled a faint, beautiful scent—was it roses? He put his arms around her, first her waist, then his hands found her behind as her tongue moved lightly over his lips and into his mouth, teasing and exploring.

Finally, they stopped kissing, and he asked, a little breathless, “Are you for dinner?”

“Dessert,” she said, then slowly and sexily moved her hips against his groin and stomach. “But I promise you don’t have to do anything you don’t want to.”

He reached for her, but she slipped away and took his hand, “Upstairs first,” she said.

She drew him a very warm bath, then slowly undressed him and told him to get in. She took the slightly rough sponge-glove that usually hung untouched in the shower, gently soaped his body, then rinsed him off. He felt the day—this awful first day—slowly washing away. She was very wet, and her panties stuck to her skin.

Louis started to get out of the tub, and she gently pushed him back.

“What—”

Now the sponge-glove gripped him gently—gently but with almost unbearable friction, moving slowly up and down.

“Rachel—” Sweat covered him, and not just from the hot water.

“Shush.”

It seemed endless—he would almost climax, and the hand in the sponge-glove would slow, almost stop. Then it didn’t stop but squeezed, loosened, squeezed again, until he came so hard that he felt his eardrums bulge.

“My God,” he said shakily when he could speak again. “Where did you learn that?”

“Girl Scouts,” she said primly.

She made stroganoff which had been simmering while he was in the tub, and Louis, who would have sworn at four o’clock that he wouldn't be hungry until Halloween, ate two helpings.

Then she led him upstairs again.

“Now,” she said, “let’s see what you can do for me.”

Overall, Louis thought he handled things pretty well.

Later, Rachel put on her old blue pajamas. Louis wore a flannel shirt and baggy corduroy pants—what Rachel called his grubs—and went to get the kids.

Missy Dandridge asked about the accident, and Louis told her about it, sharing fewer details than she'd likely find in the Bangor Daily News the next day. He didn’t enjoy it—it made him feel like a terrible gossip—but Missy wouldn't take money for babysitting, and he was thankful for the evening he and Rachel had enjoyed.

Gage was sound asleep before Louis drove the mile from Missy’s house to their own; even Ellie was yawning and tired. He changed Gage’s diaper, put him in his sleeper, and placed him in his crib. Then he read Ellie a story. As usual, she asked for Where the Wild Things Are, being a wild thing herself. Louis got her to accept The Cat in the Hat instead. She was asleep five minutes after he carried her upstairs, and Rachel tucked her in.

When he came downstairs, Rachel was in the living room with milk. A Dorothy Sayers mystery was open on her leg.

“Louis, are you really okay?”

“Honey, I’m fine,” he said. “And thanks. For everything.”

“We try our best,” she said with a playful smile. “Are you going to Jud’s for a beer?”

He shook his head. “Not tonight. I’m exhausted.”

“I hope I helped with that.”

“I think you did.”

“Then grab yourself a glass of milk, Doctor, and let’s go to bed.”

He thought he might lie awake, as he often had when he was an intern, replaying particularly rough days in his mind. But he drifted easily to sleep, as if sliding down a smooth, gentle slope. He had read that it takes most people just seven minutes to fully relax and disconnect from the day. Seven minutes for the conscious and subconscious to turn, like a spooky rotating wall in a funhouse. There was something a little strange about that.

He was almost asleep when he heard Rachel say, as if from far away, “… day after tomorrow.”

“Ummmmmm?”

“Jolander. The vet. He’s taking Church the day after tomorrow.”

“Oh.” Church. Enjoy your balls while you have them, Church, old boy. Then he slipped away from everything, down a hole, sleeping deeply and without dreams.

Chapter 16

Something woke him much later, a crash loud enough that he sat up in bed, wondering if Ellie had fallen or Gage's crib had broken. Then the moon came out from behind a cloud, filling the room with cold light, and he saw Victor Pascow in the doorway. The crash had been Pascow throwing the door open.

He stood there with his head smashed in behind his left temple. The dried blood on his face made maroon stripes like warpaint. His collarbone stuck out, white. He was grinning.

"Come on, Doctor," Pascow said. "We have places to go."

Louis looked around. His wife was a shape under her yellow blanket, sound asleep. He looked back at Pascow, who was dead but somehow not. Yet Louis felt no fear. He quickly knew why.

It’s a dream, he thought, and only in his relief did he realize he had been afraid. The dead don’t come back; it’s impossible. This young man is in a drawer in Bangor with the pathologist’s mark—a Y-cut sewn back up—on him. The pathologist probably threw his brain back into his chest after taking a sample and filled the skull with paper to stop leaks—easier than fitting the brain back in like a puzzle piece. Uncle Carl, father of poor Ruthie, had told him pathologists did that, and other things that would terrify Rachel with her fear of death. But Pascow wasn’t here—no way. Pascow was in a cold locker with a tag on his toe. And he’s certainly not wearing those red shorts in there.

Yet the urge to get up was strong. Pascow was watching him.

He threw back the covers and put his feet on the floor. The rug—a gift from Rachel’s grandmother years ago—felt cold under his feet. The dream felt very real. It was so real that he wouldn’t follow Pascow until Pascow turned and walked back down the stairs. The urge to follow was strong, but he didn’t want to be touched by a walking corpse, even in a dream.

But he did follow. Pascow’s shorts shone.

They crossed the living room, dining room, kitchen. Louis thought Pascow would unlock and unlatch the door between the kitchen and the shed where he parked the station wagon and Civic, but Pascow didn’t. Instead, he simply walked through the door. And Louis, watching, thought with mild surprise: Is that how it’s done? Amazing! Anyone could do that!

He tried it himself—and was a little amused to find the wood unyielding. He was a realist, even in his dreams. Louis twisted the knob, lifted the latch, and entered the shed-garage. Pascow was not there. Louis wondered if Pascow had simply vanished. Dream figures often did. So did places—one moment you were naked by a pool, discussing wife swapping with friends; then you blinked and were climbing a volcano. Maybe he had lost Pascow because this was the start of something new.

But as Louis left the garage, he saw him again, standing in the dim moonlight at the back of the lawn—at the path's entrance.

Now fear arrived, softly seeping into his body and filling the empty spaces with dirty smoke. He didn’t want to go there. He stopped.

Pascow glanced back, and in the moonlight, his eyes were silver. Louis felt a wave of horror in his stomach. That jutting bone, those dried blood clots. But he couldn't resist those eyes. This was a dream of being hypnotized, controlled... unable to change things, like Pascow's death. Twenty years of school couldn't help when a man was brought in, his skull crushed by a tree. They might as well have called a plumber.

Even as these thoughts raced through his mind, he was drawn onto the path. He followed the jogging shorts, as maroon as the dried blood on Pascow’s face in this light.

He didn’t like this dream. Not at all. It was too real. The cold rug, the inability to pass through the shed door when one should be able to in a dream... and now the cool dew on his bare feet, and the light night wind on his naked body, save for his shorts. Under the trees, pine needles stuck to his soles… another detail that was too real.

Never mind. Never mind. I am home in bed. It’s just a dream, no matter how vivid, and it will seem ridiculous in the morning. My mind will find its flaws.

A small dead branch poked his arm rudely, and he winced. Up ahead, Pascow was just a moving shadow, and Louis’s terror had become clear: I am following a dead man into the woods, to the Pet Sematary, and this is no dream. God help me, this is real.

They walked down the wooded hill. The path curved between the trees and plunged into the underbrush. No boots now. The ground became cold jelly under his feet, grabbing and holding, reluctantly letting go. There were sucking noises. He felt the mud between his toes, trying to separate them.

He desperately tried to cling to the idea it was a dream.

It wouldn’t work.

They reached the clearing, and the moon escaped its cloud cover again, filling the graveyard with a ghostly light. The leaning markers—bits of wood and tin cans cut with a father’s tools and hammered into rough squares, broken pieces of shale and slate—stood out clearly, casting sharp, black shadows.

Pascow stopped near SMUCKY THE CAT, HE WAS OBEDIANT and turned to Louis. The horror, the terror—he felt these things would grow in him until his body exploded under their soft but unstoppable pressure. Pascow was grinning. His bloody lips were pulled back from his teeth, and his healthy tan from working on the road, in the moon’s harsh light, looked like the white of a corpse ready to be wrapped.

He lifted an arm and pointed. Louis looked and moaned. His eyes widened, and he pressed his knuckles against his mouth. His cheeks felt cool, and he knew that he had started to cry from the extreme terror.

The fallen trees from which Jud Crandall had called Ellie in alarm had become a pile of bones. The bones were moving. They twisted and clicked, jaws and femurs and ulnas and molars and incisors; he saw the grinning skulls of humans and animals. Finger bones rattled. Here, the remains of a foot bent its pale joints.

Ah, it was moving; it was creeping—

Pascow was walking toward him, his bloody face grim in the moonlight, and the last of Louis’s clear mind began to slip away in a repeating thought: You have to scream yourself awake it doesn’t matter if you scare Rachel Ellie Gage wake the whole house the whole neighborhood have to scream yourself awake screamscreamscreamyourselfawakeawakeawake—

But only a soft breath of air came out. It was the sound of a little kid sitting and trying to learn how to whistle.

Pascow came closer and spoke.

“The door must not be opened,” Pascow said. He was looking down at Louis because Louis had fallen to his knees. Louis thought he saw compassion in his face. It wasn’t really compassion, just a terrible kind of patience. He pointed at the moving pile of bones. “Don’t go beyond, no matter how much you feel you need to, Doctor. The barrier was not meant to be broken. Remember this: there is more power here than you know. It is old and always restless. Remember.”

Louis tried again to scream. He could not.

“I come as a friend,” Pascow said—but was friend really the word Pascow had used? Louis thought not. It was like Pascow had spoken in another language that Louis could understand through dream magic… and “friend” was the closest word his mind could find for what Pascow had said. “Your destruction and the destruction of all you love is very near, Doctor.” He was close enough for Louis to smell death on him.

Pascow, reaching for him.

The soft, maddening click of the bones.

Louis started to fall as he tried to get away from that hand. His own hand hit a gravestone and knocked it over. Pascow’s face, leaning down, filled the sky.

“Doctor—remember.”

Louis tried to scream, and the world spun away—but he still heard the click of moving bones in the moonlit crypt of the night.

Chapter 17

It takes the average person seven minutes to fall asleep, but according to Hand’s Human Physiology, it takes the same person fifteen to twenty minutes to wake up. It is as if sleep is a pool; getting out is harder than getting in. When someone wakes, they come up slowly, from deep sleep to light sleep to what is sometimes called “waking sleep,” a state where they can hear sounds and even answer questions without remembering it later… except maybe as bits of a dream.

Louis heard the click and rattle of bones, but slowly the sound became sharper, more metallic. There was a bang. A yell. More metallic sounds… something rolling? Sure, his wandering mind agreed. Roll dem bones.

He heard his daughter calling “Get it, Gage! Go get it!”

Then Gage crowed with joy, the sound that made Louis open his eyes and see the ceiling of his own bedroom.

He stayed perfectly still, waiting for the reality, the good reality, the blessed reality, to fully return.

All a dream. No matter how awful, how real, it had all been a dream. Just a fossil in the mind under his mind.

The metallic sound came again. It was one of Gage’s toy cars being rolled along the upstairs hall.

“Get it, Gage!”

“Get it!” Gage yelled. “Get it-get it-get it!”

Thumpa-thumpa-thumpa. Gage’s little bare feet pounded along the hallway runner. He and Ellie were giggling.

Louis looked to his right. Rachel’s side of the bed was empty, the covers thrown back. The sun was high up. He glanced at his watch and saw it was almost eight o’clock. Rachel had let him sleep in… probably on purpose.

Usually this would have bothered him, but not this morning. He took a deep breath and let it out, happy for now to lie here with a stripe of sunlight coming in through the window, feeling the clear reality of the real world. Dust danced in the sunlight.

Rachel called upstairs. “Come down for your snack and the bus, El!”

“Okay!” The louder tap-tap of her feet. “Here’s your car, Gage. I have to go to school.”

Gage began to yell, upset. Although it was unclear—the only clear words being Gage, car, get it, and Ellie-bus, his meaning was clear: Ellie should stay. School could wait for the day.

Rachel’s voice again, “Give your dad a shake before you come down, El.”

Ellie came in, her hair in a ponytail, wearing her red dress.

“I’m awake, babe,” he said. “Go get your bus.”

“Okay, Daddy.” She came over, kissed his slightly rough cheek, and ran for the stairs.

The dream was starting to blur, to make less sense. A very good thing, too.

“Gage!” he yelled. “Come give your dad a kiss!”

Gage didn't listen. He was hurrying after Ellie downstairs, shouting as loud as he could, “Get it! Get-it-get-it-GET-IT!” Louis just saw his strong little boy’s body, wearing only diapers and rubber pants.

Rachel called up again, “Louis, was that you? Are you awake?”

“Yeah,” he said, sitting up.

“I told you it was him!” Ellie called. “I’m leaving. Bye!” The front door slammed, and Gage’s angry yell finished the sentence.

“One egg or two?” Rachel called.

Louis pushed the blankets back and put his feet on the rough rug, ready to say he didn’t want eggs, just cereal, and he’d run… but he couldn't say the words.

His feet were covered in dirt and pine needles.

His heart jumped in his chest like a wild toy. Quickly, eyes wide, biting his tongue without feeling it, he kicked the covers off. The bottom of the bed was full of needles. The sheets were stained and dirty.

“Louis?”

He saw some stray pine needles on his knees, and then he looked at his right arm. There was a scratch there on his bicep, a new scratch, exactly where the dead branch had poked him… in the dream.

I’m going to scream. I can feel it.

And he could too; it was rising from inside, a big cold bullet of fear. Reality seemed to waver. Reality—the real reality, he thought—was those needles, the dirt on the sheets, the bloody scratch on his bare arm.

I’m going to scream and then I’ll go crazy and I won’t have to worry about it anymore—

“Louis?” Rachel was coming up the stairs. “Louis, did you go back to sleep?”

He fought to regain control in those few seconds; he fought hard, like he had in those moments of confusion after Pascow had been brought into the Medical Center, dying in a blanket. He won. The thing that helped him was knowing she couldn't see him like this, his feet muddy and covered with needles, the blankets on the floor showing the dirty ground sheet.

“I’m awake,” he called out, sounding cheerful. His tongue was bleeding from where he'd bitten it without meaning to. His mind raced, and deep down, away from everything else, he wondered if he had always been close to such madness; if everyone was.

“One egg or two?” She had stopped on the second or third step. Thank God.

“Two,” he said, hardly knowing what he was saying. “Scrambled.”

“Good for you,” she said, and went back downstairs again.

He closed his eyes briefly in relief, but in the darkness he saw Pascow’s silver eyes. His eyes flew open again. Louis began to move quickly, putting off any further thought. He jerked the bedclothes off the bed. The blankets were okay. He separated the two sheets, balled them up, took them into the hallway, and dumped them down the laundry chute.

Almost running, he entered the bathroom, jerked the shower handle on, and stepped under water so hot it was almost scalding, not caring. He washed the dirt from his feet and legs.

He began to feel better, more in control. Drying off, it struck him that this was how murderers must feel when they believe they have gotten rid of all the evidence. He began to laugh. He went on drying himself, but he also went on laughing. He couldn’t seem to stop.

“Hey, up there!” Rachel called. “What’s so funny?”

“Private joke,” Louis called back, still laughing. He was frightened, but the fright didn’t stop the laughter. The laughter came, rising from a belly that was as hard as stones stuck into a wall. It occurred to him that shoving the sheets down the laundry chute was absolutely the best thing he could have done. Missy Dandridge came in five days a week to vacuum, clean… and do the laundry. Rachel would never see those sheets at all until she put them back on the bed… clean. He supposed it was possible that Missy would mention it to Rachel, but he didn’t think so. She would probably whisper to her husband that the Creeds were playing some strange sex game that involved mud and pine needles instead of body paints.

This thought made Louis laugh all the harder.

The last of the giggles and chuckles dried up as he was dressing, and he realized that he felt a little better. How that could be he didn’t know, but he did. The room looked normal now except for the stripped bed. He had gotten rid of the poison. Maybe “evidence” was actually the word he was looking for, but in his mind it felt like poison.

Perhaps this is what people do with the inexplicable, he thought. This is what they do with the irrational that refuses to be broken down into the normal causes and effects that run the Western world. Maybe this was how your mind coped with the flying saucer you saw hovering silently over your back field one morning, casting its own tight little pool of shadow; the rain of frogs; the hand from under the bed that stroked your bare foot in the dead of night. There was a giggling fit or a crying fit… and since it was its own untouchable self and would not break down, you simply passed terror intact, like a kidney stone.

Gage was in his chair, eating Cocoa Bears and decorating the table with them. He was decorating the plastic mat under his high chair with Cocoa Bears and apparently shampooing with them.

Rachel came out of the kitchen with his eggs and a cup of coffee. “What was so funny, Lou? You were laughing like crazy up there. You scared me a little.”

Louis opened his mouth without knowing what he was going to say, and what came out was a joke he had heard the week before at the corner market down the road—something about a Jewish tailor who bought a parrot whose only line was “Ariel Sharon jerks off.”

By the time he finished, Rachel was laughing too—so was Gage for that matter.

Fine. Our hero has taken care of all the evidence—meaning: the muddy sheets and the crazy laughter in the bathroom. Our hero will now read the morning paper—or at least look at it—making the morning seem normal.

So thinking, Louis opened the paper.

That’s what you do, all right, he thought with great relief. You forget it like a stone, and that’s the end of it… unless there comes a campfire some night with friends when the wind is strong and the talk turns to strange events. Because on campfire nights when the wind is strong, talk is easy.

He ate his eggs. He kissed Rachel and Gage. He glanced at the square, white-painted laundry cabinet at the bottom of the chute only as he left. Everything was okay. It was another great morning. Late summer seemed like it would just go on forever, and everything was okay. He glanced at the path as he backed the car out of the garage, but that was okay too. Didn’t think twice. You forgot it like a stone.

Everything was okay until he had gotten ten miles down the road, and then the shakes hit him so hard that he had to pull off Route 2 and into the empty morning parking lot of Sing’s, the Chinese restaurant not far from the Eastern Maine Medical Center… where Pascow’s body would have been taken. The EMMC, that is, not Sing’s. Vic Pascow was never going to eat another helping of moo goo gai pan, ha-ha.

The shakes twisted his body, ripped at it, controlled it. Louis felt helpless and terrified—not terrified of anything supernatural, not in this bright sunshine, but simply terrified of the possibility that he might be losing his mind. It felt as if a long, invisible wire was being twisted through his head.

“No more,” he said. “Please, no more.”

He reached for the radio and found Joan Baez singing about diamonds and rust. Her clear, calm voice calmed him, and by the time she was done, Louis felt ready to drive on.

When he arrived at the Medical Center, he greeted Charlton and then slipped into the bathroom, thinking he must look awful. But he didn't. He was a little tired around the eyes, but even Rachel hadn't seen it. He splashed cold water on his face, dried it, combed his hair, and went to his office.

Steve Masterton and the Indian doctor, Surrendra Hardu, were inside, drinking coffee and still reviewing the first file.

“Morning, Lou,” Steve said.

“Morning.”

“Let’s hope it’s better than yesterday,” Hardu said.

“That’s right, you missed all the action.”

“Surrendra had his own excitement last night,” Masterton said, grinning. “Tell him, Surrendra.”

Hardu cleaned his glasses, smiling. “Two boys bring in their girlfriend around one in the morning,” he said. “She is very drunk and happy, celebrating being back at school. She has a bad cut on her thigh, and I tell her it will need at least four stitches, but won’t scar. Stitch away, she tells me, and so I do, bending over like this—”

Hardu showed them, bowing over an invisible thigh. Louis started to grin, guessing what was coming.

“And as I am stitching, she vomits on my head.”

Masterton laughed loudly. So did Louis. Hardu smiled calmly, as if this had happened to him countless times.

“Surrendra, how long have you been working?” Louis asked, when the laughter stopped.

“Since midnight,” Hardu said. “I’m just leaving. But I wanted to stay long enough to say hello again.”

“Well, hello,” Louis said, shaking his small, brown hand. “Now go home and sleep.”

“We’re almost done with the front cases,” Masterton said. “Say hallelujah, Surrendra.”

“I won’t,” Hardu said, smiling. “I’m not a Christian.”

“Then sing the chorus of ‘Instant Karma’ or something.”

“May you both shine on,” Hardu said, still smiling, and walked out the door.

Louis and Steve Masterton watched him go for a moment, quiet, and then looked at each other. They started laughing. To Louis, no laugh had ever felt so good… so normal.

“Good thing we finished the file,” Steve said. “Today’s the day the drug sellers start showing up.”

Louis nodded… The first of the drug salesmen would arrive at ten. As Steve liked to joke, Wednesday might be Prince Spaghetti Day, but at UMO every Tuesday was D-day. The D stood for Darvon, the most popular drug.

“Some advice, Boss,” Steve said. “I don’t know what these guys were like in Chicago, but around here they’ll do anything, from free hunting trips to the Allagash in November to free bowling at Family Fun Lanes in Bangor. One guy tried to give me an inflatable doll. Me! And I’m just a P.A.! If they can’t sell you drugs, they’ll make you need them.”

“You should have taken the doll.”

“Nah, she was a redhead. Not my type.”

“Well, I agree with Surrendra,” Louis said. “As long as it’s not like yesterday.”

Chapter 18

When the Upjohn salesman didn’t show up at ten, Louis gave in and called the registrar. He spoke to a Mrs. Stapleton, who said she’d send over Victor Pascow’s records right away. As soon as Louis hung up, the Upjohn guy arrived. He didn’t try to sell Louis anything, just asked if he wanted to buy discounted season tickets to the New England Patriots’ games.

“Nope,” Louis said.

“I didn’t think so,” the Upjohn guy said sadly, and left.

At noon, Louis went to the Bear’s Den and got a tuna sandwich and a Coke. He took them back to his office and ate lunch while looking over Pascow’s records. He was searching for a link to himself or to North Ludlow, where the Pet Sematary was… a feeling, he guessed, that there had to be a logical reason even for something as strange as this. Maybe the guy had grown up in Ludlow—had, maybe, even buried a dog or cat there.

He didn’t find the link he wanted. Pascow was from Bergenfield, New Jersey, and had come to UMO to study electrical engineering. In those few typed pages, Louis couldn’t see any connection between himself and the young man who had died in the reception room—other than the fact that they both could die.

He finished his Coke, listening to the straw rattle at the bottom, and then threw his trash in the wastebasket. Lunch had been small, but he’d enjoyed it. He felt pretty good, actually. Not now. The shaking hadn’t come back, and even the morning’s horror now felt like a bad, random surprise, like a dream, unimportant.

He tapped his fingers on his desk pad, shrugged, and picked up the phone again. He called the EMMC and asked for the morgue.

After he got the pathology clerk, he said who he was and said, “You have one of our students there, a Victor Pascow—”

“Not anymore,” the voice on the other end said. “He’s gone.”

Louis’s throat tightened. He finally managed, “What?”

“His body was flown back to his parents late last night. A guy from Brookings-Smith Mortuary came and took him. They put him on Delta, uh”—papers rustling—”Delta Flight 109. Where did you think he went? Out dancing at the Show Ring?”

“No,” Louis said. “No, of course not. It’s just…” It was just what? What was he doing pursuing this, anyway? There was no sensible way to handle it. It had to be let go, forgotten. Anything else was asking for pointless trouble. “It’s just that it seemed very fast,” he finished weakly.

“Well, he was autopsied yesterday afternoon”—that faint rustle of papers again—”at around three-twenty by Dr. Rynzwyck. By then his father had made all the arrangements. I imagine the body got to Newark by two in the morning.”

“Oh. Well, in that case—”

“Unless one of the airlines messed up and sent it somewhere else,” the pathology clerk said cheerfully. “We’ve had that happen, you know, although never with Delta. Delta’s actually pretty good. We had a guy who died on a fishing trip way up in Aroostook County, in one of those little towns that just have a couple of map coordinates for a name. Jerk choked on a pop-top while he was chugging a can of beer. Took his buddies two days to drag him out of the wilderness, and you know that by then it’s a gamble whether or not the embalming fluid will work. But they injected it and hoped for the best. Sent him home to Grand Falls, Minnesota, in the cargo hold of some airliner. But there was a mix-up. They shipped him first to Miami, then to Des Moines, then to Fargo, North Dakota. Finally somebody figured it out, but by then another three days had gone by. Nothing worked. They might as well have injected him with Kool-Aid instead of embalming fluid. The guy was totally black and smelled like spoiled pork. That’s what I heard, anyway. Six baggage handlers got sick.”

The voice on the other end of the line laughed loudly.

Louis closed his eyes and said, “Well, thank you—”

“I can give you Dr. Rynzwyck’s home phone if you want it, Doctor, but he usually plays golf up in Orono in the morning.”

“That’s okay,” Louis said.

He hung up the telephone. That settles it, he thought. While you were having that crazy dream, or whatever it was, Pascow’s body was almost certainly in a Bergenfield funeral home. That’s the end of it; let it be over.

Driving home that afternoon, a simple explanation for the dirt at the foot of the bed finally came to him, filling him with relief.

He had experienced a single episode of sleepwalking, triggered by the unexpected and extremely upsetting event of having a student seriously injured and then dying in his infirmary during his first real day on the job.

It explained everything. The dream had seemed extremely real because large parts of it were real—the feel of the carpet, the cold dew, and, of course, the dead branch that had scratched his arm. It explained why Pascow had been able to walk through the door and he had not.

A picture rose in his mind, a picture of Rachel coming downstairs last night and catching him bumping against the back door, trying in his sleep to walk through it. The thought made him grin. It would have really shocked her.

With the sleepwalking idea in mind, he was able to analyze the reasons for the dream—and he did so eagerly. He had walked to the Pet Sematary because it had become linked to another recent stressful moment. It had in fact caused a serious argument between him and his wife… and also, he thought with growing excitement, it was linked in his mind to his daughter’s first encounter with the idea of death—something his own subconscious must have been dealing with last night when he went to bed.

Damn lucky I got back to the house okay—I don’t even remember that part. Must have come back automatically.

It was a good thing he had. He couldn’t imagine what it would have been like to have woken up this morning by the grave of Smucky the Cat, confused, covered with dew, and probably scared shitless—as Rachel also would have been, undoubtedly.

But it was over now.

That’s the end of it, Louis thought with immense relief. Yes, but what about the things he said when he was dying?, his mind tried to ask, and Louis shut it up fast.

That evening, while Rachel ironed and Ellie and Gage sat in the same chair, both captivated by “The Muppet Show,” Louis mentioned casually to Rachel that he thought he'd go for a short walk—to get some fresh air.

“Will you be back in time to help me put Gage to bed?” she asked, not looking up from her ironing. “You know he goes down easier when you’re here.”

“Sure,” he said.

“Where are you going, Daddy?” Ellie asked, her eyes glued to the TV. Kermit was about to be punched in the eye by Miss Piggy.

“Just out back, hon.”

Louis went outside.

Fifteen minutes later, he stood in the Pet Sematary, looking around with curiosity and a strong sense of déjà vu. He knew he had been here before: the small grave marker for Smucky the Cat was knocked over. He had done that when he saw Pascow in what he could remember of the dream. Louis righted it without thinking and walked over to the pile of fallen trees.

He didn’t like it. The memory of the weather-beaten branches and dead trees turning into a pile of bones still made him uneasy. He made himself reach out and touch one. Balanced on the unstable pile, it rolled and fell, bouncing down the side. Louis jumped back to avoid it hitting his shoe.

He walked along the fallen trees, first to the left, then to the right. On both sides, the underbrush was so thick it was impossible to get through. And it wasn’t the kind of brush you’d want to try to push through—not if you were smart, Louis thought. There were large patches of poison ivy growing close to the ground (Louis had always heard people claim they were immune, but he knew almost no one truly was), and deeper in were some of the biggest, nastiest thorns he had ever seen.

Louis walked back to the approximate center of the fallen trees. He looked at it, his hands in the back pockets of his jeans.

You’re not going to try to climb that, are you?

Not me, boss. Why would I want to do something that stupid?

Great. You worried me there for a second, Lou. Looks like a good way to end up in the hospital with a broken ankle, doesn’t it?

Sure does! Also, it’s getting dark.

Sure that he knew exactly what he was doing, Louis started to climb the pile of fallen trees.

He was halfway up when he felt it move under his feet with a strange creaking noise.

Roll the dice, Doc.

When the pile moved again, Louis started to climb back down. His shirt had come untucked.

He made it back to the ground safely and brushed bits of bark off his hands. He walked back to the start of the path that led to his house—to his children who would want a bedtime story, to Church, who was enjoying his last day as a tomcat and lady-killer, to tea in the kitchen with his wife after the kids were asleep.

He looked at the clearing again before leaving, struck by its quiet greenness. Wisps of fog had appeared out of nowhere and were starting to wrap around the stones. Those circles... as if, without realizing it, the children of North Ludlow had built a small Stonehenge.

But, Louis, is this all there is?

Even though he had only briefly glanced over the top of the deadfall before the uneasy feeling made him anxious, he could have almost said there was a path beyond, going further into the woods.

None of your concern, Louis. You need to forget about this.

Okay, boss.

Louis turned and went home.

He stayed up an hour after Rachel went to bed that night, reading a pile of medical journals he had already read, not wanting to admit that the thought of going to bed—going to sleep—made him uneasy. He had never sleepwalked before, and there was no way to know if it was just a one-time thing… until it either happened again or didn't.

He heard Rachel get out of bed, and then she called down quietly, “Lou? Honey? Are you coming up?”

“Just about to,” he said, turning off the lamp over his desk and standing up.

It took much longer than seven minutes to turn off the machine that night. As he listened to Rachel breathing deeply and calmly in her sleep next to him, the image of Victor Pascow seemed less like a dream. He would close his eyes and see the door burst open and there he was, Our Special Guest Star, Victor Pascow, standing there in his jogging shorts, pale under his summer tan, his collarbone sticking out.

He would start to fall asleep, think about what it would be like to wake up completely, coldly, in the Pet Sematary, to see those uneven circles lit by moonlight, to have to walk back, awake, along the path through the woods. He would think these things and then suddenly wake up again.

It was after midnight when sleep finally caught him off guard. He had no dreams. He woke up right at seven-thirty, to the sound of cold autumn rain hitting the window. He pulled back the sheets with some worry. The ground sheet on his bed was perfect. No one would call his feet, with their circles of rough skin, perfect, but they were at least clean.

Louis started whistling in the shower.

Chapter 19

Missy Dandridge watched Gage while Rachel took Winston Churchill to the vet. That night Ellie stayed awake past eleven, whining that she couldn’t sleep without Church and asking for water again and again. Finally Louis refused to give her any more, saying she would wet the bed. This caused a crying fit so intense that Rachel and Louis just stared at each other, surprised.

“She’s scared for Church,” Rachel said. “Let her deal with it, Lou.”

“She can’t keep crying like that for long,” Louis said. “I hope.”

He was right. Ellie’s rough, angry cries turned into sobs and whimpers. Finally, it was quiet. When Louis went to check on her, he found her asleep on the floor, hugging the cat bed that Church hardly ever used.

He took it from her arms, put her back in bed, gently brushed her hair off her sweaty forehead, and kissed her. On a whim, he went into Rachel’s small office, wrote a quick note in big letters on a piece of paper—I WILL BE BACK TOMORROW, LOVE, CHURCH—and pinned it to the cushion on the cat bed. Then he went to his bedroom, looking for Rachel. Rachel was there. They made love and fell asleep holding each other.

Church came home on Friday of Louis’s first full week at work; Ellie fussed over him, used some of her allowance to buy him cat treats, and almost hit Gage for trying to touch him. This made Gage cry harder than any scolding from his parents ever could. Being told off by Ellie was like being told off by God.

Looking at Church made Louis feel sad. It was silly, but he still felt it. Church wasn't feisty anymore. He didn't walk like a tough guy; now he walked slowly and carefully, like someone recovering from an illness. He let Ellie feed him by hand. He didn’t seem to want to go outside, not even to the garage. He had changed. Maybe it was better that he had changed.

Rachel and Ellie didn’t seem to notice.

Chapter 20

Indian summer didn't last long. The trees turned bright colors, blazed for a short time, and then faded. After a cold, heavy rain in mid-October, the leaves began to fall. Ellie started bringing home Halloween decorations she had made at school and told Gage the story of the Headless Horseman. Gage spent that evening happily talking about someone named Itchybod Brain. Rachel started giggling and couldn’t stop. It was a happy time for them, that early autumn.

Louis’s job at the university had become a busy but enjoyable routine. He saw patients, attended meetings, and wrote the required letters to the student newspaper. He advised the students about the confidentiality of the infirmary's treatment for STDs and urged them to get flu shots, as the A-type was likely to be common again that winter. He participated in discussions. He led discussions. During the second week in October, he went to the New England Conference on College and University Medicine in Providence and presented a paper on the legal issues of treating students. Victor Pascow was mentioned in his paper under the fake name of “Henry Montez.” The paper was well received. He started working on the infirmary budget for the next school year.

His evenings fell into a pattern: time with the kids after dinner, a beer or two with Jud Crandall later. Sometimes Rachel came with him if Missy was free to babysit, and sometimes Norma joined them, but usually it was just Louis and Jud. Louis found the old man as comfortable as an old shoe, and he would talk about Ludlow history going back three hundred years as if he had lived through it all. He talked but never went on and on. He never bored Louis, although he had seen Rachel yawn while trying to hide it more than once.

He would cross the road back to his house before ten most evenings, and, more often than not, he and Rachel would make love. They hadn't made love so often since the first year of their marriage, and never so well or enjoyably. Rachel said she thought it was something in the well water; Louis thought it was the Maine air.

The awful death of Victor Pascow on the first day of the fall semester began to fade from the students' minds and from Louis’s own; Pascow’s family was no doubt still grieving.
