Louis had spoken to Pascow’s father on the phone, his voice full of tears but thankfully not visible; the father had only wanted to be sure that Louis had done everything he could, and Louis had assured him that everyone had. He didn’t tell him about the confusion, the spreading stain on the carpet, and how his son had been dead almost as soon as he was brought in, although Louis thought he would never forget those things. But for those who saw Pascow as just another death, he was already fading from memory.

Louis still remembered the dream and the sleepwalking that went with it, but it now seemed almost as if it had happened to someone else, or on a TV show he had once watched. His one visit to a prostitute in Chicago six years ago seemed like that now; they were equally unimportant, side trips that seemed more important than they were, like sounds in an echo chamber.

He didn’t think at all about what the dying Pascow had or had not said.

There was a hard frost on Halloween night. Louis and Ellie started at the Crandalls’. Ellie laughed with pleasure, pretended to ride her broom around Norma’s kitchen, and was told she was “Just the cutest thing I ever saw… isn’t she, Jud?”

Jud agreed and lit a cigarette. “Where’s Gage, Louis? I thought you’d have him dressed up too.”

They had planned to take Gage around—Rachel especially had been looking forward to it because she and Missy Dandridge had made a bug costume with twisted coat hangers wrapped in crepe paper for feelers—but Gage had gotten a bad, bronchial cold. After listening to his lungs, which sounded a bit wheezy, and seeing the thermometer outside read only forty degrees at six o’clock, Louis had said no. Rachel, though sad, had agreed.

Ellie had promised to give Gage some of her candy, but her overdone sadness made Louis wonder if she wasn’t a little happy Gage wouldn’t slow her down… or steal some attention.

“Poor Gage,” she had said in a voice usually used for people who were dying. Gage, not knowing what he was missing, sat on the sofa watching “Zoom” with Church sleeping next to him.

“Ellie-witch,” Gage replied without much interest and went back to the TV.

“Poor Gage,” Ellie said again, sighing. Louis thought of fake tears and grinned. Ellie grabbed his hand and started pulling him. “Let’s go, Daddy. Let’s go—let’s go—let’s go."

“Gage has a bit of the croup,” Louis said to Jud now.

“Well, that’s too bad,” Norma said, “but it will be better for him next year. Hold out your bag, Ellie… whoops!”

She had taken an apple and a small Snickers bar from the treat bowl on the table, but they had both fallen out of her hand. Louis was a little shocked at how bony that hand looked. He bent over and picked up the apple as it rolled across the floor. Jud got the Snickers and dropped it into Ellie’s bag.

“Oh, let me get you another apple, honey,” Norma said. “That one will get bruised.”

“It’s fine,” Louis said, trying to put it into Ellie’s bag, but Ellie stepped away, holding her bag closed.

“I don’t want a bruised apple, Daddy,” she said, looking at her father like he was crazy. “Brown spots… yuck!”

“Ellie, that’s very rude!”

“Don’t scold her for telling the truth, Louis,” Norma said. “Only children tell the whole truth, you know. That’s what makes them children. The brown spots are yucky.”

“Thank you, Mrs. Crandall,” Ellie said, giving her father a satisfied look.

“You’re very welcome, honey,” Norma said.

Jud walked them out to the porch. Two little ghosts were coming up the walk, and Ellie knew they were both friends from school. She took them back to the kitchen, and for a moment Jud and Louis were alone on the porch.

“Her arthritis has gotten worse,” Louis said.

Jud nodded and put out his cigarette in an ashtray. “Yeah. It’s been harder on her every fall and winter, but this is the worst it’s ever been.”

“What does her doctor say?”

“Nothing. He can’t say nothing because Norma hasn’t gone back to see him.”

“What? Why not?”

Jud looked at Louis, and in the light from the station wagon's headlights, waiting for the ghosts, he looked strangely vulnerable. “I wanted to ask you this when things were better, Louis, but I guess there's no good time to ask a friend for a favor. Would you take a look at her?”

From the kitchen, Louis could hear the two ghosts booing and Ellie doing her cackling—which she had been practicing all week—again. It all sounded very nice and Halloweenish.

“What else is wrong with Norma?” he asked. “Is she afraid of something else, Jud?”

“She’s been having chest pains,” Jud said quietly. “She won’t go to Dr. Weybridge anymore. I’m a little worried.”

“Is Norma worried?”

Jud hesitated and then said, “I think she’s scared. I think that’s why she doesn’t want to go to the doctor. One of her oldest friends, Betty Coslaw, died in the EMMC just last month. Cancer. She and Norma were the same age. She’s scared.”

“I’d be glad to examine her,” Louis said. “No problem at all.”

“Thanks, Louis,” Jud said, relieved. “If we catch her one night, all work together, I think—”

Jud stopped, tilting his head to the side. He looked at Louis.

Later, Louis couldn't quite recall how his feelings changed. Trying to understand it just made him confused. All he knew was that curiosity quickly turned into a feeling that something was terribly wrong. He and Jud looked at each other, both caught off guard. It took him a moment to figure out how to react.

“Hoooo-hoooo,” the Halloween ghosts in the kitchen chanted. “Hooo-hooo.” Then, the h-sound disappeared, and the cry grew louder, truly scary: “oooo-OOOOOO—”

And then one of the ghosts began to scream.

“Daddy!” Ellie’s voice was frantic and scared. “Daddy! Missus Crandall fell down!”

“Ah, Jesus,” Jud almost groaned.

Ellie ran onto the porch, her black dress flapping. She held her broom in one hand. Her green face, now long with worry, looked like a tiny drunkard in the final stages of sickness. The two little ghosts followed her, crying.

Jud rushed through the door, surprisingly quick for a man over eighty. No, more than quick. Almost agile. He was calling his wife’s name.

Louis bent down and put his hands on Ellie’s shoulders. “Stay right here on the porch, Ellie. Understand?”

“Daddy, I’m scared,” she whispered.

The two ghosts ran past them and down the walk, candy bags rattling, screaming their mother’s name.

Louis ran down the front hall and into the kitchen, ignoring Ellie, who was calling for him to come back.

Norma lay on the lumpy linoleum by the table in a mess of apples and small Snickers bars. It seemed she had knocked over the bowl as she reached for something. It lay nearby like a small Pyrex flying saucer. Jud was rubbing one of her wrists, and he looked up at Louis with a worried face.

“Help me, Louis,” he said. “Help Norma. She’s dying, I think.”

“Move aside,” Louis said. He kneeled and landed on an apple, crushing it. He felt juice soak through the knee of his old pants, and the sweet smell of apple filled the kitchen.

Here it is again, like Pascow, Louis thought and then pushed the thought away so quickly it was like it was on wheels.

He felt for her pulse and found it weak, faint, and fast—not really a beat but just small twitches. A very irregular heartbeat, close to cardiac arrest. You and Elvis Presley, Norma, he thought.

He opened her dress, showing a creamy yellow silk slip. Moving smoothly now, he turned her head to the side and started CPR.

“Jud, listen to me,” he said. “Put the heel of your left hand a third of the way up her breastbone—four centimeters above that little notch. Grip your left wrist with your right hand, to add pressure. Keep it firm, but easy on her ribs—no need to panic yet. And don’t crush her lungs.”

“I’m here,” Jud said.

“Take Ellie,” he said. “Go across the street. Be careful—watch for cars. Tell Rachel what’s happened. Tell her I want my bag. Not the one in the study, but the one on the high shelf in the upstairs bathroom. She’ll know which one. Tell her to call Bangor MedCu and send an ambulance.”

“Bucksport is closer,” Jud said.

“Bangor is faster. Go. Don’t call; let Rachel do that. I need that bag.” And once she knows what’s happening, Louis thought, I don’t think she’ll bring it over.

Jud left. Louis heard the screen door slam. He was alone with Norma Crandall and the smell of apples. From the living room came the steady tick of the clock.

Norma suddenly made a long, snoring sound. Her eyelids fluttered. And Louis was suddenly filled with a cold, awful certainty.

She’s going to open her eyes… oh God, she’s going to open her eyes and start talking about the Pet Sematary.

But she only looked at Louis with a confused kind of recognition, and then her eyes closed again. Louis was ashamed of himself and this silly fear that was so unlike him. At the same time, he felt hope and relief. There had been some pain in her eyes but not terrible pain. His first thought was that this had not been a serious seizure.

Louis was breathing hard now and sweating. Only TV paramedics could make CPR look easy. A good, steady chest massage used a lot of energy, and the muscles between his arms and shoulders would ache tomorrow.

“Can I do anything?”

He looked around. A woman in pants and a brown sweater stood uncertainly in the doorway, one hand in a fist between her breasts. The mother of the ghosts, Louis thought.

“No,” he said, and then: “Yes. Wet a cloth, please. Squeeze it out. Put it on her forehead.”

She moved to do it. Louis looked down, Norma’s eyes were open again.

“Louis, I fell down,” she whispered. “Think I fainted.”

“You’ve had some kind of heart problem,” Louis said. “Doesn’t seem too bad. Just relax and don’t talk, Norma.”

He paused for a moment and then checked her pulse again. It was too fast. Her heart was sending a signal: it would beat normally, then speed up briefly in a way that almost but didn't become a dangerous rhythm, and then go back to beating normally. Beat-beat-beat, WHACK-WHACK-WHACK, beat-beat-beat-beat-beat. It wasn’t good, but it was a little better than a totally irregular heartbeat.

The woman came over with the cloth and put it on Norma’s forehead. She stepped away nervously. Jud came back in with Louis’s bag.

“Louis?”

“She’s going to be fine,” Louis said, looking at Jud but really talking to Norma. “Medics on their way?”

“Your wife is calling them,” Jud said. “I didn’t stay.”

“No… hospital,” Norma whispered.

“Yes, hospital,” Louis said. “Five days to watch you, some medicine, then home to rest, Norma. And if you say another word, I’ll make you eat all these apples. Cores and everything.”

She smiled weakly, then closed her eyes again.

Louis opened his bag, searched around, found the Isodil, and shook one of the pills, so tiny it easily could have fit on the moon of one fingernail, into his palm. He recapped the bottle and pinched the pill between his fingers.

“Norma, can you hear me?”

“Yes.”

“Want you to open your mouth. You did your trick, now you get your treat. I’m going to put a pill under your tongue. Just a small one. I want you to hold it there until it dissolves. It’s going to taste a little bitter but never mind that. All right?”

She opened her mouth. Stale breath from her dentures came out, and Louis felt a moment of aching sorrow for her, lying here on her kitchen floor in a mess of apples and Halloween candy. It occurred to him that once she had been seventeen, her breasts admired by the young men of the neighborhood, all her teeth her own, and the heart under her blouse a tough little engine.

She settled her tongue over the pill and grimaced a little. The pill tasted a little bitter, all right. It always did. But she was no Victor Pascow, beyond help and beyond reach. He thought Norma was going to live to fight another day. Her hand reached in the air, and Jud took it gently.

Louis got up then, found the overturned bowl, and began to pick up the treats. The woman, who introduced herself as Mrs. Buddinger from down the road, helped him and then said she thought she had better go back to the car. Her two boys were frightened.

“Thank you for your help, Mrs. Buddinger,” Louis said.

“I didn’t do anything,” she said simply. “But I’ll get on my knees tonight and thank God you were here, Dr. Creed.”

Louis waved a hand, embarrassed.

“That goes for me, too,” Jud said. His eyes met Louis’s and held them. They were steady. He was in control again. His brief moment of confusion and fear was gone. “I owe you one, Louis.”

“Get off it,” Louis said and nodded toward Mrs. Buddinger as she left. She smiled and waved back. Louis found an apple and began to eat it. The Spy was so sweet that Louis’s taste buds cramped for a moment… but it wasn't totally unpleasant. Won one tonight, Lou, he thought and ate the apple with pleasure. He was very hungry.

“I do though,” Jud said. “When you need a favor, Louis, come to me first.”

“All right,” Louis said, “I’ll do that.”

The ambulance from Bangor MedCu arrived twenty minutes later. As Louis stood outside watching the orderlies load Norma into the back, he saw Rachel looking out the living room window. He waved to her. She lifted a hand back.

He and Jud stood together and watched the ambulance pull away, lights flashing, siren silent.

“Guess I’ll go up to the hospital now,” Jud said.

“They won’t let you see her tonight, Jud. They’ll want to do an EKG and then put her in intensive care. No visitors for the first twelve hours.”

“Is she going to be okay, Louis? Really okay?”

Louis shrugged. “No one can promise that. It was a heart attack. If it means anything, I think she’ll be fine. Maybe better than ever, once she starts taking some medicine.”

“Ayuh,” Jud said, lighting a Chesterfield.

Louis smiled and looked at his watch. He was surprised to see it was only ten minutes to eight. It felt like much more time had passed.

“Jud, I want to get Ellie so she can finish trick-or-treating.”

“Yeah, of course you do.” This came out as Coss y’do. “Tell her to get all the treats she can, Louis.”

“I will,” Louis promised.

Ellie was still in her witch costume when Louis got home. Rachel had tried to get her into her nightgown, but Ellie had refused, hoping that the game, stopped because of the heart attack, might still happen. When Louis told her to put her coat back on, Ellie whooped and clapped.

“It’s going to be very late for her, Louis.”

“We’ll take the car,” he said. “Come on, Rachel. She’s been looking forward to this for a month.”

“Well…” She smiled. Ellie saw it and shouted again. She ran for the coat closet. “Is Norma all right?”

“I think so.” He felt good. Tired but good. “It was a small one. She’s going to have to be careful, but when you’re seventy-five you have to realize you can't do everything you used to anyway.”

“It’s lucky you were there. Almost like divine intervention.”

“I’ll settle for luck.” He grinned as Ellie came back. “You ready, Witch Hazel?”

“I’m ready,” she said. “Come on—come on—come on!”

On the way home with half a bag of candy an hour later (Ellie protested when Louis finally stopped, but not too much; she was tired), his daughter surprised him by saying: “Did I make Mrs. Crandall have the heart attack, Daddy? When I wouldn’t take the apple with the bruise on it?”

Louis looked at her, surprised, wondering where children got such strange, partly superstitious ideas. Step on a crack, break your mother’s back. Loves me, loves me not. Daddy’s stomach, Daddy’s head, smile at midnight, Daddy’s dead. That made him think of the Pet Sematary again and those simple circles. He wanted to smile at himself but couldn't quite.

“No, honey,” he said. “When you were with those two ghosts—”

“Those weren’t ghosts, just the Buddinger twins.”

“Well, when you were with them, Mr. Crandall was telling me that his wife had been having little chest pains. In fact, you might have been responsible for saving her life or at least for keeping it from being much worse.”

Now it was Ellie’s turn to look surprised.

Louis nodded. “She needed a doctor, honey. I’m a doctor. But I was only there because it was your night to go trick-or-treating.”

Ellie thought about this for a long time and then nodded. “But she’ll probably die anyway,” she said plainly. “People who have heart attacks usually die. Even if they live, pretty soon they have another one and another one and another one until… boom!”

“And where did you learn these wise words, may I ask?”

Ellie only shrugged—a shrug much like Louis's, he was amused to see.

She let him carry in her bag of candy—a huge sign of trust—and Louis thought about her attitude. The thought of Church’s death had made her almost hysterical. But the thought of grandmotherly Norma Crandall dying… Ellie seemed to take that calmly, as if it were expected, a given. What had she said? Another one and another one, until… boom!

The kitchen was empty, but Louis could hear Rachel moving around upstairs. He set Ellie’s candy down on the counter and said, “It doesn’t always happen that way, Ellie. Norma’s heart attack was very small, and I was able to treat her right away. I don’t think her heart was damaged much at all. She—”

“Oh, I know,” Ellie agreed, almost happily. “But she’s old, and she’ll die pretty soon anyway. Mr. Crandall too. Can I have an apple before I go to bed, Daddy?”

“No,” he said, looking at her thoughtfully. “Go up and brush your teeth, babe.”

Does anyone really think they understand kids? he wondered.

When the house was quiet and they were in their side-by-side twin beds, Rachel asked softly, “Was it very bad for Ellie, Lou? Was she upset?”

No, he thought. She knows old people die regularly, just like she knows to let the grasshopper go when it spits… like she knows that if you stumble on the number thirteen when you’re skipping rope, your best friend will die… like she knows that you put the graves in shrinking circles up in the Pet Sematary…

“Nope,” he said. “She handled herself very well. Let’s go to sleep, Rachel, okay?”

That night, as they slept in their house and as Jud lay awake in his, there was another hard frost. The wind rose in the early morning, ripping most of the remaining leaves, which were now a dull brown, from the trees.

The wind woke Louis, and he sat up on his elbows, mostly asleep and confused. There were steps on the stairs… slow, dragging steps. Pascow had come back. Only now, he thought, two months had passed. When the door opened he would see a rotting horror, the jogging shorts covered with mould, the flesh fallen away in big holes, the brain decayed to mush. Only the eyes would be alive… horribly bright and alive. Pascow would not speak this time; his vocal cords would be too decayed to make sounds. But his eyes… they would beckon him to come.

“No,” he breathed, and the steps died out.

He got up, went to the door, and pulled it open, his lips pulled back in a grimace of fear and resolve, his flesh shrinking. Pascow would be there, and with his raised arms he would look like a long-dead conductor about to call for the first thundering phrase of Walpurgisnacht.

Nothing like that, as Jud might have said. The landing was empty… silent. There was no sound but the wind. Louis went back to bed and slept.

Chapter 21

The next day, Louis called the intensive care unit at the EMMC. Norma’s condition was still listed as critical; that was normal for the first twenty-four hours after a heart attack. Louis got a more positive update from Weybridge, her doctor, however. “I wouldn’t even call it a small heart attack,” he said. “No scarring. She owes you a lot, Dr. Creed.”

On impulse, Louis stopped by the hospital later that week with flowers, and found that Norma had been moved to a room with another patient downstairs—a very good sign. Jud was with her.

Norma was thrilled with the flowers and called a nurse for a vase. Then she told Jud how to arrange them until they were in water, arranged just how she wanted, and placed on the dresser in the corner.

“Mother’s feeling much better,” Jud said dryly after he had adjusted the flowers for the third time.

“Don’t be a smart aleck, Judson,” Norma said.

“No, ma’am.”

Finally, Norma looked at Louis. “I want to thank you for what you did,” she said with a shyness that was completely genuine and therefore even more touching. “Jud says I owe you my life.”

Embarrassed, Louis said, “Jud is exaggerating.”

“Not much, he isn’t,” Jud said. He squinted at Louis, almost smiling but not quite. “Didn’t your mother tell you to always accept a thank-you, Louis?”

She hadn’t mentioned it, at least not that Louis recalled, but he thought she had once said that acting humble was almost as bad as being proud.

“Norma,” he said, “I was happy to help with anything I could.”

“You’re a good man,” Norma said. “Take my husband out for a beer. I’m feeling sleepy again, and I can’t seem to get rid of him.”

Jud jumped up eagerly. “Great! I’m up for that, Louis. Let’s go before she changes her mind.”

The first snow fell a week before Thanksgiving. They got another four inches on November twenty-second, but the day before the holiday was clear, blue, and cold. Louis took his family to Bangor International Airport and watched them leave on the first part of their trip back to Chicago to see Rachel’s parents.

“It’s not right,” Rachel said, probably for the twentieth time since they started seriously talking about it a month ago. “I don’t like the thought of you being all alone in the house on Thanksgiving. It’s supposed to be a family holiday, Louis.”

Louis shifted Gage, who looked huge and wide-eyed in his first big-boy coat, to his other arm. Ellie was at one of the big windows, watching an Air Force helicopter take off.

“I’m not going to be sad,” Louis said. “Jud and Norma are having me over for turkey and all the fixings. Honestly, I’m the one who feels bad. I’ve never liked these big holiday gatherings anyway. I start drinking during some football game at three in the afternoon and fall asleep at seven, and the next day my head feels awful. I just don’t like sending you off with the two kids.”

“I’ll be fine,” she said. “Flying first class, I feel like royalty. And Gage will sleep on the flight from Logan to O’Hare.”

“You hope,” he said, and they both laughed.

The flight was called, and Ellie hurried over. “That’s us, Mommy. Come on—come on—come on. They’ll leave without us.”

“No, they won’t,” Rachel said. She held her three pink boarding cards in one hand. She was wearing her fur coat, some fake stuff that was a rich brown… maybe it was trying to look like muskrat, Louis thought. Whatever it was, it made her look beautiful.

Maybe he showed some of what he felt, because she hugged him quickly, almost squashing Gage between them. Gage looked surprised but not too upset.

“Louis Creed, I love you,” she said.

“Mom-eee,” Ellie said, now very impatient. “come on-come on-c—”

“Oh, all right,” she said. “Be good, Louis.”

“Okay,” he said, grinning, “I’ll be careful. Say hello to your folks, Rachel.”

“Oh, you,” she said and wrinkled her nose at him. Rachel wasn’t fooled; she knew why Louis was skipping this trip. “Fun-nee.”

He watched them go onto the plane… and disappear for the next week. He already felt homesick and lonely for them. He moved to the window where Ellie had been, hands in his coat pockets, watching the baggage handlers load the plane.

The truth was simple. Not only Mr. but also Mrs. Irwin Goldman of Lake Forest had disliked Louis from the start. He came from a poor background, but that was just the beginning. Even worse, he expected their daughter to support him while he went to medical school, where he would probably fail.

Louis could have handled all this, in fact had been doing so. Then something had happened which Rachel did not know about and never would… not from Louis, anyway. Irwin Goldman had offered to pay for Louis’s entire medical school. The price of this “scholarship” (Goldman’s word) was that Louis should break off his engagement with Rachel right away.

Louis Creed was not at the best time of his life to deal with such an insult, but such dramatic offers (or bribes, to be honest) are rarely made to those who are at the best time—which might be around the age of eighty-five. He was tired, for one thing. He was spending eighteen hours a week in classes, another twenty studying, another fifteen waiting tables in a pizza place near the Whitehall Hotel. He was also nervous. Mr. Goldman’s strangely cheerful manner that evening was completely different from his previous cold behavior, and Louis thought that when Goldman invited him into the study for a cigar, a look had passed from him to his wife. Later—much later, when time had given him some perspective—Louis would think that horses must feel the same vague anxiety when they smell the first smoke of a prairie fire. He began expecting Goldman to reveal at any moment that he knew Louis had been sleeping with his daughter.

When Goldman instead made his unbelievable offer—even taking his checkbook from the pocket of his smoking jacket like a prop in a silly play—Louis had exploded. He accused Goldman of trying to keep his daughter like a museum piece, of having no regard for anyone but himself, and of being a bossy, insensitive jerk. It would be a long time before he would admit to himself that part of his rage had been relief.

All of these little insights into Irwin Goldman’s character, though perhaps true, had no polite touch. Any sense of light comedy vanished; if there was humor in the rest of the conversation, it was of a much more crude kind. Goldman told him to get out and that if he ever saw Louis on his doorstep again, he would shoot him like a worthless dog. Louis told Goldman to take his checkbook and shove it up his ass. Goldman said he had seen bums in the gutter who had more potential than Louis Creed. Louis told Goldman he could also shove his goddamn BankAmericard and his American Express Gold Card right up there beside his checkbook.

None of this was a good start to building good relations with the future in-laws.

In the end Rachel had brought them around (after each man had had a chance to regret the things he had said, although neither of them had ever changed his mind about the other). There was no more drama, certainly no terribly theatrical from-this-day-forward-I-have-no-daughter scene. Goldman would have probably suffered through Rachel’s marriage to a monster before disowning her. Nevertheless the face above the collar of Irwin Goldman’s morning coat on the day Louis married Rachel looked a lot like the faces sometimes seen carved on Egyptian coffins. Their wedding present had been a six-place setting of Spode china and a microwave oven. No money. For most of Louis’s chaotic med school days, Rachel had worked as a clerk in a women’s clothing store. And from that day to this day, Rachel only knew that things had been and continued to be “tense” between her husband and her parents… particularly between Louis and her father.

Louis could have gone to Chicago with his family, although the university schedule would have meant flying back three days earlier than Rachel and the kids. That was no big deal. On the other hand, four days with his in-laws would have been.

The children had softened his in-laws a lot, as children often do. Louis suspected that he himself could have completed the reconciliation simply by pretending he had forgotten that evening in Goldman’s study. It wouldn’t even matter that Goldman knew he was pretending. But the fact was (and he at least had the guts to be honest about it with himself) that he did not quite want to reconcile. Ten years was a long time, but it was not quite long enough to take away the bad taste that had come into his mouth when, in Goldman’s study over glasses of brandy, the old man had opened one side of that ridiculous smoking jacket and removed the checkbook inside. Yes, he had felt relief that the nights—five of them in all—that he and Rachel had spent in his narrow, sagging apartment bed had not been discovered, but that surprised disgust was its own thing, and the years between then and now had not changed it.

He could have come, but he preferred to send his father-in-law his grandchildren, his daughter, and a message.

The Delta 727 pulled away from the ramp, turned… and he saw Ellie at one of the front windows, waving wildly. Louis waved back, smiling, and then someone—Ellie or Rachel—lifted Gage into the window. Louis waved, and Gage waved back—perhaps seeing him, perhaps only copying Ellie.

“Fly my people safe,” he muttered, then zipped his coat and went out to the parking lot. Here the wind howled and rushed with force enough to almost tear his hunter’s cap off his head, and he clapped a hand to it. He struggled with his keys to unlock the driver’s side door of his car and then turned as the jet rose beyond the terminal building, its nose tilted upward into the hard blue, its engines roaring.

Feeling very lonely indeed now—almost about to cry—Louis waved again.

He was still sad that evening when he recrossed Route 15 after a couple of beers with Jud and Norma—Norma had drunk a glass of wine, something Dr. Weybridge allowed, even suggested she have. They had moved into the kitchen tonight because of the season.

Jud had fired up the small Marek stove, and they had sat around it, the beer cold, the heat good, and Jud had talked about how the Micmac Indians had prevented a British landing at Machiss two hundred years ago. In those days the Micmacs had been quite scary, he said, and then added that he guessed there were still a few state and federal land lawyers who thought they were.

It should have been a good evening, but Louis was aware of the empty house waiting for him. Crossing the lawn and feeling the frost crunching under his shoes, he heard the telephone begin to ring in the house. He broke into a run, got through the front door, sprinted through the living room (knocking over a magazine stand), and then slid most of the way across the kitchen, his frosty shoes skidding over the linoleum. He grabbed the phone.

“Hello?”

“Louis?” Rachel’s voice, a little distant but absolutely fine. “We’re here. We made it. No problems.”

“Great!” he said and sat down to talk to her, thinking: I wish to God you were here.

Chapter 22

The Thanksgiving dinner Jud and Norma made was excellent. When it was over, Louis went home feeling full and sleepy. He went upstairs to the bedroom, enjoying the quiet a little, kicked off his shoes, and lay down. It was just after three o’clock; the day outside was bright with weak, winter sunshine.

I’ll just doze a little, he thought and fell fast asleep.

The phone ringing in the bedroom woke him up. He reached for it, trying to wake up fully, confused by the fact that it was almost dark outside. He could hear the wind blowing hard around the corners of the house and the faint, rough sound of the furnace.

“Hello,” he said. It would be Rachel, calling from Chicago again to wish him a happy Thanksgiving. She would put Ellie on and Ellie would talk and then Gage would get on and Gage would babble—and how the hell had he managed to sleep all afternoon when he had meant to watch the football game… ?

But it wasn’t Rachel. It was Jud.

“Louis? I’m afraid you might have a little problem.”

He swung out of bed, still trying to clear the sleep from his head. “Jud? What problem?”

“Well, there’s a dead cat over here on our lawn,” Jud said. “I think it might be your daughter’s.”

“Church?” Louis asked. There was a sudden sinking in his stomach. “Are you sure, Jud?”

“No, I’m not one hundred percent sure,” Jud said, “but it sure looks like him.”

“Oh. Oh shit. I’ll be right over, Jud.”

“All right, Louis.”

He hung up and just sat there for another minute. Then he went in and used the bathroom, put on his shoes, and went downstairs.

Well, maybe it isn’t Church. Jud himself said he wasn’t one hundred percent sure. Christ, the cat doesn’t even want to go upstairs anymore unless someone carries him… why would he cross the road?

But deep down he felt sure that it was Church… and if Rachel called this evening, as she almost certainly would, what was he going to say to Ellie?

Strangely, he imagined himself saying to Rachel: I know that anything, literally anything, can happen to living things. As a doctor I know that… do you want to be the one to explain to her what happened if he gets run over in the road? But he hadn’t really believed anything was going to happen to Church, had he?

He remembered one of the guys he played poker with, Wickes Sullivan, asking him once how he could be attracted to his wife but not to the naked women he saw every day. Louis had tried to explain that it wasn’t like in people’s fantasies—a woman coming in for a Pap smear or to learn how to do a breast self-exam didn’t suddenly drop a sheet and stand there like a goddess. You saw a breast, a vulva, a thigh. The rest was covered, and there was a nurse there, mostly to protect the doctor’s reputation. Wicky didn’t believe it. A tit is a tit, was Wicky’s idea, and a twat is a twat. You should either be horny all the time or never. All Louis could say was that your wife’s tit was different.

Just like your family’s supposed to be different, he thought now. Church wasn’t supposed to get killed because he was part of the family. What he hadn’t been able to make Wicky understand was that doctors separated things in their minds as easily and blindly as anyone else. A tit wasn’t a tit unless it was your wife’s tit. At the office, a tit was a case. You could talk about leukemia statistics in children until you were blue in the face and still not believe it if one of your own kids got sick. My kid? My kid’s cat, even? Doctor, you have to be kidding.

Never mind. Take this one step at a time.

But that was hard when he remembered how upset Ellie had become at the thought of Church dying someday.

Stupid cat, why did we ever have to get a cat, anyway?

But he wasn’t doing that anymore. That was supposed to keep him alive.

“Church?” he called, but there was only the furnace, humming and humming, wasting money. The couch in the living room, where Church had been spending most of his time lately, was empty. He wasn’t lying on any of the radiators. Louis rattled the cat’s dish, the one thing that always made Church come running if he could hear it, but no cat came running this time… and never would again, he was afraid.

He put on his coat and hat and headed for the door. Then he came back. Giving in to what his heart told him, he opened the cupboard under the sink and crouched down. There were two kinds of plastic bags in there—small white ones for the trash cans and big green garbage bags. Louis took one of the latter. Church had gained weight since he had been neutered.

He pushed the bag into one of the side pockets of his jacket, not liking the smooth, cold feel of the plastic under his fingers. Then he went out the front door and crossed the street to Jud’s house.

It was about five-thirty. Twilight was ending. The landscape looked lifeless. The last bit of sunset was a strange orange line on the horizon across the river. The wind blew straight down Route 15, numbing Louis’s cheeks and whipping away his white breath. He shuddered, but not from the cold. It was a feeling of loneliness that made him shudder. It was strong and convincing. There seemed no way to describe it simply. It was unseen. He just felt alone, untouched and unable to touch.

He saw Jud across the road, wrapped up in his big green coat, his face hidden by the shadow of the fur-trimmed hood. Standing on his frozen lawn, he looked like a statue, just another dead thing in this twilight scene where no bird sang.

Louis started across, and then Jud moved—waved him back. Shouted something Louis couldn’t hear over the loud whine of the wind. Louis stepped back, realizing the wind’s whine had gotten louder and sharper. A moment later an air-horn blasted and an Orinco truck roared past close enough to make his pants and jacket flap. He almost walked right in front of it.

This time he looked both ways before crossing. There was only the tanker’s taillights, disappearing into the twilight.

“Thought that ‘Rinco truck was gonna get you,” Jud said. “Be careful, Louis.” Even this close, Louis couldn’t see Jud’s face, and the uncomfortable feeling persisted that this could have been anyone… anyone at all.

“Where’s Norma?” he asked, still not looking down at the sprawled bundle of fur by Jud’s foot.

“Went to the Thanksgiving church service,” he said. “She’ll stay for the supper, I guess, although I don’t think she’ll eat anything. She’s lost her appetite.” The wind gusted, shifting the hood back momentarily, and Louis saw that it was indeed Jud—who else would it have been? “It’s mostly an excuse for a hen party,” Jud said. “They don’t eat much but sandwiches after the big meal at noon. She’ll be back around eight.”

Louis knelt down to look at the cat. Don’t let it be Church, he wished hard, as he turned its head gently on its neck with gloved fingers. Let it be someone else’s cat, let Jud be wrong.

But of course it was Church. He was not mangled or disfigured in any way; he had not been run over by one of the big tankers or semis that cruised Route 15 (just what was that Orinco truck doing out on Thanksgiving? he wondered randomly). Church’s eyes were half-open, as glazed as green marbles. A small flow of blood had come from his mouth, which was also open. Not a lot of blood; just enough to stain the white bib on his chest.

“Yours, Louis?”

“Mine,” he agreed and sighed.

He was aware for the first time that he had loved Church—maybe not as much as Ellie but in his own quiet way. In the weeks after he was neutered, Church had changed, had gotten fat and slow, had established a routine that took him between Ellie’s bed, the couch, and his dish but rarely out of the house. Now, in death, he looked to Louis like the old Church. The mouth so small and bloody, filled with needle-sharp cat’s teeth, was frozen in a fierce snarl. The dead eyes seemed furious. It was as if after the short and uneventful life as a neutered cat, Church had rediscovered his real nature in dying.

“Yeah, it’s Church,” he said. “I’ll be damned if I know how I’m going to tell Ellie about it.”

Suddenly he had an idea. He would bury Church up in the Pet Sematary with no marker or any of that foolishness. He would say nothing to Ellie on the phone tonight about Church; tomorrow he would mention casually that he hadn’t seen Church around; the day after he would suggest that perhaps Church had wandered off. Cats did that sometimes. Ellie would be upset, sure, but there would be none of the finality… no repeat of Rachel’s upsetting refusal to deal with death… just a fading away…

Coward, part of his mind said.

Yes… no argument there. But who wants this trouble?

“She really loves that cat, doesn’t she?” Jud asked.

“Yes,” Louis said, distracted. He moved Church’s head again. The cat had started to get stiff, but the head still moved much more easily than it should have. Broken neck. Right. Knowing that, he thought he could figure out what happened. Church had been crossing the road—God knows why—and a car or truck had hit him, breaking his neck and throwing him onto Jud Crandall’s lawn. Or maybe the cat’s neck broke when he hit the frozen ground. It didn’t matter. Either way, the result was the same. Church was dead.

He looked up at Jud, ready to tell him what he thought, but Jud was looking away at the fading orange light on the horizon. His hood had fallen back a bit, and his face looked thoughtful and serious… even hard.

Louis pulled the green garbage bag out of his pocket and opened it up, holding it tight so the wind wouldn’t blow it away. The sharp crackling sound of the bag seemed to bring Jud back to the present.

“Yes, I guess she really loves it,” Jud said. His use of the present tense felt a little strange… the whole scene, with the fading light, the cold, and the wind, felt strange and gothic to him.

Here’s Heathcliff out on the empty moors, Louis thought, wincing against the cold. Getting ready to put the family cat into a Hefty Bag. Great.

He grabbed Church’s tail, opened the bag, and lifted the cat. He made a disgusted, unhappy face at the sound the cat’s body made as he lifted it—rrrriiippp as he pulled it out of the frost it had settled into. The cat felt unbelievably heavy, as if death had become a real weight inside it. Christ, he feels like a bucket of sand.

Jud held the other side of the bag, and Louis dropped Church in, happy to be rid of that strange, unpleasant weight.

“What are you going to do with it now?” Jud asked.

“Put him in the garage, I guess,” Louis said. “Bury him in the morning.”

“In the Pet Sematary?”

Louis shrugged. “I guess so.”

“Going to tell Ellie?”

“I… I’ll have to think about that for a while.”

Jud was quiet for a moment, and then he seemed to make up his mind. “Wait here a minute, Louis.”

Jud moved away, not even thinking that Louis might not want to wait on this cold night. He moved with confidence and that easy grace that was so strange in an old man. And Louis found he had nothing to say anyway. He didn’t feel like himself. He watched Jud go, happy to stand there.

He lifted his face into the wind after the door clicked shut, the garbage bag with Church’s body in it rustling between his feet.

Content.

Yes, he was. For the first time since they had moved to Maine, he felt he belonged, that he was home. Standing alone in the fading light, on the edge of winter, he felt sad but also strangely excited and complete—more complete than he had been, or could recall being, since he was a child.

Something's going to happen here, Bubba. Something pretty weird, I think.

He looked up and saw cold winter stars in a darkening sky.

He didn't know how long he stood like that, but it couldn't have been long. Then a light came on Jud's porch, moved, went to the door, and down the steps. It was Jud with a big flashlight. In his other hand he held something Louis thought was a large X… then he saw it was a pick and shovel.

He gave the shovel to Louis, who took it.

"Jud, what are you doing? We can't bury him tonight."

"Yes, we can. And we will." Jud's face was hidden by the bright light.

"Jud, it's dark. It's late. And cold—"

"Come on," Jud said. "Let's get it done."

Louis shook his head and tried to start again, but the words were hard to find—the words of explanation and sense. They seemed so pointless against the wind's low cry, the many stars in the black sky.

“It can wait till tomorrow when we can see—”

“Does she love the cat?”

“Yes, but—”

Jud’s voice, soft and somehow reasonable: “And do you love her?”

“Of course I love her, she’s my dau—”

“Then come on.”

Louis went.

Twice—maybe three times—on the walk up to the Pet Sematary that night Louis tried to talk to Jud, but Jud didn’t answer. Louis gave up. That feeling of peace, strange considering the situation but completely real, stayed with him. It seemed to come from everywhere. The steady ache in his muscles from carrying Church in one hand and the shovel in the other was a part of it. The wind, deadly cold, numbing exposed skin, was a part of it; it twisted steadily in the trees. Once they got into the woods, there was hardly any snow. The bobbing light of Jud’s flash was a part of it. He felt the strong, undeniable, pull of some secret. Some dark secret.

The shadows disappeared and there was a feeling of space. Snow shone palely.

“Rest here,” Jud said, and Louis set the bag down. He wiped sweat off his forehead with his arm. Rest here? But they were here. He could see the markers in the moving, random sweep of Jud’s light as Jud sat down in the thin snow and put his face between his arms.

“Jud? Are you all right?”

“Fine. Just need to catch my breath.”

Louis sat down next to him and took several deep breaths.

“You know,” he said, “I feel better than I have in maybe six years. I know that sounds crazy when you’re burying your daughter’s cat, but it’s the truth, Jud. I feel good.”

Jud breathed deeply a couple of times. “Yeah, I know,” he said. “It happens sometimes. You don’t choose when you feel good, any more than you choose when you feel bad. And the place has something to do with it, but don’t trust that. Heroin makes addicts feel good when they shoot up, but it’s poisoning them the whole time. Poisoning their bodies and their minds. This place can be like that, Louis, and don’t forget it. I hope I’m doing the right thing. I think I am, but I can’t be sure. Sometimes my head gets mixed up. I think it's just old age.”

“I don’t know what you’re talking about.”

“This place has power, Louis. Not so much here, but… the place we’re going.”

“Jud—”

“Come on,” Jud said, standing up again. The flashlight shone on the pile of fallen trees. Jud was walking toward it. Louis suddenly remembered when he had sleepwalked. What did Pascow say in the dream he had then?

Don’t go beyond, no matter how much you want to, Doctor. The barrier should not be broken.

But now, tonight, that dream or warning or whatever it was felt like years ago, not just months. Louis felt good, almost magically alive, ready for anything, yet full of wonder. He thought this felt a lot like a dream.

Then Jud turned toward him, the hood making his face seem empty, and for a moment Louis thought it was Pascow himself standing there. He imagined the light turning around, shining on a grinning skull in fur, and his fear came back like a splash of cold water.

“Jud,” he said, “we can’t climb over that. We’ll both break a leg and probably freeze trying to get back.”

“Just follow me,” Jud said. “Follow me and don’t look down. Don’t stop and don’t look down. I know the way through, but we have to do it fast and sure.”

Louis started to think it might be a dream, that he had never woken up from his nap. If I was awake, he thought, I wouldn’t go up that mess of fallen trees any more than I’d get drunk and go skydiving. But I’m going to do it. I really think I am. So… I must be dreaming. Right?

Jud went slightly to the left, away from the middle of the fallen trees. The flashlight shined brightly on the jumbled pile of

(bones)

fallen trees and old logs. The circle of light got smaller and brighter as they got closer. Without stopping at all, without even looking to make sure he was in the right place, Jud started up. He didn't struggle; he didn't climb hunched over like someone climbing a hill of rocks or sand. He just stepped up, as if climbing stairs. He walked like a man who knows exactly where to step next.

Louis followed the same way.

He didn't look down or look for places to put his feet. He felt strangely certain that the fallen trees couldn't hurt him unless he let them. It was completely stupid, of course, like a drunk driver who thinks he's safe as long as he's wearing his lucky charm.

But it worked.

There was no sharp snap of an old branch breaking, no sickening fall into a hole filled with sharp, weathered splinters, each ready to cut and tear. His shoes (Hush Puppy loafers—not ideal for climbing dead-falls) didn’t slip on the old dry moss covering the fallen trees. He didn’t fall forward or backward. The wind howled through the fir trees around them.

For a moment he saw Jud standing on top of the deadfall, and then he started down the other side, calves disappearing, then thighs, then hips and waist. The light flashed off the swaying branches of the trees on the other side of the… the barrier. Yes, that’s what it was—why pretend otherwise? The barrier.

Louis reached the top himself and paused, right foot on an old fallen tree angled upward, left foot on something springier—a tangle of old fir branches? He didn’t look down, but switched the heavy trashbag with Church’s body from his right hand to his left, exchanging it for the lighter shovel. He turned his face into the wind and felt it rush past him, lifting his hair. It was so cold, so clean… so constant.

Moving casually, almost strolling, he started down again. Once a branch about as thick as a strong man’s wrist snapped loudly under his foot, but he felt no worry—and his falling foot was stopped by a heavier branch a few inches down. Louis barely stumbled. He guessed he now understood how company commanders in World War I could stroll along the top of the trenches with bullets flying around them, whistling “Tipperary.” It was crazy, but the craziness made it incredibly exciting.

He walked down, looking straight ahead at Jud’s bright light. Jud was standing there, waiting. Then he reached the bottom, and the excitement burst within him like a shot of fuel on embers.

“We made it!” he shouted. He put the shovel down and clapped Jud on the shoulder. He remembered climbing an apple tree to the top where it swayed in the wind like a ship’s mast. He had not felt so young or so alive in twenty years or more. “Jud, we made it!”

“Did you think we wouldn’t?” Jud asked.

Louis started to say something—Think we wouldn’t? We’re lucky we didn’t kill ourselves!—and then stopped. He had never really doubted it, not from the moment Jud approached the deadfall. And he wasn’t worried about going back.

“I guess not,” he said.

“Come on. We still have a ways to walk. Three miles or more.”

They walked. The path did keep going. In some spots it seemed very wide, but the moving light didn't show much clearly. Mostly, it was a feeling of space, like the trees had moved back. Once or twice Louis looked up and saw stars turning between the thick dark edge of trees. Once, something ran across the path in front of them, and the light caught the reflection of greenish eyes—there and gone.

At other times, the path narrowed until bushes scratched stiff fingers across Louis’s coat. He switched the bag and the shovel more often, but his shoulders now ached constantly. He fell into a walking rhythm and became almost hypnotized by it. There was power here, he felt it. He remembered being a senior in high school. He, his girlfriend, and another couple had gone way out in the backwoods and ended up making out at the end of a dead-end dirt road near a power station. They hadn’t been there long before Louis’s girlfriend said she wanted to go home, or at least somewhere else, because all her teeth (especially the ones with fillings, which was most of them) were aching. Louis had been glad to leave too. The air around the power station had made him feel nervous and too alert. This was like that, but stronger. Stronger, but not unpleasant. It was—

Jud had stopped at the bottom of a long slope. Louis bumped into him.

Jud turned toward him. “We’re almost there now,” he said calmly. “This next part is like the deadfall—you have to walk steady and easy. Just follow me and don’t look down. Did you feel us going downhill?”

“Yes.”

“This is the edge of what the Micmacs called Little God Swamp. The fur traders called it Dead Man’s Bog, and most who came once and got out never came back.”

“Is there quicksand?”

“Oh, yes, plenty of quicksand! Streams that bubble up through a big pile of quartz sand left by the glacier. We always called it silica sand, but there's probably a real name for it.”

Jud looked at him, and for a moment Louis thought he saw something bright and not very nice in the old man’s eyes.

Then Jud moved the flashlight and that look disappeared.

“There are a lot of strange things down this way, Louis. The air feels heavier… more electric… or something.”

Louis jumped.

“What’s wrong?”

“Nothing,” Louis said, thinking about that night on the dead-end road.

“You might see St. Elmo’s fire—what sailors call ghost-lights. It makes strange shapes, but it’s nothing. If you see some of those shapes and they bother you, just look away. You may hear sounds like voices, but they are the loons down south toward Prospect. The sound travels. It's strange.”

“Loons?” Louis said, sounding unsure. “This time of year?”

“Oh, yes,” Jud said again, and his voice was terribly flat and impossible to read. For a moment Louis really wished he could see the old man’s face again. That look—

“Jud, where are we going? What are we doing out here in the middle of nowhere?”

“I’ll tell you when we get there.” Jud turned away. “Watch out for the clumps of grass.”

They began to walk again, stepping from one large mound to the next. Louis didn’t think about where to step. His feet seemed to find the mounds on their own, without him trying. He slipped only once, his left shoe breaking through a thin layer of ice and sinking into cold, slimy water. He pulled it out quickly and kept going, following Jud’s swaying light. That light, moving through the woods, reminded him of the pirate stories he had enjoyed reading as a kid. Bad guys off to bury gold coins in the dark of night… and of course, one of them would fall into the hole on top of the chest, with a bullet in his heart, because the pirates believed—or so the writers of these exciting stories claimed—that the dead friend’s ghost would stay there to protect the loot.

Except we're not burying treasure. Just my daughter’s neutered cat.

He felt like laughing out loud but held it back.

He didn’t hear any “sounds like voices,” or see any strange lights, but after stepping over several clumps of grass, he looked down and saw that his feet, calves, knees, and lower thighs had disappeared into a ground fog that was perfectly smooth, perfectly white, and completely blocked his view. It was like walking through the world’s lightest snow.

The air seemed to have a kind of light in it now, and it was warmer, he was sure of it. He could see Jud ahead of him, moving steadily, with the dull end of the pickaxe over his shoulder. The pickaxe added to the image of a man determined to bury treasure.

That strange feeling of excitement stayed with him, and he suddenly wondered if maybe Rachel was trying to call him; if, back at the house, the phone was ringing and ringing, making its normal, everyday sound. If—

He almost bumped into Jud’s back again. The old man had stopped in the middle of the path. His head was tilted to one side. His mouth was tight and strained.

“Jud? What’s—”

“Shhh!”

Louis quieted down, looking around nervously. Here the ground fog was thinner, but he still couldn’t see his own shoes. Then he heard rustling bushes and snapping branches. Something was moving out there—something big.

He opened his mouth to ask Jud if it was a moose (he really thought it was a bear), then closed it. The sound carries, Jud had said.

He tilted his head, copying Jud without realizing it, and listened. The sound seemed far away, then very near, moving away and then coming toward them in a scary way. Louis felt sweat trickle down his face. He shifted the bag with Church in it from one hand to the other. His palm was damp, and the plastic felt slippery, like it would slide out of his hand. Now the thing out there seemed so close that Louis thought he’d see it any second, maybe rising up on two legs, blocking out the stars with some huge, unknown body.

He wasn't thinking about a bear anymore.

Now he didn’t know what he was thinking.

Then it moved away and vanished.

Louis opened his mouth again, ready to ask, What was that? Then a loud, crazy laugh came from the dark, rising and falling in waves, loud, sharp, and scary. Louis felt like every part of him had turned to ice, and that he had gained weight, so much weight that if he tried to run, he would sink into the swampy ground.

The laughter rose, broke into dry cracks like a crumbling rock, reached a scream, then fell into a low chuckle that might have turned into sobs before it disappeared.

Somewhere, water dripped, and above them, like a river in the sky, the wind made a steady noise. Otherwise, Little God Swamp was quiet.

Louis started to shake all over. His skin, especially on his stomach, began to crawl. Yes, crawl was the right word; his skin really seemed to be moving. His mouth was totally dry. He felt like he had no spit left. Yet he still felt excited, a crazy feeling that wouldn't go away.

“What in God’s name?” he whispered to Jud.

Jud turned to look at him, and in the dim light Louis thought the old man looked ancient. The strange, excited light was gone from his eyes. His face was tight, and his eyes showed stark terror. But his voice was steady when he spoke. “Just a loon,” he said. “Come on. Almost there.”

They went on. The clumps of grass became solid ground again. For a few moments Louis felt like he was in an open space, but the dim glow in the air was now gone, and he could barely see Jud’s back three feet in front of him. Short, frosty grass crunched like glass under his feet. Then they were in the trees again. He could smell pine, feel needles. Now and then a twig or branch scraped against him.

Louis had lost all track of time or direction, but they didn’t walk long before Jud stopped again and turned to him.

“Steps here,” he said. “Cut into rock. Forty-two or forty-four, I forget which. Just follow me. When we get to the top, we’re there.”

He started to climb again, and Louis followed.

The stone steps were wide enough, but the feeling of the ground falling away was unsettling. Now and then his shoe crunched on loose pebbles and bits of stone.

… twelve… thirteen… fourteen…

The wind was sharper, colder, quickly numbing his face. Are we above the trees? he wondered. He looked up and saw countless stars, cold lights in the darkness. Never had the stars made him feel so small, insignificant, and meaningless. He asked himself the old question—is there any intelligent life out there?—and instead of wonder, the thought brought a horrible cold feeling, as if he had asked himself what it would be like to eat a handful of wriggling bugs.

… twenty-six… twenty-seven… twenty-eight…

Who carved these, anyway? Indians? The Micmacs? Did they have tools? I’ll have to ask Jud. “Indians with tools” made him think of “fur-bearing animals,” and that made him think of the thing that had been moving near them in the woods. He stumbled, and he scraped a gloved hand along the rock wall to his left for balance. The wall felt old, chipped, grooved, and wrinkled. Like dry, worn-out skin, he thought.

“You all right, Louis?” Jud murmured.

“I’m okay,” he said, although he was almost out of breath and his muscles ached from holding Church in the bag.

… forty-two… forty-three… forty-four…

“Forty-five,” Jud said. “I forgot. Haven’t been up here in twelve years, I guess. Don’t think I’ll ever have a reason to come again. Here… up you come and up you get.”

He grabbed Louis’s arm and helped him up the last step.

“We’re here,” Jud said.

Louis looked around. He could see well enough; the starlight was faint but enough. They were standing on a rocky, rubble-covered patch of rock that stuck out of the thin earth like a dark tongue. Looking the other way, he could see the tops of the fir trees they had walked through to get to the steps. They had climbed to the top of a strange, flat-topped mesa, a geological oddity that would have seemed more normal in Arizona or New Mexico. Because the grassy top of the mesa—or hill, or cut-off mountain, or whatever it was—had no trees, the sun had melted the snow here. Turning back to Jud, Louis saw dry grass bending in the cold wind blowing in his face, and saw that it was a hill, not an isolated mesa. Ahead of them, the ground rose again toward trees. But this flat area was so obvious, and so strange compared to New England’s low and somewhat worn-out hills—

Indians with tools, his mind suddenly said.

“Come on,” Jud said and led him twenty-five yards toward the trees. The wind blew hard up here, but it felt clean. Louis saw several shapes just under the shadows of the trees—trees that were the oldest, tallest firs he had ever seen. The whole feeling of this high, lonely place was emptiness—but an emptiness that seemed to hum.

The dark shapes were piles of stones.

"The Micmacs leveled the top of this hill," Jud said. "No one knows how, any more than they know how the Mayans built their pyramids. And the Micmacs have forgotten, just like the Mayans have."

"Why? Why did they do it?"

"This was their burial ground," Jud said. "I brought you here so you could bury Ellie's cat here. The Micmacs didn't care, you know. They buried their pets right next to their owners."

This made Louis think of the Egyptians, who had gone even further: they had killed the pets of royalty so the pets' souls could go to the afterlife with their masters' souls. He remembered reading about the killing of over ten thousand pets after one pharaoh's daughter died—including six hundred pigs and two thousand peacocks. The pigs had been scented with rose perfume, the dead lady's favorite, before they were killed.

And they built pyramids too. No one knows for sure what the Mayan pyramids are for—some say navigation and timekeeping, like Stonehenge—but we know what the Egyptian pyramids were and are… huge monuments to death, the world's biggest tombstones. Here Lies Ramses II, He Was Obedient, Louis thought and laughed wildly, feeling helpless.

Jud looked at him, not surprised.

"Go on and bury your animal," he said. "I'm going to smoke. I'd help you, but you have to do it yourself. Each person buries their own. That's how it was done then."

"Jud, what's this all about? Why did you bring me here?"

"Because you saved Norma's life," Jud said, and though he sounded sincere—and Louis was sure he believed he was sincere—he suddenly felt strongly that the man was lying… or that he was being lied to and then lying to Louis. He remembered that look he had seen, or thought he had seen, in Jud's eye.

But up here none of that seemed important. The wind was more important, blowing freely around him in that steady flow, lifting his hair from his forehead and ears.

Jud sat down with his back against a tree, cupped his hands around a match, and lit a Chesterfield. “Want to rest before you start?”

“No, I’m okay,” Louis said. He could have asked more, but he didn't really want to. This felt wrong but also right, and he decided that was enough… for now. He really only needed to know one thing. “Will I really be able to dig him a grave? The soil looks thin.” Louis nodded toward the rock sticking out of the ground at the edge of the steps.

Jud nodded slowly. “Yeah,” he said. “Soil’s thin, alright. But soil that grows grass is usually deep enough to bury in, Louis. And people have been burying here a long, long time. It won’t be easy, though.”

And it wasn't. The ground was stony and hard, and he quickly saw he needed the pick to dig deep enough for Church. He started to switch back and forth, using the pick to loosen the hard earth and stones, then the shovel to dig out what he'd loosened. His hands started to hurt. His body warmed up. He felt a strong need to do a good job. He started to hum, something he did when stitching a wound. Sometimes the pick hit a rock hard enough to make sparks, and the shiver went up the handle and vibrated in his hands. He felt blisters forming on his palms and didn’t care, even though he was usually careful with his hands, like most doctors. Above and around him, the wind sang and sang, a tree-note melody.

Against this, he heard the soft drop and chunk of rock. He looked over his shoulder and saw Jud, crouched down and pulling out the bigger rocks he had dug up, making a pile.

“For your cairn,” he said when he saw Louis looking.

“Oh,” Louis said and went back to work.

He made the grave about two feet wide and three feet long—a Cadillac of a grave for a cat, he thought—and when it was about thirty inches deep and the pick was making sparks almost every time, he tossed it and the shovel aside and asked Jud if it was okay.

Jud got up and took a quick look. “Seems fine to me,” he said. “Anyway, what you think is what matters.”

“Will you tell me now what this is about?”

Jud smiled a little. “The Micmacs thought this hill was a magic place,” he said. “Thought this whole forest, from the swamp on north and east, was magic. They made this place special, and they buried their dead here, away from everything else. Other tribes stayed away from it—the Penobscots said these woods were full of ghosts. Later on, the fur trappers started saying much the same thing. I guess some of them saw the strange lights in Little God Swamp and thought they were seeing ghosts.”

Jud smiled, and Louis thought: That isn’t what you think at all.

“Later on, not even the Micmacs would come here. One of them said he saw a Wendigo here and that the ground had gone bad. They had a big meeting about it… or so I heard the story when I was young, Louis, but I heard it from that old drunk Stanny B.—that’s what we all called Stanley Bouchard—and Stanny B. would make things up if he didn’t know them.”

Louis, who only knew that the Wendigo was supposed to be a spirit of the north, said, “Do you think the ground’s gone bad?”

Jud smiled—or at least his lips moved. “I think it’s a dangerous place,” he said softly, “but not for cats or dogs or pet hamsters. Go on and bury your animal, Louis.”

Louis lowered the Hefty Bag into the hole and slowly shoveled the dirt back in. He was cold now and tired. The sound of the earth on the plastic was depressing, and while he didn’t regret coming up here, that excitement was fading, and he had begun to wish it was over. It was a long walk back home.

The sound softened, then stopped—there was only the thud of dirt on more dirt. He scraped the last bit into the hole with the shovel (there’s never enough, he thought, remembering something his uncle, who was a funeral director, had said to him a long time ago, never enough to fill the hole up again) and then turned to Jud.

“Your cairn,” Jud said.

“Look, Jud, I’m pretty tired and—”

“It’s Ellie’s cat,” Jud said, and his voice, although soft, was firm. “She’d want you to do it right.”

Louis sighed. “I suppose she would,” he said.

It took another ten minutes to pile up the rocks Jud handed him, one by one. When it was done, there was a low, cone-shaped pile of stones on Church’s grave, and Louis did feel a small, tired pleasure. It looked right, somehow, standing with the others in the starlight. He supposed Ellie would never see it—the thought of taking her through that swamp with quicksand would make Rachel’s hair turn white—but he had seen it, and it was good.

“Most of these have fallen over,” he said to Jud, standing and brushing at the knees of his pants. He was seeing more clearly now, and he could see scattered loose stones in several places. But Jud had made sure he built his cairn only from stones taken from the grave he himself had dug.

“Ayuh,” Jud said. “Told you: the place is old.”

“Are we done now?”

“Ayuh.” He clapped Louis on the shoulder. “You did good, Louis. I knew you would. Let’s go home.”

“Jud—” he began again, but Jud only grabbed the pick and walked off toward the steps. Louis got the shovel, had to trot to catch up, and then saved his breath for walking. He looked back once, but the cairn marking the grave of his daughter’s cat Winston Churchill had disappeared into the shadows, and he could not see it.

We just ran the film backward, Louis thought tiredly as they emerged from the woods and into the field overlooking his own house some time later. He did not know how much later; he had taken off his watch when he had lain down to doze that afternoon, and it would still be there on the windowsill by his bed. He only knew that he was beat, used up, done in. He could not remember feeling so worn out since his first day on Chicago’s rubbish-disposal crew one high-school summer sixteen or seventeen years ago.

They came back the same way they had gone, but he could remember very little about the trip. He stumbled on the deadfall, he remembered that—lurching forward and thinking absurdly of Peter Pan—oh Jesus, I lost my happy thoughts and down I come—and then Jud’s hand had been there, firm and hard, and a few moments later they had been trudging past the final resting places of Smucky the Cat and Trixie and Marta Our Pet Rabit and onto the path he had once walked not only with Jud but with his whole family.

It seemed that in some tired way he had thought about the dream of Victor Pascow, the one which had resulted in his sleepwalking, but he couldn't connect that night walk to this one. It had also occurred to him that the whole adventure had been dangerous—not in any dramatic way but in a very real one. That he had badly blistered his hands while almost sleepwalking was the least of it. He could have killed himself on the deadfall. Both of them could have. It was hard to see how that fit with being sober. In his current exhaustion, he was willing to blame it on confusion and emotional upset over the death of a pet the whole family had loved.

And after a while, there they were, home again.

They walked toward it together, not speaking, and stopped again in Louis’s driveway. The wind moaned and whined. Silently, Louis handed Jud his pick.

“I should get going,” Jud said at last. “Louella Bisson or Ruthie Parks will be bringing Norma home and she’ll wonder where I am.”

“Do you have the time?” Louis asked. He was surprised that Norma wasn’t home yet; he felt like it must be midnight.

“Oh, yes,” Jud said. “I keep track of the time as long as I’m dressed and then I don’t worry about it.”

He pulled a watch out of his pants pocket and flipped open the decorated cover.

“It’s eight-thirty,” he said and snapped the cover shut again.

“Eight-thirty?” Louis repeated dumbly. “That’s it?”

“How late did you think it was?” Jud asked.

“Later than that,” Louis said.

“I’ll see you tomorrow, Louis,” Jud said and started to walk away.

He turned to Louis, looking a bit puzzled.

“Jud, what did we do tonight?”

“Why, we buried your daughter’s cat.”

“Is that all we did?”

“Just that,” Jud said. “You’re a good man, Louis, but you ask too many questions. Sometimes people have to do things that just feel right. That feel right in their hearts, I mean. And if they do those things and then don’t feel right afterward, full of questions and kind of like they have a bad feeling, but inside their heads instead of their stomachs, they think they messed up. Do you know what I mean?”

“Yes,” Louis said, thinking that Jud must have been reading his mind as they walked downhill through the field toward the house lights.

“What they don’t realize is that maybe they should be questioning those feelings of doubt before they question their own hearts,” Jud said, watching him closely. “What do you think, Louis?”

“I think,” Louis said slowly, “that you might be right.”

“And the things that are in a man’s heart—it doesn’t help him much to talk about those things, does it?”

“Well—”

“No,” Jud said, as if Louis had already agreed. “It doesn’t.” And in his calm voice that was so sure and firm, in that voice which somehow chilled Louis, he said: “They are secret things. Women are supposed to be good at keeping secrets, and I guess they do keep some, but any woman who knows anything would tell you she’s never really seen into any man’s heart. A man’s heart is harder, Louis—like the ground up there in the old Micmac graveyard. Solid rock is close. A man grows what he can… and he takes care of it.”

“Jud—”

“Don’t ask questions, Louis. Accept what’s done and follow your heart.”

“But—”

“But nothing. Accept what’s done, Louis, and follow your heart. We did what was right this time… at least, I hope to God it was right. Another time it could be wrong—wrong as hell.”

“Will you at least answer one question?”

“Well, let’s hear it, and then we’ll see.”

“How did you know about that place?” This question had also come to Louis on the way back, along with the suspicion that Jud himself might be part Micmac—although he didn’t look like it; he looked like all his ancestors had been completely Anglo.

“Why, from Stanny B.,” he said, looking surprised.

“He just told you?”

“No,” Jud said. “It’s not the kind of place you just tell someone about. I buried my dog Spot up there when I was ten. He was chasing a rabbit and ran into some rusty barbed wire. The wounds got infected, and it killed him.”

Something about that was off, something that didn’t match something Louis had heard before, but he was too tired to figure out the difference. Jud said nothing more, just looked at him with his unreadable old man’s eyes.

“Goodnight, Jud,” Louis said.

“Goodnight.”

The old man crossed the road, carrying his pick and shovel.

“Thanks!” Louis called out without thinking.

Jud didn’t turn; he just raised one hand to show he heard.

And in the house, suddenly, the telephone began to ring.

Louis ran, wincing at the aches in his upper thighs and lower back, but by the time he got into the warm kitchen, the phone had already rung six or seven times. It stopped ringing right as he touched it. He picked it up anyway and said hello, but there was only the empty hum.

That was Rachel, he thought. I’ll call her back.

But suddenly it seemed like too much work to dial the number, to awkwardly chat with her mother—or worse, her father flashing his money—to be passed on to Rachel… and then to Ellie. Ellie would still be awake of course; it was an hour earlier in Chicago. Ellie would ask him how Church was doing.

Great, he’s fine. Got hit by an Orinco truck. Somehow I’m absolutely positive it was an Orinco truck. Anything else would lack a sense of drama, if you know what I mean. You don’t? Well, never mind. The truck killed him but didn’t hurt him much. Jud and I buried him up in the old Micmac burying ground—sort of an extra part of the Pet Sematary, if you know what I mean. Amazing walk, kiddo. I’ll take you up there sometime and we’ll put flowers by his marker—excuse me, his pile of stones. After the quicksand’s frozen over, that is, and the bears go to sleep for the winter.

He hung up the telephone, walked to the sink, and filled it with hot water. He removed his shirt and washed. He had been sweating a lot in spite of the cold, and he smelled bad.

There was some leftover meatloaf in the refrigerator. Louis cut it into slices, put them on a slice of bread, and added two thick slices of onion. He looked at this for a moment before covering it with ketchup and slamming down another slice of bread. If Rachel and Ellie had been around, they would have wrinkled their noses in the same way—yuck, gross.

Well, you missed it, ladies, Louis thought with real satisfaction and gobbled his sandwich. It tasted great. Confucius say he who smell like pig eat like wolf, he thought and smiled. He drank a lot of milk straight from the carton—another habit Rachel strongly disliked—and then he went upstairs, undressed, and got into bed without even brushing his teeth. His aches and pains had faded to one low throb that was almost comforting.

His watch was there where he had left it, and he looked at it. Ten minutes of nine. It really was amazing.

Louis turned off the light, turned over on his side, and slept.

He woke up sometime after three the next morning and shuffled to the bathroom, He was standing there urinating, blinking in the bright white bathroom light, when the problem suddenly appeared in his mind, and his eyes widened—it was as if two pieces of something which should have fitted together perfectly had instead bumped against one another and bounced back.

Tonight Jud had told him that his dog had died when he was ten—had died of infection after being cut up in rusty barbed wire. But on the late-summer day when all of them had walked up to the Pet Sematary together, Jud said that his dog had died of old age and was buried there—he had even pointed out the marker, although the years had worn away the writing.

Louis flushed the toilet, turned out the light, and went back to bed. Something else was wrong, too—and he quickly realized what it was. Jud had been born at the start of the century, and that day at the Pet Sematary he had told Louis his dog had died during the first year of World War I. That would have been when Jud was fourteen, if he meant when the war started in Europe. When he was seventeen, if he meant when America joined the war.

But tonight he had said that Spot died when he, Jud, was ten.

Well, he’s an old man, and old men get their memories mixed up, he thought uncomfortably. He’s said himself that he’s noticed he's becoming more forgetful—struggling to remember names and addresses that used to come to him easily, sometimes waking up and not remembering the chores he planned the night before. For a man his age, he’s doing pretty well… senility is probably too strong a word for Jud; forgetfulness is more accurate. It’s not surprising that a man would forget when a dog died seventy years ago. Or how it died, for that matter. Forget it, Louis.

But he couldn’t fall asleep again right away; for a long time he lay awake, too aware of the empty house and the wind that howled around the roof outside.

At some point he slept without realizing it; it must have been so, because as he drifted off, he thought he heard bare feet slowly climbing the stairs and he thought, Leave me alone, Pascow, leave me alone, what’s done is done and what’s dead is dead—and the steps faded away.

And although many other strange things happened as that year went on, Louis was never bothered by the ghost of Victor Pascow again, awake or asleep.

Chapter 23

He woke up at nine the next morning. Bright sunshine filled the bedroom from the east windows. The telephone was ringing. Louis reached up and grabbed it. “Hello?”

“Hi!” Rachel said. “Did I wake you up? I hope so.”

“You woke me up, you bitch,” he said, smiling.

“Ooooh, such nasty language, you bad old bear,” she said. “I tried to call you last night. Were you over at Jud’s?”

He hesitated for just a moment.

“Yes,” he said. “Had a few beers. Norma was at some Thanksgiving dinner. I thought about calling you, but… you know.”

They talked for a while. Rachel told him about her family, which he didn't really want to hear, but he did enjoy hearing that her father's bald spot seemed to be getting bigger faster.

“You want to talk to Gage?” Rachel asked.

Louis grinned. “Yeah, I guess so,” he said. “Don’t let him hang up the phone like he did last time.”

A lot of noise at the other end. He faintly heard Rachel coaxing the kid to say hi, Daddy.

Finally, Gage said, “Hi, Dayee.”

“Hi, Gage,” Louis said cheerfully. “How are you? How’s everything going? Did you knock over your grandpa’s pipe rack again? I hope so. Maybe this time you can wreck his stamp collection too.”

Gage chattered happily for about thirty seconds, mixing his gurgles and grunts with a few words he knew—mommy, Ellie, grandpa, grandma, car (said in the best Yankee way as kaaa, Louis noticed), truck, and shit.

Rachel finally took the phone from him, as Gage wailed in protest and Louis felt a sense of relief—he loved his son and missed him a lot, but talking to a not-quite-two-year-old was like trying to play cribbage with a crazy person; the cards kept flying everywhere and sometimes you ended up moving your pegs the wrong way.

“So how’s everything there?” Rachel asked.

“Okay,” Louis said, without pausing this time—but he knew he had crossed a line, back when Rachel had asked if he had gone to Jud’s last night and he said he had. He suddenly remembered Jud Crandall saying, A man’s heart is like stony soil, Louis… a man grows what he can and takes care of it. “Well… a little boring, to be honest. Miss you.”

“Are you saying you’re not enjoying your break from this circus?”

“Oh, I like the quiet,” he said, “sure. But it gets weird after a day or so.”

“Can I talk to Daddy?” It was Ellie in the background.

“Louis? Ellie’s here.”

“Okay, put her on.”
