“Ring-Ding,” she said and ate half of it. Louis opened the beer.

“A beer this late will give me heartburn,” he said.

“Crap,” she said cheerfully. “Come on, Doc.”

Louis put down his beer and grabbed his robe pocket like he’d forgotten something—even though he'd known that small weight was there all night.

“Here,” he said. “For you. Open it now. It’s after midnight. Merry Christmas, babe.”

She turned the small box over in her hands. It was wrapped in silver paper and tied with wide, satiny blue ribbon. “Louis, what is it?”

He shrugged. “Soap. Shampoo sample. I don’t remember exactly.”

She opened it on the stairs, saw the Tiffany box, and squealed. She took out the cotton and just stood there, her mouth slightly open.

“Well?” he asked, worried. He’d never bought her real jewelry before, and he was nervous. “Do you like it?”

She took it out, laid the delicate gold chain over her fingers, and held the small sapphire to the hall light. It spun gently, seeming to send out cool blue light.

“Oh Louis, it’s so beautiful—” He saw she was crying a little and felt both moved and worried.

“Hey, babe, don’t do that,” he said. “Put it on.”

“Louis, we can’t afford—you can’t afford—”

“Shhh,” he said. “I saved some money here and there since last Christmas… but it wasn’t as much as you might think.”

“How much was it?”

“I’ll never tell you that, Rachel,” he said seriously. “No one could get it out of me, not even if they tortured me. Two thousand dollars.”

“Two thousand—!” She hugged him so suddenly and tightly that he almost fell down the stairs. “Louis, you’re crazy!”

“Put it on,” he said again.

She did. He helped her with the clasp, and then she turned around to look at him. “I want to go upstairs and look at it,” she said. “I think I want to admire myself.”

“Admire away,” he said. “I’ll let the cat out and turn on the lights.”

“When we succeed,” she said, looking straight into his eyes, “I want to take everything off except this.”

“Hurry up and admire, then,” Louis said, and she laughed.

He grabbed Church and held him over his arm—he didn’t use the broom much anymore. He figured that, despite everything, he had almost gotten used to the cat again. He went to the front door, turning off lights as he went. When he opened the door between the kitchen and garage, a rush of cold air blew around his ankles.

“Merry Christmas, Ch—”

He stopped. On the welcome mat was a dead crow. Its head was crushed. One wing was torn off and lay behind the body like a burnt piece of paper. Church immediately wiggled out of Louis’s arms and began to eagerly sniff the frozen body. As Louis watched, the cat’s head shot forward, its ears flat, and before he could look away, Church had ripped out one of the crow’s cloudy, blank eyes.

Church strikes again, Louis thought, feeling a bit sick, and turned his head—but not before he had seen the bloody, empty hole where the crow’s eye had been. Shouldn’t bother me, shouldn’t, I’ve seen worse, oh yeah, Pascow, for instance, Pascow was worse, a lot worse—

But it did bother him. His stomach flipped. The warm feeling of excitement had suddenly vanished. Christ, that bird’s almost as big as he is. Must have caught it by surprise. Completely by surprise.

This would have to be cleaned up. Nobody needed this kind of present on Christmas morning. And it was his job, wasn’t it? Of course. His and nobody else’s. He had known that in a hidden way even on the evening his family came home, when he had purposely driven over the torn-up body of the mouse Church had killed.

The ground of a man’s heart is harder, Louis.

This thought was so clear, somehow so real and loud, that Louis jumped a little, as if Jud had appeared next to him and spoken aloud.

A man grows what he can… and takes care of it.

Church was still hunched over the dead bird, eating greedily. He was working on the other wing now. There was a dark rustling sound as Church pulled it back and forth, back and forth. Never get it off the ground, Orville. That’s right, Wilbur, fucking bird’s just as dead as dogshit, might as well feed it to the cat, might as well—

Louis suddenly kicked Church, kicked him hard. The cat's rear end rose and landed awkwardly. It walked away, giving him another of its ugly yellow-green looks. "Eat me," Louis hissed at it, acting like a cat himself.

"Louis?" Rachel's voice came softly from their bedroom. "Coming to bed?"

"Be right there," he called back. I've just got this little mess to clean up, Rachel, okay? Because it's my mess. He felt around for the switch for the garage light. He quickly went back to the cupboard under the kitchen sink and got a green Hefty Bag. He took the bag back into the garage and took the shovel from its nail on the wall. He scraped up the crow and dropped it into the bag. Then he shoveled up the severed wing and slipped that in. He tied a knot in the top of the bag and dropped it into the bin on the far side of the Civic. By the time he had finished, his ankles were growing numb.

Church was standing by the garage doorway. Louis made a threatening gesture at the cat with the shovel, and it disappeared like black water.

Upstairs, Rachel was lying on her bed, wearing only the sapphire on its chain… as promised. She smiled at him lazily. "What took you so long, Chief?"

"The light over the sink was out," Louis said. "I changed the bulb."

"Come here," she said and gently tugged him toward her. Not by the hand. "He knows if you've been sleeping," she sang softly; a little smile curved up the corners of her lips. "He knows if you're awake… oh my, Louis dear, what's this?"

"Something that just woke up, I think," Louis said, slipping off his robe. "Maybe we ought to see if we can get it to sleep before Santa comes, what do you think?"

She rose on one elbow; he felt her breath, warm and sweet.

"He knows if you've been bad or good… so be good… for goodness sake… Have you been a good boy, Louis?"

“I think so,” he said. His voice trembled a bit.

“Let’s see if you taste as good as you look,” she said.

The sex was good, but Louis didn’t just drift away afterward like he usually did when the sex was good—drifting away easily with himself, his wife, his life. He lay in the darkness of Christmas morning, listening to Rachel’s slow, deep breathing, and he thought about the dead bird on the doorstep—Church’s Christmas present to him.

Keep me in mind, Dr. Creed. I was alive, then I was dead, and now I’m alive again. I’ve been around the track, and I’m here to tell you that you come out the other side with your purr gone and a hunger for the hunt. I’m here to tell you that a man grows what he can and takes care of it. Don’t forget that, Dr. Creed. I’m part of what your heart will grow now. There’s your wife and your daughter and your son… and there’s me. Remember the secret and take care of your garden.

At some point, Louis slept.

Chapter 31

Their winter passed. Ellie believed in Santa Claus again—at least for a while—because of the footprints by the fireplace. Gage loved opening his presents, stopping now and then to chew on a piece of wrapping paper that looked especially good. And that year, both kids decided by the afternoon that the boxes were more fun than the toys.

The Crandalls came over on New Year’s Eve for Rachel’s eggnog, and Louis found himself looking closely at Norma. She looked pale and somehow see-through, a look he had seen before. His grandmother would have said Norma was starting to “fail,” and that was maybe a good way to put it. Suddenly her hands, so swollen and twisted by arthritis, seemed covered with age spots. Her hair looked thinner. The Crandalls went home around ten, and the Creeds celebrated the New Year together in front of the TV. It was the last time Norma was in their house.

Most of Louis’s winter break was wet and gloomy. He was glad for the warmer weather because it saved on heating costs, but the weather was still depressing. He did chores around the house, building bookshelves and cabinets for his wife, and a model Porsche in his study for himself. By the time classes started again on January 23, Louis was happy to be back at the university.

The flu finally arrived—a bad outbreak hit the campus less than a week after the semester started, and he was very busy. He found himself working ten or twelve hours a day and going home exhausted… but not really unhappy.

The warm weather ended suddenly on January 29. There was a blizzard, followed by a week of freezing temperatures. Louis was checking the healing broken arm of a young man who was hoping—but probably wouldn’t, Louis thought—to play baseball that spring when a volunteer told him his wife was on the phone.

Louis went to his office to answer the call. Rachel was crying, and he was immediately worried. Ellie, he thought. She fell off her sled and broke her arm. Or hit her head. He worried about the crazy college kids and their sleds.

“It’s not one of the kids, is it?” he asked. “Rachel?”

“No, no,” she said, crying harder. “Not one of the kids. It’s Norma, Lou. Norma Crandall. She died this morning. Around eight o’clock, right after breakfast, Jud said. He came over to see if you were here and I told him you’d left a half hour ago. He… oh Lou, he just seemed so lost and confused… so old… thank God Ellie was gone and Gage is too young to understand…”

Louis frowned, and despite the bad news, he realized he was thinking about Rachel, trying to understand her. Because it was happening again. Something he couldn’t quite explain, because it was a general feeling. That death was a secret, something scary, and it had to be kept from the children, especially from the children, like Victorian people who thought the truth about sex had to be kept from children.

“Jesus,” he said. “Was it her heart?”

“I don’t know,” she said. She wasn't crying anymore, but her voice was tight and rough. “Could you come, Louis? You’re his friend, and I think he needs you.”

You’re his friend.

Well, I am, Louis thought, a little surprised. I never thought I'd have an eighty-year-old man as a friend, but I guess I do. And then he realized they had to be friends, because of what they shared. And knowing that, he figured Jud had known they were friends long before Louis did. Jud had supported him then, and even with everything that had happened since, even with the mice, even with the birds, Louis felt that Jud’s choice had probably been right… or, if not right, at least kind. He would do what he could for Jud now, and if it meant being there as his wife died, he would be there.

“On my way,” he said and hung up.

Chapter 32

It wasn't a heart attack. It was a stroke, sudden and likely painless. When Louis called Steve Masterton that afternoon and told him what happened, Steve said he wouldn't mind going that way himself.

"Sometimes God messes around," Steve said, "and sometimes He just picks you and tells you your time is up."

Rachel didn't want to talk about it at all and wouldn't let Louis talk to her about it.

Ellie wasn't so much upset as she was surprised and curious—which Louis thought was a perfectly healthy reaction for a six-year-old. She wanted to know if Mrs. Crandall had died with her eyes shut or open. Louis said he didn't know.

Jud was holding up as well as could be expected, considering he'd shared his life with her for almost sixty years. Louis found the old man—and he looked very much like an old man of eighty-three that day—sitting alone at the kitchen table, smoking a Chesterfield, drinking a beer, and staring blankly into the living room.

He looked up when Louis came in and said, "Well, she's gone, Louis." He said this so clearly and matter-of-factly that Louis thought it hadn't really sunk in yet—hadn't hit him where it hurt. Then Jud's mouth started to quiver, and he covered his eyes with one arm. Louis went to him and put an arm around him. Jud broke down and wept. It had sunk in, all right. Jud understood perfectly. His wife had died.

"That's good," Louis said. "That's good, Jud, she would want you to cry a little, I think. Probably be mad if you didn't." He had started to cry a little himself. Jud hugged him tightly, and Louis hugged him back.

Jud cried for ten minutes or so, and then the storm passed. Louis listened carefully to what Jud said then—he listened as a doctor and as a friend. He listened for any rambling in Jud's conversation; he listened to see if Jud knew what time it was (no need to ask him where; that wouldn't prove anything because for Jud Crandall, where had always been Ludlow, Maine); he listened most of all for any use of Norma's name as if she were still alive. He found little or no sign that Jud was losing it. Louis knew that it wasn't uncommon for two old married people to die close together, a month, a week, even a day apart. The shock, he supposed, or maybe even some deep inner urge to join the one who had died (that was a thought he wouldn't have had before Church; he found that many of his thoughts about spiritual and supernatural things had changed quietly but deeply). He concluded that Jud was grieving hard but was still in his right mind. Louis sensed none of the obvious weakness in Jud that Norma had seemed to have on New Year's Eve, when the four of them had sat in the Creed's living room, drinking eggnog.

Jud brought him a beer from the fridge, his face still red and blotchy from crying.

“A bit early in the day,” he said, “but the sun’s over the yardarm somewhere in the world, and considering everything…”

“Say no more,” Louis told him and opened the beer. He looked at Jud. “Shall we drink to her?”

“I guess we better,” Jud said. “You should have seen her when she was sixteen, Louis, coming back from church with her jacket unbuttoned… you’d have been amazed. She could have made the devil swear off drinking. Thank Christ she never asked me to do it.”

Louis nodded and raised his beer a little. “To Norma,” he said.

Jud clinked his bottle against Louis’s. He was crying again but he was also smiling. He nodded. “May she have peace, and let there be no damn arthritis wherever she is.”

“Amen,” Louis said, and they drank.

It was the only time Louis saw Jud get past being a little tipsy, and even then he didn't get drunk. He reminisced; a constant stream of warm memories and stories, colorful and clear and sometimes striking, flowed from him. Yet between the stories of the past, Jud dealt with the present in a way Louis could only admire; if it had been Rachel who had simply dropped dead after her grapefruit and morning cereal, he wondered if he could have done half as well.

Jud called the Brookings-Smith Mortuary in Bangor and made as many of the arrangements as he could by telephone; he made an appointment to come in the following day and make the rest. Yes, he would have her embalmed; he wanted her in a dress, which he would provide; yes, he would pick out underwear; no, he did not want the mortuary to supply the special shoes which laced up the back. Would they have someone wash her hair? he asked. She washed it last on Monday night, and so it had been dirty when she died. He listened, and Louis, whose uncle had been in what those in the business called “the quiet trade,” knew the undertaker was telling Jud that a final wash and set was part of the service given. Jud nodded and thanked the man he was talking to, then listened again. Yes, he said, he would have her made up, but it was to be a light layer. “She’s dead and people know it,” he said, lighting a Chesterfield. “No need to doll her up.” The coffin would be closed during the funeral, he told the director calmly, but open during the visiting hours the day before. She was to be buried in Mount Hope Cemetery, where they had bought plots in 1951. He had the papers in hand and gave the mortician the plot number so that preparations could begin out there: H-101. He himself had H-102, he told Louis later on.

He hung up, looked at Louis, and said, “Prettiest cemetery in the world is right there in Bangor, as far as I’m concerned. Get yourself another beer, if you want, Louis. All of this is going to take awhile.”

Louis was about to refuse—he was feeling a little tipsy—when a strange image popped up suddenly in his mind: Jud pulling Norma’s corpse on a crude carrier through the woods. Toward the Micmac burying ground beyond the Pet Sematary.

It hit him hard. Without a word, he got up and grabbed another beer from the fridge. Jud nodded and dialed the telephone again. By three that afternoon, when Louis went home for a sandwich and soup, Jud had made a lot of progress organizing his wife’s funeral; he moved from one thing to the next like a man planning a very important dinner party. He called the North Ludlow Methodist Church, where the funeral would be held, and the Cemetery Administration Office at Mount Hope; the undertaker at Brookings-Smith would be calling them too, but Jud called first as a courtesy. It was something few people grieving ever thought of… or if they did, they could rarely bring themselves to do it. Louis admired Jud even more for it. Later he called Norma’s few living relatives and his own, flipping through an old and worn address book with a leather cover to find the numbers. And between calls, he drank beer and remembered the past.

Louis felt great admiration for him… and love?

Yes, his heart confirmed. And love.

When Ellie came down that night in her pajamas to be kissed, she asked Louis if Mrs. Crandall would go to heaven. She almost whispered the question to Louis, as if she knew it would be better if they weren’t overheard. Rachel was in the kitchen making a chicken pie, which she planned to take to Jud the next day.

Across the street, all the lights were on in the Crandall house. Cars were parked in Jud’s driveway and along the side of the road on that side for a hundred feet in both directions. The official viewing hours would be tomorrow, at the funeral home, but tonight people had come to comfort Jud as best they could, and to help him remember, and to celebrate Norma’s passing—what Jud had called “the foregoing” that afternoon. Between that house and this, a frigid February wind blew. The road was patched with black ice. The coldest part of the Maine winter was now upon them.

“Well, I don’t really know, honey,” Louis said, taking Ellie on his lap. On the TV, a gunfight was happening. A man spun and fell, unnoticed by either of them. Louis was aware—uncomfortably so—that Ellie probably knew a lot more about Ronald McDonald and Spiderman and the Burger King than she did about Moses, Jesus, and St. Paul. She was the daughter of a woman who didn’t practice Judaism and a man who no longer went to Methodist church, and he figured her ideas about the whole spirit world were very vague—not myths, not dreams, but dreams of dreams. It’s late for that, he thought randomly. She’s only five, but it’s late for that. Jesus Christ, it gets late so fast.

But Ellie was looking at him, and he should say something.

“People believe all sorts of things about what happens to us when we die,” he said. “Some people think we go to heaven or hell. Some people believe we’re born again as little children—”

“Sure, reincarnation. Like what happened to Audrey Rose in that movie on TV.”

“You never saw that!” Rachel, he thought, would have a heart attack if she thought Ellie had seen Audrey Rose.

“Marie told me at school,” Ellie said. Marie was Ellie’s self-declared best friend, a thin, grubby little girl who always looked like she might catch impetigo, ringworm, or even scurvy. Both Louis and Rachel encouraged the friendship as best they could, but Rachel had once admitted to Louis that after Marie left, she always felt like checking Ellie’s head for bugs and lice. Louis had laughed and agreed.

“Marie’s mommy lets her watch all the shows.” There was a hint of disapproval in this that Louis chose to ignore.

“Well, it’s reincarnation, but you get the idea. Catholics believe in heaven and hell, but also in limbo and purgatory. Hindus and Buddhists believe in Nirvana—”

There was a shadow on the dining room wall. Rachel. Listening.

Louis went on more slowly.

“There are probably lots more too. But the thing is, Ellie, no one really knows. People say they do, but they mean they believe it because of faith. Do you know what faith is?”

“Well…”

“Here we are, sitting in my chair,” Louis said. “Do you think my chair will still be here tomorrow?”

“Yeah, sure.”

“Then you have faith it will be here. I do, too. Faith is believing something will be, or is. Get it?”

“Yes.” Ellie nodded.

“But we don’t know it’ll be here. Someone could break in and steal it, right?”

Ellie giggled. Louis smiled.

“We just have to believe it won’t happen. Faith is great, and some religious people think faith and knowing are the same, but I don’t think so. There are too many different ideas about it. What we know is this: When we die, one of two things happens. Either our souls and thoughts live on somehow, or they don’t. If they do, anything is possible. If they don’t, it’s just nothing. The end.”

“Like going to sleep?”

He thought about it and said, “More like being put to sleep with ether, I think.”

“What do you believe, Daddy?”

The shadow on the wall moved and stopped again.

For most of his life, since college, he had believed that death was the end. He had been at many deathbeds and never felt a soul fly past him on its way to… wherever. Hadn’t he thought this when Victor Pascow died? He agreed with his Psychology teacher that the life-after-death stories in books and newspapers were probably just the mind’s last fight against dying—the human mind, making up a vision of life after death to avoid going crazy. He also agreed with a friend from college who said, one night when they stayed up talking, that the Bible had too many miracles that stopped happening when people started being rational. (“Stopped completely,” he had said at first, but others said there were still weird things happening, little mysteries in a world that was mostly clear and simple—like the Shroud of Turin, which no one could prove was fake.) “So Jesus brought Lazarus back from the dead,” this friend—who became a respected doctor—had said. “Okay, fine. If I have to believe it, I will. I mean, I had to accept that one twin can sometimes swallow the other in the womb, like a baby cannibal, and then show up with teeth in his testicles or lungs years later, and if I can believe that, I can believe anything. But I want to see the death certificate. I want proof. I’m like Thomas, who wouldn’t believe Jesus had risen until he could see the nail holes and touch his side. He was the real doctor, not Luke.”

No, he had never really believed in life after death. At least, not until Church.

“I think we continue,” he told his daughter slowly. “But I don’t know what it’s like. Maybe it’s different for everyone. Maybe you get what you believed in your life. But I think we continue, and Mrs. Crandall is probably someplace where she can be happy.”

“You believe that,” Ellie said. It wasn’t a question. She sounded amazed.

Louis smiled, a little pleased and a little embarrassed. “I guess so. And it’s time for you to go to bed. Like ten minutes ago.”

He kissed her twice, once on the lips and once on the nose.

“Do you think animals continue?”

“Yes,” he said, without thinking, and for a moment he almost added, Especially cats. The words almost came out, and his skin felt gray and cold.

“Okay,” she said and slid down. “I have to go kiss Mommy.”

“Right.”

He watched her go. At the dining room doorway, she turned back and said, “I was really dumb about Church that day, wasn’t I? Crying like that.”

“No, honey,” he said. “I don’t think you were dumb.”

“If he died now, I could handle it,” she said, then seemed to think about what she'd just said, as if a little surprised. Then she said, as if agreeing with herself: “Yes, I could.” And went to find Rachel.

Later, in bed, Rachel said, “I heard you talking with her.”

“And you don’t like it?” Louis asked. He thought maybe it was best to discuss it, if that’s what Rachel wanted.

“No,” Rachel said, with a hesitation unusual for her. “No, Louis, it’s not that. I just get… scared. And you know me. When I get scared, I get defensive.”

Louis couldn’t remember Rachel ever speaking with such difficulty, and suddenly he felt more careful than he had with Ellie earlier. He felt like he was in a minefield.

“Scared of what? Dying?”

“Not me,” she said. “I hardly even think about that… anymore. But when I was a kid, I thought about it a lot. Lost sleep. Dreamed of monsters eating me in bed, and they all looked like my sister Zelda.”

Yes, Louis thought. Here it is; finally, after all this time, here it is.

“You don’t talk about her much,” he said.

Rachel smiled and touched his face. “You’re sweet, Louis. I never talk about her. I try not to think about her.”

“I always thought you had your reasons.”

“I did. I do.”

She paused, thinking.

“I know she died… spinal meningitis…”

“Spinal meningitis,” she repeated. “There aren’t any pictures of her in the house anymore.”

“There’s a picture of a young girl in your father’s—”

“In his study. Yes, I forgot that one. And my mother still carries one in her wallet, I think. She was two years older than I was. She caught it… and she was in the back bedroom… she was in the back bedroom like a dirty secret, Louis, she was dying in there, my sister died in the back bedroom and that’s what she was, a dirty secret—she was always a dirty secret!”

Rachel suddenly broke down completely, and in the loud, rising sound of her sobs, Louis sensed she was becoming hysterical and became alarmed. He reached for her and caught a shoulder, which she pulled away from him as soon as he touched it. He could feel the whisper of her nightdress under his fingertips.

“Rachel—babe—don’t—”

“Don’t tell me don’t,” she said. “Don’t stop me, Louis. I only have the strength to tell this once, and then I don’t ever want to talk about it again. I probably won’t sleep tonight anyway.”

“Was it that horrible?” he asked, already knowing. It explained so much. Things he'd never connected or only vaguely suspected now clicked into place. He realized she'd never gone to a funeral with him—not even Al Locke's, a med student killed in a motorcycle accident with a bus. Al often visited their apartment, and Rachel liked him. Yet she skipped his funeral.

She was sick that day, Louis suddenly recalled. The flu or something. She looked bad. But she was fine the next day.

She was okay again after the funeral, he corrected himself. He remembered thinking then her sickness might be all in her head.

“It was horrible, all right. Worse than you can imagine. Louis, we watched her get worse every day, and no one could do anything. She was always in pain. Her body seemed to shrink… curl up… her shoulders hunched and her face drooping, like a mask. Her hands were like bird claws. I had to feed her sometimes. I hated it, but I did it and never complained. When the pain got too bad, they gave her drugs—weak ones at first, then ones that would have made her an addict if she'd lived. But everyone knew she wouldn't. I guess that’s why she’s such a… secret to us all. Because we wanted her to die, Louis, we wished she would die, and not just to end her pain, but to end ours. She was starting to look like a monster, and act like one… oh Christ, I know that sounds awful…”

She covered her face.

Louis touched her gently. “Rachel, it doesn’t sound awful at all.”

“It does!” she cried. “It does!”

“It just sounds true,” he said. “People with long illnesses often become demanding, unpleasant monsters. The idea of the saintly, suffering patient is a romantic lie. Once bedsores appear, they start to lash out and spread misery. They can’t help it, but that doesn’t help those around them.”

She looked at him, amazed… almost hopeful. Then distrust returned to her face. “You’re making that up.”

He smiled grimly. “Want me to show you the books? What about suicide rates? Want to see those? In families who nurse a dying patient at home, suicides skyrocket in the six months after the patient dies.”

“Suicide!”

“They swallow pills, or sniff a pipe, or blow their brains out. Their hate… their weariness… their disgust… their sorrow…” He shrugged and gently touched his closed fists together. “The people left behind start feeling like they’re guilty of murder. So they step out.”

A crazy, hurt kind of relief had come into Rachel’s puffy face. “She was demanding… hateful. Sometimes she’d pee in her bed on purpose. My mother would ask if she needed help getting to the bathroom… and later, when she couldn’t get up, if she wanted the bedpan… and Zelda would say no… and then she’d pee the bed so my mother or my mother and I would have to change the sheets… and she’d say it was an accident, but you could see the smile in her eyes, Louis. You could see it. The room always smelled of pee and her drugs… she had bottles of some dope that smelled like Smith Brothers’ Wild Cherry cough drops, and that smell was always there… some nights I wake up… even now I wake up and I think I can smell Wild Cherry cough drops… and I think… if I’m not really awake… I think ‘Is Zelda dead yet? Is she?’ … I think...”

Rachel caught her breath. Louis took her hand, and she squeezed his fingers with fierce, bright tightness.

“When we changed her, you could see how her back was twisting and knotting. Near the end, Louis, near the end it seemed like her… like her butt had somehow gotten all the way up to the middle of her back.”

Now Rachel’s wet eyes had the glassy, horrified look of a child remembering the same terrible nightmare again and again.

“And sometimes she’d touch me with her… her hands… her birdy hands… and sometimes I’d almost scream and ask her not to, and once I spilled some of her soup on my arm when she touched my face and I burned myself, and that time I did scream… and I cried and I could see the smile in her eyes then, too.

“Near the end, the drugs stopped working. She was the one who would scream then, and none of us could remember what she was like before, not even my mother. She was just this gross, hateful, screaming thing in the back bedroom… our dirty secret.”

Rachel swallowed. Her throat clicked.

“My parents were gone when she finally… when she… you know, when she…”

With terrible, wrenching effort, Rachel brought it out.

“When she died, my parents were away. They were away but I was with her. It was Passover, and they went to see friends for a bit. Just for a few minutes. I was reading a magazine in the kitchen. Well, looking at it, anyway. I was waiting—for the time to give her more medicine because she was screaming. She’d been screaming since my folks left, almost. I couldn’t read with her screaming like that. And then… see, what happened was… well… Zelda stopped screaming. Louis, I was eight… bad dreams every night… I thought she hated me because my back was straight, because I didn’t hurt all the time, because I could walk, because I was going to live… I started to think she wanted to kill me. Only, even now, Louis, I don’t really think it was all my imagination. I think she hated me. I don’t think she would have killed me, but if she could have taken over my body somehow… turned me out of it like in a fairy tale… I think she would have done that. But when she stopped screaming, I went to see if she was okay… to see if she had fallen over or slipped off her pillows. I went in and I looked at her and I thought she must have swallowed her own tongue and was choking to death. Louis”—Rachel’s voice rose, teary and frighteningly childish, as if she were going back, reliving it—”Louis, I didn’t know what to do! I was eight!”

“No, of course you didn’t,” Louis said. He hugged her, and Rachel gripped him with the desperate strength of a swimmer whose boat has flipped over in a lake. “Did someone actually give you a hard time about it?”

“No,” she said, “no one blamed me. But no one could make it better. No one could change it. No one could undo it, Louis. She hadn’t swallowed her tongue. She made a sound, a kind of, I don’t know—gaaaaaa—like that—”

In her distressed, total memory of that day she did a very good imitation of how her sister Zelda must have sounded, and Louis thought of Victor Pascow. He held his wife tighter.

“—and there was spit, spit coming down her chin—”

“Rachel, that’s enough,” he said, not quite steadily. “I know the symptoms.”

“I’m explaining,” she said stubbornly. “I’m explaining why I can’t go to poor Norma’s funeral, for one thing, and why we had that stupid fight that day—”

“Shh—that’s forgotten.”

“Not by me,” she said. “I remember it well, Louis. I remember it as well as I remember my sister Zelda choking to death in her bed on April 14, 1965.”

For a long moment there was silence in the room.

“I turned her over on her belly and thumped her back,” Rachel went on at last. “It’s all I knew to do. Her feet were going up and down… and her twisted legs… and I remember there was a sound like farting… I thought she was farting or I was, but it wasn’t farts, it was the seams under both arms of my blouse ripping out when I turned her over. She started to… to shake… and I saw that her face was turned sideways, turned into the pillows, and I thought, oh, she’s choking, Zelda’s choking, and they’ll come home and say I murdered her by choking, they’ll say you hated her, Rachel, and that was true, and they’ll say you wanted her to be dead, and that was true too. Because, Louis, see, the first thought that went through my mind when she started to go up and down in the bed like that, I remember it, my first thought was Oh good, finally, Zelda’s choking and this is going to be over. So I turned her over again and her face had gone black, Louis, and her eyes were bulging and her neck was swelled up. Then she died. I backed across the room. I guess I wanted to back out the door, but I hit the wall and a picture fell down—it was a picture from one of the Oz books that Zelda liked before she got sick with the meningitis, when she was well, it was a picture of Oz the Great and Terrible, only Zelda always called him Oz the Gweat and Tewwible because she couldn’t make that sound, and so she sounded like Elmer Fudd. My mother got that picture framed because… because Zelda liked it most of all… Oz the Gweat and Tewwible… and it fell down and hit the floor and the glass in the frame shattered and I started to scream because I knew she was dead and I thought… I guess I thought it was her ghost, coming back to get me, and I knew that her ghost would hate me like she did, but her ghost wouldn’t be stuck in bed, so I screamed… I screamed and I ran out of the house screaming ‘Zelda’s dead! Zelda’s dead! Zelda’s dead!’ And the neighbors… they came and they looked… they saw me running down the street with my blouse all ripped out under the arms… I was yelling ‘Zelda’s dead!’ Louis, and I guess maybe they thought I was crying but I think… I think maybe I was laughing, Louis. I think maybe that’s what I was doing.”

“If you were, I admire you for it,” Louis said.

“You don’t mean that, though,” Rachel said with the complete certainty of someone who has gone over a point again and again. He let it go. He thought she might eventually get rid of this awful, rotten memory that had haunted her for so long—most of it, anyway—but never this part. Never completely. Louis Creed was no psychiatrist, but he knew that there are rusty, half-buried things in every life and that people seem forced to go back to these things and pull at them, even though they hurt. Tonight Rachel had pulled almost all of it out, like some terrible and stinking rotten tooth, its crown black, its nerves infected, its roots foul. It was out. Let that last harmful cell remain; if God was good it would stay quiet except in her deepest dreams. That she had been able to remove as much as she had was almost unbelievable—it didn't just show her courage; it shouted it. Louis was in awe of her. He felt like cheering.

He sat up now and turned on the light. “Yes,” he said, “I admire you for it. And if I needed another reason to… to really hate your mother and father, I’ve got it now. You never should have been left alone with her, Rachel. Never.”

Like a child—the child of eight she had been when this awful, unbelievable thing had happened—she corrected him, “Lou, it was Passover season—”

“I don’t care if it was judgment day,” Louis said with a sudden low and rough anger that caused her to pull back a little. He was remembering the student nurses, those two candy-stripers who had the bad luck to be there the morning Pascow had been brought in dying. One of them, a tough little lady named Carla Shavers, had returned the next day and had worked out so well that even Charlton was impressed. The other they had never seen again. Louis was not surprised and did not blame her.

Where was the nurse? There should have been a nurse in attendance… they went out, they actually went out and left an eight-year-old kid in charge of her dying sister, who was probably completely insane by then. Why? Because it was Passover season. And because elegant Dory Goldman couldn’t stand the stink that particular morning and had to get away from it for just a little while. So Rachel got the duty. Right? Rachel got the duty. Eight years old, pigtails, middy blouse. Rachel got the duty. Rachel could stay and put up with the stink. What did they send her to Camp Sunset in Vermont for six weeks every year for, if not to put up with the stink of her dying, insane sister? Ten new shirt-and-jumper combinations for Gage and six new dresses for Ellie and I’ll pay your way through medical school if you’ll stay away from my daughter… but where was the overflowing checkbook when your daughter was dying of spinal meningitis and your other daughter was alone with her, you bastard? Where was the nurse?

Louis sat up, got out of bed.

“Where are you going?” Rachel asked, alarmed.

“To get you a Valium.”

“You know I don’t—”

“Tonight you do,” he said.

She took the pill and then told him the rest. Her voice stayed calm. The tranquilizer was working.

The next-door neighbor had found eight-year-old Rachel behind a tree, crouching and screaming “Zelda’s dead!” over and over. Rachel’s nose was bleeding. She had blood all over her. The same neighbor had called the ambulance and then her parents. After stopping Rachel’s nosebleed and calming her with hot tea and two aspirins, she got her parents' location—they were visiting Mr. and Mrs. Cabron across town; Peter Cabron was an accountant in her father’s business.

By that evening, big changes had happened in the Goldman household. Zelda was gone. Her room had been cleaned and aired out. All the furniture was gone. The room was empty. Later—much later—it became Dory Goldman’s sewing room.

The first nightmare came to Rachel that night. When she woke up at two in the morning, screaming for her mother, she was horrified that she could barely get out of bed. Her back was in pain. She had hurt it moving Zelda. In her burst of strength, she had lifted Zelda hard enough to tear her own blouse.

That she had strained herself trying to keep Zelda from choking was simple and obvious. To everyone, that is, except Rachel herself. Rachel was sure it was Zelda’s revenge from beyond the grave. Zelda knew Rachel was glad she was dead. Zelda knew that when Rachel ran from the house shouting Zelda’s dead at the top of her voice, she had been laughing, not screaming. Zelda knew she had been murdered and had given Rachel spinal meningitis, and soon Rachel’s back would twist and change, and she too would have to lie in bed, slowly turning into a monster, her hands turning into claws.

Soon she would scream with the pain, as Zelda had, and then she would wet the bed, and finally choke to death on her own tongue. It was Zelda’s revenge.

No one could change Rachel’s mind—not her mother, her father, or Dr. Murray, who said it was a mild back strain and told Rachel sharply (cruelly, some—Louis, for instance—would have said) to stop acting so badly. She should remember her sister had just died, Dr. Murray told her. Her parents were deep in grief, and this was not the time for Rachel to seek attention. Only the fading pain convinced her she was not a victim of Zelda’s revenge or God’s punishment. For months (or so she told Louis; it was actually years, eight of them) afterward, she would wake from nightmares in which her sister died again and again, and in the dark Rachel’s hands would fly to her back to check it was all right. In the fearful aftermath of these dreams, she often thought the closet door would bang open and Zelda would stumble out, blue and twisted, her eyes rolled back, her black tongue sticking out, her hands hooked into claws to murder the murderer cowering in her bed with her hands pressed into her back…

She hadn’t gone to Zelda’s funeral, or any funeral since.

“If you’d told me this before,” Louis said, “it would explain so much.”

“Lou, I couldn’t,” she said simply. She sounded very sleepy now. “Since then, I’ve been… I guess, a little afraid of it.”

Just a little afraid, Louis thought. Yeah, right.

“I can’t… seem to stop it. I know you’re right, that death is perfectly natural—good, even—but what I know and what happens… inside me…”

“Yeah,” he said.

“That day I blew up at you,” she said, “I knew Ellie was just crying about the idea… a way of getting used to it… but I couldn’t help it. I’m sorry, Louis.”

“No need to apologize,” he said, stroking her hair. “But what the hell, I accept it anyway, if it’ll make you feel better.”

She smiled. “It does, you know. And I feel better. I feel like I just threw up something that’s poisoned me for years.”

“Maybe you have.”

Rachel’s eyes closed and then opened again… slowly. “And don’t blame it all on my father, Louis. Please. That was a terrible time for them. The bills—Zelda’s bills—were huge. My dad had missed his chance to grow into the suburbs, and the sales in the downtown store were down. On top of that, my mother was a bit crazy herself.

“Well, it all worked out. It was as if Zelda’s death was the start of good times coming back. There had been a recession, but then the money came easier and Daddy got his loan, and since then he’s never looked back. But that’s why they’ve always been protective of me, I think. It’s not just because I’m the only one left—”

“It’s guilt,” Louis said.

“Yes, I suppose. And you won’t be mad at me if I’m sick when they bury Norma?”

“No, honey, I won’t be mad.” He paused and then took her hand. “Can I take Ellie?”

Her hand tightened in his. “Oh, Louis, I don’t know,” she said. “She’s so young—”

“She’s known where babies come from for a year or more,” he reminded her again.

She was quiet for a long time, looking up at the ceiling and biting her lips. “If you think it’s best,” she said finally. “If you think it won’t… won’t hurt her.”

“Come over here, Rachel,” he said, and that night they slept back-to-back in Louis’s bed, and when she woke up shaking in the middle of the night, the Valium worn off, he calmed her with his hands and whispered in her ear that everything was okay, and she slept again.

Chapter 33

“For man—and woman—is like the flowers in the valley, blooming today and gone tomorrow: our time is short; it comes, and then it passes. Let us pray.”

Ellie, beautiful in a navy blue dress bought just for this, dropped her head so quickly that Louis, next to her in the pew, heard her neck pop. Ellie had been to few churches, and never a funeral; the combination had made her unusually quiet.

For Louis, it was a rare moment with his daughter. Usually blinded by his love for her, as he was for Gage, he rarely looked at her without bias; but today he felt like he was watching a perfect example of a child nearing the end of life’s first big stage; a being of pure curiosity, soaking up information like crazy. Ellie had been quiet even when Jud, looking odd but classy in his black suit and dress shoes (Louis thought it was the first time he'd seen him in anything but loafers or green boots), had leaned over, kissed her, and said: “Glad you could come, honey. I bet Norma is too.”

Ellie had stared at him, eyes wide.

Now the minister, Reverend Laughlin, was saying the benediction, asking God to watch over them and give them peace.

“Will the pallbearers come forward?” he asked.

Louis started to get up, and Ellie stopped him, pulling his arm hard. She looked scared. “Daddy!” she whispered loudly. “Where are you going?”

“I’m one of the pallbearers, honey,” Louis said, sitting back down for a moment and putting his arm around her. “That means I’m going to help carry Norma out. There are four of us—me, two of Jud’s nephews, and Norma’s brother.”

“Where will I find you?”

Louis glanced down. The other three pallbearers were there, along with Jud. The rest of the people were leaving, some of them crying.

“If you just go out on the steps, I’ll meet you there,” he said. “Okay, Ellie?”

“Yes,” she said. “Just don’t forget me.”

“No, I won’t.”

He got up again, and she tugged his hand again.

“Daddy?”

“What, honey?”

“Don’t drop her,” Ellie whispered.

Louis joined the others, and Jud told him who the nephews were. They were really second or third cousins… related to Jud's father's brother. They were big guys in their twenties who looked a lot alike. Norma’s brother was probably in his late fifties, Louis guessed. He looked sad because someone in his family had died, but he seemed to be doing okay.

“Nice to meet you all,” Louis said. He felt a little awkward—like he didn't belong.

They nodded at him.

“Ellie okay?” Jud asked and nodded toward her. She was hanging back in the entryway, watching.

Sure—she just wants to make sure I don’t disappear, Louis thought and almost smiled. But then that thought brought up another one: Oz the Great and Terrible. And the smile vanished.

“Yes, I think so,” Louis said and lifted a hand to her. She raised hers back and then went outside in a swirl of navy blue dress. For a moment Louis was uncomfortably struck by how grown-up she looked. It was the kind of illusion, no matter how brief, that could make a man stop and think.

“You guys ready?” one of the nephews asked.

Louis nodded; so did Norma’s younger brother.

“Take it easy with her,” Jud said. His voice had grown rough. Then he turned away and walked slowly up the aisle with his head down.

Louis moved to the back left corner of the steel-gray American Eternal coffin Jud had picked for his wife. He grabbed his handle, and the four of them slowly carried Norma’s coffin out into the bright, still cold of February first. Someone—the church caretaker, he guessed—had spread a good layer of cinders over the slick path of packed snow. At the curb, a Cadillac hearse sat idling, sending white exhaust into the winter air. The funeral director and his strong son stood beside it, watching them, ready to help if anyone (her brother, maybe) should slip or falter.

Jud stood beside him and watched as they slid the coffin inside.

“Goodbye, Norma,” he said and lit a cigarette. “I’ll see you in a while, old girl.”

Louis put his arm around Jud's shoulders, and Norma's brother stood close on his other side, pushing the mortician and his son back. The big nephews (or second cousins, or whatever they were) had already left, their simple job of lifting and carrying done. They had grown apart from this part of the family; they knew the woman's face from pictures and maybe a few visits—long afternoons in the living room eating Norma's cookies and drinking Jud's beer, maybe not really minding the old stories of times they hadn't lived through and people they hadn't known, but aware of things they could be doing (washing and waxing a car, bowling practice, maybe just watching a boxing match on TV with friends), and glad to leave when the duty was done.

Jud's part of the family was in the past now, as far as they were concerned; it was like a small planet drifting away from the main group, shrinking, little more than a speck. The past. Pictures in an album. Old stories told in rooms that perhaps seemed too hot to them—they were not old; their joints didn't hurt; their blood wasn't thin. The past was bodies to be lifted and carried and then let go. After all, if the human body was an envelope for the human soul—God's letters to the universe—as most churches taught, then the American Eternal coffin was an envelope for the human body, and to these strong young cousins or nephews or whatever they were, the past was just something dead to be filed away.

God save the past, Louis thought and shivered for no reason other than that the day would come when he would be a stranger to his own family—his own grandchildren if Ellie or Gage had kids and he lived to see them. Things changed. Family lines faded. Young faces looking out of old photographs.

God save the past, he thought again and squeezed the old man's shoulders.

The ushers put the flowers in the back of the hearse. The electric back window closed. Louis went back to his daughter, and they walked to the station wagon together, Louis holding Ellie's arm so she wouldn't slip in her good shoes with the leather soles. Car engines were starting.

"Why are they turning on their lights, Daddy?" Ellie asked, a little surprised. "Why are they turning on their lights in the middle of the day?"

"They do it," Louis began, his voice thick, "to honor the dead, Ellie." He turned on the wagon's headlights. "Come on."

They were going home at last, the ceremony over—actually it was held at the small Mount Hope Chapel; no grave would be dug for Norma until spring—when Ellie suddenly burst into tears.

Louis looked at her, surprised but not worried. "Ellie, what is it?"

"No more cookies," Ellie cried. "She made the best oatmeal cookies I ever ate. But she won't make them anymore because she's dead. Daddy, why do people have to be dead?"

“I don’t really know,” Louis said. “I guess it’s to make space for all the new people. Little people like you and your brother Gage.”

“I’m never going to get married or have sex and babies!” Ellie cried, even harder now. “Then maybe it’ll never happen to me! It’s awful! It’s m-m-mean!”

“But it stops the pain,” Louis said quietly. “And as a doctor, I see a lot of pain. One reason I wanted the job at the university was because I was tired of seeing it every day. Young people often have pain… even bad pain… but that’s not exactly the same as suffering.”

He paused.

“Believe it or not, honey, when people get very old, death doesn’t always seem as bad or scary as it does to you. And you have many, many years ahead of you.”

Ellie cried, then sniffed, and then stopped. Before they got home, she asked if she could play the radio. Louis said yes, and she found Shakin’ Stevens singing “This Ole House” on WACZ. Soon she was singing along. When they got home, she went to her mother and chattered about the funeral. Rachel, to her credit, listened quietly, kindly, and helpfully, though Louis thought she looked pale and thoughtful.

Then Ellie asked her if she knew how to make oatmeal cookies, and Rachel put away her knitting and stood up right away, as if she had been waiting for this or something like it. “Yes,” she said. “Want to make some?”

“Yay!” Ellie shouted. “Can we really, Mom?”

“We can if your father will watch Gage for an hour.”

“I’ll watch him,” Louis said. “I’d like that.”

Louis spent the evening reading and taking notes on a long article in The Duquesne Medical Digest; the old debate about dissolving stitches had started up again. In the small world of the few people on earth who cared about stitching small wounds, it seemed as endless as the old argument about nature versus nurture.

He planned to write a letter disagreeing tonight, proving that the writer’s main ideas were wrong, his examples biased, his research almost criminally careless. In short, Louis was looking forward—with great amusement—to completely destroying the idiot's argument. He was searching in the study bookcase for his copy of Troutman’s Treatment of Wounds when Rachel came halfway down the stairs.

“Coming up, Lou?”

“I’ll be a while.” He looked up at her. “Everything okay?”

“They’re sound asleep, both of them.”

Louis looked at her closely. “Them, yeah. You’re not.”

“I’m fine. Been reading.”

“You’re okay? Really?”

“Yes,” she said and smiled. “I love you, Louis.”

“Love you too, babe.” He looked at the bookcase, and there was Troutman, right where it always was. Louis put his hand on the textbook.

“Church brought a rat into the house while you and Ellie were gone,” she said and tried to smile. “Yuck, what a mess.”

“Jeez, Rachel, I’m sorry.” He hoped he didn't sound as guilty as he felt at that moment. “Was it really bad?”

Rachel sat down on the stairs. In her pink flannel nightgown, her face clean of makeup and her forehead shining, her hair tied back into a short ponytail with a rubber band, she looked like a child. “I took care of it,” she said, “but you know, I had to chase that dumb cat out the door with the vacuum cleaner thing before he would stop guarding the… the dead thing? He growled at me. Church never growled at me before. He seems different lately. Do you think he could be sick or something, Louis?”

“No,” Louis said slowly, “but I’ll take him to the vet, if you want.”

“I guess it’s okay,” she said and then looked at him openly. “But would you come up? I just… I know you’re working, but…”

“Of course,” he said, getting up as if it were nothing important at all. And, really, it wasn’t—except he knew that now the letter would never be written because things move on, and tomorrow would bring something new. But he had caused that rat, hadn’t he? The rat that Church had brought in, probably scratched to pieces, its insides hanging out, its head maybe gone. Yes. He had caused it. It was his rat.

“Let’s go to bed,” he said, turning off the lights. He and Rachel went up the stairs together. Louis put his arm around her waist and loved her the best he could… but even as he was inside her, hard and ready, he was listening to the winter wind outside the frosty windows, wondering about Church, the cat that used to belong to his daughter and now belonged to him, wondering where it was and what it was hunting or killing. A man’s heart is hard, he thought, and the wind sang its sad, dark song, and not so far away, Norma Crandall, who had once knitted his daughter and son matching hats, lay in her gray metal coffin on a stone slab in a Mount Hope tomb; by now the white cotton the funeral home would have used to fill out her cheeks would be turning black.

Chapter 34

Ellie turned six. She came home from kindergarten on her birthday with a paper hat crooked on her head, some pictures her friends had drawn of her (in the best one, Ellie looked like a friendly scarecrow), and sad stories about getting in trouble on the playground during recess. The flu went away. They had to send two students to the EMMC in Bangor, and Surrendra Hardu probably saved the life of a very sick freshman boy with the awful name of Peter Humperton, who had seizures soon after arriving. Rachel got a small crush on the blond grocery bagger at the A & P in Brewer and told Louis at night how tight his jeans looked. “It’s probably just toilet paper,” she added. “Feel it sometime,” Louis suggested. “If he yells, it’s probably not.” Rachel laughed until she cried. The cold, quiet, very freezing time of February passed and brought the changing rains and freezes of March, potholes, and those orange roadside signs that warn about bumps. The sharpest, most personal, and most painful sadness of Jud Crandall passed, that sadness that psychologists say starts about three days after someone dies and lasts hard for four to six weeks in most cases—like that time New Englanders sometimes call “deep winter.” But time passes, and time changes one feeling into another until they become like a rainbow. Strong sadness becomes a softer sadness; soft sadness becomes mourning; mourning finally becomes remembering—a process that can take from six months to three years and still be normal. The day of Gage’s first haircut came and went, and when Louis saw his son’s hair growing in darker, he joked about it and mourned himself—but only inside.

Spring came, and it stayed for a while.

Chapter 35

Louis Creed came to believe that the last truly happy day of his life was March 24, 1984. The bad things that were coming, hanging over them like a heavy weight, were still more than seven weeks away, but looking at those weeks he didn't see anything as bright. He guessed that even if those terrible things hadn't happened, he would have remembered the day anyway. Days that feel truly good—good all the way through—are rare enough, he thought. There might only be a few really good ones in a normal person’s life, even in the best situations. It seemed to Louis that God was much more generous with pain than with happiness.

That day was a Saturday, and he was home watching Gage in the afternoon while Rachel and Ellie went to get groceries. They went with Jud in his old, noisy truck, not because their car wasn’t working, but because the old man really liked their company. Rachel asked Louis if he would be okay with Gage, and he said he would be. He was happy to see her leave; after a winter in Maine, mostly in Ludlow, he thought she needed to get out as much as possible. She had been a good sport about it, but she seemed to be getting a little restless.

Gage woke up from his nap around two o’clock, grumpy and fussy. He had discovered the terrible twos and was fully embracing them. Louis tried several ways to entertain him, but Gage didn't like any of them. To make things worse, the kid had a huge bowel movement, and Louis wasn't happy to see a blue marble in the middle of it. It was one of Ellie’s marbles. The kid could have choked. He decided the marbles had to go—Gage put everything in his mouth—but that decision didn't help keep the kid entertained until his mother got back.

Louis listened to the early spring wind blowing around the house, sending flashes of light and shadow across Mrs. Vinton’s field next door. He suddenly thought of the kite he had bought without thinking a few weeks before, on his way home from the university. Had he also bought string? He had, by God!

“Gage!” he said. Gage had found a green crayon under the couch and was scribbling in one of Ellie’s favorite books—something that would cause sibling rivalry, Louis thought and grinned. If Ellie got really mad about the scribbles Gage had made in Where the Wild Things Are before Louis could take it away, Louis would just mention the treasure he had found in Gage’s diaper.

“What!” Gage replied smartly. He was talking pretty well now; Louis thought the kid might actually be smart.

“You wanna go out?”

“Wanna go out!” Gage agreed excitedly. “Wanna go out. Where my neeks, Daddy?”

This sentence, written as it sounded, would be: Weh ma neeks, Dah-dee? That meant Where are my sneakers, Father? Louis was often struck by Gage’s speech, not because it was cute, but because he thought that small children sounded like immigrants learning a foreign language in a disorganized but friendly way. He knew that babies make all the sounds a human can… the rolling sound that’s hard for French students, the throat sounds of Australian natives, the hard sounds of German. They lose that ability as they learn English, and Louis wondered if childhood was more about forgetting than learning.

Gage’s toys were finally found… they were also under the couch. One of Louis’s other ideas was that in families with small kids, the space under living room couches starts to build up a strong and strange pull that eventually sucks in all sorts of junk—everything from bottles and diaper pins to green Crayolas and old Sesame Street magazines with food rotting between the pages.

Gage’s jacket, though, wasn’t under the couch—it was halfway down the stairs. His Red Sox cap, which Gage always wore, was the hardest to find because it was where it should be—in the closet. That was, of course, the last place they looked.

“Where goin, Daddy?” Gage asked happily, giving his father his hand.

“Going over to Mrs. Vinton’s field,” he said. “We’re going to fly a kite, buddy.”

“Kiiiyte?” Gage asked, unsure.

“You’ll like it,” Louis said. “Wait a minute, kiddo.”

They were in the garage now. Louis found his keys, unlocked the small storage closet, and turned on the light. He searched through the closet and found the Vulture, still in its bag from the store with the receipt attached. He had bought it in the middle of February, when he felt like he needed some hope.

“Dat?” Gage asked. This was Gage-speak for “What in the world do you have there, Father?”

“It’s the kite,” Louis said and pulled it out of the bag. Gage watched, interested, as Louis unfolded the Vulture, which spread its wings over about five feet of tough plastic. Its bulging, bloodshot eyes stared out at them from its small head on top of its thin, pink neck.

“Birt!” Gage yelled. “Birt, Daddy! Got a birt!”

“Yeah, it’s a bird,” Louis agreed, slipping the sticks into the pockets at the back of the kite and rummaging again for the five hundred feet of kite string that he had bought that day. He looked back over his shoulder and repeated to Gage: “You’re gonna like it, big guy.”

Gage liked it.

They took the kite over to Mrs. Vinton’s field, and Louis got it up into the breezy late-March sky right away, although he had not flown a kite since he was… what? Twelve? Nineteen years ago? God, that was awful.

Mrs. Vinton was a woman almost Jud’s age but much more frail. She lived in a brick house at the edge of her field, but now she came out only rarely. Behind the house, the field ended and the woods began—the woods that led first to the Pet Sematary and then to the Micmac burial ground beyond it.

“Kite’s flying, Daddy!” Gage screamed.

“Yeah, look at it go!” Louis shouted back, laughing and excited. He let out kite string so fast that the string grew hot and made a thin burning line across his palm. “Look at that Vulture, Gage! She’s going to beat ass!”

“Beat-ass!” Gage cried and laughed, high and joyously. The sun came out from behind a big gray spring cloud, and the temperature seemed to go up a lot almost at once. They stood in the bright, unreliable warmth of March trying to be April in the high dead grass of Mrs. Vinton’s field; above them, the Vulture soared up toward the blue, higher, its plastic wings stretched tight against that steady flow of air, still higher, and as he had done as a child, Louis felt himself going up to it, going into it, looking down as the world took on its real shape, the one mapmakers must see in their dreams; Mrs. Vinton’s field, as white and still as cobwebs after the snow melted, not just a field now but a large four-sided shape marked by rock walls on two sides, and then the road at the bottom, a straight black line, and the river valley—the Vulture saw it all with its soaring, bloodshot eyes. It saw the river like a cool gray band of steel, chunks of ice still floating in it; on the other side, it saw Hampden, Newburgh, Winterport, with a ship at dock; maybe it saw the St. Regis Mill at Bucksport below its steaming cloud of smoke, or even the end of the land itself, where the Atlantic crashed against the bare rock.

“Look at her go, Gage!” Louis yelled, laughing.

Gage was leaning so far back he might fall over. A huge grin covered his face. He was waving to the kite.

Louis let out some string and told Gage to hold out one of his hands. Gage did, not even looking around. He couldn’t take his eyes from the kite, which swung and danced in the wind and raced its shadow back and forth across the field.

Louis wrapped the kite string around Gage’s hand twice, and Gage looked down, comically surprised by the strong tug and pull.

“What!” he said.

“You’re flying it,” Louis said. “You’ve got it, my man. It’s your kite.”

“Gage flyne it?” Gage said, as if asking himself, not his father, for confirmation. He pulled the string a little; the kite nodded in the windy sky. Gage pulled the string harder; the kite swooped. Louis and his son laughed together. Gage reached out his free hand, feeling around, and Louis took it. They stood together like that in the middle of Mrs. Vinton’s field, looking up at the Vulture.

It was a moment with his son that Louis never forgot. As he had gone up into the kite as a child, he now found himself going into Gage, his son. He felt himself shrink until he was inside Gage’s small house, looking out of his eyes—looking out at a world that was so big and bright, a world where Mrs. Vinton’s field was almost as big as the Bonneville Salt Flats, where the kite soared miles above him, the string buzzing in his fist like a live thing as the wind blew around him, messing up his hair.

“Kite flyne!” Gage cried out to his father, and Louis put his arm around Gage’s shoulders and kissed the boy’s cheek, which the wind had made as red as a wild rose.

“I love you, Gage,” he said—it was just between them, and that was fine.

And Gage, who now had less than two months to live, laughed loudly and happily. “Kite flyne! Kite flyne, Daddy!”

They were still flying the kite when Rachel and Ellie came home. He and Gage had gotten it so high that they had almost run out of string, and the face of the Vulture had disappeared; it was just a small black shape in the sky.

Louis was glad to see them, and he laughed loudly when Ellie dropped the string for a moment and chased it through the grass, catching it just before the tumbling, unraveling tube ran out of string. But having them around also changed things a little, and he was not too sad to go in when, twenty minutes later, Rachel said she thought Gage had been in the wind long enough. She was afraid he would get a chill.

So the kite was pulled back in, struggling against the sky with every twist of the string, until it finally gave up. Louis tucked it, black wings, buggy bloodshot eyes, and all, under his arm and locked it in the storage closet again. That night Gage ate a huge dinner of hot dogs and beans, and while Rachel was dressing him in his Dr. Dentons pajamas, Louis took Ellie aside and had a serious talk with her about leaving her marbles around. Normally, he might have ended up yelling at her because Ellie could get quite snobby—even rude—when accused of doing something wrong. It was just her way of handling criticism, but that didn't stop it from making Louis angry when she went too far or when he was especially tired. But tonight the kite flying had put him in a good mood, and Ellie was willing to be reasonable. She agreed to be more careful and then went downstairs to watch TV until 8:30, a Saturday treat she loved. Okay, that’s settled, and it might even help, Louis thought, not knowing that marbles weren't really the problem, and chills weren't really the problem, that a large Orinco truck was going to be the problem, that the road was going to be the problem… as Jud Crandall had warned them it might be on that first day of August.

He went upstairs that night about fifteen minutes after Gage had been put to bed. He found his son quiet but still awake, finishing a bottle of milk and looking thoughtfully up at the ceiling.

Louis took one of Gage’s feet in one hand and raised it up. He kissed it, lowered it. “Goodnight, Gage,” he said.

“Kite flyne, Daddy,” Gage said.

“It really did fly, didn’t it?” Louis said, and for no reason at all he felt tears behind his eyes. “Right up to the sky, my man.”

“Kite flyne,” Gage said. “Up to the kye.”

He rolled over on his side, closed his eyes, and slept. Just like that.

Louis was stepping into the hall when he glanced back and saw yellowy-green, floating eyes staring out at him from Gage’s closet. The closet door was open… just a crack. His heart jumped into his throat, and his mouth pulled back and down in a grimace.

He opened the closet door, thinking

(Zelda it’s Zelda in the closet her black tongue puffing out between her lips)

He wasn’t sure what, but it was just Church, the cat was in the closet, and when it saw Louis it arched its back like a Halloween cat. It hissed at him, its mouth open a little, showing its sharp teeth.

“Get out of there,” Louis whispered.

Church hissed again. It didn’t move.

“Get out, I said.” He grabbed the first thing he saw among Gage’s toys, a bright plastic Chuggy-Chuggy-Choo-Choo which looked like dried blood in the dim light. He waved it at Church; the cat didn’t budge and hissed again.

And then, without thinking, Louis threw the toy at the cat, not playing around; he threw it as hard as he could, angry and scared that it was hiding in the dark closet of his son’s room and wouldn’t leave, as if it belonged there.

The toy train hit the cat right in the middle. Church made a noise and ran, bumping into the door and almost falling as it left.

Gage moved, mumbled something, shifted, and was still again. Louis felt a little sick. He was sweating on his forehead.

“Louis?” Rachel called from downstairs, sounding worried. “Did Gage fall out of his crib?”

“He’s fine, honey. Church knocked over some of his toys.”

“Oh, all right.”

He felt—whether it made sense or not—the way he might have felt if he'd seen a snake crawling on his son or a big rat sitting on the bookshelf above Gage’s crib. It was, of course, irrational. But when it had hissed at him from the closet like that.

(Zelda did you think Zelda did you think Oz the Gweat and Tewwible?)

He closed Gage’s closet door, pushing some toys back in with it. He heard the small click of the latch. After a moment's pause, he turned the closet's lock.

He went back to Gage’s crib. The kid had kicked his two blankets down to his knees. Louis untangled him, pulled the blankets up, and then just stood there, watching his son, for a long time.

Chapter 36

It’s probably wrong to think there’s a limit to the horror a person can feel. It seems the worse things get, the more awful they become. It's hard to admit, but life often shows that when things are at their darkest, one horror leads to another, one bad thing leads to worse ones, until it feels like darkness covers everything. The scariest question is how much horror can someone handle and still stay sane. It almost goes without saying that these events can seem absurdly complicated. At some point, it starts to feel almost funny. Maybe that’s when sanity either saves itself or breaks down, when your sense of humor starts to come back.

Louis Creed might have thought about this if he could think clearly after his son Gage William Creed’s funeral on May 17th. But rational thought stopped at the funeral home, where a fight with his father-in-law led to something even worse—a final, shocking, dramatic event that destroyed what was left of Rachel’s control. That day's events ended when she was dragged, screaming, from the East Room of the Brookings-Smith Mortuary, where Gage lay in his closed coffin, and sedated in the foyer by Surrendra Hardu.

The irony was that she wouldn’t have had that final, horrible breakdown if the fight between Louis Creed and Mr. Irwin Goldman of Dearborn had happened during the morning visiting hours (10 to 11:30 A.M.) instead of the afternoon (2 to 3:30 P.M.). Rachel hadn’t been there in the morning; she couldn’t bring herself to come. She stayed home with Jud Crandall and Steve Masterton. Louis didn't know how he would have gotten through the past two days without them.

It was good for Louis—good for all three of them—that Steve had arrived when he did. Louis couldn't make any decisions, not even something as small as giving his wife a shot to calm her grief. Louis hadn’t even noticed that Rachel had almost gone to the morning viewing in her housecoat, buttoned wrong. Her hair was a mess, unwashed and tangled. Her eyes were empty, bulging from sockets so deep they looked like a skull. Her skin was pale and loose. She sat at the table, eating plain toast and talking in nonsense phrases. At one point she said, “About that Winnebago you want, Lou—” Louis had last mentioned buying a Winnebago in 1981.

Louis just nodded and ate his breakfast. He was eating Cocoa Bears. It had been one of Gage’s favorite cereals, and Louis wanted it this morning. It tasted awful, but he still wanted it. He was dressed in his best suit—not black, he didn’t have one, but it was dark gray. He had shaved, showered, and combed his hair. He looked fine, even though he was in shock.

Ellie wore jeans and a yellow shirt. She brought a picture to the table. It was a blown-up Polaroid that Rachel had taken with the camera Louis and the kids had given her for her birthday. It showed Gage, smiling in his ski parka, sitting on her sled as Ellie pulled him. Rachel had caught Ellie looking back and smiling at Gage. Gage was smiling back at her.

Ellie carried the picture, but she didn’t say much.

Louis couldn't see how his wife or daughter were doing. He ate breakfast, replaying the accident over and over, but in his mind, it ended differently. In his mind, he was faster, and Gage only got a spanking for not stopping when they called out.

Steve saw how Rachel and Ellie were doing. He told Rachel not to go to the morning viewing (though “viewing” wasn’t really the right word because the coffin was closed; if it was open, Louis thought, they’d all run screaming, including him) and told Ellie not to go at all. Rachel argued. Ellie just sat, quiet and serious, holding the picture of her and Gage.

Steve gave Rachel the shot she needed and Ellie a teaspoon of clear liquid. Ellie usually complained about taking medicine, but she drank this silently and without a face. By ten that morning, she was asleep in her bed, still holding the picture of her and Gage. Rachel sat in front of the TV, watching “Wheel of Fortune.” She answered Steve's questions slowly. She was drugged, but her face no longer had the crazy look that had worried and scared the P.A. that morning.

Jud had made all the arrangements with the same calm efficiency he had used for his wife three months before. But Steve Masterton took Louis aside just before Louis left for the funeral home.

“I’ll make sure she’s there this afternoon if she can handle it,” he told Louis.

“Okay.”

“The shot will have worn off by then. Your friend Mr. Crandall says he’ll stay with Ellie during the afternoon viewing hours—”

“Right.”

“—and play Monopoly or something with her—”

“Uh-huh.”

“But—”

“Right.”

Steve stopped. They were standing in the garage, Church’s territory, the place where he brought his dead birds and dead rats. The ones that Louis owned. Outside was May sunshine, and a robin hopped across the top of the driveway, as if it had important business somewhere. Maybe it did.

“Louis,” Steve said, “you’ve got to pull yourself together.”

Louis looked at Steve, politely questioning. Not much of what Steve had said had registered—he had been thinking that if he had been a little faster he could have saved his son’s life—but a little of this last got through.

“I don’t think you’ve noticed,” Steve said, “but Ellie isn’t talking. And Rachel has had such a bad shock that her sense of time seems to be messed up.”

“Right!” Louis said. More force in reply seemed needed here. He wasn’t sure why.

Steve put a hand on Louis’s shoulder. “Lou,” he said, “they need you more now than they ever have in their life. More than they ever will again, maybe. Please, man… I can give your wife a shot, but… you… see, Louis, you gotta… oh, Christ, Louis, what a fucking mess this is!”

Louis saw with something like alarm that Steve was starting to cry. “Sure,” he said, and in his mind he saw Gage running across the lawn toward the road. They were yelling at Gage to come back, but he wouldn’t—lately the game had been to run away from Mommy-Daddy—and then they were chasing him, Louis quickly outpacing Rachel, but Gage had a big head start, Gage was laughing, Gage was running away from Daddy—that was the game—and Louis was closing the distance but too slowly, Gage was running down the gentle slope of the lawn now to the edge of Route 15, and Louis prayed to God that Gage would fall down—when little kids ran fast, they almost always fell down because a person’s control over his legs didn’t get really good until he was maybe seven or eight. Louis prayed to God that Gage would fall down, fall down, yes, fall down bloody his nose crack his skull need stitches whatever, because now he could hear the drone of a truck coming toward them, one of those big ten-wheelers that went back and forth endlessly between Bangor and the Orinco plant in Bucksport, and he had screamed Gage’s name then, and he believed that Gage had heard him and tried to stop. Gage seemed to realize that the game was over, that your parents didn’t scream at you when it was just a game, and he had tried to put on the brakes, and by then the sound of the truck was very loud, the sound of it filled the world. It was thundering. Louis had thrown himself forward in a long flying tackle, his shadow tracking the ground beneath him as the shadow of the Vulture had tracked the white late-winter grass of Mrs. Vinton’s field that day in March, and he believed that the tips of his fingers had actually brushed the back of the light jacket Gage had been wearing, and then Gage’s forward motion had carried him out into the road, and the truck had been thunder, the truck had been sunlight on high chrome, the truck had been the deep-throated, shrieking bellow of an air horn, and that had been Saturday, that had been three days ago.

“I’m okay,” he said to Steve. “I ought to go now.”

“If you can get yourself together and help them,” Steve said, wiping at his eyes with the arm of his jacket, “you’ll be helping yourself too. The three of you have got to get through it together, Louis. That’s the only way. That’s all anybody knows.”

“That’s right,” Louis agreed, and in his mind it all started to happen again, only this time he leaped two feet farther right at the end, and grabbed the back of Gage’s jacket, and none of this was happening.

While this was happening in the East Room, Ellie was idly—and quietly—moving her Monopoly piece around the board with Jud Crandall. She shook the dice with one hand and held the Polaroid of her pulling Gage on her sled with the other.

Steve Masterton had decided it was okay for Rachel to go to the viewing that afternoon—a decision he later deeply regretted.

The Goldmans had flown into Bangor that morning and were staying at the Holiday Inn. Her father had called four times by noon, and Steve had to be increasingly firm—almost threatening—with the old man by the fourth call. Irwin Goldman wanted to come out, saying that nothing could keep him from his daughter in her time of need. Steve replied that Rachel needed this time before going to the funeral home to get over some of her initial shock. He didn’t know about all that, he said, but he knew one physician’s assistant who had no intention of letting anyone into the Creed home until Rachel had appeared in public on her own. After the viewing in the afternoon, Steve said, he would gladly let the relatives take over. Until then, he wanted her left alone.

The old man swore at him and hung up the phone, breaking the connection. Steve waited to see if Goldman would show up, but Goldman had apparently decided to wait. By noon, Rachel seemed a little better. She was at least aware of the time, and she had gone out to the kitchen to see if there was anything for sandwiches. People would probably want to come back to the house after, wouldn’t they? she asked Steve.

Steve nodded.
