There was no bologna or cold roast beef, but there was a Butterball turkey in the freezer, and she put it on the counter to thaw. Steve looked into the kitchen a few minutes later and saw her standing by the sink, staring at the turkey on the counter and crying.

“Rachel?”

She looked at Steve. “Gage really liked these. He especially liked the white meat. It just occurred to me that he was never going to eat another Butterball turkey.”

Steve sent her upstairs to get dressed—the final test of her ability to cope—and when she came down wearing a simple black dress belted at the waist and carrying a small black purse, Steve decided she was all right, and Jud agreed.

Steve drove her into town. He stood with Surrendra Hardu in the lobby of the East Room and watched Rachel walk down the aisle toward the flower-covered coffin like a ghost.

“How’s it going, Steve?” Surrendra asked quietly.

“Going fucking terrible,” Steve said in a low, harsh voice. “How do you think it’s going?”

“I thought it was probably going fucking terrible,” Surrendra said and sighed.

The trouble really started at the morning viewing, when Irwin Goldman refused to shake hands with his son-in-law.

The sight of so many friends and relatives had actually pulled Louis out of his shock a little, had made him notice what was going on and be present. He had reached that stage of flexible grief that funeral directors are so used to managing and using to their advantage. Louis was moved around like a piece in a board game.

Outside the East Room was a small foyer where people could smoke and sit in comfortable, large chairs. The chairs looked like they might have come from a bargain sale at some old, failing English men’s club. Beside the door to the viewing room was a small easel, black metal with gold designs, and on this easel was a small sign that said simply GAGE WILLIAM CREED. Across this large white building, which looked deceptively like a comfortable old house, was an identical foyer, this one outside the West Room, where the sign on the easel read ALBERTA BURNHAM NEDEAU. At the back of the house was the Riverfront Room. The easel to the left of the door between the foyer and this room was blank; it was not in use on this Tuesday morning. Downstairs was the coffin showroom, each model lit by a small spotlight on the ceiling. If you looked up—Louis had, and the funeral director had frowned at him—it looked like there were many strange animals perched up there.

Jud had come with him on Sunday, the day after Gage had died, to choose a coffin. They had gone downstairs, and instead of turning right into the coffin showroom, Louis, confused, had continued straight down the hallway toward a plain white swinging door, the kind you see between restaurant dining rooms and the kitchen. Both Jud and the funeral director had said quickly and at the same time, “Not that way,” and Louis had followed them away from that swinging door obediently. He knew what was behind that door though. His uncle had been an undertaker.

The East Room was furnished with neat rows of folding chairs—the expensive ones with soft seats and backs. At the front, in an area that seemed like a combination of church and garden, was Gage’s coffin. Louis had picked the American Casket Company’s rosewood model—Eternal Rest, it was called. It was lined with soft pink silk. The mortician agreed that it was a beautiful coffin and apologized that he did not have one with a blue lining. Louis responded that he and Rachel had never cared about such things. The mortician nodded. The mortician asked Louis if he had thought about how he would pay for Gage’s funeral. If not, he said, he could take Louis into his office and quickly go over three of their more popular plans—

In Louis’s mind, an announcer suddenly spoke up cheerfully: I got my kid’s coffin free, for Raleigh coupons!

Feeling like he was in a dream, he said, “I’m going to pay for everything with my MasterCard.”

“Fine,” the mortician said.

The coffin was only four feet long—a tiny coffin. Yet it cost over six hundred dollars. Louis guessed it was on supports, but the flowers hid them, and he didn’t want to get too close. The flower smell made him want to throw up.

At the front of the aisle, by the door to the lobby, was a book on a stand. A pen was chained to the stand. The funeral director put Louis here to "greet his friends and family."

People were supposed to write their names and addresses in the book. Louis had no idea why this was done and didn't ask now. He figured he and Rachel would keep the book after the funeral. That seemed the strangest thing. He had a high school yearbook, a college yearbook, and a med school yearbook. There was also a wedding book, with MY WEDDING DAY stamped on the cover, starting with Rachel trying on her veil with her mother and ending with a photo of two pairs of shoes outside a hotel room. There was also a baby book for Ellie, but they stopped filling it out quickly. It was too cute, with spaces for MY FIRST HAIRCUT (add a lock of hair) and WHOOPS! (add a picture of baby falling).

Now, this book would be added to the others. What should they call it?

Louis wondered as he stood beside the stand, waiting for people to arrive. MY DEATHBOOK? FUNERAL AUTOGRAPHS? THE DAY WE BURIED GAGE? Or something more serious, like A DEATH IN THE FAMILY?

He turned the book to its cover, which was like the wedding book's cover.

The cover was blank.

As expected, Missy Dandridge arrived first that morning. Good-hearted Missy had watched Ellie and Gage many times. Louis remembered that Missy had taken the kids the night Victor Pascow died. She had taken the kids, and Rachel had made love to him, in the tub and then in bed.

Missy was crying hard. When she saw Louis's calm face, she cried more and reached for him. Louis hugged her, realizing that this was how it worked—some kind of human connection that eased the pain of loss, releasing it, breaking up the shock with sorrow.

"I'm so sorry," Missy was saying, brushing her dark blond hair back from her pale face. "Such a dear sweet little boy. I loved him so much, Louis, I’m so sorry, it's a terrible road, I hope they put that truck driver in jail forever, he was going way too fast, he was so sweet, so dear, so bright, why would God take Gage, I don't know, we can't understand, can we, but I'm sorry, sorry, so sorry."

Louis comforted her, held her and comforted her. He felt her tears on his collar, the press of her breasts against him. She wanted to know where Rachel was, and Louis told her that Rachel was resting. Missy promised to go see her and that she would sit with Ellie anytime, for as long as they needed her. Louis thanked her.

She had started away, still sniffing, her eyes redder than ever above her black handkerchief. She was moving toward the coffin when Louis called her back. The funeral director, whose name Louis could not even remember, had told him to have them sign the book, and he was determined to have them do it.

"Mystery guest, sign in please," he thought and almost burst into loud, hysterical laughter.

It was Missy's sad, heartbroken eyes that drove the laughter away.

"Missy, would you sign the book?" he asked her, and because something else seemed to be needed, he added, "For Rachel."

"Of course," she said. "Poor Louis and poor Rachel." And suddenly Louis knew what she was going to say next, and for some reason he dreaded it; yet it was coming, unavoidable, like a black bullet from a killer's gun, and he knew that he would be struck over and over by this bullet in the next endless ninety minutes, and then again in the afternoon, while the wounds of the morning were still bleeding:

"Thank God he didn't suffer, Louis. At least it was quick."

"Yes, it was quick, all right," he thought about saying to her—ah, how that would shatter her face all over again, and he felt a strong urge to do it, to simply throw the words into her face. "It was quick, no doubt about that, that's why the coffin's closed, nothing could have been done about Gage even if Rachel and I liked dressing up dead relatives in their best like store mannequins and putting makeup on their faces. It was quick, Missy-my-dear, one minute he was there on the road and the next minute he was lying in it, but way down by the Ringers' house. It hit him and killed him and then it dragged him and you better believe it was quick. A hundred yards or more all told, the length of a football field. I ran after him, Missy, I was screaming his name over and over again, almost as if I expected he would still be alive, me, a doctor. I ran ten yards and there was his baseball cap and I ran twenty yards and there was one of his Star Wars sneakers, I ran forty yards and by then the truck had run off the road and the trailer had jackknifed in that field beyond the Ringers’ barn. People were coming out of their houses and I went on screaming his name, Missy, and at the fifty-yard line there was his jumper, it was turned inside-out, and on the seventy-yard line there was the other sneaker, and then there was Gage."

Suddenly the world went gray. Everything disappeared from his view. Dimly he could feel the corner of the stand which held the book digging into his palm, but that was all.

“Louis?” Missy’s voice. Far away. The faint sound of pigeons in his ears.

“Louis?” Closer now. Worried.

The world came back into focus.

“Are you okay?”

He smiled. “Fine,” he said. “I’m okay, Missy.”

She signed for herself and her husband—Mr. and Mrs. David Dandridge—in neat, round handwriting; she added their address—Rural Box 67, Old Bucksport Road—and then looked quickly at Louis before looking away, as if just writing her address on the road where Gage had died was a crime.

“Take care, Louis,” she whispered.

David Dandridge shook his hand and mumbled something unclear, his large adam’s apple moving up and down. Then he hurried after his wife down the aisle for the usual look at a coffin made in Storyville, Ohio, a place Gage had never been and no one knew him.

After the Dandridges, they all came, moving slowly in a line, and Louis greeted them, their handshakes, their hugs, their tears. His collar and the upper sleeve of his dark gray suit coat quickly became damp. The smell of the flowers began to reach even the back of the room, filling the place with the smell of a funeral. It was a smell he remembered from his childhood—that sweet, heavy, mortuary smell of flowers. Louis was told thirty-two times how lucky it was that Gage hadn’t suffered. He was told twenty-five times that God works in mysterious ways. Ending the line was he’s with the angels now, a total of twelve times.

It began to bother him. Instead of these little sayings losing what little meaning they had (like your own name loses its meaning if you say it over and over), they seemed to hit harder each time, digging deeper. By the time his mother-in-law and father-in-law showed up, he felt like a beaten-up boxer.

His first thought was that Rachel had been right—and how right. Irwin Goldman had indeed aged. He was—what? Fifty-eight, fifty-nine? Today he looked like a carved and serious seventy. He looked almost ridiculously like Israel’s Prime Minister Menachem Begin with his bald head and Coke-bottle glasses. Rachel had told Louis that Goldman had aged when she came back from her Thanksgiving trip, but Louis had not expected this. Of course, he thought, maybe it hadn’t been this bad at Thanksgiving. The old man hadn’t lost one of his two grandchildren at Thanksgiving.

Dory walked beside him, her face almost hidden under two—maybe three—layers of heavy black netting. Her hair was fashionably blue, the color that older, wealthy American women liked. She held her husband’s arm. All Louis could really see behind the veil was the shine of her tears.

Suddenly he decided it was time to forgive and forget. He could not hold the old grudge any longer. Suddenly it was too heavy. Maybe it was the combined weight of all those clichés.

“Irwin. Dory,” he murmured. “Thank you for coming.”

He gestured with his arms, as if to shake hands with Rachel’s father and hug her mother at the same time, or maybe even to hug them both. Either way he felt his own tears start for the first time, and for a moment he had the crazy idea that they could fix everything, that Gage would do that much for them in his dying, as if this were some romantic novel where death led to reconciliation, where it could cause something better than this endless, stupid, grinding pain which just went on and on and on.

Dory started toward him, gesturing, maybe starting to hold out her own arms. She said something—”Oh, Louis…” and something else that was mumbled—and then Goldman pulled his wife back. For a moment the three of them stood in a scene that no one noticed except themselves (unless maybe the funeral director, standing quietly in the far corner of the East Room, saw—Louis figured that Uncle Carl would have seen), Louis with his arms partly outstretched, Irwin and Dory Goldman standing as stiff and straight as a couple on a wedding cake.

Louis saw that there were no tears in his father-in-law’s eyes; they were bright and clear with hate (does he think I killed Gage to hurt him? Louis wondered). Those eyes seemed to size Louis up, to find him the same small and useless man who had taken his daughter away and brought her to this sorrow… and then to dismiss him. His eyes shifted to Louis’s left—to Gage’s coffin, in fact—and only then did they soften.

Still Louis made one last try. “Irwin,” he said. “Dory. Please. We have to work together on this.”

“Louis,” Dory said again—kindly, Louis thought—and then they were past him, Irwin Goldman maybe pulling his wife along, not looking to the left or the right, certainly not looking at Louis Creed. They approached the coffin, and Goldman fumbled a small black skullcap out of his suit coat pocket.

You didn’t sign the book, Louis thought, and then a silent surge of such bitter acid rose through his stomach that his face twisted in pain.

The morning viewing ended at last. Louis called home. Jud answered and asked him how it had gone. All right, Louis said. He asked Jud if he could talk to Steve.

“If she can dress herself, I’m going to let her come this afternoon,” Steve said. “Okay by you?”

“Yes,” Louis said.

“How are you, Lou? No bullshit and straight on—how are you?”

“All right,” Louis said briefly. “Coping.” I had all of them sign the book. All of them except Dory and Irwin, and they wouldn’t.

“All right,” Steve said. “Look, shall we meet you for lunch?”

Lunch. Meeting for lunch. This seemed like such a strange idea that Louis thought of the science fiction novels he had read as a teenager—novels by Robert A. Heinlein, Murray Leinster, Gordon R. Dickson. The natives here on Planet Quark have an odd custom when one of their children dies, Lieutenant Abelson: they “meet for lunch.” I know how grotesque and barbaric that sounds, but remember, this planet has not been changed to be like Earth yet.

“Sure,” Louis said. “What’s a good restaurant for halftime between funeral viewings, Steve?”

“Take it easy, Lou,” Steve said, but he didn’t seem entirely displeased. In this state of crazy calm, Louis felt better able to see into people than ever before in his life. Perhaps it was an illusion, but right now he suspected Steve was thinking that even a sudden burst of sarcasm, squirted out like an abrupt mouthful of bile, was preferable to his earlier state of disconnection.

“Don’t worry,” he said to Steve now. “What about Benjamin’s?”

“Sure,” Steve said. “Benjamin’s would be fine.”

He had made the call from the funeral director's office. Now, as Louis walked past the East Room on his way out, he saw that it was almost empty. Irwin and Dory Goldman sat in the front row, heads bowed. They looked to Louis as if they might sit there forever.

Benjamin’s was the right choice. Bangor was a town where people ate lunch early, and around one o’clock it was almost deserted. Jud had come with Steve and Rachel, and the four of them ate fried chicken. At one point, Rachel went to the ladies’ room and stayed there so long that Steve became worried. He was about to ask a waitress to check on her when she came back to the table, her eyes red.

Louis picked at his chicken and drank a lot of Schlitz beer. Jud drank the same amount, not talking much.

Their four meals were mostly uneaten. Louis, with his unusually clear understanding, saw the waitress, a heavy girl with a pretty face, wondering whether to ask if their meals were all right. She looked again at Rachel’s red eyes and decided it would be the wrong question. Over coffee, Rachel said something so suddenly and bluntly that it shocked them all—especially Louis, who was finally getting sleepy from the beer. “I’m going to give his clothes to the Salvation Army.”

“Are you?” Steve said after a moment.

“Yes,” Rachel said. “They can still be used. All his sweaters… his corduroy pants… his shirts. Someone will be glad to have them. They’re all in good condition. Except for the ones he was wearing, of course. They’re… ruined.”

The last word was a choked sob. She tried to drink coffee, but couldn’t. A moment later, she was crying into her hands.

There was a strange moment then. There were crossing lines of tension then. They all seemed to focus on Louis. He felt this with the same clear understanding he’d had all day, and this was the clearest and surest of all. Even the waitress felt those lines of awareness coming together. He saw her stop at a table near the back where she was putting down placemats and silverware. For a moment, Louis was puzzled, and then he understood: they were waiting for him to comfort his wife.

He couldn’t do it. He wanted to do it. He knew it was his job to do it. But he couldn’t. It was the cat that stopped him. Suddenly and for no good reason. The cat. The damn cat. Church with his ripped mice and the birds he had killed. When he found them, Louis had cleaned up the messes right away, without complaining or saying anything, certainly without arguing. He had, after all, bought them. But had he bought this?

He saw his fingers. Louis saw his fingers. He saw his fingers lightly brushing Gage’s jacket. Then Gage’s jacket was gone. Then Gage was gone.

He looked into his coffee cup and let his wife cry beside him, without comfort.

After a moment—it was probably a short time, but it felt long then and later—Steve put an arm around her and hugged her gently. He looked at Louis with angry eyes. Louis turned away toward Jud, but Jud was looking down, as if ashamed. There was no help there.

Chapter 37

“I knew something like this would happen,” Irwin Goldman said. That was how the trouble started. “I knew it when she married you. ‘You’ll have all the grief you can handle and more,’ I said. And look at this. Look at this… this mess.”

Louis slowly looked at his father-in-law, who had popped up like a nasty surprise in a skullcap; and then, without thinking, he looked around for Rachel, who should have been by the book on the stand—it was her afternoon shift—but Rachel was gone.

The afternoon viewing had fewer people, and after about half an hour, Louis had gone to the front row and sat on the aisle, barely noticing anything (just the sickening smell of the flowers) except that he was very tired and sleepy. It was partly the beer, he guessed. His mind was finally ready to shut down. Probably a good thing. Maybe after twelve or sixteen hours of sleep, he could comfort Rachel a little.

After a while, his head had dropped until he was looking at his hands, loosely clasped between his knees. The soft voices in the back were calming. He had been glad that Irwin and Dory weren’t there when the four of them got back from lunch, but he should have known it was too good to last.

“Where’s Rachel?” Louis asked now.

“With her mother. Where she should be.” Goldman spoke with the obvious victory of someone who has made a great deal. He smelled of Scotch. A lot of it. He stood in front of Louis like a small, aggressive lawyer facing a clearly guilty man in court. He was unsteady.

“What did you say to her?” Louis said, starting to feel alarmed. He knew Goldman had said something. It was obvious in the man’s face.

“Nothing but the truth. I told her this is what happens when you marry against your parents’ wishes. I told her—”

“Did you say that?” Louis asked in disbelief. “You didn’t really say that, did you?”

“That and more,” Irwin Goldman said, “I always knew it would come to this—this or something like it. I knew what kind of a man you were the first time I saw you.” He leaned forward, breathing out Scotch fumes. “I saw through you, you prancing little fake of a doctor. You tricked my daughter into a stupid, pointless marriage and then you turned her into a kitchen maid and then you let her son be run down in the highway like a… a chipmunk.”

Most of this confused Louis. He was still trying to understand how this stupid little man could have—

“You said that to her?” he repeated. “You said it?”

“I hope you rot in hell!” Goldman said, and heads turned quickly toward the sound of his voice. Tears started to come out of Irwin Goldman’s bloodshot brown eyes. His bald head shone under the dim fluorescent lights. “You made my wonderful daughter into a kitchen maid… ruined her future… took her away… and let my grandson die a dirty death on a country road.”

His voice rose to a loud, bossy scream.

“Where were you? Sitting around while he was playing in the road? Thinking about your stupid medical articles? What were you doing, you shit? You stinking shit! Killer of children! Ki—”

There they were. There they were at the front of the East Room. There they were, and Louis saw his arm go out. He saw the sleeve of his suit coat pull back from the cuff of his white shirt. He saw the soft shine of one cufflink. Rachel had given him the set for their third wedding anniversary, never knowing that her husband would someday wear these cufflinks to the funeral of their then-unborn son. His fist was just something tied to the end of his arm. It hit Goldman’s mouth. He felt the old man’s lips squash and spread back. It was a gross feeling, really—squashing a slug with your fist might feel something like that. There was no satisfaction in it. Beneath the flesh of his father-in-law’s lips he could feel the hard, firm shape of his dentures.

Goldman stumbled backward. His arm hit Gage’s coffin, knocking it sideways. One of the vases, heavy with flowers, fell over with a crash. Someone screamed.

It was Rachel, struggling with her mother, who was trying to hold her back. The people who were there—ten or fifteen in all—seemed stuck between fear and embarrassment. Steve had taken Jud back to Ludlow, and Louis was somewhat grateful for that. This was not a scene he would have wanted Jud to see. It was inappropriate.

“Don’t hurt him!” Rachel screamed. “Louis, don’t hurt my father!”

“You like to hit old men, do you?” Irwin Goldman, of the overflowing checkbook, yelled in a high voice. He was grinning, his mouth full of blood. “You like to hit old men? I’m not surprised, you stinking bastard. That doesn't surprise me at all.”

Louis turned toward him, and Goldman hit him in the neck. It was an awkward, sideways, chopping blow, but Louis wasn’t ready for it. A shocking pain that would make it hard to swallow for the next two hours exploded in his throat. His head snapped back, and he fell to one knee in the aisle.

First the flowers, now me, he thought. What do the Ramones say? Hey-ho, let’s go! He thought he wanted to laugh, but he couldn't. What came out of his sore throat was a small groan.

Rachel screamed again.

Irwin Goldman, his mouth dripping blood, marched over to where his son-in-law was kneeling and kicked Louis hard in the kidneys. The pain was a bright burst of agony. He put his hands down on the rug to keep from falling flat on his face.

“You don’t do so good even against old men, sonny!” Goldman yelled, his voice cracking with excitement. He kicked at Louis again, missing the kidney this time, hitting Louis on the upper part of his left buttock with his old, black shoe. Louis grunted in pain, and this time he fell onto the carpet. His chin hit with a loud crack. He bit his tongue.

“There!” Goldman yelled. “There’s the kick in the ass I should have given you the first time you came sniffing around, you bastard. There!” He kicked Louis in the butt again, this time hitting the other buttock. He was weeping and grinning. Louis saw for the first time that Goldman hadn’t shaved—a sign of mourning. The funeral director rushed toward them. Rachel had broken free from Mrs. Goldman and was also running toward them, screaming.

Louis rolled clumsily onto his side and sat up. His father-in-law kicked at him again, and Louis caught his shoe in both hands—it hit solidly into his palms like a well-caught football—and pushed backward as hard as he could.

Yelling, Goldman flew backward at an angle, waving his arms to keep his balance. He fell on Gage’s Eternal Rest casket, which had been made in the town of Storyville, Ohio, and which had been expensive.

Oz the Great and Terrible has just fallen on top of my son’s coffin, Louis thought, dazed. The casket fell from the stand with a huge crash. The left end fell first, then the right. The latch broke. Even over the screams and the crying, even over Goldman’s yells, who was just playing a children’s game of Pin the Tail on the Donkey, Louis heard the lock snap.

The coffin didn’t actually burst open, spilling Gage’s sad, hurt body onto the floor for everyone to stare at, but Louis felt sick knowing they were only spared that because the coffin had landed on its bottom instead of its side. It easily could have fallen the other way. Still, in that brief moment before the lid slammed shut on its broken latch, he saw a flash of gray—the suit they had bought to bury Gage in. And a bit of pink. Gage’s hand, maybe.

Sitting there on the floor, Louis covered his face and began to cry. He didn’t care about his father-in-law, or missiles, or stitches that last versus ones that dissolve, or the universe ending. Right then, Louis Creed wished he were dead. And suddenly, strangely, a picture popped into his head: Gage in Mickey Mouse ears, Gage laughing and shaking hands with a big Goofy on Main Street in Disney World. He saw it clearly.

One of the wooden supports had fallen over; the other leaned casually against the low platform where a minister might stand to give a speech. Goldman was lying in the flowers, also crying. Water dripped from the knocked-over vases. The flowers, some crushed, smelled even stronger.

Rachel was screaming and screaming.

Louis couldn’t react to her screams. The picture of Gage in Mickey Mouse ears was fading, but not before he heard a voice saying there would be fireworks later that night. He sat with his face in his hands, not wanting them to see him anymore, his tear-stained face, his loss, his guilt, his pain, his shame, most of all his cowardly wish to be dead and out of this darkness.

The funeral director and Dory Goldman led Rachel out. She was still screaming. Later, in another room (one Louis guessed was just for people who were very upset—the Crying Room, maybe) she went silent. Louis, still numb but calm and in control, gave her a sedative this time, after making sure they were alone.

At home, he led her to bed and gave her another shot. Then he pulled the covers up and looked at her pale face.

“Rachel, I’m sorry,” he said. “I’d give anything to take that back.”

“It’s all right,” she said in a strange, flat voice and then turned over on her side, away from him.

He felt the tired old question Are you all right? come to his lips and stopped it. It wasn’t a real question; it wasn’t what he really wanted to know.

“How bad are you?” he asked finally.

“Pretty bad, Louis,” she said, then made a sound that might have been a laugh. “I’m terrible, actually.”

He felt he should say more, but he couldn't. He suddenly resented her, Steve Masterton, Missy Dandridge and her husband with his pointy Adam's apple, the whole damn group. Why did he always have to be the one giving? What kind of crap was that?

He turned off the light and left. He realized he couldn't give much more to his daughter.

For a crazy moment, looking at her in the dim room, he thought she was Gage. He thought the whole thing had been a horrible nightmare, like his dream of Pascow leading him into the woods, and for a moment his tired mind clung to the idea. The shadows helped—there was only the moving light of the small TV Jud had brought up for her to fill the time. The long, long hours.

But it wasn't Gage, of course; it was Ellie, who was now not only holding the picture of her pulling Gage on the sled, but sitting in Gage's chair. She had taken it from his room and brought it into hers. It was a small director's chair with a canvas seat and back. The name GAGE was painted on the back. Rachel had ordered four of these chairs by mail. Each family member had one with their name on it.

Ellie was too big for Gage's chair. She was squeezed into it, and the canvas bottom sagged. She held the Polaroid to her chest and stared at the TV, where a movie was playing.

“Ellie,” he said, turning off the TV, “bedtime.”

She got out of the chair and folded it up. She seemed to want to take the chair to bed with her.

Louis hesitated, wanting to say something about the chair, and finally said, “Do you want me to tuck you in?”

“Yes, please,” she said.

“Do you… would you want to sleep with Mommy tonight?”

“No, thanks.”

“You sure?”

She smiled a little. “Yes. She steals the covers.”

Louis smiled back. “Come on then.”

Instead of trying to put the chair in bed with her, Ellie unfolded it by the head of the bed, and a funny image came to Louis—it looked like the world’s smallest psychiatrist's office.

She undressed, putting the picture of her and Gage on her pillow while she did. She put on her baby doll pajamas, picked up the picture, went into the bathroom, put it down to wash, brush, floss, and take her fluoride tablet. Then she picked it up again and got into bed with it.

Louis sat down beside her and said, “I want you to know, Ellie, that if we keep loving each other, we can get through this.”

Each word was like moving a handcar full of wet bales, and the effort made Louis feel exhausted.

“I’m going to wish really hard,” Ellie said calmly, “and pray to God for Gage to come back.”

“Ellie—”

“God can give him back if He wants to,” Ellie said. “He can do anything He wants to.”

“Ellie, God doesn’t do things like that,” Louis said uncomfortably, and he pictured Church sitting on the closed toilet seat, staring at him with those dirty eyes as Louis lay in the tub.

“He does so,” she said. “In Sunday School, the teacher told us about this man Lazarus. He was dead, and Jesus brought him back to life. He said ‘Lazarus, come forth,’ and the teacher said if he’d just said ‘Come forth,’ everyone in that graveyard would have come out, and Jesus only wanted Lazarus.”

Something ridiculous came out of his mouth (but the day had been full of ridiculous things): “That was a long time ago, Ellie.”

“I’m going to keep things ready for him,” she said. “I’ve got his picture, and I’m going to sit in his chair—”

“Ellie, you’re too big for Gage’s chair,” Louis said, taking her hot, sick hand. “You’ll break it.”

“God will help it not to break,” Ellie said. Her voice was calm, but Louis saw the dark circles under her eyes. Looking at her made his heart hurt so much that he turned away. Maybe when Gage’s chair broke, she would start to understand what had happened a little better.

“I’m going to carry the picture and sit in his chair,” she said. “I’m going to eat his breakfast too.” Gage and Ellie each had their own breakfast cereals; Ellie had once said Gage’s tasted like dead bugs. If Cocoa Bears was the only cereal in the house, Ellie would sometimes eat a boiled egg… or nothing at all. “I’ll eat lima beans even though I hate them, and I’ll read all of Gage’s picture books and I’ll… I’ll… you know… get things ready… in case…”

She was crying now. Louis didn't try to comfort her but just brushed her hair back from her forehead. What she said had a strange kind of logic. Keeping the lines open. Keeping things fresh. Keeping Gage alive in their minds, refusing to let him fade away; remembering when Gage did this… or that… yeah, that was great… good old Gage, what a kid. When it stopped hurting, it stopped mattering. She understood, Louis thought, how easy it would be to let Gage be dead.

"Ellie, don't cry anymore," he said. "This isn't forever."

She cried forever… for fifteen minutes. She actually fell asleep before her tears stopped. But eventually she slept, and downstairs the clock struck ten in the quiet house.

Keep him alive, Ellie, if that's what you want, he thought and kissed her. The therapists would probably say it's really unhealthy, but I'm for it. Because I know the day will come—maybe as soon as this Friday—when you forget to bring the picture and I'll see it lying on your bed in this empty room while you ride your bike around the driveway or walk in the field behind the house or go over to Kathy McGown's house to make clothes with her Sew Perfect. Gage won't be with you, and that's when Gage disappears from whatever place he holds in little girls' hearts and starts to become Something That Happened in 1984. A memory from the past.

Louis left the room and stood for a moment at the head of the stairs, thinking—not seriously—about going to bed.

He knew what he needed and went downstairs to get it.

Louis Albert Creed started carefully to get drunk. Downstairs in the cellar were five cases of Schlitz Light beer. Louis drank beer, Jud drank it, Steve Masterton drank it, Missy Dandridge would sometimes have a beer or two while watching the kids (kid, Louis reminded himself, going down the cellar stairs). Even Charlton, on the few times she had come over to the house, preferred a beer—as long as it was a light beer—to a glass of wine. So one day last winter Rachel had gone out and bought a huge ten cases when Schlitz Light was on sale at the Brewer A & P. Stop you running down to Julio's in Orrington every time somebody drops in, she had said. And you're always quoting Robert Parker to me, love—any beer that's in the refrigerator after the stores close is good beer, right? So drink this and think about the money you're saving. Last winter. When things had been okay. When things had been okay. It was funny how quickly and easily your mind made that important division.

Louis brought up a case of beer and shoved the cans into the fridge. Then he took one can, closed the fridge door, and opened the beer. Church came moving slowly and stiffly out of the pantry at the sound of the refrigerator door and stared questioningly up at Louis. The cat didn't come too close; Louis had maybe kicked it too many times.

"Nothing for you," he told the cat. "You had your can of Calo today. If you want something else, go kill a bird."

Church stood there, looking up at him. Louis drank half the can of beer and felt it go to his head almost at once.

“You don’t even eat them, do you?” he asked. “Just killing them is enough for you.”

Church walked into the living room, seeming to decide there would be no food, and Louis followed after a moment.

He thought again randomly, Hey-ho, let’s go.

Louis sat in his chair and looked at Church again. The cat was lying on the rug by the TV, watching Louis closely, probably ready to run if Louis got angry and decided to kick it.

Instead, Louis raised his beer. “To Gage,” he said. “To my son, who might have been an artist or an Olympic swimmer or the motherfucking President of the United States. What do you say, asshole?”

Church looked at him with those dull, strange eyes.

Louis drank the rest of his beer in big gulps that hurt his sore throat, got up, went to the fridge, and got another one.

After three beers, Louis felt somewhat balanced for the first time that day. After the first six-pack, he felt he might be able to sleep in an hour or so. He came back from the fridge with his eighth or ninth (he had lost count and was unsteady), and saw Church; the cat was dozing—or pretending to—on the rug. The thought came so easily it must have been there all along, waiting to come forward:

When are you going to do it? When are you going to bury Gage in the extra part of the Pet Sematary?

And right after that:

Lazarus, come forth.

Ellie’s sleepy, dazed voice:

The teacher said if he’d just said “Come forth,” probably everybody in that graveyard would have come out.

A chill struck him with such force that Louis grabbed himself as the shudder went through him. He suddenly remembered Ellie’s first day of school, how Gage had fallen asleep on his lap while he and Rachel listened to Ellie chatter about “Old MacDonald” and Mrs. Berryman; he had said Just let me put the baby to bed, and when he took Gage upstairs a horrible feeling had struck him, and now he understood: Back in September part of him had known Gage was going to die soon. Part of him had known that Oz the Great and Terrible was coming. It was nonsense, it was garbage, it was pure superstition… and it was true. He had known. Louis spilled some beer on his shirt, and Church looked up wearily to see if the evening’s cat-kicking was about to start.

Louis suddenly remembered the question he had asked Jud; he remembered how Jud’s arm had jerked, knocking two empty beer bottles off the table. One of them had shattered. You don’t even want to talk about such things, Louis!

But he did want to talk about them—or at least think about them. The Pet Sematary. What was beyond the Pet Sematary. The idea was deadly attractive. It made a kind of sense he couldn't deny. Church had been killed in the road; Gage had been killed in the road. Here was Church—changed of course, unpleasant in some ways—but here. Ellie, Gage, and Rachel all dealt with him. He killed birds, true, and had turned a few mice inside out, but killing small animals was a cat thing to do. Church had not turned into Frankencat. He was, in many ways, as good as ever.

You’re making excuses, a voice whispered. He’s not as good as ever. He’s spooky. The crow, Louis… remember the crow?

“Good God,” Louis said aloud in a shaky, distracted voice he barely recognized as his own.

God, oh yes, fine, sure. If there had ever been a time to call on God outside of a ghost or vampire novel, this was it. So just what—what in the name of God—was he thinking about? He was thinking about a dark, evil idea he still couldn’t believe. Worse, he was lying to himself. Not just making excuses, but outright lying.

So what’s the truth? You want the truth so bad, what’s the truth?

That Church wasn’t really a cat anymore—that’s the first thing. He looked and acted like a cat, but he was just a poor copy. People couldn’t see through it, but they could feel it. He remembered when Charlton was at the house for a small pre-Christmas dinner party. They were sitting here, talking after eating, and Church jumped onto her lap. Charlton pushed him off right away, her mouth twisting with quick distaste.

It wasn’t a big deal. No one even said anything. But… it happened. Charlton felt what the cat wasn’t. Louis finished his beer and got another. If Gage came back changed like that, it would be horrible.

He opened the can and drank a lot. He was drunk now, really drunk, and he’d have a bad headache tomorrow. How I Went to My Son’s Funeral with a Hangover by Louis Creed, author of How I Just Missed Him at the Crucial Moment and many other works.

Drunk. Of course. And he thought he’d gotten drunk so he could think about this crazy idea clearly.

Even with everything, the idea was tempting, with a sick glow and appeal. Yes, mostly it had appeal.

Jud was back, speaking in his mind:

You do it because it grabs you. You do it because that burial place is a secret, and you want to share it… you make up reasons… they seem good… but you mostly do it because you want to. Or because you have to.

Jud’s voice, low and slow with his Yankee accent, Jud’s voice making him cold, giving him goosebumps, making the hair on his neck stand up.

These are secret things, Louis… a man’s heart is like stony ground… like the soil in the old Micmac burying ground. A man grows what he can… and takes care of it.

Louis started thinking about the other things Jud had told him about the Micmac burying ground. He started gathering the information, sorting through it, and putting it together—just like he used to get ready for big tests.

The dog. Spot.

I could see all the spots where the barbed wire had caught him—there was no fur in those spots, and the skin looked dented in.

The bull. Another thought surfaced in Louis’s mind.

Lester Morgan buried his best bull up there. Black Angus bull, named Hanratty… Lester pulled him all the way up there on a sled… shot him dead two weeks later. That bull got mean, really mean. But he’s the only animal I ever heard of that did.

He turned mean.

A man's heart is harder ground.

He turned really mean.

He’s the only animal I ever heard of that did.

Mostly you do it because once you’ve been up there, it’s yours.

The skin looked dented in.

Hanratty, isn’t that a silly name for a bull?

A man grows what he can… and takes care of it.

They’re my rats. And my birds. I bought the fuckers.

It’s your place, a secret place, and it belongs to you, and you belong to it.

He turned mean, but he’s the only animal I ever heard of that did.

What do you want to buy next, Louis, when the wind blows hard at night and the moon makes a white path through the woods to that place? Want to climb those stairs again? When they’re watching a horror movie, everyone in the audience knows the hero or the heroine is stupid to go up those stairs, but in real life they always do—they smoke, they don’t wear seat belts, they move their family next to a busy highway where the big rigs drone back and forth all day and all night. So, Louis, what do you say? Want to climb the stairs? Would you like to keep your dead son or go for what’s behind Door Number One, Door Number Two, or Door Number Three?

Hey-ho, let’s go.

Turned mean… only animal… the flesh looked… a man… yours… his…

Louis dumped the rest of the beer down the sink, feeling suddenly that he was going to throw up. The room was moving around in great swinging motions.

There was a knock at the door.

For a long time—it felt like a long time, anyway—he thought it was just in his head, a dream. But the knocking kept happening, steady and unstoppable. Suddenly, Louis remembered the story of the monkey’s paw, and a cold fear filled him. He felt it strongly—like a dead hand from a fridge, a dead hand that had come alive and slipped under his shirt to grab his heart. It was a dumb image, excessive and dumb, but it didn’t feel dumb. No.

Louis went to the door on numb feet and lifted the latch with shaky fingers. As he opened it, he thought: It'll be Pascow. Like they said about Jim Morrison, back from the dead and bigger than ever. Pascow standing there in his jogging shorts, larger than life and as rotten as old bread, Pascow with his messed-up head, Pascow warning him again: Don’t go up there. What was that old Animals song? Baby please don’t go, baby PLEASE don’t go, you know I love you so, baby please don’t go…

The door opened, and standing there on his porch in the windy darkness of midnight, between the day of the funeral home visit and the day of his son’s burial, was Jud Crandall. His thin white hair blew wildly in the cold dark.

Louis tried to laugh. Time felt like it was repeating itself. It was Thanksgiving again. Soon they would put Ellie’s cat Winston Churchill’s stiff, unnatural body into a plastic bag and leave. Oh, don’t ask what it is; let's just go.

“Can I come in, Louis?” Jud asked. He took out a pack of Chesterfields and put one in his mouth.

“Well,” Louis said. “It’s late, and I’ve been drinking a lot of beer.”

“Yep, I can smell it,” Jud said. He lit a match. The wind blew it out. He lit another with cupped hands, but his hands shook and the wind got it again. He got a third match, ready to light it, and looked up at Louis. “I can’t light this,” Jud said. “You gonna let me in or not, Louis?”

Louis stepped aside and let Jud walk in.

Chapter 38

They sat at the kitchen table with beers—the first time they'd had them in the kitchen, Louis realized, a little surprised. From across the living room, Ellie cried out in her sleep, and they both froze like statues in a game. The cry didn't happen again.

“Okay,” Louis said, “why are you here at twelve-fifteen the morning my son is buried? You’re a friend, Jud, but this is too much.”

Jud drank, wiped his mouth with his hand, and looked straight at Louis. There was something clear and sure in his eyes, and Louis finally looked away.

“You know why I’m here,” Jud said. “You’re thinking about things you shouldn't be thinking about, Louis. Worse, I think you’re planning them.”

“I was just thinking about going to bed,” Louis said. “I have a burial to go to tomorrow.”

“I've caused you more pain tonight than I should have,” Jud said softly. “I might even be responsible for your son's death.”

Louis looked up, surprised. “What—? Jud, don’t be crazy!”

“You’re thinking of trying to take him up there,” Jud said. “Don’t say you haven't thought about it, Louis.”

Louis didn't answer.

“How far does its influence go?” Jud said. “Can you tell me that? No. I can’t answer that myself, and I’ve lived my whole life in this part of the world. I know about the Micmacs, and they always thought of that place as holy… but not in a good way. Stanny B. told me that. My father told me too—later on. After Spot died the second time. Now the Micmacs, the state of Maine, and the U.S. government are fighting in court about who owns that land. Who does own it? No one really knows, Louis. Not anymore. Different people claimed it at one time or another, but no claim ever lasted. Anson Ludlow, the great-grandson of the town's founder, for one. His claim was maybe the best for a white man, since Joseph Ludlow the Elder got the whole thing as a gift from Good King Georgie back when Maine was just a big part of Massachusetts. But even then, he would have been in a tough court fight because other Ludlows also claimed the land, along with a guy named Peter Dimmart, who said he could prove he was a Ludlow from an affair. And Joseph Ludlow the Elder was poor in money but rich in land near the end of his life, and now and then he’d just give someone two or four hundred acres when he was drunk.”

“Were none of those papers recorded?” Louis asked, fascinated.

“Oh, our grandfathers were real sticklers for recording papers,” Jud said, lighting a new cigarette from the end of the old one. “The original paper for your land goes like this.” Jud closed his eyes and quoted, “ ‘From the great old maple on top of Quinceberry Ridge to the edge of Orrington Stream; that's how the land runs from north to south.’ ” Jud grinned, but not happily. “But the great old maple fell down around 1882 and was rotten to moss by 1900, and Orrington Stream filled with dirt and turned to marsh in the ten years between the end of World War I and the stock market crash. It made a real mess! It didn’t matter to old Anson, anyway. He was killed by lightning in 1921, right up around that cemetery.”

Louis stared at Jud. Jud sipped his beer.

“It doesn’t matter. There are lots of places where the history of who owns what is so mixed up it never gets straightened out; only the lawyers make money. Hell, Dickens knew that. I guess the Indians will get it back in the end, and I think that’s right. But that doesn’t really matter, Louis. I came over here tonight to tell you about Timmy Baterman and his dad.”

“Who’s Timmy Baterman?”

“Timmy Baterman was one of about twenty boys from Ludlow who went overseas to fight Hitler. He left in 1942. He came back in a coffin with a flag on it in 1943. He died in Italy. His daddy, Bill Baterman, lived his whole life in this town. He almost went crazy when he got the telegram… and then he got very quiet. He knew about the Micmac cemetery, you see. And he’d decided what he wanted to do.”

The chill was back. Louis stared at Jud for a long time, trying to see if the old man was lying. He wasn’t. But the fact that this story was coming up just now was too convenient.

“Why didn’t you tell me this the other night?” he said finally. “After we… after we buried the cat? When I asked you if anyone had ever buried a person up there, you said no one ever had.”

“Because you didn’t need to know,” Jud said. “Now you do.”

Louis was silent for a long time. “Was he the only one?”

“The only one I know myself,” Jud said seriously. “The only one to ever try it? I doubt that, Louis. I really doubt it. I’m like the preacher in Ecclesiastes—I don’t think there’s anything new in the world. Sometimes the glitter on top changes, but that’s all. What’s been tried once has been tried before… and before… and before.”

He looked down at his age-spotted hands. In the living room, the clock softly chimed twelve-thirty.

“I figured a man in your job is used to seeing the diseases behind the symptoms… and I decided I had to be honest with you when Mortonson at the funeral home told me you’d ordered a grave liner instead of a sealing vault.”

Louis looked at Jud for a long time, saying nothing. Jud blushed deeply but didn’t look away.

Finally Louis said: “Sounds like you did some investigating, Jud. I’m sorry that happened.”

“I didn’t ask him what you bought.”

“Not directly, maybe.”

But Jud didn’t reply, and even though his blush had gotten worse—his face was almost purple now—his eyes still didn’t move.

At last, Louis sighed. He felt incredibly tired. “Oh, forget it. I don’t care. Maybe you’re right. Maybe I was thinking about it. If I was, it wasn’t a serious thought. I didn’t think much about what I was ordering. I was thinking about Gage.”

“I know you were thinking about Gage. But you knew the difference. Your uncle was an undertaker.”

Yes, he had known the difference. A sealing vault was a construction project, something meant to last a very long time. Concrete was poured into a rectangle made of forms and reinforced with steel, and after the service at the grave, a crane lowered a slightly curved concrete top onto it. The lid was sealed with something like the stuff road crews used to patch potholes. Uncle Carl had told Louis that sealant—called Ever-Lock—really gripped tight after all that weight had been on it for a while.

Uncle Carl, who loved to tell stories (especially to his own kind, and Louis, who had worked with him during the summers, was like an apprentice undertaker), told his nephew about an order to dig up a body that he’d gotten from the Cook County D.A.’s office. Uncle Carl went to Groveland to watch the exhumation. They could be tricky, he said—people who only knew about digging up bodies from horror movies with Boris Karloff as Dr. Frankenstein’s monster and Dwight Frye as Igor were wrong about it. Opening a sealing vault wasn't a job for two guys with picks and shovels—unless they had about six weeks to spare. This one went okay… at first. The grave was opened, and the crane hooked onto the top of the vault. But the top didn’t just come off, like it should have. Instead, the whole vault, its concrete sides already a little wet and stained, started to rise out of the ground. Uncle Carl yelled for the crane operator to stop. Uncle Carl wanted to go back to the funeral home and get something to weaken the sealant's grip.

The crane operator either didn’t hear or wanted to lift the whole thing, like a kid playing with a toy crane at an arcade. Uncle Carl said that the fool almost did it, too. The vault was mostly out—Uncle Carl and his helper could hear water dripping from the bottom of the vault into the grave (it had been a wet week in Chicago) when the crane just tipped over and crashed into the grave. The crane operator hit the windshield and broke his nose. That day cost Cook County about $3,000—$2,100 more than usual. The real point of the story for Uncle Carl was that the crane operator was elected president of the Chicago Teamsters union six years later.

Grave liners were simpler. A liner was just a plain concrete box, open at the top. It was put in the grave on the morning of the funeral. After the service, the coffin was lowered into it. Then the sextons brought the top, which was usually in two pieces. These pieces were lowered into the ends of the grave, where they stood up like bookends. Iron rings were set into the concrete at the ends of each piece. The sextons would run chains through them and lower them gently onto the top of the grave liner. Each section weighed sixty, maybe seventy pounds—eighty at most. And no sealer was used.

It was easy for a man to open a grave liner; that’s what Jud was suggesting.

Easy for a man to dig up his son's body and bury it somewhere else.

Shhhhh… shhhh. We won't talk about such things. These are secrets.

“Yes, I guess I knew the difference between a sealing vault and a grave liner,” Louis said. “But I wasn’t thinking about… about what you think I was thinking about.”

“Louis—”

“It’s late,” Louis said. “It’s late, I’m drunk, and my heart hurts. If you feel like you have to tell me this story, then tell me and let’s finish it.” Maybe I should have started with martinis, he thought. Then I could have been safely passed out when he came knocking.

“All right, Louis. Thank you.”

“Just go on.”

Jud paused a moment, thinking, then began to speak.

Chapter 39

“Back then—during the war, I mean—the train still stopped in Orrington, and Bill Baterman had a hearse at the depot to meet the freight train carrying his son Timmy’s body. Four railroad men unloaded the coffin. I was one of them. There was an army guy on board from Graves Registration—the army’s wartime undertakers, Louis—but he didn’t get off. He was drunk in a boxcar with twelve coffins.

“We put Timmy in the back of the hearse—back then, they were sometimes called ‘hurry-up wagons’ because the main thing was to bury them before they decayed. Bill Baterman stood by, his face hard and… dry, I guess. He didn’t cry. Huey Garber drove the train that day and said the army guy was having quite a trip. Huey said they’d flown a lot of coffins to Limestone in Presque Isle, then the coffins and their keeper took the train south.

“The army guy walks up to Huey, pulls a fifth of rye whiskey from his shirt, and says in a soft, southern voice, ‘Well, Mr. Engineer, you’re driving a mystery train today, did you know that?’

“Huey shakes his head.

“ ‘Well, you are. That’s what they call a funeral train in Alabama.’ Huey said the guy took out a list and squinted at it. ‘We’ll drop off two coffins in Houlton, then one for Passadumkeag, two for Bangor, one for Derry, one for Ludlow, and so on. I feel like a milkman. Want a drink?’

“Huey says no, because the Bangor and Aroostook doesn’t like train drivers drinking rye, and the Graves Registration guy doesn’t mind, and Huey doesn’t mind the guy’s drunkenness. They even shook hands on it, Huey said.

“So they left, dropping off coffins every other stop. Eighteen or twenty in all. Huey said it went on to Boston, and there were weeping relatives at every stop except Ludlow… where he saw Bill Baterman, who looked dead inside and waiting for his soul to rot. When he got off that train, he woke up the army guy, and they went to some bars—fifteen or twenty—and Huey got drunker than ever and went to a prostitute, which he’d never done, and woke up with crabs so bad they made him shiver, and he said if that was a mystery train, he never wanted to drive one again.

“Timmy’s body was taken to Greenspan Funeral Home on Fern Street—across from the New Franklin Laundry—and two days later he was buried in Pleasantview Cemetery with military honors.

“You know, Louis: Mrs. Baterman had been dead ten years, along with her second child, and that mattered. A second child might have eased the pain, don’t you think? Might have reminded Bill that others feel pain and need help. I guess you’re luckier—having another child, I mean. A child and a wife who are alive and well.”

According to the letter Bill got from the lieutenant in charge of his boy’s platoon, Timmy was shot down on the road to Rome on July 15, 1943. His body was shipped home two days later, and it got to Limestone on the nineteenth. It was put aboard Huey Garber’s mystery train the very next day. Most of the GIs who got killed in Europe were buried in Europe, but all of the boys who went home on that train were special—Timmy had died charging a machine-gun nest, and he had won the Silver Star. After he died.

Timmy was buried—I might be wrong about this, but I think it was on July 22. It was four or five days later that Marjorie Washburn, who was the mailwoman in those days, saw Timmy walking up the road toward York’s Livery Stable. Well, Margie almost drove right off the road, and you can understand why. She went back to the post office, tossed her leather bag with all her undelivered mail still in it on George Anderson’s desk, and told him she was going home and to bed right then.

“Margie, are you sick?” George asks. “You are just as white as a gull’s wing.”

“I’ve had the fright of my life, and I don’t want to talk to you about it,” Margie Washburn says. “I’m not going to talk to Brian about it, or my mom, or anybody. When I get up to heaven, if Jesus asks me to talk to Him about it, maybe I will. But I don’t believe it.” And out she goes.

Everybody knew Timmy was dead; there was his obituary in the Bangor Daily News and the Ellsworth American just the week before, picture and all, and half the town turned out for his funeral up to the city. And here Margie saw him, walking up the road—stumbling up the road, she finally told old George Anderson—only this was twenty years later, and she was dying, and George told me it seemed to him like she wanted to tell somebody what she’d seen. George said it seemed to him like it bothered her, you know.

Pale he was, she said, and dressed in an old pair of chino pants and a faded flannel hunting shirt, although it must have been ninety degrees in the shade that day. Margie said all his hair was sticking up in the back. “His eyes were like raisins stuck in bread dough. I saw a ghost that day, George. That’s what scared me so. I never thought I’d see such a thing, but there it was.”

Well, word got around. Pretty soon some other people saw Timmy, too. Missus Stratton—well, we called her “missus,” but so far as anyone knew she could have been single or divorced or a widow; she had a little two-room house down where the Pedersen Road joins the Hancock Road, and she had a lot of jazz records, and sometimes she’d be willing to throw you a little party if you had a ten-dollar bill that wasn’t being used. Well, she saw him from her porch, and she said he walked right up to the edge of the road and stopped there.

He just stood there, she said, his hands dangling at his sides and his head pushed forward, looking like a boxer who’s ready to get knocked out. She said she stood there on her porch, heart beating fast, too scared to move. Then she said he turned around, and it was like watching a drunk man try to turn around. One leg went way out and the other foot turned, and he almost fell over. She said he looked right at her and all the strength just left her hands and she dropped the basket of washing she had, and the clothes fell out and got dirty all over again.

She said his eyes… she said they looked as dead and dusty as marbles, Louis. But he saw her… and he grinned… and she said he talked to her. Asked her if she still had those records because he wouldn’t mind dancing with her. Maybe that very night. And Missus Stratton went back inside, and she wouldn’t come out for most of a week, and by then it was over anyway.

A lot of people saw Timmy Baterman. Many of them are dead now—Missus Stratton is, for one, and others have moved on, but there are a few old folks like me left around who’ll tell you if you ask them.

“We saw him, I tell you, walking back and forth along the Pedersen Road, a mile east of his daddy’s house and a mile west. Back and forth he went, back and forth all day, and for all anyone knew, all night. Shirt untucked, pale face, hair all stuck up in spikes, fly unzipped sometimes, and this look on his face… this look…”

Jud paused to light a cigarette, then shook the match out, and looked at Louis through the haze of drifting blue smoke. And although the story was, of course, completely insane, there was no lie in Jud’s eyes.

“You know, they have these stories and these movies—I don’t know if they’re true—about zombies down in Haiti. In the movies they just sort of shuffle along, with their dead eyes staring straight ahead, real slow and sort of clumsy. Timmy Baterman was like that, Louis, like a zombie in a movie, but he wasn’t. There was something more. There was something going on behind his eyes, and sometimes you could see it and sometimes you couldn’t see it. Something behind his eyes, Louis. I don’t think that thinking is what I want to call it. I don’t know what in the hell I want to call it.

“It was sly, that was one thing. Like him telling Missus Stratton he wanted to dance with her. There was something going on in there, Louis, but I don’t think it was thinking and I don’t think it had much—maybe nothing at all—to do with Timmy Baterman. It was more like a… a radio signal that was coming from somewhere else. You looked at him and you thought, ‘If he touches me, I’m gonna scream.’ Like that.

“Back and forth he went, up and down the road, and one day after I got home from work—this must have been, oh, I’m going to say it was July 30 or so—here is George Anderson, the postmaster, don’t you know, sitting on my back porch, drinking iced tea with Hannibal Benson, who was then our second selectman, and Alan Purinton, who was fire chief. Norma sat there too but never said a thing.

“George kept rubbing the stump at the top of his right leg. Lost most of that leg working on the railroad, he did, and the stump used to bother him something fierce on those hot and humid days. But here he was, misery or not.

“ ‘This has gone far enough,’ George says to me. ‘I got a mail-woman who won’t deliver out on the Pedersen Road—that’s one thing. It’s starting to cause trouble with the government, and that’s something else.’

“ ‘What do you mean, it’s causing trouble with the government?’ I asked.

“Hannibal said he’d had a call from the War Department. Some lieutenant named Kinsman whose job it was to sort out bad behavior from plain old foolishness. ‘Four or five people have written anonymous letters to the War Department,’ Hannibal says, ‘and this Lieutenant Kinsman is starting to get a little bit concerned. If it was just one fellow who had written one letter, they’d laugh it off. If it was just one fellow writing a whole bunch of letters, Kinsman says he’d call the state police up in Derry Barracks and tell them they might have a crazy person who hates the Baterman family in Ludlow. But these letters all came from different people. He said you could tell that by the handwriting, name or no name, and they all say the same crazy thing—that if Timothy Baterman is dead, he makes one hell of a lively corpse walking up and down Pedersen Road with his bare face hanging out.

“ ‘This Kinsman is going to send a fellow out or come himself if this doesn’t settle down,’ Hannibal finishes up. ‘They want to know if Timmy’s dead, or AWOL, or what because they don’t like to think their records are all messed up. Also they’re gonna want to know who was buried in Timmy Baterman’s box, if he wasn’t.’

“Well, you can see what a mess it was, Louis. We sat there almost an hour, drinking iced tea and talking it over. Norma asked us if we wanted sandwiches, but no one did.

“We talked it over and over, and finally decided we had to go out there to the Baterman place. I’ll never forget that night, not if I live to be twice as old as I am now. It was hot, hotter than hell, with the sun going down like a bucket of guts behind the clouds. None of us wanted to go, but we had to. Norma knew it before any of us. She got me inside on some excuse and said, ‘Don’t let them waste time and put this off, Judson. You've got to get this taken care of. It’s a terrible thing.”

Jud looked at Louis steadily.

“That’s what she called it, Louis. It was her word. Terrible thing. And she kind of whispered in my ear, ‘If anything happens, Jud, you just run. Never mind the others; they’ll have to look out for themselves. You remember me and get out of there as fast as you can if anything happens.’

“We drove over in Hannibal Benson’s car—that son of a bitch got all the gas he wanted, I don’t know how. Nobody said much, but all four of us were smoking like chimneys. We were scared, Louis, as scared as we could be. But the only one who really said anything was Alan Purinton. He says to George, ‘Bill Baterman has been up to no good in that woods north of Route 15, and I’ll swear to it.’ Nobody answered, but I remember George nodding his head.

“Well, we got there, and Alan knocked, but nobody answered, so we went around to the back, and there the two of them were. Bill Baterman was sitting there on his back steps with a pitcher of beer, and Timmy was at the back of the yard, just staring up at that red, bloody sun as it went down. His whole face was orange with it, like he’d been skinned alive. And Bill… he looked like the devil had gotten him after his time to relax. He was swimming in his clothes, and I figured he’d lost forty pounds. His eyes had gone back in their sockets until they were like little animals in a pair of caves… and his mouth kept twitching on the left side.”

Jud paused, seemed to think, and then nodded slightly. “Louis, he looked damned.

“Timmy looked around at us and grinned. Just seeing him grin made you want to scream. Then he turned and went back to looking at the sun go down. Bill says, ‘I didn’t hear you boys knock,’ which was a total lie, of course, since Alan banged on that door loud enough to wake… to wake up a deaf man.

“No one seemed like they were going to say anything, so I says, ‘Bill, I heard your boy was killed over in Italy.’

“ ‘That was a mistake,’ he says, looking right at me.

“ ‘Was it?’ I says.

“ ‘You see him standing right there, don’t you?’ he says.

“ ‘So who do you think was in that coffin you had buried out at Pleasantview?’ Alan Purinton asks him.

“ ‘I’ll be damned if I know,’ Bill says, ‘and I’ll be damned if I care.’ He goes to get a cigarette and spills them all over the back porch, then breaks two or three trying to pick them up.

“ ‘They'll probably have to dig it up,’ Hannibal says. ‘You know that, don’t you? I had a call from the goddam War Department, Bill. They are going to want to know if they buried some other mother’s son under Timmy’s name.’

“ ‘Well, what the hell?’ Bill says in a loud voice. ‘That’s nothing to me, is it? I got my boy. Timmy came home the other day. He’s been shell-shocked or something. He’s a little strange now, but he’ll come around.’

“ ‘Let’s stop this, Bill,’ I says, and all at once I was pretty mad at him. ‘If they dig up that army coffin, they’re gonna find it dead empty, unless you went to the trouble of filling it up with rocks after you took your boy out of it, and I don’t think you did. I know what happened, Hannibal and George and Alan here know what happened, and you know what happened too. You been messing around up in the woods, Bill, and you have caused yourself and this town a lot of trouble.’

“ ‘You fellas know your way out, I guess,’ he says. ‘I don’t have to explain myself to you, or answer to you, or nothing. When I got that telegram, the life ran right out of me. I felt her go, just like piss down the inside of my leg. Well, I got my boy back. They had no right to take my boy. He was only seventeen. He was all I had left of his dear mother, and it was illegal. So screw the army, and screw the War Department, and screw the United States of America, and screw you boys too. I got him back. He’ll come around. And that’s all I got to say. Now you all just march your boots back where you came from.’

“And his mouth is twitching, and there’s sweat all over his forehead in big drops, and that was when I saw he was crazy. It would have driven me crazy too. Living with that… that thing.”

Louis was feeling sick to his stomach. He had drunk too much beer too fast. Pretty soon it was all going to come up. The heavy feeling in his stomach told him it would be coming up soon.

Well, there wasn’t much else we could do. We got ready to go. Hannibal says, ‘Bill, God help you.’

“Bill says, ‘God never helped me. I helped myself.’

“That was when Timmy walked over to us. He even walked wrong, Louis. He walked like an old, old man. He’d put one foot high up and then bring it down and then kind of shuffle and then lift the other one. It was like watching a crab walk. His hands dangled down by his legs. And when he got close enough, you could see red marks across his face on the slant, like pimples or little burns. I figure that’s where the Kraut machine gun got him. Must have damn near blown his head off.

“And he stank of the grave. It was a black smell, like everything inside him was just lying there, spoiled. I saw Alan Purinton put a hand up to cover his nose and mouth. The stench was just awful. You almost expected to see grave maggots squirming around in his hair—”

“Stop,” Louis said hoarsely. “I’ve heard enough.”

“You ain’t,” Jud said. He spoke with worn-out seriousness. “That’s it, you ain’t. And I can’t even make it as bad as it was. Nobody could understand how bad it was unless they was there. He was dead, Louis. But he was alive too. And he… he… he knew things.”

“Knew things?” Louis sat forward.

“Yep. He looked at Alan for a long time, kind of grinning—you could see his teeth, anyway—and then he spoke in this low voice; you felt like you had to strain forward to hear it. It sounded like he had gravel down in his throat. ‘Your wife is sleeping with that man she works with down at the drugstore, Purinton. What do you think of that? She screams when she comes. What do you think of that?’

“Alan, he kind of gasped, and you could see it had hit him. Alan’s in a nursing home up in Gardener now, or was the last I heard—he must be pushing ninety. Back when all this happened, he was forty or so, and there had been some talk around about his second wife. She was his second cousin, and she had come to live with Alan and Alan’s first wife, Lucy, just before the war. Well, Lucy died, and a year and a half later Alan up and married this girl. Laurine, her name was. She was no more than twenty-four when they married. And there had been some talk about her, you know. If you were a man, you might have called her ways sort of free and easy and let it go at that. But the women thought she might be loose. And maybe Alan had had a few thoughts in that direction too because he says, ‘Shut up! Shut up or I’ll knock you down, whatever you are!’

“ ‘Shush now, Timmy,’ Bill says, and he looks worse than ever, you know, like maybe he’s going to puke or faint dead away, or do both. ‘You shush, Timmy.’

“But Timmy didn’t pay any attention. He looks around at George Anderson and he says, ‘That grandson you think so highly of is just waiting for you to die, old man. He only wants the money, the money he thinks you have locked away in your lockbox at the Bangor Eastern Bank. That’s why he sucks up to you, but behind your back he makes fun of you, him and his sister. Old wooden-leg, that’s what they call you,’ Timmy says, and Louis, his voice—it changed. It got mean. It sounded like the way that grandson of George’s would have sounded if… you know, if the things Timmy was saying were true.

“ ‘Old wooden-leg,’ Timmy says, ‘and won’t they be surprised when they find out you’re poor as a church mouse because you lost it all in 1938? Won’t they be surprised, George? Won’t they just be surprised?’

“George, he backed away then, and his wooden leg buckled under him, and he fell back on Bill’s porch and spilled his pitcher of beer, and he was as white as your undershirt, Louis.

“Bill, he gets him back on his feet somehow, and he’s yelling at his boy, ‘Timmy, you stop it! You stop it!’ But Timmy wouldn’t. He said something bad about Hannibal, and then he said something bad about me too, and by then he was… raving, I’d say. Yeah, he was raving, all right. Screaming. And we started to back away, and then we started to run, dragging George along the best we could by the arms because he’d gotten the straps and harnesses on that fake leg twisted somehow, and it was all off to one side with the shoe turned around backward and dragging on the grass.

“The last I saw of Timmy Baterman, he was on the back lawn by the clothesline, his face all red in the setting sun, those marks standing out on his face, his hair all crazy and dusty somehow… and he was laughing and screeching over and over again ‘Old wooden-leg! Old wooden-leg! And the cuckold! And the whoremaster! Goodbye, gentlemen! Goodbye! Goodbye!’ And then he laughed, but it was screaming, really… something inside him… screaming… and screaming… and screaming.”

Jud stopped. His chest moved up and down rapidly.

“Jud,” Louis said. “The thing this Timmy Baterman told you—was it true?”

“It was true,” Jud muttered. “Christ! It was true. I used to go to a whorehouse in Bangor sometimes. Nothing many a man hasn’t done, although I suppose there are plenty that walk the straight and narrow. I just would get the urge—the need, maybe—to sleep with a stranger now and then. Or pay some woman to do the things a man can’t bring himself to ask his wife to do. Men have secrets, Louis. It wasn’t a terrible thing, what I did, and all of that has been behind me for the last eight or nine years, and Norma would not have left me if she had known. But something in her would have died forever. Something dear and sweet.”

Jud’s eyes were red and swollen and bleary. The tears of the old are particularly unlovely, Louis thought. But when Jud reached across the table for Louis’s hand, Louis took it firmly.

“He told us only the bad,” he said after a moment. “Only the bad. God knows there is enough of that in any human being’s life, isn’t there? Two or three days later, Laurine Purinton left Ludlow for good, and folks in town who saw her before she got on the train said she had two black eyes and had cotton stuffed up both sides of her nose. Alan, he would never talk about it. George died in 1950, and if he left anything to that grandson and granddaughter of his, I never heard about it. Hannibal got kicked out of office because of something that was just like what Timmy Baterman accused him of. I won’t tell you exactly what it was—you don’t need to know—but misusing town funds for his own use comes close enough to cover it, I reckon. There was even talk of trying him on fraud charges, but it never amounted to much. Losing the position was enough punishment for him anyway; his whole life was playing the big shot.

But there was good in those men too. That’s what I mean; that’s what people always find it so hard to remember. Hannibal got the fund started for the Eastern General Hospital, right before the war. Alan Purinton was one of the most generous, giving men I ever knew. And old George Anderson only wanted to keep running the post office forever.

It was only the bad it wanted to talk about though. It was only the bad it wanted us to remember because it was bad… and because it knew we meant danger for it. The Timmy Baterman that went off to fight the war was a nice, regular kid, Louis, maybe a little slow but goodhearted. The thing we saw that night, looking up into that red sun… that was a monster. Maybe it was a zombie or a dybbuk or a demon. Maybe there’s no name for such a thing as that, but the Micmacs would have known what it was, name or no.

“What?” Louis said, feeling numb.

“Something that had been touched by the Wendigo,” Jud said calmly. He took a deep breath, held it for a moment, let it out, and looked at his watch.

“Welladay. It’s late, Louis. I’ve talked nine times as much as I meant to.”

“I doubt that,” Louis said. “You’ve been very expressive. Tell me what happened in the end.”

“There was a fire at the Baterman place two nights later,” Jud said. “The house burned to the ground. Alan Purinton said there was no doubt the fire was set on purpose. Heating oil had been splashed all over that little house. You could smell it strongly for three days after the fire was out.”

“So they both burned up.”

“Oh, yes, they burned. But they were dead before that. Timmy was shot twice in the chest with a pistol Bill Baterman kept nearby, an old Colt’s. They found it in Bill’s hand. What he’d done, or so it looked like, was kill his boy, lay him on the bed, and then spill out that heating oil. Then he sat down in his easy chair by the radio, flicked a match, and killed himself with that Colt .45.”

“Jesus,” Louis said.

“They were pretty burned, but the county doctor thought Timmy Baterman had been dead for two or three weeks.”

Silence, ticking out.

Jud got up. “I wasn’t kidding when I said I might have killed your boy, Louis, or helped it happen. The Micmacs knew that place, but that doesn’t mean they made it what it was. The Micmacs weren’t always here. They came maybe from Canada, maybe from Russia, maybe from Asia a long time ago. They stayed here in Maine for a thousand years, or maybe two—it’s hard to tell, because they didn’t change the land much. And now they’re gone again… like we’ll be gone, someday, though I guess we’ll change things more, for better or worse. But the place will stay no matter who’s here, Louis. It’s not like someone owned it and could take its secret when they left. It’s an evil, rotten place, and I shouldn’t have taken you up there to bury that cat. I know that now. It has a power you’ll avoid if you care about your family and yourself. I wasn’t strong enough to fight it. You saved Norma’s life, and I wanted to do something for you, and that place twisted my good wish into its own evil purpose. It has a power… and I think that power changes, like the moon. It’s been full of power before, and I’m scared it’s getting full again. I’m scared it used me to get to you through your son. Do you see what I mean, Louis?” His eyes begged Louis.

“You’re saying the place knew Gage was going to die, I think,” Louis said.

“No, I’m saying the place might have made Gage die because I showed you the power in the place. I’m saying I might have murdered your son with good intentions, Louis.”

“I don’t believe it,” Louis said at last, shakily. Didn’t; wouldn’t. Couldn’t.

He held Jud’s hand tightly. “We’re burying Gage tomorrow. In Bangor. And he will stay in Bangor. I don’t plan to go up there to the Pet Sematary or past it ever again.”

“Promise me!” Jud said sharply. “Promise.”

“I promise,” Louis said.

But in his mind, the thought stayed—a small, bright hope that wouldn’t go away.

Chapter 40

But none of that happened.
