All of it—the loud Orinco truck, the fingers that barely touched Gage’s jacket and then slipped off, Rachel getting ready for the viewing in her housecoat, Ellie holding Gage’s picture and putting his chair next to her bed, Steve Masterton’s tears, the fight with Irwin Goldman, Jud Crandall’s awful story of Timmy Baterman—all of it existed only in Louis Creed’s mind during the few seconds he chased his laughing son toward the road. Behind him, Rachel screamed again—Gage, come back, don’t RUN!—but Louis didn’t waste his breath. It was going to be close, very close, and yes, one of those things did happen: from up the road he heard the truck’s engine and a memory sparked, and he heard Jud Crandall talking to Rachel on their first day in Ludlow: You want to watch them around the road, Missus Greed. It’s a bad road for kids and pets.

Now Gage was running down the gentle slope of lawn that met the edge of Route 15, his short, strong legs pumping, and he should have fallen, but he kept going, and now the truck was very loud, that low, rumbling sound Louis sometimes heard from his bed as he drifted to sleep. Then it seemed comforting, but now it terrified him.

Oh my dear God oh my dear Jesus let me catch him don’t let him get into the road!

Louis sped up and leaped, throwing himself forward like a football player making a tackle; he saw his shadow moving along the grass below him, and he thought of the kite, the Vulture, its shadow stretching across Mrs. Vinton’s field, and as Gage ran into the road, Louis’s fingers brushed the back of his jacket… and then caught it.

He yanked Gage backward and landed on the ground, his face hitting the rough gravel, giving himself a bloody nose. A sharp pain shot through his groin—Ohhh, if I’d known I was playing football, I would have worn my cup—but the pain in his nose and groin faded as he heard Gage’s cry of pain and anger when his bottom landed on the shoulder, and he fell backward onto the lawn, hitting his head. A moment later, his cries were drowned out by the truck and its loud air horn.

Louis managed to get up, despite the heavy feeling in his stomach, and held his son. Rachel ran to them, also crying, shouting to Gage, “Never run in the road, Gage! Never, never, never! The road is bad! Bad!” Gage was so surprised by this tearful lecture that he stopped crying and stared at his mother.

“Louis, your nose is bleeding,” she said, then hugged him so suddenly and tightly that he could barely breathe.

“That isn’t the worst of it,” he said. “I think I’m sterile, Rachel. Oh boy, the pain.”

And she laughed so hard that for a few moments he worried about her, and he thought: If Gage really had died, I think it would have broken her.

But Gage didn’t die; all of that was just a horribly detailed moment of imagination as Louis ran faster than his son’s death across a green lawn on a sunny May afternoon.

Gage went to elementary school, and at seven he started going to camp, where he showed a great and surprising talent for swimming. He also surprised his parents by handling a month apart with no obvious emotional issues. By the time he was ten, he was spending the whole summer at Camp Agawam in Raymond, and at eleven he won two blue ribbons and a red one at the Four Camps Swimathon that ended the summer. He grew tall, but he was still the same Gage, sweet and a little surprised by the things the world offered… and for Gage, things were never bitter or bad.

He was an honors student in high school and on the swimming team at John Bapst, the private school he wanted to go to because of its swimming pool. Rachel was upset, Louis not surprised when, at seventeen, Gage said he wanted to become Catholic. Rachel thought it was all because of the girl Gage was dating; she imagined them getting married soon (“if that little tramp with the St. Christopher’s medal isn’t sleeping with him, I’ll eat my hat, Louis,” she said), his college plans ruined, his Olympic dreams gone, and lots of little Catholics running around by the time Gage was forty. By then he would be (according to Rachel) a cigar-smoking truck driver with a beer belly, praying his way to an early death.

Louis thought his son’s reasons were better, and even though Gage converted (and on the day he did, Louis sent a mean postcard to Irwin Goldman; it said, Maybe you’ll have a Jesuit grandson yet. Your non-Jewish son-in-law, Louis), he didn’t marry the nice (and definitely not a tramp) girl he had dated through most of his senior year.

He went to Johns Hopkins, made the Olympic swimming team, and on one long, amazing, and incredibly proud afternoon sixteen years after Louis had raced a truck to save his son’s life, he and Rachel—who was now mostly gray, but colored it—watched their son win a gold medal for the U.S.A. When the NBC cameras zoomed in on him, standing with his wet head back, his eyes open and calm and looking at the flag as the national anthem played, the ribbon around his neck, and the gold against his skin, Louis cried. He and Rachel both cried.

“I guess this is the best it gets,” he said softly and turned to hug his wife. But she was looking at him with growing fear, her face seeming to age as if hurt by time; the national anthem faded and when Louis looked back at the TV he saw a different boy there, a black boy with curly hair that still had drops of water in it.

This is the best it gets.

His cap.

His cap is…

… oh dear God, his cap is full of blood.

Louis woke up in the cold, bleak light of a rainy seven o’clock, clutching his pillow. His head pounded fiercely with his heartbeat; the ache grew and faded, grew and faded. He burped acid that tasted like old beer, and his stomach churned miserably. He had been crying; the pillow was wet with his tears, as if he had wandered in and out of some corny country song in his sleep. Even in the dream, he thought, some part of him had known the truth and had cried for it.

He got up and staggered to the bathroom, heart racing weakly, his mind broken up by the strength of his hangover. He reached the toilet just in time and threw up a lot of last night’s beer.

He kneeled on the floor, eyes closed, until he felt able to stand up. He reached for the handle and flushed the toilet. He went to the mirror to see how bloodshot his eyes were, but the glass had been covered with a square of cloth. Then he remembered. Drawing on a past she claimed to hardly remember, Rachel had covered all the mirrors in the house, and she took off her shoes before coming inside.

No Olympic swimming team, Louis thought blankly as he walked back to his bed and sat down. The sour taste of beer coated his mouth and throat, and he swore to himself (not for the first time or the last) that he would never touch that poison again. No Olympic swimming team, no 3.0 GPA in college, no Catholic girlfriend or conversion, no summer camp, no nothing. His sneakers had been ripped off; his sweater turned inside out; his sweet little boy’s body, so strong and sturdy, nearly torn apart. His cap had been full of blood.

Now, sitting on his bed in the grip of this numbing hangover, rainwater trickling down the window beside him, his grief came for him completely, like some sad woman from a hospital ward. It came and broke him down, weakened him, took away whatever defenses were left, and he put his face in his hands and cried, rocking back and forth on his bed, thinking he would do anything to have a second chance, anything at all.

Chapter 41

Gage was buried at two o’clock that afternoon. By then the rain had stopped. Scattered clouds still moved overhead, and most of the people who came to mourn carried black umbrellas the funeral home provided.

At Rachel’s request, the funeral director, who led the short, simple service by the grave, read the part from Matthew that starts with “Let the little children come to me.” Louis, standing on one side of the grave, looked across at his father-in-law. For a moment Goldman looked back at him, and then he looked down. He wasn't going to argue today. The bags under his eyes looked huge, and around his black silk cap, fine, white hair blew in the breeze. With his gray and black beard on his cheeks, he looked more like a homeless man than ever. He seemed like a man who didn't really know where he was. Louis tried, but still couldn't feel sorry for him.

Gage’s small white coffin, its latch fixed, sat on shiny metal bars over the grave. The edges of the grave were covered with bright green fake grass that hurt Louis’s eyes. Several baskets of flowers sat on top of this fake and cheerful surface. Louis looked past the funeral director. There was a low hill, covered with graves, family plots, and one tall monument with the name PHIPPS on it. Just above the sloping top of PHIPPS, he could see a bit of yellow. Louis looked at this, thinking about it. He kept looking at it even after the funeral director said, “Let us bow our heads for a moment of quiet prayer.” It took Louis a few minutes, but he understood. It was a digger. A digger parked over the hill where the mourners wouldn’t have to see it. And, when the funeral was over, the worker would crush his cigarette with his workboot, put it in his pocket (workers at the cemetery were usually fired for leaving their cigarettes on the ground—it looked bad because many people died of lung cancer), jump in the digger, start it up, and cut his son off from the sun forever… or at least until the day of the Resurrection.

Resurrection… ah, that’s a word

(that you should forget about and you know it).

When the funeral director said “Amen,” Louis took Rachel’s arm and led her away. Rachel protested a little—she wanted to stay longer, please, Louis—but Louis insisted. They went to the cars. He saw the funeral director taking umbrellas with the home’s name on the handles from the mourners and giving them to an assistant. The assistant put them in an umbrella stand that looked strange on the wet grass. He held Rachel’s arm with his right hand and Ellie’s white-gloved hand with his left. Ellie was wearing the same dress she had worn to Norma Crandall’s funeral.

Jud came over as Louis helped his family into the car. Jud also looked like he’d had a hard night.

“You okay, Louis?”

Louis nodded.

Jud bent to look into the car. “How are you, Rachel?” he asked.

“I’m all right, Jud,” she whispered.

Jud touched her shoulder gently and then looked at Ellie. How about you, dear one?”

“I’m fine,” Ellie said and gave him a big, fake smile to show him how fine she was.

“What’s that picture you got there?”

For a moment Louis thought she would keep it to herself, but then shyly she passed it to Jud. He held it in his big fingers, fingers that looked clumsy and fit for working on big trucks or trains—but they were also the fingers that had pulled a bee stinger from Gage’s neck like a magician… or a surgeon.

“Why, that’s real nice,” Jud said. “You pulling him on a sled. Bet he liked that, didn’t he, Ellie?”

Ellie nodded, starting to cry.

Rachel started to say something, but Louis squeezed her arm—be still awhile.

“I used to pull him a lot,” Ellie said, crying, “and he’d laugh and laugh. Then we’d go in and Mommy would make us cocoa and say, ‘Put your boots away,’ and Gage would grab them all up and scream ‘Boots! Boots!’ so loud it hurt your ears. Remember that, Mom?”

Rachel nodded.

“Yeah, I bet that was fun,” Jud said, handing the picture back. “And he may be dead now, Ellie, but you can keep your memories of him.”

“I’m going to,” she said, wiping her face. “I loved Gage, Mr. Crandall.”

“I know you did, dear.” He leaned in and kissed her, and when he pulled back, his eyes looked at Louis and Rachel coldly. Rachel looked back, confused and a little hurt, not understanding. But Louis understood: What are you doing for her? Jud’s eyes asked. Your son is dead, but your daughter is not. What are you doing for her?

Louis looked away. There was nothing he could do for her, not yet. She would have to deal with her grief as best she could. He was thinking too much about his son.

Chapter 42

By evening, new clouds had rolled in and a strong west wind had started. Louis put on his light jacket, zipped it, and took the car keys from the hook on the wall.

“Where you going, Lou?” Rachel asked. She didn’t sound very interested. After supper she had started crying again, and even though she was weeping softly, she couldn’t seem to stop. Louis had made her take a Valium. Now she sat with the newspaper open to the barely started crossword puzzle. In the other room, Ellie sat quietly watching “Little House on the Prairie” with Gage’s picture on her lap.

“I thought I’d get a pizza.”

“Didn’t you eat enough earlier?”

“I just wasn’t hungry then,” he said, telling the truth, and then adding a lie: “I am now.”

That afternoon, between three and six, the final part of Gage’s funeral had happened at the Ludlow house. This was the part with food. Steve Masterton and his wife came with a hamburger and noodle casserole. Charlton showed up with a quiche. “It will keep until you want it, if you don’t eat it all,” she told Rachel. “Quiche is easy to warm up.” The Dannikers from up the road brought a baked ham. The Goldmans appeared—neither of them would speak to Louis or even get close to him, which he didn’t mind—with a variety of cold cuts and cheeses. Jud also brought cheese—a large wheel of his old favorite, Mr. Rat. Missy Dandridge brought a key lime pie. And Surrendra Hardu brought apples. The food seemed to go beyond religious differences.

This was the funeral party, and even though it was quiet, it wasn’t completely sad. There was less drinking than at a normal party, but there was some. After a few beers (just the night before he had promised he would never drink again, but in the cold afternoon light the previous evening seemed like a very long time ago) Louis thought about sharing some little funeral stories his Uncle Carl had told him—that at Sicilian funerals unmarried women sometimes cut off a piece of the dead person’s shroud and slept with it under their pillows, believing it would bring them luck in love; that at Irish funerals fake weddings were sometimes performed, and the toes of the dead were tied together because of an old Celtic belief that it kept the dead person’s ghost from walking. Uncle Carl said that the custom of tying D.O.A. tags to the big toes of dead bodies had started in New York, and since all of the early morgue workers had been Irish, he thought this was a leftover of that old superstition. Then, looking at their faces, he decided such stories would be taken the wrong way.

Rachel had broken down only once, and her mother was there to comfort her. Rachel held onto Dory Goldman and cried against her shoulder in a way that let everything out, which she hadn’t been able to do with Louis, maybe because she saw them both as responsible for Gage’s death, or maybe because Louis, lost in his own strange thoughts, hadn’t encouraged her to grieve. Either way, she had turned to her mother for comfort, and Dory was there to give it, her tears mixing with her daughter’s. Irwin Goldman stood behind them, his hand on Rachel’s shoulder, and looked at Louis with a bitter victory across the room.

Ellie walked around with a silver tray full of canapés, little rolls with a toothpick stuck through each one. She kept her picture of Gage tucked tightly under her arm.

Louis received condolences. He nodded and thanked those offering sympathy. And if his eyes seemed distant, his manner a little cold, people assumed he was thinking of the past, of the accident, of the future without Gage; none (perhaps not even Jud) would have guessed that Louis had started to consider the methods of grave robbing… only as a thought experiment, of course; he didn't plan to do anything. It was just a way to keep his mind busy.

It wasn't as if he planned to do anything.

Louis stopped at the Orrington Corner Store, bought two six-packs of cold beer, and called Napoli’s to order a pepperoni-and-mushroom pizza.

“Can I get a name for the order, sir?”

Oz the Great and Terrible, Louis thought.

“Lou Creed.”

“Okay, Lou, we’re really busy, so it’ll be about forty-five minutes—is that okay?”

“Sure,” Louis said and hung up. As he got back into the Civic and started the engine, he realized that even though there were probably twenty pizza places in the Bangor area, he had chosen the one closest to Pleasantview, where Gage was buried. Well, what the hell? he thought uncomfortably. They make good pizza. No frozen dough. They toss it in the air and catch it on their fists, right where you can watch, and Gage used to laugh—

He stopped that thought.

He drove past Napoli’s to Pleasantview. He figured he had known he would do that, but what was the harm? None.

He parked across the street and crossed the road to the wrought-iron gates, which shone in the last light of day. Above them, in a semicircle, were wrought-iron letters spelling PLEASANTVIEW. The view was, to Louis, neither pleasant nor unpleasant. The cemetery was nicely landscaped on rolling hills; there were long rows of trees (but in these last few minutes of fading light, the shadows those trees cast seemed deep and as darkly unpleasant as still water in a quarry) and a few lonely weeping willows. It wasn’t quiet. The turnpike was nearby—the drone of traffic came on the steady, cold wind—and the glow in the darkening sky was Bangor International Airport.

He reached for the gate, thinking, They’ll be locked, but they were not. Maybe it was too early to lock them, and if they locked them at all it would only be to protect the place from drunks, vandals, and teenage lovers. The days of the body snatchers

(there’s that word again)

were over. The right-hand gate swung open with a faint screech, and after a glance over his shoulder to make sure he was alone, Louis stepped through. He closed the gate behind him and heard the click of the latch.

He stood in this small suburb of the dead, looking around.

A fine and private place, he thought, but none, I think, do there embrace. Who? Andrew Marvel? And why did the human mind store up such amazing piles of useless junk, anyway?

Jud’s voice spoke in his mind then, worried and—scared? Yes. Scared.

Louis, what are you doing here? You’re looking down a road you don’t want to take.

He pushed the voice aside. If he was hurting anyone, it was only himself. No one needed to know he had been here as the daylight faded to dark.

He began to walk toward Gage’s grave, taking one of the winding paths. In a moment he was in a lane of trees; they rustled their new leaves mysteriously over his head. His heart was beating too loudly in his chest. The graves and monuments were in rough rows. Somewhere there would be a caretaker’s building, and in it would be a map of Pleasantview’s twenty or so acres, neatly and logically divided into sections, each section showing the occupied graves and the unsold plots. Real estate for sale. One-room apartments. Sleepers.

Not much like the Pet Sematary, he thought, and this made him stop and think for a moment, surprised. No, it wasn’t. The Pet Sematary had given him the feeling of order rising almost unnoticed from chaos. Those rough circles, one inside the other, going in toward the center, the crude markers, crosses made of boards. As if the children who buried their pets there had made the pattern without even realizing it, as if…

For a moment Louis saw the Pet Sematary as a kind of advertisement… a come-on, like they had on freak row at the carnival. They’d bring out the fire-eater and let you watch his show for free because the owners knew you wouldn’t buy the steak unless you saw it cooking, you wouldn’t pay the money if you didn’t see the flash—

Those graves, those graves in their almost Druid-like circles.

The graves in the Pet Sematary copied the oldest religious symbol of all: circles getting smaller showing a spiral going down, not to a point, but to forever; order from chaos or chaos from order, depending on how you thought. It was a symbol the Egyptians had carved on the tombs of the Pharaohs, a symbol the Phoenicians had drawn on the burial mounds of their dead kings; it was found on cave walls in ancient Mycenae; the leaders of Stonehenge had made it as a clock to measure the universe; it appeared in the Bible as the whirlwind from which God had spoken to Job.

The spiral was the oldest sign of power in the world, man’s oldest symbol of that twisty bridge that may exist between the world and the unknown.

Louis reached Gage’s grave at last. The earthmover was gone. The fake grass had been taken away, rolled up by some whistling worker thinking about a beer after work at the Fairmount Lounge, and stored in a shed somewhere. Where Gage lay there was a neat rectangle of bare, raked earth, maybe five feet by three feet. The headstone hadn’t been put up yet.

Louis kneeled. The wind blew through his hair, messing it up. The sky was almost completely dark now. Clouds were moving fast across it.

No one has shined a light in my face and asked me what I’m doing here. No dog has barked. The gate was open. The days of the body snatchers are over. If I came up here with a pick and a shovel—

He snapped back to reality. He was just playing a dangerous game with himself if he thought that Pleasantview was unguarded at night. What if he was found up to his waist in his son’s new grave by the caretaker or the watchman? It might not get in the papers, but then again it might. He could be charged with a crime. What crime? Grave robbing? Not likely. Damaging property would be more likely. And whether it was in the paper or not, people would find out. People would talk; it was too good a story not to tell: Local doctor is caught digging up his two-year-old son, who recently died in a car accident. He would lose his job. Even if he didn't, Rachel would be scared by the rumors, and Ellie might be teased at school until her life was miserable. He might have to prove he was sane to avoid charges.

But I could bring Gage back to life! Gage could live again!

Did he really believe it?

The truth was, he did. He had told himself over and over, both before Gage's death and after, that Church wasn't really dead, just stunned. That Church had dug his way out and come home. A children's story with scary parts—Winnie the Pooh. A master unknowingly piles stones over a living animal. The loyal animal digs out and comes home. Fine. Except it wasn't true. Church had been dead. The Micmac burial ground had brought him back.

He sat by Gage's grave, trying to put all the facts in order as logically as this dark magic allowed.

Timmy Baterman, now. First, did he believe the story? And second, did it matter?

Despite how easy it would be, he believed most of it. It was clear that if a place like the Micmac burial ground existed (and it did) and if people knew about it (and some of the older people in Ludlow did), then sooner or later someone would try it. Human nature, as Louis understood it, made it hard to believe it had stopped at a few pets and valuable animals.

All right, then—did he also believe that Timmy Baterman had turned into some kind of all-knowing demon?

That was a harder question, and he was careful because he didn't want to believe it, and he had seen what that kind of thinking could lead to before.

No, he didn't want to believe Timmy Baterman had been a demon, but he wouldn't—absolutely couldn't—let what he wanted affect his judgment.

Louis thought about Hanratty, the bull. Hanratty, Jud said, had turned mean. So, in his way, had Timmy Baterman. Hanratty had later been "put down" by the same man who had somehow dragged the bull's body up to the Micmac burial ground on a sled. Timmy Baterman had been "put down" by his father.

But because Hanratty had gone bad, did that mean all animals went bad? No. Hanratty the bull didn't prove that was always the case; Hanratty was actually an exception. Look back at the other animals—Jud's dog Spot, the old woman's parakeet, Church himself. They had all come back changed, and the change had been obvious in all cases, but in the case of Spot, at least, the change hadn't been so great that Jud had hesitated to recommend the process of… of…

(resurrection)

Yes, of resurrection to a friend years later. Of course, later he had tried to justify, and beat around the bush, and had said a lot of scary, confused nonsense that wasn't even real philosophy.

How could he refuse this chance—this unbelievable chance—because of the Timmy Baterman story? One example didn't prove anything.

You’re twisting the facts to fit what you want to believe, his mind argued. At least be honest about the change in Church. Even if you don't count the animals—the mice and the birds—what about how he is now? Confused… that's the best word, it says it all. The day we were out with the kite. Remember how Gage was that day? How full of life, reacting to everything? Wouldn't it be better to remember him like that? Do you want to bring back a zombie from a cheap horror movie? Or even just a mentally slow little boy? A boy who eats with his hands and stares blankly at the TV and will never learn to write his name? What did Jud say about his dog? “It was like washing a piece of meat.” Is that what you want? A piece of living meat? And even if you're okay with that, how do you explain your son's return to your wife? To your daughter? To Steve Masterton? To the world? What happens when Missy Dandridge sees Gage riding his tricycle in the yard? Can't you hear her screams, Louis? Can't you see her scratching her face? What do you tell the reporters? What do you say when a TV crew from “Real People” shows up, wanting to film your resurrected son?

Did any of this matter, or was it just fear talking? Did he think these things couldn't be handled? That Rachel wouldn't greet her dead son with joy?

Yes, he knew Gage might come back… changed. But would that change how much he loved him? Parents loved children who were born blind, Siamese twins, children born with messed-up insides. Parents begged for mercy for children who had grown up to rape, murder, and torture innocent people.

Did he think he couldn't love Gage if Gage wore diapers until he was eight? If he didn't learn to read until he was twelve? If he never learned at all? Could he just throw his son away like a… a mistake, when there was another option?

But, Louis, God, you don't live alone! People will say—

He cut that thought off sharply. Of all the things to worry about now, public attention was the least important.

Louis looked down at Gage’s grave and felt a wave of awe and horror. Without thinking, his fingers had made a pattern in the dirt—a spiral.

He swept his hands through the dirt, erasing the pattern. Then he left Pleasantview, hurrying and feeling like he was trespassing, convinced he would be seen, stopped, and questioned at every turn.

He was late picking up his pizza, and even though it had been left on top of one of the big ovens, it was partly cold and greasy, tasting like cooked clay. Louis ate one slice and then threw the rest out the window, box and all, as he drove back to Ludlow. He wasn't usually one to litter, but he didn't want Rachel to see a mostly uneaten pizza in the trash at home. It might make her wonder if pizza was really what he had gone to Bangor for.

Louis started thinking about the timing and circumstances.

Time. Time could be extremely, even critically, important. Timmy Baterman had been dead for a while before his father could take him to the Micmac burial ground. Timmy was shot on the nineteenth… Timmy was buried—I might be wrong, but I think it was July twenty-second. Four or five days later, Marjorie Washburn… saw Timmy walking up the road.

Okay, say Bill Baterman did it four days after his son's burial… no. If he was going to guess, he should guess conservatively. Say three days. For the sake of argument, assume Timmy Baterman came back to life on July twenty-fifth. That was six days between the boy's death and his return, a conservative estimate. It might have been as long as ten days. For Gage, it had been four days. Time was slipping away, but he could still beat Bill Baterman's time by a lot. If…

If he could create similar conditions to those that brought Church back to life. Church had died at the perfect time, right? His family was away when Church was hit and killed. No one knew, except for him and Jud.

His family had been in Chicago.

For Louis, the final piece clicked into place.

"You want us to what?" Rachel asked, staring at him in shock.

It was a quarter to ten. Ellie was in bed. Rachel had taken another Valium after cleaning up from the funeral party ("funeral party" was another horrible phrase full of hidden contradictions, like "visiting hours," but there seemed to be no other way to describe how they had spent their afternoon) and had seemed dazed and quiet ever since he got back from Bangor… but this had gotten through to her.

“To go back to Chicago with your mother and father,” Louis repeated patiently. “They’re leaving tomorrow. If you call them now and Delta right after, you might get on their plane.”

“Louis, are you crazy? After you and my father fought—”

Louis heard himself speaking quickly and smoothly, which wasn't like him. It gave him a strange, cheap thrill. He felt like a substitute football player who suddenly gets the ball and runs for a seventy-yard touchdown, dodging players and thinking fast with surprising ease. He wasn't usually a good liar, and he hadn't planned this conversation, but now a stream of believable lies, half-truths, and clever excuses flowed out of him.

“The fight is one reason I want you and Ellie to go back with them. We need to fix things, Rachel. I knew it… felt it… at the funeral home. When the fight started, I was trying to make peace.”

“But this trip… I don’t think it’s a good idea, Louis. We need you. And you need us.” She looked at him doubtfully. “At least, I hope you need us. And we’re not in a good place to—”

“—not in a good place to stay here,” Louis said firmly. He felt like he was getting a fever. “I’m glad you need me, and I need you and Ellie. But right now, this is the worst place for you, honey. Gage is everywhere in this house, in every corner. For you and me, sure. But it’s even worse for Ellie, I think.”

He saw pain in her eyes and knew he had affected her. Part of him felt guilty for this easy victory. All the books he’d read about death said that a grieving person's first strong desire is to leave the place where it happened… but giving in to that urge can be harmful because it lets them avoid facing the new reality. The books said it was best to stay, to fight grief on its own ground until it faded into memory. But Louis couldn't risk that with his family at home. He had to get them away, at least for a while.

“I know,” she said. “It just… hits you everywhere. I moved the couch while you were in Bangor… I thought vacuuming would distract me… from things, and I found four of his little Matchbox cars under there, like they were waiting for him to come back and… you know, play with them…” Her voice trembled, then broke. Tears ran down her face. “And that’s when I took the second Valium because I started crying again, like now… oh, this is such a mess… hold me, Lou, please hold me?”

He held her, and he did it well, but he felt fake. His mind raced with ways to use her tears to his advantage. Some nice guy, huh? Oh well, let's go.

“How long does it last?” she cried. “Does it ever stop? If we could just have him back, Louis, I swear I’d watch him better, it would never happen, and just because that driver was speeding doesn’t excuse me—us. I didn’t know pain like this was possible, really. It comes, again and again, and it hurts so much, Louis, I can’t rest, even when I sleep. I dream it, over and over, I see him running to the road… and I scream at him…”

“Shhh,” he said. “Rachel, shhhh.”

She lifted her puffy face to him. “It wasn’t even like he was doing something wrong, Louis. It was just a game to him… the truck came at the wrong time… and Missy Dandridge called while I was still crying… and said she read in the Ellsworth American that the driver tried to kill himself.”

“What?”

“He tried to hang himself in his garage. He’s in shock and very depressed, the paper said…”

“Too bad he didn’t succeed,” Louis said harshly, but his voice sounded far away to himself, and he felt a chill spreading through him. The place has a power, Louis… it’s been full of power before, and I’m afraid it’s coming back to full again. “My boy’s dead and he’s out on a thousand dollars’ bail and he’ll go on feeling depressed and suicidal until some judge takes away his license for ninety days and gives him a light fine.”

“Missy says his wife has taken the kids and left him,” Rachel said quietly. “She didn’t get that from the paper, but from someone who knows someone down Ellsworth way. He wasn’t drunk. He wasn’t on drugs. He didn’t have any speeding tickets before. He said that when he got to Ludlow, he just felt like speeding up. He said he didn’t even know why. So around and around it goes.”

He just felt like speeding up.

The place has a power…

Louis pushed these thoughts away. He gripped his wife’s forearm gently. “Call your mother and father. Do it now. There’s no need for you and Ellie to be in this house another day. Not another day.”

“Not without you,” she said. “Louis, I want us… I need us to stay together.”

“I’ll follow you in three days—four at the most.” If things went well, Rachel and Ellie might be back here in forty-eight hours. “I have to find someone to cover for me, at least part-time, at the university. I have sick days and vacation days, but I don’t want to leave Surrendra in a tough spot. Jud can watch the house while we’re gone, but I want to turn off the electricity and store our food in the Dandridges’ freezer,”

“Ellie’s school…”

“Forget it. It’s out in three weeks anyway. They’ll understand, given the situation. They’ll let her out early. It’ll all work out just—”

“Louis?”

He stopped. “What?”

“What are you hiding?”

“Hiding?” He looked at her honestly, clearly. “I don’t know what you’re talking about.”

“Don’t you?”

“No. I don’t.”

“Never mind. I’ll call them right now… if that’s what you really want.”

“It is,” he said, and the words seemed to echo in his mind like a heavy bell.

“It might even be best… for Ellie.” She looked at him with her red-rimmed eyes, still a little out of it from Valium. “You look feverish, Louis. Like you might be getting sick.”

She went to the telephone and called the motel where her parents were staying before Louis could reply.

The Goldmans were thrilled with Rachel’s idea. They weren’t so crazy about Louis joining them in three or four days, but in the end they wouldn’t have to worry about it, of course. Louis had no intention of going to Chicago. He had suspected that if there was a problem, it would be getting plane tickets so late. But luck was on his side there too. There were still seats available on Delta’s Bangor to Cincinnati flight, and a quick check showed two cancellations on a Cincinnati to Chicago flight. It meant that Rachel and Ellie would only be able to travel with the Goldmans as far as Cincinnati, but they would get to Chicago less than an hour later.

It’s almost like magic, Louis thought, hanging up the telephone, and Jud’s voice responded quickly, It’s been full of power before, and I’m scared...

Oh, get fucked, he told Jud’s voice rudely. I’ve learned to accept a lot of strange things in the last ten months, my good old friend. But am I ready to believe that a haunted piece of land can affect airline tickets? I don’t think so.

“I’ll have to pack,” Rachel said. She was looking at the flight information Louis had written down on the pad by the phone.

“Just take the one big suitcase,” Louis said.

She looked at him wide-eyed, a little surprised. “For both of us? Louis, you’re kidding.”

“All right, take a couple of tote bags too. But don’t wear yourself out packing a different outfit for the next three weeks,” he said, thinking, Especially since you may be back in Ludlow very soon. “Take enough for a week, ten days. You’ve got the checkbook and the credit cards. Buy what you need.”

“But we can’t afford—” she started, sounding unsure. She seemed unsure about everything now, easily swayed and confused. He recalled her strange, random remark about the Winnebago he'd once casually mentioned buying.

“We have the money,” he said.

“Well… I guess we could use Gage’s college fund if we had to, though it would take a couple of days to get the money from the savings account and a week to cash in the treasury bills—”

Her face started to fall apart again. Louis held her. She’s right. It just keeps hitting you, never stops. “Rachel, don’t,” he said. “Don’t cry.”

But of course, she did—she had to.

While she was upstairs packing, the phone rang. Louis jumped to answer it, thinking it would be someone from Delta, saying there had been a mistake and no flights were available. I should have known it was going too well.

But it wasn’t Delta. It was Irwin Goldman.

“I’ll get Rachel,” Louis said.

“No.” For a moment, there was nothing but silence. He’s probably sitting there, trying to figure out what to call you first.

When Goldman spoke again, his voice was tight. He seemed to be forcing the words out against a strong inner struggle. “It’s you I want to talk to. Dory wanted me to call and say sorry for my… for my behavior. I guess… Louis, I guess I wanted to say sorry too.”

Why, Irwin! How big of you! My God, I think I just wet my pants!

“You don’t need to apologize,” Louis said. His voice was dry and mechanical.

“What I did was unforgivable,” Goldman said. Now he didn’t just seem to be pushing the words out; he seemed to be coughing them out. “You suggesting that Rachel and Eileen come out has made me see what a good man you have been about this… and how awful I have been.”

There was something very familiar in this speech, something eerily familiar—

Then he got it, and his mouth suddenly pulled together in a tight pucker, as if he had bitten straight through a plump yellow lemon. Rachel’s way—she was completely unaware of it, Louis was sure—of saying contritely, Louis, I’m sorry I was such a bitch, after her bitchiness had gotten her her own way about something she really wanted. Here was that voice—robbed of Rachel’s liveliness and merriness, true—but that same voice saying, I’m sorry I was such a jerk, Louis.

The old man was getting his daughter and granddaughter back; they were running home from Maine to Daddy. Thanks to Delta and United, they were coming back to where they belonged, back to where Irwin Goldman wanted them. Now he could afford to be generous. As far as old Irwin knew, he had won. So let’s just forget that I took a swing at you over your dead son’s body, Louis, or that I kicked you when you were down, or that I knocked his coffin off its stand and snapped the latch so you could see—or think you saw—that one last flash of your child’s hand. Let’s forget all of that. Let the past be the past.

Terrible as it may be, Irwin, you old prick, I’d wish for you to drop dead right this second, if it wouldn’t mess up my plans.

“That’s all right, Mr. Goldman,” he said evenly. “It was… well… an emotional day for all of us.”

“It was not all right,” he insisted, and Louis realized—although he did not want to—that Goldman was not just being polite, was not just saying that he was sorry he had been such a jerk now that he was getting his own way. The man was nearly weeping, and he was speaking with a slow and trembling urgency. “It was a terrible day for all of us. Thanks to me. Thanks to a stupid, stubborn old man. I hurt my daughter when she needed my help… I hurt you, and maybe you needed my help too, Louis. That you do this… act this way… after I acted that way… it makes me feel like garbage. And I think that is just the way I should feel.”

Oh let him stop this, let him stop before I start to scream at him and ruin everything.

“Rachel’s probably told you, Louis, we had another daughter—”

“Zelda,” Louis said. “Yes, she told me about Zelda.”

“It was hard,” Goldman said, his voice shaking. “Hard for all of us. Maybe hardest for Rachel—Rachel was there when Zelda died—but hard for Dory and me too. Dory almost broke down—”

What do you think Rachel had? Louis wanted to shout. Do you think a kid can’t break down? Twenty years later she’s still afraid of death. And now this happens. This awful, terrible thing. It’s amazing she isn’t in the hospital, being fed through a tube. So don’t talk to me about how hard it was for you and your wife, you bastard.

“Ever since Zelda died, we have… I guess we have held on to Rachel… always wanting to protect her… and to make it up to her. Make up for the problems she had with her… her back… for years afterward. Make up for not being there.”

Yes, the old man was really crying. Why did he have to be crying? It made it harder for Louis to hold on to his pure hate. Harder, but not impossible. He pictured Goldman reaching into his smoking jacket for his big checkbook… but he suddenly saw Zelda Goldman in the background, a restless ghost in a sickbed, her pale face full of anger and pain, her hands like claws. The Goldman ghost. The Great and Terrible Oz.

“Please,” he said. “Please, Mr. Goldman. Irwin. No more. Let’s not make things worse than they have to be, okay?”

“I think now that you are a good man and that I was wrong about you, Louis. Oh, listen, I know what you think. Am I that stupid? No. Stupid, but not that stupid. You think I’m saying all of this because now I can, you’re thinking oh yeah, he’s getting what he wants and once he tried to buy me off, but… but Louis, I swear…”

“No more,” Louis said gently. “I can’t… I really can’t take any more.” Now his voice was shaking too. “Okay?”

“All right,” Goldman said and sighed. Louis thought it was a sigh of relief. “But let me say again that I’m sorry. You don’t have to accept it. But that is what I called to say, Louis. I’m sorry.”

“All right,” Louis said. He closed his eyes. His head was throbbing. “Thank you, Irwin. I accept your apology.”

“Thank you,” Goldman said. “And thank you… for letting them come. Maybe it’s what they both need. We’ll wait for them at the airport.”

“Fine,” Louis said, and a thought suddenly came to him. It was crazy and appealing in its very sanity. He would forgive and forget… and he would let Gage stay in his Pleasantview grave. Instead of trying to reopen a door that had slammed shut, he would lock it and bolt it shut and throw away the key. He would do exactly what he had told his wife he was going to do: settle their affairs here and catch a plane back to Chicago. Maybe they would spend the whole summer there, him and his wife and his sweet daughter. They would go to the zoo and the planetarium and go boating on the lake. He would take Ellie to the top of the Sears Tower and show her the Midwest stretching out like a big, flat gameboard, rich and peaceful. Then when mid-August came, they would come back to this house which now seemed so sad and shadowy, and maybe it would be like starting over. Maybe they could start weaving with new thread. What was on the Creed loom now was ugly, covered in drying blood.

But wouldn't that be like murdering his son? Killing him again?

A voice inside tried to argue that it wasn't, but he wouldn't listen. He quickly shut the voice up.

“Irwin, I should go now. I want to make sure Rachel has what she needs and then get her to bed.”

“All right. Goodbye, Louis. And again—”

If he says he's sorry one more time, I'll fucking scream.

“Goodbye, Irwin,” he said and hung up the phone.

Rachel was surrounded by clothes when he came upstairs. Blouses on the beds, bras over the backs of chairs, slacks on hangers hung over the doorknob. Shoes were lined up like soldiers under the window. She seemed to be packing slowly but well. Louis could see it would take her at least three suitcases (maybe four), but he also saw no point in arguing with her about it. Instead, he jumped in and helped.

“Louis,” she said as they closed the last suitcase (he had to sit on it before Rachel could snap it shut), “are you sure you don’t want to tell me something?”

“For God’s sake, hon, what is this about?”

“I don’t know what it’s about,” she replied calmly. “That’s why I’m asking.”

“What do you think I’m going to do? Sneak off to a brothel? Join the circus? What?”

“I don’t know. But this feels wrong. It feels like you’re trying to get rid of us.”

“Rachel, that’s crazy!” He said this with a force that was partly annoyance. Even in this situation, he felt a little irritated that she saw through him so easily.

She smiled weakly. “You were never a very good liar, Lou.”

He started to argue again, but she stopped him.

“Ellie dreamed you were dead,” she said. “Last night. She woke up crying, and I went to her. I slept with her for two or three hours and then came back to you. She said that in her dream you were sitting at the kitchen table and your eyes were open, but she knew you were dead. She said she could hear Steve Masterton screaming.”

Louis looked at her, worried. “Rachel,” he said finally, “her brother just died. It’s normal for her to dream that other members of her family—”

Yes, I figured that much out myself. But the way she said it… the details… it felt like a prophecy to me.

She laughed weakly.

“Or maybe you had to be there.”

“Yes, maybe so,” Louis said.

It felt like a prophecy to me.

“Come to bed with me,” Rachel said. “The Valium’s all gone, and I don’t want to take any more. But I’m afraid. I’ve been having my own dreams…”

“Dreams of what?”

“Of Zeida,” she said simply. “The last few nights since Gage died, when I go to sleep, Zelda’s there. She says she’s coming for me, and this time she’ll get me. That both she and Gage will get me. For letting them die.”

“Rachel, that’s—”

“I know. Just a dream. Normal enough. But come to bed with me and keep the dreams away if you can, Louis.”

They lay together in the dark, crowded into Louis’s single bed.

“Rachel? You still awake?”

“Yes.”

“I want to ask you something.”

“Go ahead.”

He hesitated, not wanting to hurt her more but needing to know.

“Do you remember when we were worried about him when he was nine months old?” he asked finally.

“Yes. Yes, of course I do. Why?”

When Gage was nine months old, Louis had become very worried about the size of his head. It was way off Louis’s chart, which showed the normal head sizes for babies each month. At four months, Gage’s head size had started to get bigger than normal, and then it kept growing. He could hold his head up fine—which was a good sign—but Louis had still taken him to George Tardiff, who was maybe the best nerve doctor in the Midwest. Rachel had wanted to know what was wrong, and Louis had told her the truth: he was worried that Gage might have water on the brain. Rachel’s face had turned very white, but she had stayed calm.

“He seems normal to me,” she said.

Louis nodded. “He does to me too. But I don’t want to ignore this, babe.”

“No, you mustn’t,” she said. “We mustn’t.”

Tardiff had measured Gage’s skull and frowned. Tardiff poked two fingers at Gage’s face, Three Stooges style. Gage flinched. Tardiff smiled. Louis’s heart warmed a little. Tardiff gave Gage a ball to hold. Gage held it for a while and then dropped it. Tardiff picked up the ball and bounced it, watching Gage’s eyes. Gage’s eyes followed the ball.

“I’d say there’s a fifty-fifty chance he’s hydrocephalic,” Tardiff said to Louis in his office later. “No—the odds may actually be a bit higher than that. If so, it’s mild. He seems very alert. The new shunt operation should take care of the problem easily… if there is a problem.”

“A shunt means brain surgery,” Louis said.

“Minor brain surgery.”

Louis had looked into the process soon after he began to worry about the size of Gage’s head, and the shunt operation, designed to drain excess fluid, had not looked very minor to him. But he kept his mouth shut, telling himself just to be grateful the operation existed at all.

“Of course,” Tardiff went on, “there’s still a good chance that your kid just has a real big head for a nine-month-old. I think a CAT-scan is the best place to start. Do you agree?”

Louis had agreed.

Gage spent a night in Our Sisters of Charity Hospital and was given general anesthesia. His sleeping head was put into a device that looked like a giant clothes dryer. Rachel and Louis waited downstairs while Ellie spent the day at Grandma and Grandda’s, watching “Sesame Street” nonstop on Grandda’s new video recorder. For Louis, those had been long, gray hours in which he found himself adding up possibilities of varying horribleness and comparing results. Death under general anesthesia, death during a shunt operation, mild slowness as a result of hydrocephalus, extreme slowness as a result of same, epilepsy, blindness… oh, there were all sorts of possibilities. For really complete disaster plans, Louis remembered thinking, see your local doctor.

Tardiff had come into the waiting room around five o’clock. He had three cigars. He stuck one in Louis’s mouth, one in Rachel’s (she was too stunned to argue), and one in his own.

“The kid is fine. No water on the brain.”

“Light this thing,” Rachel had said, crying and laughing at the same time. “I’m going to smoke it till I throw up.”

Smiling, Tardiff lit their cigars.

God was saving him for Route 15, Dr. Tardiff, Louis thought now.

“Rachel, if he had had water on the brain, and if the tube hadn’t worked… could you have still loved him?”

“What a strange question, Louis!”

“Could you?”

“Yes, of course. I would have loved Gage no matter what.”

“Even if he was mentally disabled?”

“Yes.”

“Would you have wanted him in a home?”

“No, I don’t think so,” she said slowly. “I guess, with the money you’re making now, we could pay for that… a really good place, I mean… but I think I’d want him with us if we could… Louis, why do you ask?”

“Well, I guess I was still thinking of your sister Zelda,” he said. He was still surprised at this strange ease. “Wondering if you could have gone through that again.”

“It wouldn’t have been the same,” she said, sounding almost amused. “Gage was… well, Gage was Gage. He was our son. That would have made all the difference. It would have been hard, I guess, but… would you have wanted him in a home? A place like Pineland?”

“No.”

“Let’s go to sleep.”

“That’s a good idea.”

“I feel like I can sleep now,” she said. “I want to forget about today.”

“I agree,” Louis said.

A long time later she said sleepily, “You’re right, Louis… just dreams and nothing real…”

“Sure,” he said, and kissed her ear. “Now sleep.”

It felt like a prediction to me.

He stayed awake for a long time, and before he slept, the moon's curve peeked in the window at him.

Chapter 43

The next day was cloudy but very warm, and Louis was sweating a lot by the time he had checked Rachel’s and Ellie’s luggage and gotten their tickets from the computer. He thought that being able to keep busy was a good thing, and he felt only a small, painful reminder of the last time he had put his family on a plane to Chicago, at Thanksgiving.

Ellie seemed far away and a little strange. Several times that morning Louis had looked up and seen a look of strange thought on her face.

"She's up to something," he told himself.

She didn’t say anything when told they were all going to Chicago, she and Mommy first, maybe for the whole summer, and just kept eating her breakfast (Cocoa Bears). After breakfast she went quietly upstairs and put on the dress and shoes Rachel had set out for her. She had brought the picture of her pulling Gage on her sled to the airport with her, and she sat quietly in one of the plastic chairs in the lower lobby while Louis waited in line for their tickets and the loudspeaker announced arriving and departing flights.

Mr. and Mrs. Goldman arrived forty minutes before the flight. Irwin Goldman looked smart (and didn’t seem to be sweating) in a cashmere coat even though it was sixty degrees; he went to the Avis desk to return his car while Dory Goldman sat with Rachel and Ellie.

Louis and Goldman joined the others at the same time. Louis was a little afraid that they might repeat the "my son, my son" scene, but he was spared. Goldman just gave him a weak handshake and mumbled hello. The quick, embarrassed look he gave his son-in-law confirmed what Louis had woken up thinking that morning: the man must have been drunk.

They went upstairs on the escalator and sat in the boarding lounge, not talking much. Dory Goldman nervously touched her copy of an Erica Jong novel but didn’t open it. She kept looking, a little nervously, at the picture Ellie was holding.

Louis asked his daughter if she wanted to walk over to the bookstore with him and pick out something to read on the plane.

Ellie had been looking at him in that thoughtful way again. Louis didn’t like it. It made him nervous.

“Will you behave at Grandma and Grandda’s?” he asked her as they walked over.

“Yes,” she said. “Daddy, will I get in trouble for missing school? Andy Pasioca says there’s someone who catches kids who skip school.”

“Don’t you worry about that,” he said. “I’ll sort things out with the school, and you can start again in the fall without any problems.”

“I hope I’ll be okay in the fall,” Ellie said. “I’ve never been in a grade before. Only kindergarten. I don’t know what kids do in grades. Homework, maybe.”

“You’ll be fine.”

“Daddy, are you still angry at Grandda?”

He stared at her. “What makes you think I don’t like your grandda, Ellie?”

She shrugged as if she didn't care much about it. “When you talk about him, you always look angry.”

“Ellie, that’s rude.”

“Sorry.”

She gave him that strange, dreamy look and then wandered off to look at the shelves of children’s books—Mercer Meyer and Maurice Sendak and Richard Scarry and Beatrix Potter and that famous old favorite, Dr. Seuss. How do they figure this stuff out? Or do they just know? How much does Ellie know? How’s it affecting her? Ellie, what’s going on behind that pale little face? Mad at him—Jesus!

“Can I have these, Daddy?” She was holding out a Dr. Seuss and a book Louis hadn’t seen since he was a kid—the story of Little Black Sambo and how the tigers had gotten his clothes one day.

I thought they’d taken that one out of print, Louis thought, surprised.

“Yes,” he said, and they stood in a short line at the cash register. “Your grandpa and I get along fine,” he said and thought again of his mother’s story of how when a woman really wanted a baby, she “found” one. He remembered his own dumb promises to himself that he would never lie to his own children. Over the last few days he had become quite a good liar, he felt, but he would not let himself think about it now.

“Oh,” she said and went quiet.

The silence made him nervous. To break it he said, “So do you think you’ll have a good time in Chicago?”

“No.”

“No? Why not?”

She looked up at him with that dreamy expression. “I’m scared.”

He put his hand on her head. “Scared? Honey, what of? You’re not scared of the plane, are you?”

“No,” she said. “I don’t know what I’m scared of. Daddy, I dreamed we were at Gage’s funeral, and the funeral man opened his coffin, and it was empty. Then I dreamed I was home, and I looked in Gage’s crib, and that was empty too. But there was dirt in it.”

Lazarus, come forth.

For the first time in months, he remembered the dream he had after Pascow’s death—the dream, and then waking up to find his feet dirty and the foot of the bed covered in pine needles and mud.

The hairs on the back of his neck prickled.

“Just dreams,” he said to Ellie, and his voice sounded, at least to him, perfectly normal. “They’ll go away.”

“I wish you were coming with us,” she said, “or that we were staying here. Can we stay, Daddy? Please? I don’t want to go to Grandma and Grandpa’s… I just want to go back to school. Okay?”

“Just for a little while, Ellie,” he said. “I’ve got”—he swallowed—”a few things to do here, and then I’ll be with you. We can decide what to do next.”

He expected an argument, maybe even an Ellie-style tantrum. He might even have welcomed it—something he knew, because that look was not. But there was only that pale, disturbing silence that seemed so deep. He could have asked her more but found he didn’t dare; she had already told him more than he maybe wanted to hear.

Soon after he and Ellie returned to the waiting area, the flight was called. Boarding passes were shown, and the four of them got in line. Louis hugged his wife and kissed her hard. She held onto him for a moment and then let him go so he could pick Ellie up and kiss her cheek.

Ellie looked at him seriously with her wise eyes. “I don’t want to go,” she said again but so quietly that only Louis could really hear over the shuffling and talking of the boarding passengers. “I don’t want Mommy to go either.”

“Ellie, come on,” Louis said. “You’ll be fine.”

“I’ll be fine,” she said, “but what about you? Daddy, what about you?”

The line had started to move. People were walking down the jetway to the 727. Rachel pulled Ellie’s hand, and for a moment she resisted, holding up the line, her eyes fixed on her father—and Louis remembered her impatience last time, her cries of come on—come on—come on.

“Daddy?”

“Go now, Ellie. Please.”

Rachel looked at Ellie and saw that dark, dreamy look for the first time. “Ellie?” she said, surprised and, Louis thought, a little afraid. “You’re holding up the line, baby.”

Ellie’s lips trembled and turned white. Then she let herself be led into the jetway. She looked back at him, and he saw pure terror in her face. He raised his hand to her, pretending to be cheerful.

Ellie did not wave back.

Chapter 44

As Louis left the BIA terminal, a coldness settled over him. He realized he was really going to do this. His mind, which had been sharp enough to get him through med school mostly on a scholarship and what his wife made selling coffee and pastries from 5 to 11 A.M. six days a week, had taken over the problem and broken it down into pieces, as if it were just another exam—the biggest he had ever faced. And he planned to ace it, one hundred percent.

He drove to Brewer, the small city across the Penobscot River from Bangor. He found a parking spot across the street from Watson’s Hardware.

“Can I help you?” the clerk asked.

“Yes,” Louis said. “I’d like a heavy flashlight—one of the square ones—and something to cover the light.”

The clerk was a small, thin man with a high forehead and sharp eyes. He smiled now, but it wasn't a friendly smile. “Going hunting at night, are you?”

“I’m sorry?”

“Gonna use a light to hunt deer tonight?”

“Not at all,” Louis said, not smiling. “I don’t have a license for that.”

The clerk blinked and then decided to laugh. “In other words, mind your own business, huh? Well, look—you can’t cover one of those big lights completely, but you can get a piece of felt and poke a hole in the middle. It'll make the beam very narrow.”

“That sounds fine,” Louis said. “Thanks.”

“Sure. Anything else for you today?”

“Yes,” Louis said. “I need a pick, a shovel, and a spade. Short-handled shovel, long-handled spade. A strong piece of rope, eight feet long. A pair of work gloves. A canvas tarp, maybe eight by eight.”

“I can get all that,” the clerk said.

“I’ve got a septic tank to dig up,” Louis said. “It seems I’m breaking the zoning rules, and I’ve got some very nosy neighbors. I don’t know if covering my light will help, but I thought I’d try it. I could get a big fine.”

“Uh-oh,” the clerk said, “better get a clothespin for your nose while you’re at it.”

Louis laughed politely. His purchases cost $58.60. He paid cash.

As gas prices rose, they used the big station wagon less and less. It had a bad wheel-bearing for a while, but Louis kept putting off the repair. This was partly because he didn’t want to spend the two hundred it would probably cost, but mostly because it was a pain. Now, when he really needed the big old car, he didn’t dare risk it. The Civic was a hatchback, and Louis was worried about going back to Ludlow with the pick, shovel, and spade in there. Jud Crandall’s eyes were sharp, and he was smart too. He would know what was going on.

Then he realized there was no real reason to go back to Ludlow anyway. Louis crossed the Chamberlain Bridge back into Bangor and checked into the Howard Johnson’s Motor Lodge on the Odlin Road—again near the airport, again near Pleasantview Cemetery where his son was buried. He checked in under the name Dee Dee Ramone and paid cash for his room.

He tried to nap, thinking he would be grateful for the rest before tomorrow morning. Like in some old novel, there was hard work ahead of him tonight—enough to last a lifetime.

But his brain just wouldn't stop.

He lay on the generic motel bed under a plain motel print of pretty boats docked by a quaint old wharf in a pretty New England harbor, fully dressed except for his shoes, his wallet, coins, and keys on the night table beside him, his hands behind his head. He still felt cold and totally disconnected from his family, the places he knew so well, and even his work. This could have been any Howard Johnson's in the world—in San Diego, Duluth, Bangkok, or Charlotte Amalie. He was nowhere, and now and then a strange thought struck him: before he saw those familiar places and faces again, he would see his son.

His plan kept playing in his mind. He looked at it from every angle, examining it for weaknesses. He felt like he was walking on a narrow beam over a pit of insanity. Madness was all around him, softly fluttering like the wings of owls with big golden eyes: he was heading into madness.

The voice of Tom Rush echoed softly in his head: O death your hands are clammy… I feel them on my knees… you came and took my mother… won’t you come back after me?

Madness. Madness all around, close, hunting him.

He walked the balance beam of reason; he studied his plan.

Tonight, around eleven o'clock, he would dig up his son's grave, take the body from the coffin, wrap Gage in a piece of the tarp, and put it in the trunk of the Civic. He would put the coffin back and fill in the grave. He would drive to Ludlow, take Gage's body from the trunk… and take a walk. Yes, he would take a walk.

If Gage returned, the single path split into two possibilities. In one, he saw Gage returning as Gage, maybe stunned or slow or even mentally impaired (only deep down did Louis let himself hope that Gage would return whole, and just as he had been—but surely even that was possible, wasn’t it?), but still his son, Rachel’s son, Ellie’s brother.

In the other, he saw some kind of monster coming out of the woods behind the house. He had accepted so much that he didn't question the idea of monsters, or even demons, bodiless beings of evil from another world that might take over a reanimated body that had lost its original soul.

Either way, he and his son would be alone. And he would…

I will figure it out.

Yes. That's what he'd do.

I will figure it out, not just his body but his spirit too. I'll think about the trauma of the accident, which he might not even remember. Remembering what happened with Church, I'll expect some mental slowness, maybe a little, maybe a lot. I'll decide if we can bring Gage back into our family based on what I see in a day or two. And if he's too far gone—or if he comes back like Timmy Baterman did, like something evil—I'll kill him.

As a doctor, he felt he could easily kill Gage, if Gage was just a body holding something else, some other being. He wouldn't let himself be fooled by its cries or tricks. He'd kill it like he'd kill a rat with the plague. No need to make a big deal about it. A pill in water, maybe two or three. If he had to, a shot. He had morphine in his bag. The next night, he'd put the body back in Pleasantview and bury it again, just hoping he'd get lucky a second time (you don't even know if you'll get lucky once, he reminded himself). He thought about the easier and safer option of the Pet Sematary, but he didn't want his son up there. There were many reasons. A kid burying their pet years later might find the body—that was one reason. But the biggest reason was simpler. The Pet Sematary might be… too close.

After the reburial, he'd fly to Chicago and be with his family. Rachel and Ellie would never need to know about his failed experiment.

Then, thinking about the other way things could go—the way he desperately hoped for with all his love for his son: he and Gage would leave the house when he was done examining him, they'd leave at night. He'd take some papers with him and never come back to Ludlow. He and Gage would stay at a motel—maybe even this one he was lying in now.

The next morning, he'd cash out all their accounts, turning everything into American Express traveler's checks (don't leave home with your resurrected son without them, he thought) and cash. He and Gage would fly somewhere—Florida, most likely. From there, he'd call Rachel, tell her where he was, tell her to bring Ellie and get on a plane without telling her parents where she was going. Louis thought he could convince her. Don't ask questions, Rachel. Just come. Come now. Right away.

He'd tell her which motel they were at. She and Ellie would arrive in a rental car. He'd bring Gage to the door when they knocked. Maybe Gage would be wearing a bathing suit.

And then—

But he didn't dare think further than that; instead, he went back to the beginning of the plan and started going over it again. He figured that if things worked out, it would mean getting all the little details needed for new identities so Irwin Goldman couldn't use his money to find them. It could be done.

He dimly remembered arriving at the Ludlow house, tense, tired, and more than a little scared, and having some daydream about just driving down to Orlando and getting a job as a medic at Disney World. Maybe that wasn’t so crazy after all.

He pictured himself, dressed in white, reviving a pregnant woman who had foolishly gone on the Magic Mountain ride and had fainted. Stand back, stand back, give her some air, he heard himself saying, and the woman opened her eyes and smiled gratefully at him.

As his mind played out this pleasant daydream, Louis fell asleep. He slept as his daughter awoke in an airplane somewhere above Niagara Falls, screaming from a nightmare of clutching hands and stupid yet merciless eyes; he slept as the stewardess hurried down the aisle to see what was wrong; he slept as Rachel, totally upset, tried to calm her; he slept as Ellie cried over and over again: It’s Gage! Mommy! It’s Gage! it’s Gage! Gage is alive! Gage has got the knife from Daddy’s bag! Don’t let him get me! Don’t let him get Daddy!

He slept as Ellie finally quieted and lay shaking against her mother’s breast, her eyes wide and tearless, and as Dory Goldman thought what an awful thing all of this had been for Eileen, and how much she reminded Dory of Rachel after Zelda had died.

He slept and woke up at a quarter past five, with the afternoon light beginning to slant down toward the coming night.

Strange stuff, he thought dully and got up.

Chapter 45

By the time United Airlines flight 419 landed at O’Hare Airport and let its passengers off at ten minutes past three, central standard time, Ellie Creed was close to panic, and Rachel was very scared.

If you lightly touched Ellie on the shoulder, she jumped and looked around at you with wide eyes, and her whole body shook constantly. It was as if she was charged with electricity. The nightmare on the airplane had been bad enough, but this… Rachel simply didn’t know what to do.

Going into the terminal, Ellie tripped and fell. She didn’t get up but just lay there on the carpet with people walking around her (or looking down at her with that slightly sympathetic but distant look of people who are traveling and don’t want to be bothered) until Rachel picked her up.

“Ellie, what’s wrong?” Rachel asked.

But Ellie wouldn’t answer. They walked across the lobby toward the luggage carousels, and Rachel saw her mother and father waiting there. She waved at them with her free hand, and they came over.

“They said we shouldn’t go to the gate and wait,” Dory said, “so we thought… Rachel? How’s Eileen?”

“Not good.”

“Is there a bathroom, Mommy? I’m going to throw up.”

“Oh, God,” Rachel said in despair and took her hand. There was a bathroom across the lobby, and she quickly led Ellie toward it.

“Rachel, should I come?” Dory called.

“No, get the luggage, you know what it looks like. We’re okay.”

Thankfully the ladies’ room was empty. Rachel led Ellie to one of the stalls, searching her purse for a dime, and then she saw—thank God—that the locks on three of them were broken. Over one broken lock, someone had written in grease pencil: SIR JOHN CRAPPER WAS A SEXIST PIG!

Rachel pulled the door open quickly; Ellie was now moaning and holding her stomach. She gagged twice, but nothing came up; it was just the dry heaves of total nervous exhaustion.

When Ellie said she felt a little better, Rachel took her over to the sinks and washed her daughter’s face. Ellie was terribly pale, and there were circles under her eyes.

“Ellie, what is wrong? Can’t you tell me?”

“I don’t know what’s wrong,” she said. “But I knew something was wrong ever since Daddy told me about the trip. Because something was wrong with him.”

Louis, what are you hiding? You were hiding something. I could see it; even Ellie could see it.

It struck her that she had also been nervous all day, as if waiting for something bad to happen. She felt the way she did in the two or three days before her period, tense and on edge, ready to laugh or cry or get a headache that would hit hard and then be gone three hours later.

“What?” she said now to Ellie’s reflection in the mirror. “Honey, what could be wrong with Daddy?”

“I don’t know,” Ellie said. “It was the dream. Something about Gage. Or maybe it was Church. I don’t remember. I don’t know.”

“Ellie, what was your dream?”

“I dreamed I was in the Pet Sematary,” Ellie said. “Paxcow took me to the Pet Sematary and said Daddy was going to go there and something terrible was going to happen.”

“Paxcow?” A jolt of terror, both sharp and unclear, struck her. What was that name, and why did it feel familiar? She felt she had heard that name—or one like it—but she couldn’t remember where. “You dreamed someone named Paxcow took you to the Pet Sematary?”

“Yes, that’s what he said his name was. And—” Her eyes suddenly widened.

“Do you remember something else?”

“He said that he was sent to warn but that he couldn’t interfere. He said that he was… I don’t know… that he was near Daddy because they were together when his soul was ta—ta—I can’t remember!” she cried.

“Honey,” Rachel said, “I think you dreamed about the Pet Sematary because you’re still thinking about Gage. And I’m sure Daddy is fine. Do you feel any better now?”

“No,” Ellie whispered. “Mommy, I’m scared. Aren’t you scared?”

“Huh-uh,” Rachel said, with a quick little shake of her head and a smile—but she was, she was scared; and that name, Paxcow, troubled her with its familiarity. She felt she had heard it in some awful situation months or even years ago, and that uneasy feeling would not leave her.

She felt something—something big, growing, and about to explode. Something awful that needed to be stopped. But what? What?

“I’m sure everything is okay,” she told Ellie. “Want to go back to Grandma and Grandda?”

“I guess so,” Ellie said without energy.

A Puerto Rican woman led her very young son into the ladies’ room, scolding him. A large wet stain had spread on the crotch of the little boy’s shorts, and Rachel was reminded of Gage with a kind of paralyzing sadness. This new sorrow was like novocaine, numbing her worries.

“Come on,” she said. “We’ll call Daddy from Grandda’s house.”

“He was wearing shorts,” Ellie said suddenly, looking back at the little boy.

“Who was, honey?”

“Paxcow,” Ellie said. “He was wearing red shorts in my dream.”

That brought the name into focus for a moment, and Rachel felt that knee-weakening fear again… then it disappeared.

They could not get close to the luggage carousel; Rachel could just see the top of her father’s hat, the one with the feather. Dory Goldman was holding two seats against the wall for them and waving. Rachel brought Ellie over.

“Are you feeling any better, dear?” Dory asked.

“A little bit,” Ellie said. “Mommy—”

She turned to Rachel and stopped. Rachel was sitting straight up, her hand covering her mouth, her face pale. She understood. It had suddenly hit her with a terrible thud. Of course, she should have known right away, but she had tried not to think about it. Of course.

“Mommy?”

Rachel turned slowly to her daughter, and Ellie could hear her neck creak. She took her hand away from her mouth.

“Did the man in your dream tell you his first name, Eileen?”

“Mommy, are you all—”

“Did the man in your dream tell you his first name?”

Dory was looking at her daughter and granddaughter as if they had both lost their minds.

“Yes, but I can’t remember… Mommy, you’re hurrrrting me—”

Rachel looked down and saw her free hand was gripping Ellie’s arm tightly.

“Was it Victor?”

Ellie gasped. “Yes, Victor! He said his name was Victor! Mommy, did you dream about him too?”

“Not Paxcow,” Rachel said. “Pascow.”

“That’s what I said. Paxcow.”

“Rachel, what’s wrong?” Dory said. She took Rachel’s free hand and felt how cold it was. “And what’s wrong with Eileen?”

“It’s not Eileen,” Rachel said. “It’s Louis, I think. Something is wrong with Louis. Or something will be wrong. Sit with Ellie, Mom. I want to call home.”

She got up and went to the telephones, searching her purse for a quarter. She called collect, but no one answered. The phone just kept ringing.

“Will you try your call later?” the operator asked her.

“Yes,” Rachel said and hung up.

She stood there, staring at the phone.

He said he was sent to warn, but he couldn’t step in. He said he was… he was near Daddy because they were together when his soul was dis—dis—I can’t remember!

“Discorporated,” Rachel whispered. Her fingers dug into her handbag. “Oh my God, was that the word?”

She tried to grab her thoughts, to sort them out. Was something happening, something more than just sadness over Gage’s death and this strange trip that felt like running away? How much did Ellie know about the young man who died on Louis’s first day at work?

Nothing, her mind replied firmly. You hid it from her, like you hid anything about death—even her cat possibly dying, remember that dumb argument in the pantry? You hid it. Because you were scared then, and you’re scared now. His name was Pascow, Victor Pascow, and how desperate is this now, Rachel? How bad is it? What in God’s name is going on?

Her hands shook so much she needed two tries to put the quarter in. She called the university infirmary and got Charlton, who seemed a bit confused but took the call. No, she hadn’t seen Louis and would be surprised if he came in today. She offered Rachel her sympathy again. Rachel accepted it and asked Charlton to have Louis call her at her parents’ house if he did come in. Yes, he had the number, she answered Charlton’s question, not wanting to tell the nurse (who probably knew anyway; Charlton didn’t miss much) that her parents’ house was across the country.

She hung up, feeling hot and shaky.

She heard Pascow’s name somewhere else, that’s all. You don’t raise a kid in a bubble, like a… hamster. She heard it on the radio. Or some kid told her at school, and she remembered it. Even that word she couldn’t say—if it was a mouthful like “discorporated” or “discorporeal,” so what? That proves nothing except that the subconscious is a sticky trap like they say.

She remembered a college psych teacher who said that if things were right, your memory could replay the names of everyone you’d met, every meal you ate, the weather every day of your life. He made a good case, saying the mind was a computer with tons of memory—not 16K, 32K, or 64K, but maybe a billion K: a thousand billion. How much could each of these “chips” hold? No one knew. But there were so many, he said, they didn’t need to be erased to be reused. The conscious mind had to dim some as protection against overload. “You might not remember where your socks are,” the teacher said, “if the whole Encyclopedia Britannica was in the next memory cells.”

The class laughed politely.

But this isn’t a psych class under good lights with all that easy jargon on the board and some know-it-all professor happily rambling through the last fifteen minutes. Something is terribly wrong here and you know it—you feel it. I don’t know what it has to do with Pascow, or Gage, or Church, but it has something to do with Louis. What? Is it—

Suddenly a thought as cold as ice struck her. She picked up the telephone again and felt in the coin slot for her dime. Was Louis thinking about suicide? Was that why he had gotten rid of them, almost pushed them out the door? Had Ellie somehow had a… a… oh, forget psychology! Had she had a psychic feeling of some sort?

This time she made the call collect to Jud Crandall. It rang five times… six… seven. She was about to hang up when his voice, breathless, answered. “H’lo?”

“Jud! Jud, this is—”

“Just a minute, ma’am,” the operator said. “Will you accept a collect call from Mrs. Louis Creed?”

“Ayuh,” Jud said.

“Pardon, sir, is that yes or no?”

“I guess I will,” Jud said.

There was a pause as the operator translated country talk into regular English. Then: “Thank you. Go ahead, ma’am.”

“Jud, have you seen Louis today?”

“Today? I don’t think so, Rachel. But I went to Brewer this morning for groceries. I was in the garden behind the house this afternoon. Why?”

“Oh, it’s probably nothing, but Ellie had a nightmare on the plane, and I thought I’d calm her down if I could.”

“Plane?” Jud’s voice seemed to get a bit sharper. “Where are you, Rachel?”

“Chicago,” she said. “Ellie and I came back to visit my parents.”

“Louis didn’t come with you?”

“He’ll be here by the end of the week,” Rachel said, trying to keep her voice steady. There was something in Jud’s voice she didn’t like.

“Did he want you to go there?”

“Well… yes. Jud, what’s wrong? Something is wrong, isn’t it? And you know what it is.”

“Maybe you should tell me the child’s dream,” Jud said after a long pause. “I’d like you to.”

Chapter 46

After he and Rachel finished talking, Jud put on his light coat—the day had become cloudy and the wind had started to blow—and crossed the road to Louis’s house, stopping on his side to check carefully for trucks before crossing. The trucks were the reason for all this. The damned trucks.

Except they weren’t.

He could feel the Pet Sematary pulling at him—and something more. Its voice used to be like a tempting lullaby, a voice of possible comfort and a dreamy kind of power, but now it was deeper and more than ominous—it was threatening and harsh. Stay out of this, you.

But he wouldn’t stay out of it. His duty went back too far.

He saw that Louis’s Honda Civic was gone from the garage. Only the big Ford wagon was there, looking dusty and unused. He tried the back door of the house and found it open.

“Louis?” he called, knowing Louis wouldn’t answer, but needing to break through the heavy silence of the house somehow.
