Oh, getting old was starting to be a pain—his limbs felt heavy and awkward most of the time, his back was a misery after only two hours in the garden, and it felt like a screw was stuck in his left hip.

He began to go through the house carefully, looking for the signs he had to look for—world’s oldest housebreaker, he thought without much humor and kept looking. He didn’t find any of the things that would have seriously upset him: boxes of toys saved back from the Salvation Army, clothes for a small boy put aside behind a door or in the closet or under a bed… or worst of all, the crib set up again in Gage’s room. There were none of the signs at all, but the house still felt unpleasantly empty, as if it were waiting to be filled with… well, something.

Maybe I should take a little trip out to Pleasantview Cemetery. See if anything’s going on there. Might even run into Louis Creed. I could buy him dinner, or something.

But the danger wasn’t at Pleasantview Cemetery in Bangor; it was here, in this house, and beyond it.

Jud left again and crossed the road to his own house. He pulled a six-pack of beer out of the kitchen fridge and took it into the living room. He sat down in front of the bay window that looked out on the Creed house, opened a beer, and lit a cigarette. The afternoon faded around him, and as it did so often these last few years, he found his mind turning back and back in a widening circle. If he had known what Rachel Creed had been thinking earlier, he could have told her that what her psych teacher said might be true, but when you got older, that fading of memory broke down little by little, the same way that everything else in your body broke down, and you found yourself recalling places and faces and events with an eerie certainty. Old memories grew bright again, the colors becoming true, the voices losing that tinny echo of time and regaining their original sound. It wasn’t a failure of information at all, Jud could have told him. The name for it was old age.

In his mind Jud again saw Lester Morgan’s bull Hanratty, his eyes rimmed with red, charging at everything in sight, everything that moved. Charging at trees when the wind moved the leaves. Before Lester gave up and stopped it, every tree in Hanratty’s fenced field was gored with his senseless anger and his horns were splintered and his head was bleeding. When Lester put Hanratty down, Lester had been sick with fear—the way Jud himself was right now.

He drank beer and smoked. Daylight faded. He did not turn on the light. Gradually the tip of his cigarette became a small red dot in the darkness. He sat and drank beer and watched Louis Creed’s driveway. He believed that when Louis came home from wherever he was, he would go over and have a little talk with him. Make sure Louis wasn’t planning to do anything he shouldn’t.

And still he felt the soft pull of whatever it was, whatever sick power it was that lived in that devil's place, reaching down from its bluff of rotted stone where all those cairns had been built.

Stay out of this, you. Stay out of it or you’re going to be very, very sorry.

Ignoring it as best he could, Jud sat and smoked and drank beer. And waited.

Chapter 47

While Jud Crandall sat in the ladder-backed rocker, watching for him from his bay window, Louis ate a large, bland dinner in the Howard Johnson’s dining room.

The food was plentiful and boring—exactly what his body seemed to crave. Outside, it had gotten dark. The headlights of passing cars searched like fingers. He shoveled the food in. A steak. A baked potato. A side of bright green beans, a color nature never intended. A slice of apple pie with a scoop of ice cream melting on top. He ate at a corner table, watching people come and go, wondering if he might see someone he knew. In a way, he hoped that would happen. It would lead to questions—where’s Rachel, what are you doing here, how’s it going?—and maybe the questions would lead to trouble, and maybe trouble was what he really wanted. A way out.

And in fact, a couple he knew came in just as he finished his apple pie and his second cup of coffee. Rob Grinnell, a doctor from Bangor, and his pretty wife Barbara. He waited for them to see him, sitting alone in the corner, but the hostess led them to the booths on the far side of the room, and Louis lost sight of them except for an occasional glimpse of Grinnell’s graying hair.

The waitress brought Louis his check. He signed it, adding his room number under his signature, and left by the side door.

Outside, the wind had risen to near-gale force. It was a steady, droning sound, making the electrical wires hum strangely. He could see no stars but sensed clouds rushing past overhead at high speed. Louis stood on the walk for a moment, hands in pockets, face tilted into the wind. Then he turned back, went up to his room, and turned on the television. It was too early to do anything serious, and the night wind was too full of possibilities. It made him nervous.

He watched four hours of TV, eight half-hour comedy programs in a row. He realized it had been a long time since he had watched so much TV without stopping. He thought that all the female leads on the sitcoms were what he and his friends had called “teases” back in high school.

In Chicago, Dory Goldman was complaining, “Fly back? Honey, why do you want to fly back? You just got here!”

In Ludlow, Jud Crandall sat by his bay window, smoking and drinking beer, motionless, looking back at his own past and waiting for Louis to come home. Sooner or later Louis would come home, like Lassie in that old movie. There were other ways up to the Pet Sematary and the place beyond, but Louis didn’t know them. If he planned to do it, he would start from his own yard.

Unaware of these other events, like slow-moving bullets aimed not at where he was, but at where he would be, Louis sat and watched the HoJo color television. He had never seen any of these programs before, but he had heard about them: a black family, a white family, a little kid who was smarter than the rich adults he lived with, a woman who was single, a woman who was married, a woman who was divorced. He watched it all, sitting in the HoJo chair and glancing out now and then at the windy night.

When the eleven o’clock news ended, he turned off the TV and went to do what he'd decided, maybe when he saw Gage’s bloody baseball cap in the road. The cold feeling was back, stronger than before, but underneath was something else—a spark of eagerness, passion, or maybe desire. It didn't matter. It warmed him against the cold and kept him steady in the wind. As he started the Honda, he thought Jud might be right about that place getting stronger, because he felt it around him now, leading (or pushing) him, and he wondered:

Could I stop? Could I stop even if I wanted to?

Chapter 48

“You want to what?” Dory asked again. “Rachel… you’re upset… a night’s sleep…”

Rachel only shook her head. She couldn’t explain to her mother why she had to go back. The feeling had grown in her like a rising wind—a faint stirring in the grass, barely noticeable; then the air starts to move faster and harder, and there is no peace; then the gusts become strong enough to make eerie screaming sounds around the edges of the roof; then they are shaking the house, and you realize it’s like a hurricane, and if the wind gets much stronger, things will fall down.

It was six o’clock in Chicago. In Bangor, Louis was just sitting down to his big, bland meal. Rachel and Ellie had barely touched their dinners. Rachel kept looking up from her plate to see her daughter staring at her, asking what she was going to do about whatever trouble Daddy was in, asking her what she was going to do.

She waited for the phone to ring, for Jud to call and say Louis had come home, and once it did ring—she jumped, and Ellie almost spilled her milk—but it was just a lady from Dory’s bridge club, wanting to know if she had gotten home okay.

They were having their coffee when Rachel suddenly threw down her napkin and said, “Daddy… Mom… I’m sorry, but I have to go home. If I can get a flight, I’m going tonight.”

Her mother and father stared at her, but Ellie closed her eyes with a grown-up look of relief—it would have been funny if not for the pale, strained look of her skin.

They didn’t understand, and Rachel couldn’t explain any more than she could explain how those tiny puffs of wind, so weak they barely move the tips of grass, can slowly grow strong enough to knock down a steel building. She didn’t believe that Ellie had heard about Victor Pascow’s death on the news and stored it away in her mind.

“Rachel. Honey.” Her father spoke slowly, kindly, as if talking to someone in a temporary but dangerous fit. “This is just a reaction to your son’s death. You and Ellie are both reacting strongly to that, and who could blame you? But you’ll just collapse if you try to—”

Rachel didn’t answer him. She went to the phone in the hall, found AIRLINES in the Yellow Pages, and dialed Delta’s number while Dory stood nearby, telling her they should think about this, shouldn’t they, they should talk about it, maybe make a list… and behind her, Ellie stood, her face still dark—but now lit by enough hope to give Rachel some courage.

“Delta Airlines,” the voice on the other end said brightly. “This is Kim, may I help you?”

“I hope so,” Rachel said. “It’s really important that I get from Chicago to Bangor tonight. It’s… it’s kind of an emergency, I’m afraid. Can you check the flights for me?”

With doubt: “Yes, ma’am, but this is really last minute.”

“Well, please check,” Rachel said, her voice breaking a little. “I’ll take standby, anything.”

“All right, ma’am. Please hold.” The line went smoothly silent.

Rachel closed her eyes, and after a moment she felt a cool hand on her arm. She opened her eyes and saw that Ellie had moved next to her. Irwin and Dory stood together, talking quietly and looking at them. The way you look at people you think are crazy, Rachel thought tiredly. She forced a smile for Ellie.

“Don’t let them stop you, Mommy,” Ellie said in a low voice. “Please.”

“No way, big sister,” Rachel said and then winced—it was what they had called her ever since Gage was born. But she was no one’s big sister anymore, was she?

“Thank you,” Ellie said.

“It’s really important, isn’t it?”

Ellie nodded.

“Honey, I think so. But you could help me by telling me more. Is it just the dream?”

“No,” Ellie said. “It’s… it’s everything now. It’s all through me now. Can’t you feel it, Mommy? Something like a—”

“Something like a wind.”

Ellie sighed, a little shaky.

“But you don’t know what it is? You don’t remember anything else about your dream?”

Ellie thought hard and then shook her head, not wanting to. “Daddy. Church. And Gage. That’s all I remember. But I don’t remember how they fit together, Mommy!”

Rachel hugged her tightly. “It will be all right,” she said, but her heart still felt heavy.

“Hello, ma’am,” the clerk said.

“Hello?” Rachel held Ellie and the phone even tighter.

“I think I can get you to Bangor, ma’am—but you’ll be getting in very late.”

“That doesn’t matter,” Rachel said.

“Do you have a pen? It’s complicated.”

“Yes, right here,” Rachel said, taking a small pencil from the drawer. She found the back of an envelope to write on.

Rachel listened closely, writing everything down. When the airline clerk was done, Rachel smiled slightly and made an O with her thumb and forefinger to show Ellie it would work. Probably going to work, she thought. Some of the connections looked very, very close… especially in Boston.

“Please book it all,” Rachel said. “And thank you.”

Kim took Rachel’s name and credit card number. Rachel finally hung up, drained but relieved. She looked at her father. “Daddy, will you drive me to the airport?”

“Maybe I should say no,” Goldman said. “I think I might need to stop this craziness.”

“Don’t you dare!” Ellie cried loudly. “It’s not crazy! It’s not!”

Goldman blinked and stepped back at this small but fierce outburst.

“Take her, Irwin,” Dory said quietly into the silence that followed. “I’m starting to feel nervous too. I’ll feel better knowing Louis is okay.”

Goldman looked at his wife and finally turned to Rachel. “I’ll take you, if that’s what you want,” he said. “I… Rachel, I’ll come with you, if you want that.”

Rachel shook her head. “Thank you, Daddy, but I got all the last seats. It’s like God saved them for me.”

Irwin Goldman sighed. He looked very old at that moment, and Rachel suddenly realized that her father looked like Jud Crandall.

“You have time to pack a bag, if you want,” he said. “We can be at the airport in forty minutes, if I drive the way I used to when your mother and I were first married. Find her your tote bag, Dory.”

“Mommy,” Ellie said. Rachel turned to her. Ellie’s face was now shiny with light sweat.

“What, honey?”

“Be careful, Mommy,” Ellie said.

Chapter 49

The trees were just blurry shapes against the cloudy sky, lit from behind by the airport lights in the distance. Louis parked the Honda on Mason Street, which bordered Pleasantview to the south. The wind there was so strong it almost pulled the car door out of his hand. He had to force it shut. The wind tugged at his jacket as he opened the Honda’s trunk and took out the tarp-covered bundle of tools.

He stood in a dark space between two streetlights, holding the canvas-wrapped bundle, carefully checking for cars before crossing to the wrought-iron fence that marked the graveyard's edge. He didn't want to be seen at all if he could avoid it, not even by someone who would forget him instantly. Next to him, the branches of an old elm creaked in the wind, making Louis think of lynchings. God, he was so scared. This wasn't just risky; it was insane.

No cars. On Mason Street, the streetlights stretched out in perfect white circles, lighting up the sidewalk where kids would ride bikes, jump rope, and play hopscotch after school, barely noticing the graveyard, except maybe at Halloween when it seemed a little spooky. Maybe they would even dare to cross the street and hang a paper skeleton on the fence, giggling at the old jokes: It’s the most popular place in town; people are dying to get in. Why is it wrong to laugh in the graveyard? Because everyone there is always in a grave mood.

“Gage,” he muttered. Gage was in there, behind that fence, unfairly trapped under the dark earth, and that was no joke. Gonna get you out, Gage, he thought. Gonna get you out, big guy, or die trying.

Louis crossed the street with the heavy bundle, stepped onto the curb, glanced both ways again, and tossed the canvas roll over the fence. It clinked softly when it landed. He dusted his hands and walked away, having memorized the spot. Even if he forgot, he could just follow the fence until he was across from his Civic, and he would find it.

But would the gate be open this late?

He walked down Mason Street to the stop sign, the wind pushing him. Moving shadows danced on the road.

He turned onto Pleasant Street, still following the fence. Car headlights flashed, and Louis stepped behind an elm tree. It wasn't a police car, he saw, just a van heading toward Hammond Street and probably the turnpike. Once it passed, Louis continued walking.

Of course it will be unlocked. It’s got to be.

He reached the gate, which looked like a cathedral made of iron, thin and elegant in the shifting shadows from the streetlights. He reached out and tried it.

Locked.

You idiot, of course it’s locked—did you really think someone would leave a cemetery unlocked in an American city after eleven at night? No one trusts that much, buddy, not anymore. So what do you do now?

Now he would have to climb and just hope no one looked away from the Carson Show long enough to see him climbing the iron fence like the world’s oldest, slowest kid.

Hey, police? I just saw the world’s oldest, slowest kid climbing into Pleasantview Cemetery. Looked like he was dying to get in. Yeah, looked like a serious matter to me. Kidding? Oh no, I’m totally serious. Maybe you ought to look into it.

Louis continued up Pleasant Street and turned right at the next corner. The tall iron fence went on and on beside him. The wind cooled and dried the sweat on his forehead and temples. His shadow grew and shrank in the streetlights. Every now and then he glanced at the fence, and then he stopped and made himself really look at it.

You’re going to climb that thing? Don’t make me laugh.

Louis Creed was a pretty tall man, a little over six-two, but the fence was easily nine feet high, each iron bar ending in a fancy, arrow-like point. Fancy, that is, until you slipped while swinging your leg over and the force of your falling two hundred pounds drove one of those arrow points into your groin, destroying your testicles. And there you would be, stuck like a pig at a barbecue, yelling until someone called the police and they came and pulled you off and took you to the hospital.

The sweat kept coming, making his shirt stick to his back. Everything was quiet except for the faint sound of late traffic on Hammond Street.

There had to be a way to get in there.

Had to be.

Come on, Louis, face the facts. You may be crazy, but you’re not that crazy. Maybe you could climb to the top of that fence, but it would take a trained gymnast to swing over those points without getting stuck. And even if you can get in, how are you going to get yourself and Gage’s body out?

He went on walking, vaguely aware that he was circling the cemetery but not doing anything useful.

All right, here’s the answer. I’ll just go home to Ludlow tonight and come back tomorrow, in the late afternoon… I’ll go in through the gate around four o’clock and find a place to hide until it’s midnight or a little later. I will, in other words, put off until tomorrow what I should have been smart enough to think of today.

Good idea, O Great Swami Louis… and in the meantime, what do I do about that big pile of stuff 1 threw over the wall? Pick, shovel, flashlight… you might as well stamp GRAVE-ROBBING EQUIPMENT on every damn piece of it.

It landed in the bushes. Who’s going to find it, for Chrissake?

On the surface that made sense. But this was no sensible thing he was doing, and his heart told him quietly and surely that he couldn’t come back tomorrow. If he didn’t do it tonight, he would never do it. He would never be able to get himself this worked up again. This was the moment, the only time he was ever going to have.

There were fewer houses up this way—an occasional square of yellow light shone on the other side of the street, and once he saw the gray-blue flicker of a TV—and looking through the fence he saw that the graves were older here, more rounded, sometimes leaning forward or backward from the freezes and thaws of many years. There was another stop sign up ahead, and another right turn would put him on a street roughly parallel to Mason Street, where he had begun. And when he got back to the beginning, what did he do? Collect two hundred dollars and go around again? Admit defeat?

Car headlights turned down the street. Louis stepped behind another tree, waiting for it to pass. This car was moving very slowly, and after a moment a white spotlight shone out from the passenger side and ran flickering along the wrought-iron fence. His heart squeezed painfully in his chest. It was a police car, checking the cemetery.

He pressed himself tight against the tree, the rough bark against his cheek, hoping desperately that it was big enough to hide him. The spotlight ran toward him. Louis put his head down, trying to hide the white blur of his face. The spotlight reached the tree, disappeared for a moment, and then reappeared on Louis’s right. He slipped around the tree a little. He had a momentary glimpse of the dark bubbles on the cruiser’s roof. He waited for the taillights to glow brighter red, for the doors to open, for the spotlight to suddenly turn back on its ball joint, hunting for him like a big white finger. Hey, you! You behind that tree! Come on out where we can see you, and we want to see both hands empty! Come out NOW!

The police car kept on going. It reached the corner, signaled politely, and turned left. Louis slumped back against the tree, breathing fast, his mouth sour and dry. He figured they would drive past his parked Honda, but that didn’t really matter. Parking from 6 P.M. to 7 A.M. was allowed on Mason Street. There were many other cars parked there. Their owners lived in the apartment buildings across the street.

Louis glanced up at the tree he was hiding behind.

Just above his head, the tree split. He thought he could—

Without thinking more about it, he reached into the split and pulled himself up, scrambling with his tennis shoes for grip, sending a little shower of bark down to the sidewalk. He got a knee up and a moment later had one foot planted firmly in the fork of the elm. If the police car came back, their spotlight would find a very strange bird in this tree. He needed to move quickly.

He pulled himself up onto a higher branch, one that hung over the very top of the fence. He felt ridiculously like the twelve-year-old he guessed he had once been. The tree was not still; it rocked easily, almost comfortably, in the steady wind. Its leaves rustled and whispered. Louis looked at the situation and then, before he could lose his nerve, he dropped off into space, holding on to the branch with his hands clasped together over it. The branch was a little thicker than a strong man’s forearm. With his sneakers dangling about eight feet over the sidewalk, he pulled himself hand over hand toward the fence. The branch dipped but didn’t seem like it would break. He was vaguely aware of his shadow following along on the sidewalk below, a shapeless black ape-shape. The wind chilled his sweaty armpits, and he shivered despite the sweat running down his face and neck. The branch dipped and swayed with his movements. The farther out he moved, the more the branch dipped. His hands and wrists were getting tired, and he was afraid his sweaty palms might slip.

He reached the fence. His tennis shoes dangled about a foot below the arrow tips. The tips didn’t look blunt at all from this angle. They looked very sharp. Sharp or not, he realized it wasn’t just his balls that were in danger here. If he fell and landed on one of those things, his weight would drive it up into his lungs. The returning cops would find a gruesome early Halloween decoration on the Pleasantview fence.

Breathing fast, almost gasping, he groped for the fence points with his feet, needing a moment’s rest. For a moment he hung there, his feet moving in the air, searching but not finding.

Light touched him and grew.

Oh Christ, that’s a car, a car is coming—!

He tried to move his hands forward, but his palms slipped. His clasped fingers were coming apart.

Still struggling to hold on, he turned his head to the left, looking under his straining arm. It was a car, but it sped through the intersection up the street without slowing. Lucky. If it had—

His hands slipped again. He felt bark fall onto his hair.

One foot found a hold, but now his other pants leg had caught on one of the arrow points. And Christ, he couldn’t hang on much longer. Desperately, Louis jerked his leg. The branch dipped. His hands slipped again. There was a sound of tearing cloth, and then he was standing on two of the arrow points. They dug into the soles of his tennis shoes, and the pressure quickly became painful, but Louis stood on them anyway. The relief in his hands and arms was greater than the pain in his feet.

What a sight I must be, Louis thought with faint and sad amusement. Holding the branch with his left hand, he wiped his right hand across his jacket. Then he wiped off the left while he held with the right.

He stood on the points for a moment longer and then moved his hands forward along the branch. It was slim enough for him to lace his fingers together comfortably now. He swung forward like Tarzan, feet leaving the arrow points. The branch dipped sharply, and he heard a worrying cracking sound. He let go, dropping blindly.

He landed badly. One knee hit against a gravestone, sending a sharp pain up his thigh. He rolled over on the grass, holding the knee, lips pulled back in a sort of grin, hoping that he hadn’t broken his kneecap. At last the pain began to fade a little, and he found that he could bend the joint. It would be all right if he kept moving and didn’t let it stiffen up on him. Maybe.

He got to his feet and started to walk along the fence back toward Mason Street and his equipment. His knee was bad at first, and he limped, but the pain smoothed out to a dull ache as he went. There was aspirin in the Honda’s first-aid kit. He should have remembered to bring that with him. Too late now. He kept an eye out for cars and moved back deeper into the cemetery when one came.

On the Mason Street side, which was likely to be busier, he kept well back from the fence until he was opposite the Civic. He was about to trot down to the fence and pull his bundle out of the bushes when he heard footsteps on the sidewalk and a woman’s soft laughter. He sat down behind a large grave marker—it hurt his knee too much to squat—and watched a couple walk up the far side of Mason Street. They were walking with their arms around each other’s waists, and something about their movement from one white patch of light to the next made Louis think of some old TV show. In a moment he had it: “The Jimmy Durante Hour.” What would they do if he rose up now, a shaky shadow in this silent city of the dead, and cried out to them: “Goodnight, Mrs. Calabash, wherever you are!”

They stopped in the patch of light just beyond his car and embraced. Watching them, Louis felt a kind of sick wonder and self-hatred. Here he was, crouched behind a tombstone like a monster in some cheap comic-book story, watching lovers. Is the line so thin, then? he wondered, and that thought also felt familiar. So thin you can simply cross it with this little fuss, mess, and bother? Climb a tree, crawl along a branch, drop into a graveyard, watch lovers…dig holes? That simple? Is it madness? I spent eight years becoming a doctor, but I’ve become a grave robber in one simple step—what I suppose people would call a ghoul.

He pressed his fists against his mouth to stop himself from making a sound and looked for that inner coldness, that sense of being cut off. It was there, and Louis drew it gratefully around him.

When the couple finally walked on, Louis watched them with simple impatience. They climbed the steps of one of the apartment buildings. The man fumbled for a key, and a moment later they were inside. The street was silent again except for the constant beat of the wind, rustling the trees and tumbling his sweaty hair over his forehead.

Louis ran down to the fence, bent low, and felt through the brush for his canvas bundle. Here it was, rough under his fingers. He picked it up, listening to the muffled clank from inside. He carried it over to the broad graveled drive that led in through the gates and paused to get his bearings. Straight up here, go left at the fork. No problem.

He walked along the edge of the drive, wanting to stay in the shadow of the elms in case there was a full-time caretaker and he happened to be out.

He bore left at the fork, approaching Gage’s grave now, and suddenly, terribly, realized he could not remember what his son had looked like. He paused, staring off into the rows of graves, the frowning fronts of the monuments, and tried to picture him. He remembered individual features—his blond hair, still so fine and light, his slanting eyes, his small, white teeth, the little twist of scar on his chin from when he had fallen down the back steps of their place in Chicago. He could see these things but could not put them together. He saw Gage running toward the road, running toward his death by the Orinco truck, but Gage’s face was turned away. He tried to picture Gage as he had been in his crib on the night of the kite-flying day and could see only darkness in his mind.

Gage, where are you?

Have you ever thought, Louis, that you may be doing your son a disservice? Perhaps he’s happy where he is… maybe all of that isn’t the nonsense you always thought it was. Maybe he’s with the angels or maybe he’s just sleeping. And if he’s sleeping, do you really know what you might wake up?

Oh Gage, where are you? I want you home with us.

But was he really in control of himself? Why couldn’t he picture Gage’s face, and why was he ignoring everyone’s warning—Jud’s, the dream of Pascow, the fear in his own troubled heart?

He thought of the grave markers in the Pet Sematary, those crude circles, spiraling down into the unknown, and then the coldness came over him again. Why was he standing here, trying to picture Gage’s face anyway?

He would be seeing it soon enough.

The headstone was there now; it read simply GAGE WILLIAM CREED, followed by the two dates. Someone had come today to pay respects, he saw; there were fresh flowers. Who could it have been? Missy Dandridge?

His heart beat heavily but slowly in his chest. This was it then; if he was going to do it, he better start. There was only so much night left, and then the day would come.

Louis looked into his heart one last time and saw that yes, he meant to go through with this. He nodded almost without moving and reached for his pocketknife. He had tied his bundle with Scotch tape, and now he cut it. He unrolled the tarp at the foot of Gage’s grave like a bedroll and then arranged things exactly as he would arrange tools to stitch a cut or do a small office procedure.

Here was the flashlight with its lens covered in felt as the store clerk had said. The felt was also held with tape. He had made a small circle in the middle by putting a penny on the felt and cutting around it with a scalpel. Here was the short pick, which he shouldn’t need—he had brought it just in case. He wouldn't have a sealed lid to deal with, and he shouldn’t find any rocks in a new grave. Here was the shovel, the spade, the rope, the work gloves. He put the gloves on, grabbed the spade, and started.

The ground was soft, the digging easy. The grave’s shape was clear, the dirt he was throwing out softer than the earth at the edge. His mind automatically compared how easy this was to the rocky, hard ground where, if everything worked out, he would be reburying his son later tonight. Up there, he would need the pick. Then he tried to stop thinking altogether. It only got in the way.

He threw the dirt on the ground to the left of the grave, working at a steady pace that became harder to keep up as the hole got deeper. He stepped into the grave, smelling the damp smell of fresh dirt, a smell he remembered from summers with Uncle Carl.

Digger, he thought and stopped to wipe sweat from his face. Uncle Carl had told him that was the nickname for every graveyard worker in America. Their friends called them Digger.

He started again.

He stopped only once more, to check his watch. It was twenty after twelve. He felt time slipping away like something greasy.

Forty minutes later, the spade scraped across something, and Louis bit his lip hard enough to draw blood. He got the flashlight and shined it down. Here was more dirt, and across it, a grayish-silver line. It was the top of the grave liner. Louis got most of the dirt off, but he didn’t want to make too much noise, and nothing was louder than a shovel scraping concrete in the middle of the night.

He climbed out of the grave and got the rope. He put it through the iron rings on one half of the grave-liner top. He got out of the grave again, spread out the tarp, lay down on it, and grabbed the ends of the rope.

Louis, I think this is it. Your last chance.

You’re right. It’s my last chance and I’m taking it.

He wrapped the ends of the rope around his hands and pulled. The square of concrete came up easily, scraping on the turning end. It stood upright over a square of blackness, now a vertical tombstone instead of a horizontal grave cover.

Louis pulled the rope out of the rings and tossed it aside. He wouldn’t need it for the other half; he could stand on the sides of the grave liner and pull it up.

He got down into the grave again, moving carefully, not wanting to knock over the cement slab he had already pulled up and crush his toes or break the thing, which was quite thin. Pebbles rattled down into the hole, and he heard some of them hit Gage’s coffin.

Bending, he grabbed the other half of the grave-liner top and pulled upward. As he did so, he felt something squish coldly under his fingers. When he had this second half of the top standing on end, he looked down at his hand and saw a fat earthworm wriggling weakly there. With a choked cry of disgust, Louis wiped it off on the dirt wall of his son’s grave.

Then he shone his flashlight downward.

Here was the coffin he had last seen resting on chrome runners over the grave at the funeral service, surrounded by that awful green Astroturf. This was the safety-deposit box in which he was supposed to bury all his hopes for his son. Fury, clean and white hot, the opposite of his former coldness, rose up in him. Idiotic! The answer was no!

Louis groped for the spade and found it. He raised it over his shoulder and brought it down on the coffin’s latch once, twice, a third time, a fourth. His lips were drawn back in a furious grimace.

Going to get you out, Gage, you just wait and see!

The latch had splintered on the first hit, and he probably didn't need to hit it anymore, but he kept going, not just wanting to open the coffin, but to damage it. He finally regained some sense and stopped with the shovel raised for another hit.

The blade was bent and scratched. He threw it aside and climbed out of the grave on legs that felt weak and like rubber. He felt nauseous, and the anger had disappeared as fast as it had arrived. The coldness returned in its place, and he had never felt so alone and cut off; he felt like an astronaut who had floated away from their ship during a spacewalk and was now drifting in a vast darkness, breathing on borrowed time. Did Bill Baterman feel like this? he wondered.

He lay on the ground, on his back this time, waiting to see if he was calm and ready to go on. When the rubbery feeling had left his legs, he sat up and slipped back down into the grave. He shone the flashlight on the latch and saw it wasn't just broken, but destroyed. He had swung the shovel in a blind rage, but every hit had landed right there, bull’s eye, as if guided. The wood around it had splintered.

Louis slipped the flashlight under his arm. He squatted down a little. His hands reached out, like the hands of a catcher in a circus act, waiting to do his part in a dangerous catch.

He found the groove in the lid and slipped his fingers into it. He paused for a moment—you couldn't really call it a hesitation—and then he opened his son’s coffin.

Chapter 50

Rachel Creed almost caught her flight from Boston to Portland. Almost. Her Chicago plane left on time (a miracle), was cleared quickly into LaGuardia (another), and left New York only five minutes late. It arrived at the gate in Boston fifteen minutes late—at 11:12 P.M. That left her with thirteen minutes.

She still might have caught her connecting flight, but the shuttle bus that goes around the Logan terminals was late. Rachel waited, now in a constant state of worry, shifting from foot to foot as if she needed to use the restroom, switching the travel bag her mother had loaned her from one shoulder to the other.

When the shuttle still hadn’t come at 11:25, she began to run. Her heels were low but still high enough to cause her trouble. One of her ankles twisted painfully, and she stopped long enough to take off the shoes. Then she ran on in her pantyhose, past Allegheny and Eastern Airlines, breathing hard, starting to get a pain in her side.

Her breath was hot in her throat, that pain in her side sharper. Now she was running past the international terminal, and there, up ahead, was Delta’s triangular sign. She rushed in through the doors, almost dropped one shoe, caught it. It was 11:37.

One of the two clerks on duty looked up at her.

“Flight 104,” she gasped. “The Portland flight. Has it left?”

The clerk glanced behind him at the monitor. “Still at the gate, it says here,” he said, “but they announced final boarding five minutes ago. I’ll call ahead. Any bags to check?”

“No,” Rachel gasped, and brushed her sweaty hair out of her eyes. Her heart was racing in her chest.

“Then don’t wait for me to call. I will—but I suggest you run very fast.”

Rachel didn’t run very fast—she couldn’t anymore. But she tried her best. The escalator was off for the night, and she hurried up the stairs, tasting a metallic tang in her mouth. She reached security and almost threw the tote bag at the surprised guard, then waited for it to come through, her hands clenching and unclenching. She grabbed it as soon as it came out of the X-ray machine and ran again, the bag flying behind her and hitting her hip.

She looked up at one of the monitors as she ran.

FLIGHT 104 PORTLAND SCHED 11:25P GATE 31 BOARDING.

Gate 31 was at the far end of the concourse—and as she glanced at the monitor, BOARDING changed to DEPARTING, blinking fast.

A frustrated cry escaped her. She ran into the gate area just as the gate attendant removed the sign that read: FLIGHT 104 BOSTON—PORTLAND 11:25.

“It’s gone?” she asked, shocked. “It’s really gone?”

The attendant looked at her kindly. “It left the gate at 11:40. I’m sorry, ma’am. You tried hard, if that helps.” He pointed to the large windows. Rachel saw a big 727 with Delta markings, its lights shining brightly, beginning to take off.

“Christ, didn’t anyone tell you I was coming?” Rachel cried.

“When they called from downstairs, 104 was already moving. If I’d called it back, it would have been stuck with all the other planes going to Runway 30, and that pilot would have been furious. And there are a hundred or so passengers on board. I’m very sorry. If you’d been just four minutes sooner—”

She walked away, not hearing the rest. She was halfway back to security when she felt dizzy. She stumbled into another gate area and sat down until the feeling passed. Then she put her shoes back on, picking a flattened cigarette butt off her stocking first. My feet are dirty and I don’t care, she thought sadly.

She walked back toward the terminal.

The security guard looked at her kindly. “Missed it?”

“I missed it, all right,” Rachel said.

“Where were you going?”

“Portland. Then Bangor.”

“Well, why don’t you rent a car? If you really need to be there, that is? Usually, I’d say stay at a hotel near the airport, but if I ever saw someone who looked like they really needed to be there, it’s you.”

“I’m that lady, all right,” Rachel said. She thought about it. “Yes, I guess I could do that, couldn’t I? If any of the rental places has a car.”

The security guard laughed. “Oh, they’ll have cars. The only time they don’t have cars at Logan is when the airport’s fogged in. Which is often.”

Rachel barely heard her. She was already trying to figure it out in her head.

She couldn’t get to Portland in time to catch her Bangor flight even if she drove up the highway as fast as she could. So, think about driving straight through. How long? That depended on the distance. Two hundred and fifty miles, that's what she remembered. Something Jud had said maybe. It would be at least 12:15 before she left, probably closer to 12:30 A.M. It was all highway. She thought she had a good chance of driving the whole way at sixty-five without getting pulled over for speeding. She quickly did the math in her head, dividing sixty-five into two hundred and fifty. Almost four hours. Well… call it four even. She would have to stop once to use the bathroom. And even though sleep seemed impossible right now, she knew herself well enough to know she would also have to stop for a large black coffee. Still, she could be back in Ludlow before dawn.

Thinking about all this, she headed for the stairs—the car rental places were one floor below the main areas.

“Good luck, honey,” the security guard said. “Be safe.”

“Thanks,” Rachel said. She felt like she needed some good luck.

Chapter 51

The smell hit him first, and Louis flinched back, gagging. He held onto the edge of the grave, breathing hard, and just when he thought he could control his stomach, his entire big, tasteless meal came up quickly. He threw up on the far side of the grave and then put his head on the ground, panting. Finally, the nausea passed. Teeth clenched, he took the flashlight out of his armpit and shone it down into the open coffin.

A deep horror that felt almost like awe came over him—the kind of feeling usually saved for the worst nightmares, the ones you can barely recall when you wake up.

Gage’s head was gone.

Louis’s hands were shaking so badly he had to hold the flashlight with both hands, gripping it like a policeman learns to grip his gun at the shooting range. Still, the beam jumped back and forth, and it took a moment before he could aim the narrow beam back into the grave.

It’s impossible, he told himself, just remember that what you thought you saw is impossible.

He slowly moved the narrow beam up Gage’s three-foot length, from the new shoes to the suit pants, the little coat (ah, Christ, no two-year-old should ever wear a suit), to the open collar, to—

His breath caught in a harsh sound that was too angry to be a gasp, and all his fury at Gage’s death came back in a rush, drowning fears of the supernatural, the unnatural, his growing certainty that he had gone into the land of the insane.

Louis fumbled in his back pocket for his handkerchief and pulled it out. Holding the light in one hand, he leaned into the grave again, almost losing his balance. If one of the pieces of grave liner had fallen now, it would have surely broken his neck. Gently he used his handkerchief to wipe away the damp moss that was growing on Gage’s skin—moss so dark that he had briefly thought Gage’s whole head was gone.

The moss was damp but just a thin layer. He should have expected it; there had been rain, and a grave liner was not waterproof. Shining his light to either side, Louis saw that the coffin was lying in a thin puddle. Beneath the light slime of growth, he saw his son. The mortician, knowing that the coffin could not be opened after such a terrible accident, had still done the best he could—morticians almost always did. Looking at his son was like looking at a badly made doll. Gage’s head bulged oddly. His eyes had sunken deep behind closed lids. Something white stuck out from his mouth like an albino tongue, and Louis thought at first that they had perhaps used too much embalming fluid. It was tricky to use, and with a child it was almost impossible to tell how much was enough… or too much.

Then he realized it was only cotton. He reached in and plucked it out of the boy’s mouth. Gage’s lips, oddly relaxed and seeming somehow too dark and too wide, closed with a faint but audible plip! He threw the cotton into the grave where it floated in the shallow puddle and gleamed a disgusting white. Now one of Gage’s cheeks had a hollow, old-man’s look.

“Gage,” he whispered, “going to take you out now, okay?”

He prayed no one would come along now, a caretaker making a 12:30 walk through the cemetery, something like that. But it was no longer a matter of not being caught; if someone else’s flashlight beam caught him as he stood here in the grave doing his awful work, he would grab the bent, scarred spade and smash it into the intruder’s head.

He worked his arms under Gage. The body flopped around loosely, and a sudden, awful certainty came over him: when he lifted Gage, Gage’s body would break apart and he would be left with the pieces. He would be left standing with his feet on the sides of the grave liner with the pieces, screaming. And that was how they would find him.

Go on, you chicken, go on and do it!

He got Gage under the arms, aware of the foul dampness, and lifted him that way, as he had lifted him so often from his evening bath. Gage’s head lolled all the way to the middle of his back. Louis saw the grinning circle of stitches which held Gage’s head onto his shoulders.

Somehow, panting, his stomach cramping from the smell and from the boneless, loose feel of his son’s horribly smashed body, Louis wrestled the body out of the coffin. At last he sat on the edge of the grave with the body in his lap, his feet dangling in the hole, his face a horrible pale color, his eyes black holes, his mouth drawn down in a trembling bow of horror and pity and sorrow.

“Gage,” he said and began to rock the boy in his arms. Gage’s hair lay against Louis’s wrist, as lifeless as wire. “Gage, It will be all right, I swear, Gage, it will be all right, this will end, this is just the night, please, Gage, I love you, Daddy loves you.”

Louis rocked his son.

By quarter of two, Louis was ready to leave the cemetery. Actually handling the body had been the worst of it—that was the point at which that inner traveler, his mind, seemed to float the farthest into the emptiness. And yet now, resting, his back a throbbing hurt in which exhausted muscles jumped and twitched, he felt it might be possible to get back. All the way back.

He put Gage’s body on the tarp and wrapped it up. He secured it with long strips of tape, then cut the rope in two and tied the ends neatly. It could have been a rolled-up rug, nothing more. He closed the coffin, then after a moment, reopened it and put the bent spade in. Let Pleasantview keep that old thing; it wouldn’t have his son. He closed the coffin and lowered half of the cement grave cover. He thought about just dropping the other half but worried it would break. After a moment, he threaded his belt through the iron rings and used it to gently lower the cement square into place. Then he used the shovel to fill the hole. There wasn’t enough dirt to fill it completely. The grave might look sunken. Maybe not. Maybe someone would notice and not care. He wouldn’t think about it or worry tonight—he still had too much to do. More crazy work. And he was very tired.

Hey-ho, let’s go.

“Indeed,” Louis muttered.

The wind picked up, howling through the trees and making him look around. He put the shovel, the unused pick, the gloves, and the flashlight beside the bundle. He wanted to use the light but didn’t. Leaving the body and tools, Louis walked back to the high iron fence, arriving about five minutes later. Across the street was his Civic, parked neatly. So close, yet so far.

Louis looked at it for a moment, then went in a different direction.

He moved away from the gate, walking along the fence until it turned away from Mason Street at a right angle. There was a drainage ditch, and Louis looked in. What he saw made him shudder. There were piles of rotting flowers, layer upon layer, washed down by rain and snow.

Christ.

No, not Christ. These offerings were for a much older God. People have called Him different things, but Rachel’s sister named Him well: Oz the Great and Terrible, God of dead things in the ground, God of rotting flowers in ditches, God of the Mystery.

Louis stared into the ditch as if hypnotized. Finally, he looked away with a gasp—like someone waking up or being called from a trance at the count of ten.

He went on. He hadn’t walked far before finding what he sought, a piece of information his mind had stored on the day of Gage’s burial.

Here, in the windy dark, was the cemetery's crypt.

Coffins were kept there in the winter when it was too cold to dig in the frozen ground. It was also used when there were many deaths at once.

Louis knew that these times of many deaths happened now and then. In any group of people, there were times when many died for no clear reason.

"It all evens out," Uncle Carl told him. "If nobody dies for two weeks in May, Lou, I can count on ten funerals for two weeks in November. But it's rarely November, and never around Christmas, even though people think that's when many die. That stuff about Christmas depression is nonsense. Just ask any funeral director. Most people are happy around Christmas and want to live. So they do live. We usually get a big rush in February. The flu gets the old people, and there's pneumonia, of course—but that's not all. There are people who fight cancer hard for a year or more. Then February comes, and they seem to get tired, and the cancer just finishes them off. On January 31, they're getting better and feel great. By February 24, they're buried. People have heart attacks, strokes, and kidney failure in February. It's a bad month. People get tired in February. We're used to it in this business. But then, for no reason, the same thing will happen in June or October. Never in August. August is slow. Unless a gas line explodes or a bus goes off a bridge, you never fill up the crypt in August. But there have been Februarys when we've had coffins stacked three high, hoping for a thaw so we can bury some before we have to rent an apartment."

Uncle Carl had laughed. And Louis, feeling like he knew something even doctors didn't, had laughed too.

The crypt's double doors were set into a grassy hill, a shape as natural and pleasing as a woman's breast. This hill (which Louis thought was man-made) reached just below the decorative arrow tips of the iron fence, which stayed level instead of following the hill's curve.

Louis looked around, then climbed up the hill. On the other side was an empty square of ground, maybe two acres. No… not quite empty. There was a single shed, separate from everything else. Probably belongs to the cemetery, Louis thought. That's where they keep their equipment.

The streetlights shone through the leaves of trees—old elms and maples—that hid this area from Mason Street. Louis saw no other movement.

He slid back down, afraid of falling and hurting his knee again, and went back to his son's grave. He almost tripped over the tarp. He saw he would have to make two trips, one with the body and another for the tools. He bent over, wincing at the pain in his back, and picked up the stiff tarp. He could feel Gage's body inside and tried to ignore the thought that he had gone crazy.

He carried the body to the hill with the crypt, which had two steel sliding doors (making it look like a two-car garage). He saw what he had to do to get the forty-pound bundle up the steep hill now that his rope was gone and got ready. He backed up and ran at the hill, leaning forward, letting his momentum carry him. He got almost to the top before his feet slipped on the short, slick grass, and he threw the tarp as far as he could as he fell. It landed almost at the top of the hill. He climbed the rest of the way up, looked around again, saw no one, and laid the rolled-up tarp against the fence. Then he went back for the rest of his things.

He gained the top of the hill again, put the gloves on, and piled the flashlight, pick, and shovel next to the tarp. Then he rested, back against the fence, hands on his knees. His new digital watch from Rachel told him it was 2:01.

He gave himself five minutes to rest and then tossed the shovel over the fence. He heard it hit the grass. He tried to stuff the flashlight into his pants, but it wouldn’t fit. He slipped it through the fence and listened to it roll down the hill, hoping it wouldn’t break. He wished he had a backpack.

He took his roll of tape from his jacket pocket and taped the pick to the canvas, going around and around, pulling the tape tight. He did this until the tape was gone and then put the empty roll back in his pocket. He lifted the bundle and threw it over the fence (his back hurt; he’d pay for it all week, he thought) and then let it drop, wincing at the soft thud.

Now he swung one leg over the fence, grabbed two of the points, and swung his other leg over. He skidded down, digging his shoes into the earth between the fence, and dropped to the ground.

He walked down the hill and felt through the grass. He found the shovel right away—the streetlights made the blade gleam. He couldn’t find the flashlight for a moment—how far could it have rolled? He got on his hands and knees and felt through the thick grass, his breath and heartbeat loud.

At last he spotted it, a black shadow a few feet away—like the hill hiding the cemetery, its shape gave it away. He grabbed it, covered the lens, and pushed the switch. His palm lit up briefly, and he switched the flashlight off. It was okay.

He used his pocketknife to cut the pick free and took the tools to the trees. He stood behind the biggest, looking both ways along Mason Street. It was deserted. He saw only one light on—a yellow square—in an upstairs room. Someone awake, perhaps.

Moving quickly, Louis stepped onto the sidewalk. After the darkness of the cemetery, he felt exposed under the streetlights; here he stood, near the cemetery, with a pick, shovel, and flashlight. If someone saw him, it would be obvious.

He crossed the street rapidly, heels clicking. There was his Civic, a short distance away. To Louis, it looked like miles. Sweating, he walked toward it, listening for a car, footsteps, or a window opening.

He got to his Honda, leaned the pick and shovel against it, and looked for his keys. They weren’t there, not in either pocket. He began to sweat. His heart raced, and he clenched his teeth against the panic.

He probably lost them when he fell from the tree, hit the grave marker with his knee, and rolled over. His keys were somewhere in the grass, and if he struggled to find his flashlight, how could he find his keys? It was over. One bit of bad luck and it was over.

Now wait, hold on. Check your pockets again. Your change is there—and if your change didn’t fall out, your keys didn’t either.

He checked his pockets again, slowly, taking out the change, even turning his pockets inside out.

No keys.

Louis leaned against the car, wondering what to do. He would have to climb back in, he guessed. Leave his son where he was, take the flashlight, climb back in, and spend the night searching for—

Suddenly, an idea struck him.

He bent down and looked into the Civic. His keys were dangling from the ignition.

He grunted softly, then ran to the driver’s side, yanked the door open, and grabbed the keys. He suddenly heard the firm voice of Karl Malden, the stern father figure with the potato nose and old-fashioned hat: Lock your car. Take your keys. Don’t let a good boy go bad.

He went to the back of the Civic and opened the hatchback. He put in the pick, shovel, and flashlight, then slammed it shut. He had walked twenty or thirty feet when he remembered his keys. This time, he had left them dangling from the hatchback lock.

Stupid! he cursed himself. If you’re going to be this stupid, just forget the whole thing!

He went back and got his keys.

He had Gage in his arms and was almost back to Mason Street when a dog started barking somewhere. No—it didn’t just start barking. It started to howl, its rough voice filling the street. Auggggh-ROOOO! Auggggh-ROOOOOO!

He stood behind one of the trees, wondering what could happen next, wondering what to do. He stood there expecting lights to start turning on all along the street.

But only one light did turn on, at the side of a house just across from where Louis stood in the shadows. A moment later a rough voice yelled, “Shut up, Fred!”

Auggggh-ROOOOOO! Fred responded.

“Shut him up, Scanlon, or I’m calling the police!” someone yelled from the side of the street Louis was on, making him jump, making him realize how wrong he was to think the street was empty. There were people all around him, hundreds of eyes, and that dog was ruining sleep, his only friend. Goddamn you, Fred, he thought. Oh, goddamn you.

Fred began another howl; he got well into the Auggggh, but before he could get past the start of a good ROOOOOO, there was a hard hit followed by soft whimpers and yips.

Silence followed by the faint slam of a door. The light at the side of Fred’s house stayed on for a moment, then clicked off.

Louis felt like he should stay in the shadows, to wait; it would be better to wait until the noise had died down. But time was passing.

He crossed the street with his bundle and walked back to the Civic, seeing no one. Fred was quiet. He held his bundle in one hand, got his keys, opened the hatchback.

Gage would not fit.

Louis tried the bundle up and down, side to side, then at an angle. The Civic’s back was too small. He could have bent and crammed the bundle in—Gage wouldn’t have minded—but Louis just couldn’t do it.

Come on, come on, come on, let’s get out of here, let’s not go too far.

But he stood, confused, out of ideas, holding the bundle with his son’s body. Then he heard a car coming, and without thinking, he took the bundle to the passenger side, opened the door, and put the bundle on the seat.

He shut the door, ran to the back of the Civic, and slammed the hatchback. A car sped through the intersection, and Louis heard loud, drunk voices. He got in the car, started it, and was about to turn on the headlights when a terrible thought hit him. What if Gage was facing the wrong way, sitting there with his knees and hips bent backward, his empty eyes looking out the back window instead of forward?

It doesn’t matter, his mind screamed, with a fury caused by exhaustion. Can you understand that? It just doesn’t matter!

But it does. It does matter. It’s Gage in there, not just a pile of rags!

He reached over and gently pressed his hands against the tarp, feeling for the shape underneath. He looked like a blind man trying to figure out what something was. Finally, he felt a bump that had to be Gage’s nose—facing the right way.

Only then could he start the car and drive the twenty-five minutes back to Ludlow.

Chapter 52

At one o'clock that morning, Jud Crandall's telephone rang, its sharp sound filling the empty house and waking him up. He had been dreaming he was twenty-three again, sitting on a bench in the B & A train yard with George Chapin and René Michaud. They were sharing a bottle of strong, homemade whiskey while a powerful nor'easter wind howled outside. The wind drowned out all other sounds, even the trains of the B & A railroad. So they sat and drank near the potbellied stove, watching the red glow of the coals behind the cloudy glass. The fire cast diamond-shaped shadows on the floor as they told stories they had kept inside for years, like hidden treasures boys keep under their beds. These were stories saved for nights like this. Like the stove's glow, they were dark tales with a red-hot center, wrapped in the howling wind. He was twenty-three, and Norma was alive (though in bed, he was sure; she wouldn't expect him home on such a wild night). René Michaud was telling a story about a Jewish salesman in Bucksport who—

That was when the phone began to ring, and he jerked awake, his neck stiff. A heavy, sour feeling dropped into him like a stone—the weight of sixty years, from twenty-three to eighty-three, all at once. And then he thought: You've been sleeping, boy. You can't run things that way… not tonight.

He got up, straightening his stiff back, and went to the phone.

It was Rachel.

"Jud? Has he come home?"

"No," Jud said. "Rachel, where are you? You sound closer."

"I am closer," Rachel said. Though she did sound nearer, there was a distant hum on the line. It was the sound of the wind, somewhere between them. The wind was strong tonight—a sound that always made Jud think of dead voices, sighing together, maybe singing something too far away to understand. "I'm at the rest area in Biddeford on the Maine Turnpike."

"Biddeford!"

"I couldn't stay in Chicago. It was getting to me, too… whatever got Ellie, it was getting me too. And you feel it. It's in your voice."

"Yep." He took a Chesterfield from his pack and put it in his mouth. He lit a wooden match and watched it flicker as his hand shook. His hands hadn't shaken—not before this nightmare started anyway. Outside, he heard that dark wind blow hard. It grabbed the house and shook it.

The power's growing. I can feel it.

A faint fear in his old bones. It was like glass, thin and easily broken.

"Jud, please tell me what's going on!"

He guessed she had a right to know—a need to know. And he guessed he would tell her. He would eventually tell her the whole story. He would show her how it had all built up, step by step. Norma's heart attack, the cat's death, Louis's question—has anyone ever buried a person up there?—Gage's death… and God only knew what Louis might be doing right now. He would eventually tell her. But not on the phone.

"Rachel, why are you on the highway instead of on a plane?"

She explained how she had missed her connecting flight in Boston. "I rented a car, but I'm not making good time. I got a little lost getting from the airport to the highway, and I've only just gotten into Maine. I don't think I can get there until dawn. But Jud… please. Please tell me what's happening. I'm so scared, and I don't even know why."

"Rachel, listen to me," Jud said, "drive up to Portland and stay there, do you hear me? Check into a motel and get some—"

"Jud, I can't do th—"

"—and get some sleep. Don't worry, Rachel. Something might be happening here tonight, or it might not. If something is—if it's what I think—then you wouldn't want to be here anyway. I can handle it, I think. I better be able to handle it because what's happening is my fault. If nothing's happening, then you get here this afternoon, and that will be fine. I think Louis will be very happy to see you."

“I can’t sleep tonight, Jud.”

“Yes,” he said, thinking he had felt the same—heck, Peter probably felt the same the night Jesus was arrested. Falling asleep on guard. “Yes, you can. Rachel, if you fall asleep driving that rental car and crash and get killed, what will happen to Louis? And Ellie?”

“Tell me what’s happening! If you tell me that, Jud, maybe I’ll listen to you. But I have to know!”

“When you get to Ludlow, come here,” Jud said. “Not to your house. Come here first. I’ll tell you everything I know, Rachel. And I’m watching for Louis.”

“Tell me,” she said.

“No, ma’am. Not on the phone. I won’t. Rachel, I can’t. You go now. Drive to Portland and rest.”

There was a long pause, as she thought it over.

“All right,” she said finally. “Maybe you’re right. Jud, tell me one thing. Tell me how bad it is.”

“I can handle it,” Jud said calmly. “Things are as bad as they can get.”

Outside, a car’s headlights appeared, moving slowly. Jud stood up a little, watching it, and then sat down again when it sped past the Creed house and disappeared.

“All right,” she said. “I guess. This whole drive has felt like a weight on my head.”

“Let the weight go, my dear,” Jud said. “Please. Save yourself for tomorrow. Things here will be all right.”

“You promise you’ll tell me the whole story?”

“Yes. We’ll have a beer, and I’ll tell you everything.”

“Goodbye, then,” Rachel said, “for now.”

“For now,” Jud agreed. “I’ll see you tomorrow, Rachel.”

Before she could say anything else, Jud hung up the telephone.

He thought there were caffeine pills in the medicine cabinet, but he couldn't find them. He put the rest of the beer back in the refrigerator—not without regret—and settled for a cup of black coffee. He took it back to the bow window and sat down again, sipping and watching.

The coffee—and the conversation with Rachel—kept him awake and alert for forty-five minutes, but then he started to nod again.

No sleeping on guard duty, old man. You let it get to you; you bought something, and now you have to pay for it. So no sleeping on guard duty.

He lit a fresh cigarette, took a deep drag, and coughed a rough, old man's cough. He put the cigarette on the ashtray's edge and rubbed his eyes with both hands. Outside, a big truck roared past, its bright lights cutting through the windy, uneasy night.

He caught himself dozing off again, snapped awake, and abruptly slapped himself across the face, hard enough to make his ears ring. Now, terror woke up in his heart, a sneaky visitor who had broken into that secret place.

It’s puttin me to sleep… hypnotizin me… somethin. It doesn’t want me awake. Because he’ll be comin back pretty soon. Yeah, I feel that. And it wants me out of the way.

“No,” he said firmly. “No way at all. You hear me? I’m puttin a stop to this. This has gone far enough.”

The wind whined around the roof, and the trees across the road shook their leaves in hypnotic patterns. His mind went back to that night by the Defiant stove in the coupling shed, which used to be where the Evarts Furniture Mart was in Brewer now. They had talked all night, he and George and René Michaud, and now he was the only one left—René crushed between two boxcars on a stormy night in March of 1939, George Chapin dead of a heart attack just last year. Out of so many, he was the only one left, and old people get foolish. Sometimes the foolishness looks like kindness, and sometimes it looks like pride—a need to tell old secrets, to pass things on, to pour from the old glass to the new one, to…

So this Jewish salesman comes in and he says “I got something you never seen before. These postcards, they just look like women in bathing suits until you rub them with a wet cloth, and then—”

Jud’s head nodded. His chin settled slowly, gently, against his chest.

“—they’re as naked as the day they were born! But when they dry, the clothes, they come back on! And that ain’t all! I got—”

René telling this story in the coupling shed, leaning forward, smiling, and Jud holds the bottle—he feels the bottle and his hand closes around it in the air.

In the ashtray, the cigarette ash on the end of the cigarette grew longer. Finally, it tipped forward into the ashtray and burned out, its shape left in the neat roll of ash like a symbol.

Jud slept.

And when the taillights flashed outside and Louis turned the Honda Civic into his driveway some forty minutes later and drove it into the garage, Jud didn't hear, stir, or wake up, any more than Peter woke up when the Roman soldiers came to take a wanderer named Jesus into their custody.

Chapter 53

Louis located a new roll of packing tape in a kitchen drawer, and a coil of rope sat in the garage corner near the snow tires from last winter. He used the tape to stick the pick and shovel together tightly and the rope to make a simple sling.

Tools in the sling. Gage in his arms.

He put the sling over his back, then opened the car door, taking the bundle out. Gage felt much heavier than Church had. He might be crawling by the time he carried his boy up to the Micmac burial ground—and he still had to dig the grave, struggling with the hard, rocky ground.

Well, he would handle it. Somehow.

Louis Creed walked out of his garage, pausing to turn off the light with his elbow, and stood for a moment where the driveway met the grass. He could see the path to the Pet Sematary ahead despite the darkness; the short grass of the path seemed to glow.

The wind moved through his hair, and for a moment the old fear of the dark came back, making him feel weak, small, and scared. Was he really going into the woods with this dead child, under the trees where the wind blew, from darkness into darkness? And all alone this time?

Don’t think. Just do.

Louis started walking.

When he reached the Pet Sematary twenty minutes later, his arms and legs were shaking with tiredness, and he collapsed with the tarp on his knees, breathing hard. He rested there for another twenty minutes, almost falling asleep, no longer scared—exhaustion had taken over, it seemed.

Finally he got to his feet again, not really believing he could climb the fallen trees, only knowing in some numb way that he must try. The bundle in his arms seemed to weigh two hundred pounds instead of forty.

But what had happened before happened again; it was like suddenly, vividly remembering a dream. No, not remembering; reliving. When he placed his foot on the first fallen treetrunk, that queer sensation rushed through him again, a feeling that was almost joy. The weariness did not leave him, but it became bearable—unimportant, really.

Just follow me. Follow me and don’t look down, Louis. Don’t hesitate and don’t look down. I know the way through, but it has to be done quick and sure.

Quick and sure, yes—the way Jud had removed the stinger.

I know the way through.

But there was only one way through, Louis thought. Either it let you through or it did not. Once before, he had tried to climb the fallen trees by himself and hadn’t been able to. This time he mounted it quickly and surely, as he had on the night Jud had shown him the way.

Up and up, not looking down, his son’s body in its canvas shroud cradled in his arms. Up until the wind funneled secret passages and chambers through his hair again, flipping it, parting it against the grain.

He stood on the top for a moment and then descended quickly, as if going down a set of stairs. The pick and shovel rattled and clinked dully against his back. In no more than a minute, he was standing on the springy, needle-covered ground of the path again, the fallen trees bulking behind him, higher than the graveyard fence had been.

He moved up the path with his son, listening to the wind moan in the trees. The sound held no terror for him now. The night’s work was almost done.

Chapter 54

Rachel Creed passed the sign reading EXIT 8 KEEP RIGHT FOR PORTLAND WESTBROOK, put on her blinker, and guided the Avis Chevette toward the exit ramp. She could see a green Holiday Inn sign clearly against the night sky. A bed, sleep. An end to this constant, painful, unexplained tension. Also an end—for a little while, at least—to her grieving emptiness for the child who was no longer there. This grief, she had discovered, was like a bad tooth extraction. There was numbness at first, but even through the numbness you felt pain curled up like a cat swishing its tail, pain waiting to happen. And when the novocaine wore off, oh boy, you sure weren’t disappointed.

He told her that he was sent to warn… but that he couldn’t interfere. He told her he was near Daddy because they were together when his soul left his body.

Jud knows, but he won’t tell. Something is going on. Something. But what?

Suicide? Is it suicide? Not Louis; I can’t believe that. But he was lying about something. It was in his eyes… oh shit, it was all over his face, almost as if he wanted me to see the lie… see it and put a stop to it… because part of him was scared… so scared…

Scared? Louis is never scared!

Suddenly she jerked the Chevette’s steering wheel hard over to the left, and the car responded with the quickness that small cars have, the tires wailing. For a moment she thought it was going to turn over. But it didn’t, and she was moving north again, exit 8 with its comforting Holiday Inn sign slipping behind her. A new sign came in view, reflective paint twinkling eerily. NEXT EXIT ROUTE 12 CUMBERLAND CUMBERLAND CENTER JERUSALEM’S LOT FALMOUTH FALMOUTH FORESIDE. Jerusalem’s Lot, she thought randomly, what an odd name. Not a pleasant name, for some reason… Come and sleep in Jerusalem.

But there would be no sleep for her tonight; Jud’s advice notwithstanding, she now meant to drive straight through. Jud knew what was wrong and had promised her he would put a stop to it, but the man was eighty-some years old and had lost his wife only three months before. She would not put her trust in Jud. She should never have allowed Louis to push her out of the house the way he had, but she had been weakened by Gage’s death. Ellie with her Polaroid picture of Gage and her pinched face—it had been the face of a child who has survived a tornado or a sudden bombing from a clear blue sky. There had been times in the dark watches of the night when she had longed to hate Louis for the grief he had caused inside her, and for not giving her the comfort she needed (or allowing her to give the comfort she needed to give), but she could not. She loved him too much still, and his face had been so pale… so watchful…

The Chevette’s speedometer needle hung poised just a bit to the right of sixty miles an hour. A mile a minute. Two hours and a quarter to Ludlow maybe. Maybe she could still beat the sunrise.

She fumbled with the radio, turned it on, found a rock-and-roll station out of Portland. She turned up the volume and sang along, trying to keep herself awake. The station began to fade in and out half an hour later, and she retuned to an Augusta station, rolled the window down, and let the restless night air blow in on her.

She wondered if the night would never end.

Chapter 55

Louis was lost in his dream again; every so often he glanced down to make sure he was carrying a body in a tarp, not a green trash bag. He recalled how, when he woke up after Jud took him to the woods with Church, he could hardly remember what they had done. But now he also remembered how strong his senses had been, how alive he had felt, how they seemed to reach out and touch the woods as if the woods were alive and communicating with him.

He followed the path, remembering where it widened like a road, where it narrowed so he had to turn sideways to keep his bundle from getting stuck in the bushes, and where it went through tall, majestic trees. He smelled the sharp scent of pine, and he heard the strange crunch of needles under his feet—a feeling as much as a sound.

Finally, the path began to slope downward more steeply. Soon, his foot splashed in shallow water and sank into the mud… the quicksand, if Jud was right. Louis looked down and saw standing water among reeds and ugly, low bushes with broad, almost tropical leaves. He remembered the light seemed brighter that other night, too. More electric.

This part is like a trap—you have to walk carefully and steadily. Just follow me and don’t look down.

Yes, okay… and by the way, have you ever seen plants like these in Maine? In Maine or anywhere else? What are they?

Never mind, Louis. Just… let’s go.

He walked again, glancing at the wet, swampy ground just long enough to find the next clump of grass, then looking ahead. Faith is accepting gravity, he thought; not something from a college class, but something his high school physics teacher had said near the end of class… something Louis had never forgotten.

He accepted that the Micmac burial ground could bring back the dead and walked into Little God Swamp with his son, not looking down or back. The swamp was louder now than in late autumn. Peepers sang constantly in the reeds, a shrill sound Louis found strange and unwelcome. A frog occasionally croaked somewhere in the distance. About twenty steps into the swamp, something buzzed past him… maybe a bat.

The mist began to swirl around him, first covering his shoes, then his legs, finally surrounding him in a glowing white bubble. The light seemed brighter, pulsing like a strange heartbeat. He had never felt nature so strongly as a single, powerful force, a real being… possibly aware. The swamp was alive, but not with pleasant sounds. If asked to explain what he felt, he couldn't. He only knew it was full of potential and strength. Inside it, Louis felt small and mortal.

Then there was a sound, and he remembered it from before: a high, gulping laugh that turned into a sob. There was silence, then the laugh came again, rising to a crazy shriek that froze Louis's blood. The mist drifted around him. The laughter faded, leaving only the wind's drone, heard but not felt. Of course; it had to be a hollow in the earth. If the wind could get in here, it would have ripped the mist to shreds… and Louis wasn’t sure he wanted to see what might be revealed.

You may hear sounds like voices, but it’s just the loons down south toward Prospect. Sound travels. It’s funny.

“Loons,” Louis said, barely recognizing the cracked, ghostly sound of his own voice. But he sounded amused. God help him, he actually sounded amused.

He hesitated, then moved on. As if to punish him for stopping, his foot slipped from the next clump of grass, and he almost lost his shoe, pulling it free from the mud under the shallow water.

The voice—if that’s what it was—came again, from the left. Then from behind him… right behind him, it seemed, as if he could turn and see something bloody less than a foot from his back, all teeth and shining eyes… but this time Louis didn’t slow down. He looked straight ahead and kept walking.

Suddenly the mist darkened, and Louis saw a face hanging in the air, grinning and babbling. Its eyes, tilted up like in a Chinese painting, were yellowish-gray, sunken, gleaming. The mouth was pulled down; the lower lip was inside out, showing teeth stained brown and worn down. But Louis noticed the ears, which were not ears but curving horns… not devil’s horns, but ram’s horns.

This ghostly head seemed to be speaking—laughing. Its mouth moved, but the turned-down lip never went back to normal. Black veins pulsed. Its nostrils flared, as if alive, and blew out white vapor.

As Louis got closer, the head’s tongue lolled out. It was long and pointed, dirty yellow. It was covered in peeling scales, and as Louis watched, one flipped up like a manhole cover and a white worm oozed out. The tongue's tip moved lazily in the air where its Adam’s apple should have been… it was laughing.

He clutched Gage closer, hugging him for protection, and his feet slipped on the grassy clumps where they barely held.

You might see St. Elmo’s fire, what sailors call foo-lights. It can make funny shapes, but it’s nothing. If the shapes bother you, just look away…

Jud's voice in his head gave him some strength. He started moving forward again, stumbling at first, then finding his balance. He didn't look away but noticed that the face—if that's what it was and not just a trick of the mist and his own mind—always seemed to be the same distance away. And after a few seconds or minutes, it simply faded into the drifting mist.

That was not St. Elmo’s fire.

No, of course it wasn’t. This place was full of spirits; it was dark with them. You could look around and see something that would drive you crazy. He would not think about it. There was no need to think about it. There was no need to—

Something was coming.

Louis stopped completely, listening to that sound… that unstoppable, approaching sound. His mouth fell open, every muscle holding his jaw shut simply giving way.

It was a sound like nothing he had ever heard—a living sound, a big sound. Somewhere nearby, growing closer, branches were snapping off. There was a crackling of bushes breaking under huge feet. The soft ground under Louis’s feet began to shake along with it. He realized he was moaning

(oh my God oh my dear God what is that what is coming through this fog?)

and again holding Gage tightly to his chest; he realized that the peepers and frogs had gone silent, he realized that the wet air had taken on a strange, sickening smell like warm, spoiled pork.

Whatever it was, it was huge.

Louis’s wondering, terrified face tilted up and up, like a man watching a rocket go up. The thing thumped toward him, and there was the cracking sound of a tree—not a branch, but a whole tree—falling over somewhere close by.

Louis saw something.
