The mist turned a dull gray for a moment, but this blurry, vague mark was more than sixty feet high. It wasn't a shadow or a ghost; he could feel the air move as it passed, could hear its huge feet hit the ground, the mud sucking as it moved on.

For a moment, he thought he saw two yellow-orange sparks high above him. Sparks like eyes.

Then the sound began to fade. As it went away, a peeper called out hesitantly—one. Another answered. A third joined in; a fourth made it a lively discussion; a fifth and sixth turned it into a peeper convention. The sounds of the thing moving (slow but not clumsy; perhaps that was the worst part, the feeling that it was moving with purpose) were fading to the north. Less and less… gone.

Finally, Louis began to move again. His shoulders and back were frozen and aching. He was soaked in sweat from neck to ankles. The first mosquitoes of the season, newly hatched and hungry, found him and started their late-night snack.

The Wendigo, dear God, that was the Wendigo—the creature that roams the north, the creature that can touch you and turn you into a cannibal. That was it. The Wendigo had just passed within sixty yards of him.

He told himself not to be silly, to be like Jud and not think about what might be seen or heard beyond the Pet Sematary—they were loons, they were St. Elmo’s fire, they were the New York Yankees’ bullpen. Let them be anything but the creatures that jump and crawl and slither and stumble in the world between. Let there be God, let there be Sunday morning, let there be smiling ministers in white robes… but please, not these dark and horrible things on the dark side of the universe.

Louis walked on with his son, and the ground began to feel solid again under his feet. Just a few moments later, he came to a fallen tree, its top visible in the fading mist like a gray-green feather duster dropped by a giant's maid.

The tree was broken off—splintered off—and the break was so fresh that the yellowish-white wood still oozed sap that felt warm to Louis as he climbed over… and on the other side was a huge dent that he had to climb out of, and although juniper and low bushes had been crushed into the ground, he wouldn't let himself believe it was a footprint. He could have looked back to see if it looked like one once he was past it, but he wouldn't. He just walked on, skin cold, mouth hot and dry, heart racing.

The squelching of mud under his feet soon stopped. For a while, there was the faint rustling of pine needles again. Then there was rock. He was almost at the end.

The ground began to rise faster. He banged his shin hard on a rock sticking out. But this was not just a rock. Louis reached out awkwardly with one hand (the strap on his elbow, which had gone numb, screamed briefly) and touched it.

Steps here. Cut into the rock. Just follow me. We get to the top and we’re there.

So he began to climb and the excitement came back, once more pushing back the exhaustion… at least a little. His mind counted the steps as he rose into the cold, as he climbed back into that constant river of wind, stronger now, shaking his clothes, making the piece of canvas tarp Gage was wrapped in sound like gunshots like a lifted sail.

He tilted his head back once and saw the wild spread of the stars. There were no star patterns he knew, and he looked away again, uneasy. Beside him was the rock wall, not smooth but broken and chipped and crumbly, taking here the shape of a boat, here the shape of a badger, here the shape of a man’s face with hooded, frowning eyes. Only the steps that had been carved from the rock were smooth.

Louis reached the top and just stood there with his head down, swaying, gasping breath in and out of his lungs. They felt like badly punched bags, and there seemed to be a big splinter stuck in his side.

The wind ran through his hair like a dancer, roared in his ears like a dragon.

The light was brighter tonight; had it been cloudy the other time or had he just not been looking? It didn’t matter. But he could see, and that was enough to start another chill creeping down his back.

It was just like the Pet Sematary.

Of course you knew that, his mind whispered as he looked at the piles of rocks that had once been cairns. You knew that, or should have known it—not circles inside circles but the spiral…

Yes. Here on top of this rock table, its face turned up to cold starlight and to the black spaces between the stars, was a gigantic spiral, made by what the old people would have called Different Hands. But there were no real cairns, Louis saw; every one of them had been broken apart as something buried beneath came back to life… and clawed its way out. Yet the rocks themselves had fallen in such a way that the shape of the spiral was clear.

Has anyone ever seen this from the air? Louis wondered absently, thinking of those desert drawings some Indian tribe made in South America. Has anyone ever seen it from the air, and if they did, what did they think, I wonder?

He kneeled and set Gage’s body on the ground with a relieved groan.

Finally, he started to wake up fully. He used his pocketknife to cut the tape holding the pick and shovel across his back. They fell to the ground with a clink. Louis rolled over and lay down for a moment, spread out, staring blankly at the stars.

What was that thing in the woods? Louis, Louis, do you really think anything good can come from a play where something like that is part of it?

But now it was too late to turn back, and he knew it.

Besides, he babbled to himself, it might still be okay; you have to take risks to get something, maybe you have to risk to love. There’s still my bag, not the one downstairs but the one in our bathroom on the high shelf, the one I sent Jud for the night Norma had her heart attack. There are syringes, and if something happens… something bad… no one has to know but me.

His thoughts turned into a mumbling, droning prayer as his hands reached for the pick… and still on his knees, Louis began to dig into the earth. Each time he swung the pick down, he collapsed over it, like an old Roman falling on his sword. Yet little by little, the hole took shape and got deeper. He clawed the rocks out, and mostly he just pushed them aside along with the growing pile of stony dirt. But some of them he saved.

For the cairn.

Chapter 56

Rachel slapped her face until it tingled, but she still kept nodding off. Once she snapped fully awake (she was in Pittsfield now and had the turnpike all to herself), it seemed for a second that many silvery, cruel eyes were looking at her, twinkling like cold, hungry fire.

Then they turned out to be the small reflectors on the guardrail posts. The Chevette had drifted far into the breakdown lane.

She jerked the wheel to the left, the tires screeching, and she thought she heard a faint tick! that might have been her right front bumper just barely touching a guardrail post. Her heart leaped and began to bang so hard that she saw spots before her eyes, growing and shrinking with its beat. And yet a moment later, despite her close call, her scare, and Robert Gordon shouting “Red Hot” on the radio, she was dozing off again.

A crazy, paranoid thought came to her. “Paranoid, sure,” she muttered under the rock and roll. She tried to laugh—but she couldn’t. Not really. Because the thought stayed, and in the dark, it seemed strangely believable. She felt like a cartoon character who has run into a giant slingshot's rubber band. The poor guy finds it harder and harder to move forward, until the rubber band's energy equals the runner's energy… inertia becomes what?… basic physics… something holding her back… stay out of this… and an object at rest stays at rest… Gage’s body, for instance… once moving…

This time the tires screamed louder, the shave was much closer; for a moment there was the high, grating sound of the Chevette running along the guardrail cables, scraping paint down to the shining metal, and for a moment the wheel didn’t respond, and then Rachel was standing on the brake, sobbing; she had been asleep this time, not just dozing but asleep and dreaming at sixty miles an hour, and if there had been no guardrail… or if there had been an overpass support…

She pulled over, put the car in park, and wept into her hands, confused and afraid.

Something is trying to keep me away from him.

When she felt in control, she began to drive again—the car's steering seemed fine, but she figured Avis would have some questions when she returned their car to BIA tomorrow.

Never mind. One thing at a time. I need coffee—that’s the first thing.

When the Pittsfield exit appeared, Rachel took it. About a mile down the road she saw bright streetlights and heard the steady low rumble of diesel engines. She pulled in, had the Chevette filled up (“Someone put a pretty good dent along the side of her,” the gas station attendant said, almost impressed), and then went into the diner, which smelled of fried grease, rubbery eggs… and, thankfully, of good strong coffee.

Rachel had three cups, one after another, like medicine—black, with lots of sugar. A few truckers sat at the counter or in the booths, teasing the waitresses, who all looked like tired nurses with bad news under the bright fluorescent lights in the early morning.

She paid and went back out to the Chevette. It wouldn’t start. When she turned the key, the solenoid made a dry clicking sound, but nothing else.

Rachel began to hit the steering wheel slowly and weakly with her fists. Something was trying to stop her. There was no reason for this car, brand-new and with less than five thousand miles on it, to have died like this, but it had. Somehow it had, and here she was, stuck in Pittsfield, still almost fifty miles from home.

She listened to the steady hum of the big trucks, and it suddenly struck her with cruel certainty that the truck that had killed her son was here among them… not humming but laughing.

Rachel lowered her head and began to cry.

Chapter 57

Louis tripped and fell flat on the ground. For a moment he didn’t think he could get up—getting up was too much—he would just lie there, listening to the chorus of peepers from Little God Swamp behind him and feeling the chorus of aches and pains in his body. He would lie there until he fell asleep. Or died. Probably the latter.

He remembered slipping the canvas bundle into the hole he had dug, and pushing most of the dirt back into the hole with his bare hands. And he thought he remembered piling the rocks up, building from a wide base to a point…

From then to now he remembered very little. He must have gotten back down the steps or he wouldn’t be here, which was… where? Looking around, he thought he recognized one of the groves of great old pines not far beyond the deadfall. Could he have made it all the way back through Little God Swamp without realizing it? He guessed it was possible. Just barely.

This is far enough. I’ll just sleep here.

But it was that thought, so falsely comforting, that made him get up and move again. Because if he stayed here, that thing might find him… that thing might be in the woods and looking for him right now.

He rubbed his hand up his face, palm first, and was surprised to see blood on his hand… he'd given himself a nosebleed at some point. “Who cares?” he muttered roughly and reached around him until he found the pick and shovel again.

Ten minutes later the deadfall appeared ahead. Louis climbed it, stumbling but somehow not falling until he was almost down. Then he glanced at his feet, a branch snapped (don’t look down, Jud had said), another branch tumbled, making his foot slip, and he fell with a thud on his side, the wind knocked out of him.

I’ll be damned if this isn’t the second graveyard I’ve fallen into tonight… and I’ll be damned if two isn’t enough.

He began to feel around for the pick and shovel again, and finally found them. For a moment he looked around, able to see by starlight. Nearby was the grave of SMUCKY. He was obedient, Louis thought wearily. And TRIXIE, KILT ON THE HIGHWAY. The wind still blew strongly, and he could hear the faint ting-ting-ting of a piece of metal—maybe it had been a Del Monte can, cut by a grieving pet owner with his father’s tin snips and then flattened with a hammer and nailed to a stick—and that brought the fear back again. He was too tired to feel it as more than a sickening pulse. He had done it. That steady ting-ting-ting sound coming out of the darkness made it real to him more than anything else.

He walked through the Pet Sematary, past the grave of MARTA OUR PET RABBIT who had DIED MARCH 1 1965, and near the mound of GEN. PATTON; he stepped over the broken piece of wood that marked the final resting place of POLYNESIA. The tick of metal was louder now, and he paused, looking down. Here on a slightly tilted board stuck in the ground, was a tin rectangle, and by starlight Louis read, RINGO OUR HAMSTER, 1964-1965. It was this piece of tin that was ticking against the boards of the Pet Sematary’s entry arch. Louis reached down to bend the piece of tin back… and then froze, his skin crawling.

Something was moving back there. Something was moving on the other side of the fallen trees.

What he heard was a quiet sound—the soft crackle of pine needles, the dry snap of a twig, the rustle of bushes. They were almost lost under the sound of the wind through the pines.

“Gage?” Louis called weakly.

Just realizing what he was doing—standing here in the dark and calling his dead son—made his skin tighten and his hair stand up. He began to shake uncontrollably, as if he had a bad fever.

“Gage?”

The sounds had stopped.

Not yet; it’s too soon. Don’t ask me how I know, but I do. That isn’t Gage over there. That’s… something else.

He suddenly thought of Ellie telling him, He called “Lazarus, come forth”… because if He hadn’t called for Lazarus by name, everyone in that graveyard would have risen.

On the other side of the fallen trees, those sounds had started again. On the other side of the barrier. Almost—but not quite—hidden by the wind. As if something blind were following him with old instincts. His extremely scared brain created awful, sickening images: a giant mole, a big bat that dragged itself through the bushes instead of flying.

Louis backed out of the Pet Sematary, not turning his back to the deadfall—that ghostlike glimmer, a pale mark on the dark—until he was far down the path. Then he began to hurry, and about a quarter of a mile before the path left the woods and entered the field behind his house, he found the strength to run.

Louis tossed the pick and shovel carelessly inside the garage and stood for a moment at the end of his driveway, looking first back the way he had come and then up at the sky. It was a little after four in the morning, and he figured dawn wasn't far off. Light would already be well across the Atlantic, but for now, here in Ludlow, the night was strong. The wind blew steadily.

He went into the house, feeling his way along the side of the garage and unlocking the back door. He went through the kitchen without turning on a light and stepped into the small bathroom between the kitchen and the dining room. Here he did turn on a light, and the first thing he saw was Church, curled up on top of the toilet tank, staring at him with those dull yellow-green eyes.

“Church,” he said. “I thought someone let you out.”

Church only looked at him from atop the toilet tank. Yes, someone had let Church out; he had done it himself. He remembered that very clearly. Just as he remembered replacing the window pane in the basement that time and then telling himself that that had solved the problem. But who was he kidding? When Church wanted to get in, Church got in. Because Church was different now.

It didn’t matter. In this tired, drained aftermath, nothing seemed to matter. He felt less than human, like one of George Romero’s slow, stumbling movie-zombies, or someone who had escaped from T. S. Eliot’s poem about the empty men. I should have been a pair of rough claws, crawling through Little God Swamp and up to the Micmac burial ground, he thought and gave a dry chuckle.

“Head full of straw, Church,” he said in his hoarse voice. He was unbuttoning his shirt now. “That’s me. You better believe it.”

There was a nice bruise forming on his left side, about halfway up his ribs, and when he took off his pants he saw that the knee he had banged on the gravestone was swelling up like a balloon. It had already turned a nasty purple-black, and he figured that as soon as he stopped moving it, the joint would become stiff and painfully stubborn—as if it had been dipped in cement. It looked like one of those injuries that might talk to him on rainy days for the rest of his life.

He reached out a hand to stroke Church, wanting some comfort, but the cat leaped down from the toilet tank, staggering in that clumsy and strangely unfeline way, and left for some other place. It gave Louis one flat, yellow glance as it went.

There was Ben-Gay in the medicine cabinet. Louis lowered the toilet seat, sat down, and smeared a blob on his bad knee. Then he rubbed some more on the small of his back—a clumsy job.

He left the toilet and walked into the living room. He turned on the hall light and stood there at the foot of the stairs for a moment, looking around, dazed. How strange it all seemed! This was where he had stood on Christmas Eve when he had given Rachel the sapphire. It had been in the pocket of his robe. There was his chair, where he had tried to explain death to Ellie after Norma Crandall’s heart attack—facts he had found he couldn't accept himself. The Christmas tree had been in that corner, Ellie’s paper turkey—the one that had reminded Louis of a futuristic crow—had been taped in that window, and long before that, the room had been empty except for the moving boxes, filled with their things and brought from the Midwest. He remembered thinking that their things looked small and unimportant, boxed up like that—a weak defense between his family and the cold, unknown world where no one knew them.

How strange it all seemed… and how he wished they had never heard of the University of Maine, or Ludlow, or Jud and Norma Crandall, or any of it.

He went upstairs in his underwear, and in the bathroom he got the stool, stood on it, and took down the small black bag from the top of the medicine cabinet. He took this into the bedroom, sat down, and began to rummage through it. Yes, there were syringes in case he needed one, and among the tape and scissors and wrapped surgical thread were several vials of poison.

If needed.

Louis snapped the bag shut and put it by the bed. He turned off the light, then lay down, hands behind his head. To lie here resting was wonderful. His thoughts went to Disney World again. He saw himself in a white uniform, driving a white van with the mouse-ears logo—nothing to show it was a rescue unit, nothing to scare the customers.

Gage was sitting beside him, his skin tan, his eyes bright. Here, was Goofy, shaking hands with a little boy; the kid was amazed. Here was Winnie the Pooh posing with two laughing grandmas in pants so a third grandma could take their pictures; here was a little girl crying, “I love you, Tigger! I love you, Tigger!”

He and his son were on patrol. He and his son were the guards in this magic land, and they drove in their white van with the covered red light. They were not looking for trouble, but they were ready for it. It could be here, in a place of happiness; some man buying film could clutch his chest, a pregnant woman might feel labor pains as she walked down the steps, a teenage girl might collapse in a fit, her body jerking as her brain failed. There was sunstroke and heatstroke and stroke, and maybe a lightning strike; there was, even, Oz himself—he might be seen near the monorail or looking down from one of the flying Dumbos—Louis and Gage knew him as just another park figure like Goofy or Mickey or Tigger or Donald Duck. He was the one no one wanted to meet, the one no one wanted to introduce to his kids. Louis and Gage knew him; they had met him in New England. He was waiting to choke you, to smother you, to kill you with electricity—Available at Any Outlet. There was death in peanuts, steak, cigarettes. He was always around, he watched all the paths between life and death. Needles, poison, wires, fires. Skates that sent kids into traffic. When you showered, Oz was there—Shower with a Friend. When you got on a plane, Oz took your ticket. He was in the water, the food. Who’s out there? you cried in the dark, and it was his answer: Don’t be afraid, it’s just me. Hi! You got cancer, too bad! Blood in acid on the road. Pills, eat them up. That blue color of the fingernails—the brain takes all the oxygen, even under the nails. Hi, folks, I’m Oz, call me Oz—we’re old friends. Just here to give you heart failure or a blood clot; got to see a woman give birth, then I’ve got a fire to do in Omaha.

And that thin voice is crying, “I love you, Tigger! I love you! I believe in you, Tigger! I will always love you and believe in you, and I will stay young, and the only Oz in my heart will be that faker! I love you…

We drive… my son and I… because it’s not about war or sex but that fight against Oz. He and I, in our white van under this sky, we drive. And the red light is covered, but it is there… and only we know because a man’s heart is hard; a man grows what he can… and cares for it.

Thinking these thoughts, Louis Creed drifted away, losing touch with reality, until all thoughts stopped and sleep took him.

Just before the first light of dawn appeared in the east, footsteps sounded on the stairs. They were slow and heavy, but determined. A shadow moved in the darkness of the hall. A smell came with it—a stench. Louis, even in his deep sleep, mumbled and turned away from that smell. There was the steady sound of breathing.

The figure stood outside the master bedroom door for a while, not moving. Then it came inside. Louis’s face was buried in his pillow. White hands reached out, and there was a click as the black doctor’s bag by the bed was opened.

A quiet clink and shift as the things inside were moved.

The hands searched, pushing aside drugs and vials and syringes without interest. Now they found something and held it up. In the dim early light, there was a glint of silver.

The shadowy thing left the room.

Chapter 58

Jud Crandall woke with a start, nearly tumbling from his chair. He didn't know how long he'd slept—maybe fifteen minutes, maybe three hours. His watch showed five minutes to five. He felt like everything in the room had been slightly moved, and his back hurt from sleeping upright.

Oh you stupid old man, look what you gone and done!

But he knew better; deep down, he knew. It wasn't just him. He hadn't just dozed off; he'd been put to sleep.

That scared him, but something else scared him more: what had woken him? He thought he'd heard a sound, some—

He held his breath, listening over the soft rustle of his heart.

There it was—a sound, but not the one that woke him. The faint creak of hinges.

Jud knew every sound in this house—which boards creaked, which steps squeaked, where the wind liked to howl and sing when it was wild, like last night. He knew this sound too. The heavy front door, the one between his porch and the hall, had just opened. And now he remembered the sound that had awakened him: the slow stretching of the spring on the screen door between the porch and the walk.

“Louis?” he called, without much hope. It wasn't Louis out there. Whatever it was, it had come to punish an old man for his pride.

Footsteps moved slowly up the hall toward the living room.

“Louis?” he tried to call again, but only a faint croak came out because now he could smell the thing that had entered his house at the end of the night. It was a dirty, low smell—like poisoned mud flats.

Jud could see big shapes in the dim light—Norma’s wardrobe, the Welsh dresser, the tall chest of drawers—but no details. He tried to stand on legs that felt like water, his mind screaming that he needed more time, that he was too old to face this again without more time; Timmy Baterman had been bad enough, and Jud had been young then.

The swing door opened and let in shadows. One of the shadows was bigger than the others.

Dear God, that stink.

Shuffling steps in the darkness.

“Gage?” Jud finally stood up. He saw the neat roll of cigarette ash in the Jim Beam ashtray out of the corner of his eye. “Gage, is that y—”

A horrible crying sound came, and for a moment Jud’s bones turned to ice. It was not Louis’s son returned from the grave but some horrible monster.

No. It was neither.

It was Church, crouched in the hall doorway, making that sound. The cat’s eyes glowed like dirty lamps. Then his eyes moved and stared at the thing that had come in with the cat.

Jud began to back away, trying to grab his thoughts, trying to stay sane in the face of that smell. Oh, it was cold in here—the thing had brought its coldness with it.

Jud wobbled on his feet—the cat was winding around his legs, making him unsteady. It was purring. Jud kicked at it, chasing it away. It showed its teeth and hissed.

Think! Oh, think, you stupid old man, it might not be too late, even now it might not be too late… it’s back but it can be killed again… if you can just do it… if you can just think…

He backed away toward the kitchen, and he suddenly remembered the drawer beside the sink. There was a meat cleaver in that drawer.

His thin legs bumped the swinging door to the kitchen and he pushed it open. The thing that had come into his house was still unclear, but Jud could hear it breathing. He could see one white hand swinging back and forth—there was something in that hand, but he couldn’t make it out. The door swung back as he entered the kitchen, and Jud finally turned and ran to the drawer. He yanked it open and found the cleaver’s worn wooden handle. He grabbed it and turned toward the door again; he even took a step or two toward it. Some of his courage had returned.

Remember, it isn’t a kid. It might scream or something when it sees you’ve figured it out; it might cry. But you won’t be fooled. You’ve been fooled too many times already, old man. This is your last chance.

The swing door opened again, but at first only the cat came through. Jud’s eye followed it for a moment and then he looked up again.

The kitchen faced east, and dawn’s first light came in through the windows, faint and milky white. Not much light but enough. Too much.

Gage Creed came in, wearing his burial suit. Moss was growing on the suit’s shoulders and lapels. Moss had dirtied his white shirt. His fine blond hair was covered in dirt. One eye was turned to the side; it stared off with terrible focus. The other was fixed on Jud.

Gage was grinning at him.

“Hello, Jud,” Gage said in a childish but perfectly clear voice. “I’ve come to send your rotten, stinking old soul straight to hell. You messed with me once. Did you think I wouldn’t come back sooner or later and mess with you?”

Jud raised the cleaver. “Come on and show yourself, whatever you are. We’ll see who messes with who.”

“Norma’s dead, and no one will miss you,” Gage said. “She was such a cheap slut. She slept with all your friends, Jud. She let them do it every way. That’s what she liked best. She’s burning in hell, arthritis and all. I saw her there, Jud. I saw her there.”

It took two steps toward him, shoes leaving muddy marks on the worn floor. It held one hand out as if to shake; the other was hidden behind its back.

“Listen, Jud,” it whispered—and then its mouth opened, showing small teeth, and though the lips didn’t move, Norma’s voice came out.

“I laughed at you! We all laughed at you! How we laaaaaauuughed—”

“Stop it!” The cleaver shook in his hand.

“We did it in our bed, Herk and I did it, I did it with George, I did it with all of them, I knew about your whores but you never knew you married a whore and how we laughed, Jud! We screwed together and we laaaaaaaaaughed at—”

“STOP IT!” Jud screamed. He jumped at the small, swaying figure in its dirty burial suit, and that was when the cat shot out of the darkness under the butcher block where it had been hiding. It was hissing, its ears flat, and it tripped Jud as easily as could be. The cleaver flew out of his hand. It skittered across the bumpy and faded floor, blade and handle quickly switching places as it spun. It hit the wall with a light clang and slid under the refrigerator.

Jud knew he’d been tricked again, and the only comfort was that it was the last time. The cat was on his legs, mouth open, eyes blazing, hissing like a kettle. And then Gage was on him, grinning a happy, dark grin, eyes round, rimmed with red, and his right hand came out from behind his back, and Jud saw that what he was holding was a scalpel from Louis’s black bag.

“Oh my dear Jesus,” Jud said, and put his right hand up to block the blow. And then he saw something strange; he must be losing his mind because it looked like the scalpel was on both sides of his palm at the same time. Then something warm started to drip down on his face, and he understood.

“I’m gonna mess with you, old man!” the Gage-thing chuckled, blowing its poisoned breath in his face. “I’m gonna mess with you! I’m gonna mess with you all… I… want!”

Jud struggled and grabbed Gage’s wrist. Skin peeled off like paper in his hand.

The scalpel was yanked out of his hand, leaving a vertical cut.

“All…I…WANT!”

The scalpel came down again.

And again.

And again.

Chapter 59

“Try it now, ma’am,” the truck driver said. He was looking into the engine area of Rachel’s rented car.

She turned the key. The Chevette’s engine roared to life. The truck driver slammed the hood down and came around to her window, wiping his hands on a big blue handkerchief. He had a pleasant, red face. A Dysart’s Truck-Stop cap was tilted back on his head.

“Thank you so much,” Rachel said, close to tears. “I just didn’t know what I was going to do.”

“Aw, a kid could have fixed that,” the trucker said. “But it was strange. Never seen something like that happen on such a new car, anyway.”

“Why? What was it?”

“One of your battery cables came right off. Nobody messed with it, did they?”

“No,” Rachel said, and she thought again of that feeling she’d had, that feeling of running into the rubber band of the world’s biggest slingshot.

“Must have shaken loose just from driving, I guess. But you won’t have any more trouble with your cables anyway. I tightened them up real good.”

“Could I give you some money?” Rachel asked shyly.

The trucker roared with laughter. “Not me, lady,” he said. “We’re the knights of the road, remember?”

She smiled. “Well… thank you.”

“You’re welcome.” He grinned, surprisingly cheerful this early.

Rachel smiled back and drove carefully across the parking lot to the access road. She checked for traffic and five minutes later was back on the turnpike, headed north. The coffee had helped more than she thought it would. She felt wide awake, not sleepy at all, her eyes wide open. That slight feeling of unease touched her again, that strange feeling that she was being controlled. The battery cable coming off like that…

So she could be delayed just long enough for…

She laughed nervously. Long enough for what?

For something that couldn’t be undone to happen.

That was dumb. Ridiculous. But Rachel started to drive faster anyway.

At five o’clock, as Jud was fighting off a scalpel stolen from his friend Dr. Louis Creed’s bag, and as her daughter woke up suddenly in bed, screaming from a nightmare she couldn't remember, Rachel left the turnpike, drove the Hammond Street Cutoff near the cemetery where only a shovel was now buried in her son’s coffin, and crossed the Bangor-Brewer Bridge. By 5:15, she was on Route 15 and headed for Ludlow.

She had decided to go straight to Jud’s; she would at least keep that promise. The Civic wasn’t in their driveway, and although it might be in the garage, their house looked empty and asleep. She didn't feel that Louis might be home.

Rachel parked behind Jud’s truck and got out of the Chevette, looking around carefully. The grass was wet with dew, shining in the clear, new light. A bird sang somewhere and then stopped. A few times since she was a child, she had been awake and alone at dawn without having to do something. Then, she felt lonely but also uplifted—a strange sense of newness and lasting connection. This morning, she felt nothing so clean and good. There was only a heavy feeling of unease that she couldn’t blame entirely on the terrible day before and her recent loss.

She walked up the porch steps and opened the screen door, planning to use the old-fashioned bell on the front door. She had loved that bell the first time she and Louis came over; you twisted it, and it made a loud but musical sound that was old-fashioned and delightful.

She reached for it now, then looked down at the porch floor and frowned. There were muddy footprints on the mat. Looking around, she saw they went from the screen door to the front door. Very small footprints. A child’s footprints, it seemed. But she had been driving all night, and it hadn't rained. Windy, but no rain.

She looked at the footprints for a long time—too long, really—and realized she had to force herself to touch the bell. She grabbed it… and then her hand dropped again.

I’m just nervous, that’s all. Nervous about the sound of that bell in this quiet. He’s probably asleep and it will startle him awake…

But that wasn’t what she was afraid of. She had been anxious, scared in some deep way ever since she had struggled to stay awake, but this sharp fear was new, something only about those small footprints. Footprints that were the size—

Her mind tried to stop this thought, but it was too tired, too slow.

—of Gage’s feet.

Oh, stop it, please stop it?

She reached out and twisted the bell.

The sound was even louder than she remembered, but less musical—a harsh, choked scream in the stillness. Rachel jumped back, letting out a nervous little laugh that wasn't funny at all. She waited for Jud’s footsteps, but they didn't come. There was silence, and more silence, and she was starting to wonder if she could bring herself to turn that iron-butterfly shape again, when a sound did come from behind the door, a sound she never would have imagined.

Waow!… Waow!… Waow!

“Church?” she asked, surprised and confused. She leaned forward, but of course couldn't see inside; the door's glass panel was covered with a neat white curtain. Norma’s work. “Church, is that you?”

Waow!

Rachel tried the door. It was unlocked. Church was there, sitting in the hallway with its tail coiled neatly around its feet. The cat’s fur was streaked with something dark. Mud, Rachel thought, and then saw that the drops of liquid caught in Church’s whiskers were red.

He raised one paw and began to lick it, his eyes never leaving her face.

“Jud?” she called out, really worried now. She stepped just inside the door.

The house gave back no answer; only silence.

Rachel tried to think, but suddenly images of her sister Zelda began to fill her mind, blurring her thoughts. How her hands had twisted. How she used to slam her head against the wall sometimes when she was angry—the paper was all torn there, the plaster beneath torn and broken. This was no time to think of Zelda, not when Jud might be hurt. What if he had fallen down? He was an old man.

Think about that, not about the dreams you had as a kid, dreams of opening the closet and having Zelda jump out at you with her blackened, grinning face, dreams of being in the bathtub and seeing Zelda’s eyes peering out of the drain, dreams of Zelda hiding in the basement behind the furnace, dreams—

Church opened his mouth, showing his sharp teeth, and cried Waow! again.

Louis was right, we should never have had him fixed. He's never seemed right since then. But Louis said it would take away all his aggressive instincts. He was wrong about that, anyway; Church still hunts. He—

Waow! Church cried again, then turned and ran up the stairs.

"Jud?" she called again. "Are you up there?"

Waow! Church cried from the top of the stairs, as if to say yes, and then he disappeared down the hall.

How did he get in, anyway? Did Jud let him in? Why?

Rachel shifted from one foot to the other, wondering what to do next. The worst part was that all of this seemed... seemed somehow planned, as if something wanted her to be here, and—

And then there was a groan from upstairs, low and full of pain—Jud's voice, definitely Jud's voice. He's fallen in the bathroom or maybe tripped, broken a leg, or sprained his hip, maybe. Old bones break easily, and what in the world are you thinking, girl, standing here shifting back and forth like you have to pee. That was blood on Church, blood. Jud's hurt and you're just standing here! What's wrong with you?

"Jud!" The groan came again, and she ran up the stairs.

She had never been up here before, and because the hall's only window faced west, toward the river, it was still very dark. The hallway ran straight and wide beside the stairwell and toward the back of the house, the cherrywood rail gleaming with soft elegance. There was a picture of the Acropolis on the wall and

(it’s Zelda all these years she’s been after you and now it’s her turn open the right door and she’ll be there with her hunched and twisted back smelling of pee and death it’s Zelda it’s her turn and finally she caught up with you)

The groan came again, low, from behind the second door on the right.

Rachel began to walk toward that door, her heels clicking on the floorboards. It seemed to her that she was going through some kind of warp—not a time warp or a space warp but a size warp. She was getting smaller. The picture of the Acropolis was floating higher and higher, and that cut-glass doorknob would soon be at eye level. Her hand stretched out for it… and before she could even touch it, the door was yanked open.

Zelda stood there.

She was hunched and twisted, her body so cruelly deformed that she had become a dwarf, little more than two feet high; and for some reason Zelda was wearing the suit they had buried Gage in. But it was Zelda, all right, her eyes alight with an insane glee, her face a wrinkled purple; it was Zelda screaming, “I finally came back for you, Rachel, I’m going to twist your back like mine and you’ll never get out of bed again never get out of bed again NEVER GET OUT OF BED AGAIN—”

Church was perched on one of her shoulders and Zelda’s face swam and changed, and Rachel saw with spiraling, sickening horror that it really wasn’t Zelda at all—how could she have made such a stupid mistake? It was Gage. His face was not black but dirty, smeared with blood. And it was swollen, as if he had been terribly hurt and then put back together again by rough, uncaring hands.

She cried his name and held her arms out. He ran to her and climbed into them, and all the time one hand remained behind his back, as if holding a bunch of flowers picked in someone’s back field.

“I brought you something, Mommy!” he screamed. “I brought you something, Mommy! I brought you something, I brought you something!”

Chapter 60

Louis Creed woke up with the sun shining right in his eyes. He tried to get up and winced at the sharp pain in his back. It was awful. He fell back on the pillow and looked down at himself. Still fully dressed. Jesus.

He lay there for a long moment, getting ready to face the stiffness that had settled into every muscle, and then he sat up.

“Oh, shit,” he whispered. For a few seconds the room swayed gently but noticeably. His back throbbed like a bad tooth, and when he moved his head, it felt as if the muscles in his neck had been replaced by rusty saw blades. But his knee was really the worst. The Ben-Gay hadn’t done anything for it. He should have given himself a shot of cortisone. His pants were pulled tightly against the knee by the swelling; it looked like there was a balloon under there.

“Really messed it up,” he muttered. “Boy, oh boy, did I ever.”

He bent it very slowly so he could sit on the edge of the bed, lips pressed so tightly together that they were white. Then he began to flex it a bit, listening to the pain talk, trying to decide just how bad it really was, if it might be—

Gage! Is Gage back?

That got him on his feet in spite of the pain. He stumbled across the room like Matt Dillon’s old sidekick Chester. He went through the door and across the hall into Gage’s room. He looked around wildly, his son’s name shaking on his lips. But the room was empty. He limped down to Ellie’s room, which was also empty, and then into the spare room. That room, which faced the highway, was also empty. But—

There was a strange car across the road. Parked behind Jud’s truck.

So what?

So a strange car over there could mean trouble, that was obvious.

Louis moved the curtain and looked at the car more closely. It was a small blue car, a Chevette. And curled up on top of it, apparently sleeping, was Church.

He looked for a long time before dropping the curtain. Jud had company, that was all—so what? And it was maybe too early to worry about what might happen with Gage; Church hadn’t come back until almost one o’clock, and it was only nine o’clock now. Nine o’clock on a beautiful May morning. He would just go downstairs and make some coffee, get the heating pad and wrap it around his knee, and—

—and what’s Church doing on top of that car?

“Oh, come on,” he said aloud and started to limp back down the hall. Cats slept anywhere; it was typical.

Except Church doesn’t cross the road anymore, remember?

“Forget it,” he muttered and stopped halfway down the stairs (which he was going down almost sideways). Talking to himself, that was bad. That was—

What was that thing in the woods last night?

The thought came to him suddenly, making him tighten his lips the way the pain in his knee had done when he swung it out of bed. He had dreamed about the thing in the woods last night. His dreams of Disney World had seemed to blend easily into dreams of that thing. He dreamed that it had touched him, ruining all good dreams forever, rotting all good intentions. It was the Wendigo, and it had turned him into not just a cannibal but the father of cannibals. In his dream he had been in the Pet Sematary again but not alone. Bill and Timmy Baterman had been there. Jud had been there, looking ghostly and dead, holding his dog Spot on a clothesrope leash. Lester Morgan was there with Hanratty the bull on a length of car-towing chain. Hanratty was lying on his side, looking around with a stupid, drugged fury. And for some reason Rachel was there too, and she’d had some sort of accident at the dinner table—spilled a bottle of catsup or maybe dropped a dish of cranberry jelly, maybe, because her dress was splattered with red stains.

And then, rising behind the deadfall to a huge height, its skin a cracked reptilian yellow, its eyes great hooded foglamps, its ears not ears at all but massive curling horns, was the Wendigo, a beast that looked like a lizard born of a woman. It pointed its horny, nailed finger at all of them as they craned their necks up and up to watch it…

“Stop,” he whispered and shuddered at the sound of his own voice. He would go out into the kitchen, he decided, and make himself breakfast just as if it were any ordinary day. A bachelor breakfast, full of comforting cholesterol. A couple of fried-egg sandwiches with mayo and a slice of Bermuda onion on each one. He felt sweaty and dirty and gross, but he would save the shower for later; right now getting undressed seemed like too much work, and he was afraid he might have to get the scalpel out of his bag and actually cut the leg of the pants open in order to let his swollen knee out. A terrible way to treat good tools, but none of the knives in the house would cut the heavy jeans fabric, and Rachel’s sewing scissors certainly would not work.

But first, breakfast.

So he crossed the living room and then detoured into the front entry and looked out at the small blue car in Jud’s driveway. It was covered with dew, which meant it had been there for some time. Church was still on the roof but not sleeping. He seemed to be staring right at Louis with his ugly yellow-green eyes.

Louis stepped back quickly, as if someone had caught him peeking.

He went into the kitchen, rattled out a frying pan, put it on the stove, got eggs from the fridge. The kitchen was bright and crisp and clear. He tried to whistle—a whistle would bring the morning into its proper focus—but he could not. Things looked right, but they weren’t right. The house seemed dreadfully empty, and last night’s work weighed on him. Things were wrong, out of place; he felt a shadow hovering, and he was afraid.

He limped into the bathroom and took a couple of aspirin with a glass of orange juice. He was working his way back to the stove when the telephone rang.

He did not answer it immediately but turned and looked at it, feeling slow and stupid, a sucker in some game which he was only now realizing he did not understand in the least.

Don’t answer that, you don’t want to answer that because that’s the bad news, that’s the end of the leash that leads around the corner and into the darkness, and I don’t think you want to see what’s on the other end of that leash, Louis, I really don’t think you do, so don’t answer that phone, run, run now, the car’s in the garage, get in it and take off, but don’t answer that phone—

He crossed the room and picked it up, standing there with one hand on the dryer as he had so many times before, and it was Irwin Goldman, and even as Irwin said hello Louis saw the tracks crossing the kitchen—small, muddy tracks—and his heart seemed to freeze in his chest, and he believed he could feel his eyeballs swelling in his head, starting from their sockets; he believed that if he could have seen himself in a mirror at that moment he would have seen a face from a seventeenth-century painting of a mental hospital. They were Gage’s tracks, Gage had been here, he had been here in the night, and so where was he now?

“It’s Irwin, Louis… Louis? Are you there? Hello?”

“Hello, Irwin,” he said, and he already knew what Irwin would say. He understood the blue car. He understood everything. The leash… the leash going into the darkness… he was moving fast along it now, hand over hand. Oh, if he could let go before he saw what was at the end! But it was his leash. He had bought it.

“For a moment, I thought we’d lost connection,” Goldman was saying.

“No, the phone slipped out of my hand,” Louis said. His voice was calm.

“Did Rachel get home okay last night?”

“Oh yes,” Louis said, thinking of the blue car, Church on top of it, the blue car that was so still. His eye followed the muddy footprints on the floor.

“I should talk to her,” Goldman said. “Right away. It’s about Eileen.”

“Ellie? What about Ellie?”

“I really think Rachel—”

“Rachel’s not here right now,” Louis said sharply. “She’s gone to the store for bread and milk. What about Ellie? Come on, Irwin!”

“We had to take her to the hospital,” Goldman said slowly. “She had a bad dream, or many of them. She was very upset and wouldn’t calm down. She—”

“Did they sedate her?”

“What?”

“Sedation,” Louis said impatiently, “did they give her something to calm her down?”

“Yes, oh yes. They gave her a pill, and she went back to sleep.”

“Did she say anything? What scared her so badly?” He was gripping the phone tightly now.

Silence from Irwin Goldman’s end—a long silence. This time Louis did not interrupt, as much as he wanted to.

“That was what scared Dory so badly,” Irwin said finally. “She talked a lot before she got… before she was crying too hard to understand. Dory herself was almost… you know.”

“What did she say?”

“She said Oz the Great and Terrible had killed her mother. Only she didn’t say it that way. She said… she said ‘Oz the Gweat and Tewwible,’ which was the way our other daughter always used to say it. Our daughter Zelda. Louis, believe me when I say I would much rather have asked Rachel this question, but how much have you and she told Eileen about Zelda and how she died?”

Louis had closed his eyes; the world seemed to be rocking gently under his feet, and Goldman’s voice sounded distant, as if coming through thick mists.

You might hear sounds like voices, but it's just the loons down south near Prospect. The sound travels.

“Louis, are you there?”

“Will she be okay?” Louis asked, his voice far away. “Will Ellie be okay? Did you find out what's wrong?”

“She's in shock from the funeral,” Goldman said. “My doctor came. Lathrop. A good man. He said she had a bit of a fever and might not even remember anything when she wakes up this afternoon. But I think Rachel should come back. Louis, I’m scared. I think you should come back too.”

Louis didn't answer. God watches over everyone, or so they say. But Louis was just a man, and he was focused on those muddy footprints.

“Louis, Gage is dead,” Goldman said. “I know that's hard to accept—for you and Rachel—but your daughter is alive, and she needs you.”

Yes, I accept that. You might be an old fool, Irwin, but maybe what happened between your daughters on that day in April 1965 taught you something about understanding. She needs me, but I can't come, because I’m afraid—so afraid—that my hands are covered in her mother’s blood.

Louis looked at his hands. Louis looked at the dirt under his nails, which was just like the dirt that made those footprints on the kitchen floor.

“Okay,” he said, “I understand. We’ll be there as soon as we can, Irwin. Tonight, if possible. Thank you.”

“We did our best,” Goldman said. “Maybe we’re too old. Maybe, Louis, we always were.”

“Did she say anything else?” Louis asked.

Goldman’s reply hit him like a death knell. “A lot, but I only caught one other thing: ‘Paxcow says it’s too late.’ ”

He hung up and wandered back toward the stove, vaguely planning to finish breakfast or clean up, he wasn't sure which. Halfway across the kitchen, a wave of dizziness washed over him, his vision went gray, and he fainted—“swoon” felt like the right word because it seemed to take so long. He fell down and down through blurry depths, twisting and turning, looping and spinning, then crashed onto his bad knee. A jolt of pain shot through his head, snapping him back with a scream. For a moment, he could only crouch there, tears welling up.

Finally, he struggled to his feet, swaying. But his head was clear again. That was something, wasn’t it?

The urge to run hit him again, stronger than ever. He actually felt the comforting shape of his car keys in his pocket. He would get in the Civic and drive to Chicago. He would get Ellie and leave from there. Of course, Goldman would know something was wrong, terribly wrong, but he would get her anyway… snatch her, if he had to.

Then his hand dropped from his keys. It wasn't futility, guilt, despair, or his deep exhaustion that stopped him. It was the sight of those muddy footprints on the kitchen floor. He pictured them tracing a path across the entire country—first to Illinois, then to Florida—across the entire world, if necessary. What you bought, you owned, and what you owned eventually came home.

The day would come when he opened a door and there would be Gage, a twisted version of himself, grinning a hollow grin, his clear blue eyes turned yellow and stupidly clever. Or Ellie would open the bathroom door for her morning shower, and there would be Gage in the tub, his body covered in faded scars and bumps from his accident, clean but smelling of the grave.

Oh yes, that day would come—he had no doubt.

“How could I have been so stupid?” he said to the empty room, talking to himself again, not caring. “How?”

Grief, not stupidity, Louis. There is a difference… small, but vital. The battery that burying ground lives on. Growing in power, Jud said, and he was right—and you’re part of its power now. It has fed on your grief… no, more than that. It’s doubled it, tripled it, magnified it. And it isn’t just grief it feeds on. Sanity. It’s eaten your sanity. The flaw is just the inability to accept, not uncommon. It’s cost you your wife, and it’s almost surely cost you your best friend as well as your son. This is it. What comes when you’re too slow wishing away the thing that knocks on your door in the middle of the night is simple enough: total darkness.

I could kill myself now, he thought, and I guess that's possible, isn't it? I have the means in my bag. It has controlled everything, controlled it from the start. The burial ground, the Wendigo, whatever it is. It forced our cat into the road, and maybe it forced Gage into the road, too. It brought Rachel home, but only when it wanted to. Surely I’m meant to do that… and I want to.

But things have to be fixed, right?

Yes. They did.

He had to think about Gage. Gage was still out there. Somewhere.

He followed the footprints through the dining room and the living room and back up the stairs. They were blurry there because he had walked over them on his way down without seeing them. They led into the bedroom. He was here, Louis thought in amazement, he was right here, and then he saw that his medical bag was unzipped.

The things inside, which he always arranged so carefully, were now a mess. But it didn't take Louis long to see that his scalpel was gone, and he put his hands over his face and sat like that for a while, a soft, sad sound coming from his throat.

Finally, he opened the bag again and started to look through it.

Downstairs again.

The sound of the pantry door opening. The sound of a cupboard opening, then slamming shut. The loud hum of the can opener. Lastly, the sound of the garage door opening and closing. And then the house stood empty in the May sunshine, as it had stood empty on that August day the year before, waiting for the new people to arrive… as it would wait for other new people to arrive sometime in the future. Maybe a young married couple, with no children (but with hopes and dreams). Bright young marrieds who liked Mondavi wine and Löwenbräu beer—he might be in charge of the Northeast Bank’s credit department, she with a dental hygienist’s license or maybe three years of working as an optometrist’s assistant. He would chop half a cord of wood for the fireplace, she would wear high-waisted corduroy pants and walk in Mrs. Vinton’s field, collecting fall grasses in November for a table centerpiece, her hair in a ponytail, the brightest thing under the gray skies, completely unaware that an invisible Vulture rode the air currents overhead. They would be proud of not being superstitious, of being practical in getting the house despite its history—they would tell their friends that it was a bargain and joke about the ghost in the attic, and they would all have another Löwenbräu or another glass of Mondavi, and they would play backgammon or Mille Bourne.

And maybe they would have a dog.

Chapter 61

Louis stopped on the side of the road to let a big truck carrying fertilizer speed past, then crossed to Jud’s house, his shadow stretching behind him. He held a can of cat food.

Church saw him coming and sat up, watching.

“Hi, Church,” Louis said, looking at the quiet house. “Want some food?”

He put the cat food on the trunk of the car and watched Church jump down and start eating. Louis put his hand in his pocket. Church looked at him, stiffening, as if he knew what was coming. Louis smiled and moved away from the car. Church ate again, and Louis took out a syringe. He unwrapped it and filled it with morphine. He put the bottle back in his jacket and walked to Church, who looked wary. Louis smiled at the cat and said, “Eat up, Church. Let’s go, okay?” He petted the cat, felt its back arch, and when Church ate again, Louis grabbed it and stuck the needle into its leg.

Church went wild, struggling, spitting, and clawing, but Louis held on and pushed the plunger all the way. Then he let go. The cat jumped off the car, hissing, eyes wild. The needle fell out and broke. Louis didn’t care. He had more.

The cat started for the road, then turned toward the house, as if remembering something. It got halfway and then began to wobble. It made it to the steps, jumped to the first one, then fell. It lay on its side at the bottom of the steps, breathing weakly.

Louis glanced into the car. If he needed more proof, he had it: Rachel’s purse, her scarf, and plane tickets spilling out of a folder.

When he turned to walk to the porch, Church’s side had stopped moving. Church was dead. Again.

Louis stepped over it and went up the porch steps.

“Gage?”

It was cool in the front hall. Cool and dark. The single word dropped into the silence like a stone down a deep well. Louis threw another.

“Gage?”

Nothing. Even the ticking of the clock in the parlor had stopped. This morning there had been no one to wind it.

But there were tracks on the floor.

Louis went into the living room. There was the smell of cigarettes, old and long gone. He saw Jud’s chair by the window. It was pushed to the side, as if he had gotten up quickly. There was an ashtray on the windowsill, and in it a neat roll of cigarette ash.

Jud sat here watching. Watching for what? For me of course, watching for me to come home. Only he missed me. Somehow he missed me.

Louis glanced at the four beer cans lined up in a neat row. Not enough to make him fall asleep, but maybe he had gotten up to go to the bathroom. Whatever it was, it was just a little too perfect to be completely accidental, wasn’t it?

The muddy tracks went toward the chair by the window. Mixed with the human tracks were a few faint, ghostly cat prints. As if Church had walked in and out of the grave dirt left by Gage’s small shoes. Then the tracks headed for the swinging door leading into the kitchen.

Heart pounding, Louis followed the tracks.

He pushed the door open and saw Jud’s feet sticking out, his old green work pants, his checked flannel shirt. The old man was lying flat in a large puddle of drying blood.

Louis put his hands over his face, as if to block what he was seeing. But he couldn't; he saw eyes, Jud’s eyes, open, seeming to blame him, maybe even blaming himself for starting this.

But did he? Louis wondered. Did he really do that?

Jud had been told by Stanny B., and Stanny B. had been told by his father, and Stanny B.’s father had been told by his father, the last trader to the Indians, a Frenchman from the north when Franklin Pierce was President.

“Oh Jud, I’m so sorry,” he whispered.

Jud’s empty eyes stared at him.

“So sorry,” Louis repeated.

His feet seemed to move on their own, and he was suddenly thinking about last Thanksgiving, not the night he and Jud had taken the cat up to the Pet Sematary and beyond, but the turkey dinner Norma had made, all of them laughing and talking, the two men drinking beer and Norma with a glass of white wine, and she had taken the white lawn tablecloth from the lower drawer as he was now, but she had put it on the table and held it down with lovely pewter candlesticks, while he—

Louis watched it fall over Jud’s body like a parachute, hiding that dead face. Almost at once, tiny rose-shaped spots of deep, dark red began to stain the white lawn.

“I’m sorry,” he said for a third time. “So sor—”

Then something moved above, something scraped, and he stopped speaking. It was soft, it was quiet, but it was done on purpose. Yes, he was sure of it. A sound he was meant to hear.

His hands wanted to shake, but he wouldn't let them. He walked to the kitchen table with its checkered oilcloth and reached into his pocket. He took out three more Becton-Dickson syringes, removed their paper covers, and lined them up. He took out three more vials and filled each syringe with enough morphine to kill a horse—or Hanratty the bull, if needed. He put them back in his pocket.

He left the kitchen, crossed the living room, and stood at the bottom of the stairs.

"Gage?"

From somewhere in the shadows above came a giggle—a cold, dark laugh that made Louis's skin crawl.

He started up.

It was a long walk to the top of those stairs. He could picture a man about to be executed taking a walk almost as long (and as horribly short) to the gallows with his hands tied, knowing he would lose control when he couldn't even whistle.

He reached the top at last, one hand in his pocket, staring at the wall. How long did he stand there? He didn't know. He could feel his sanity starting to break down. It was a real feeling, a true thing. It was interesting. He imagined a tree weighed down with ice in a bad storm would feel this way—if trees could feel anything—right before falling over. It was interesting… and kind of funny.

"Gage, want to go to Florida with me?"

That giggle again.

Louis turned and saw his wife, to whom he had once brought a rose in his teeth, lying halfway down the hall, dead. Her legs were spread out like Jud’s had been. Her back and head were bent at an angle against the wall. She looked like a woman who had fallen asleep while reading in bed.

He walked toward her.

Hello, darling, he thought, you came home.

Blood had splashed the wallpaper in strange, silly shapes. She had been stabbed a dozen times, two dozen, who knew? His scalpel had done this.

Suddenly he saw her, really saw her, and Louis Creed began to scream.

His screams echoed loudly through the house where only death now lived and walked. Eyes wide, face pale, hair standing up, he screamed; the sounds came from his sore throat like the bells of hell, terrible cries that meant the end not of love but of his mind; all the awful images rushed into his head at once. Victor Pascow dying on the infirmary floor, Church coming back with bits of green plastic in his whiskers, Gage’s baseball cap lying in the road, full of blood, but most of all that thing he had seen near Little God Swamp, the thing that had pushed the tree over, the thing with the yellow eyes, the Wendigo, creature of the north, the dead thing whose touch brings awful desires.

Rachel had not just been killed.

Something had been… something had been at her.

(!CLICK!)

That click was in his head. It was the sound of something breaking and burning out for good, the sound of lightning striking right on target, the sound of a door opening.

He looked up, dazed, the scream still stuck in his throat, and there was Gage at last, his mouth bloody, his chin dripping, his lips pulled back in a horrible grin. In one hand he held Louis’s scalpel.

As he brought it down, Louis pulled back without thinking. The scalpel zipped past his face, and Gage lost his balance. He is as clumsy as Church, Louis thought. Louis kicked his feet out from under him. Gage fell awkwardly, and Louis was on him before he could get up, straddling him, one knee holding down the hand with the scalpel.

“No,” the thing under him gasped. Its face twisted. Its eyes were evil, like an insect's in their dumb hate. “No, no, no—”

Louis grabbed for one of the syringes, got it out. He had to be quick. The thing under him was like a slippery fish and it wouldn’t let go of the scalpel no matter how hard he pressed on its wrist. And its face seemed to change as he looked at it. It was Jud’s face, dead and staring; it was the damaged face of Victor Pascow, eyes rolling blankly; it was, like a mirror, Louis’s own, pale and crazy. Then it changed again and became the face of that thing in the woods—the low forehead, the dead yellow eyes, the tongue long and split, grinning and hissing.

“No, no, no-no-no—”

It bucked under him. The syringe flew out of Louis’s hand and rolled a short way down the hall. He reached for another, got it out, and jammed it straight into Gage’s lower back.

It screamed under him, body arching, nearly throwing him off. Grunting, Louis got the third syringe and jammed this one into Gage’s arm, pushing the plunger all the way down. He got off then and began to back slowly down the hallway. Gage slowly got to his feet and began to stumble toward him. After five steps, the scalpel fell from its hand. It hit the floor blade first and stuck in the wood, shaking. After ten steps, that strange yellow light in its eyes began to fade. After a dozen, it fell to its knees.

Now Gage looked up at him and for a moment Louis saw his son—his real son—his face sad and filled with pain.

“Daddy!” he cried, and then fell forward on his face.

Louis stood there for a moment, then went to Gage, moving carefully, expecting a trick. But there was no trick, no sudden grab with clawed hands. He slid his fingers down Gage’s throat, found the pulse, and held it. He was a doctor for the last time in his life, checking the pulse, checking until there was nothing, nothing inside, nothing outside.

When it was finally over, Louis got up and walked slowly down the hall to a distant corner. He crouched there, curling into a ball, squeezing himself into the corner, tighter and tighter. He realized he could make himself smaller by putting a thumb in his mouth, so he did.

He stayed like that for over two hours… and then, slowly, a dark and believable idea came to him. He took his thumb out of his mouth. It made a small popping sound. Louis got himself

(hey-ho let’s go)

moving again.

In the room where Gage had hidden, he pulled the sheet off the bed and carried it into the hall. He wrapped his wife’s body in it, gently, with love. He was humming without realizing it.

He found gasoline in Jud’s garage. Five gallons of it in a red can next to the Lawnboy. More than enough. He started in the kitchen where Jud still lay under the Thanksgiving tablecloth. He soaked it, then went into the living room with the can still turned over, spraying yellow gas over the rug, the sofa, the magazine rack, the chairs, and then out into the downstairs hall and toward the back bedroom. The smell of gas was strong and heavy.

Jud’s matches were by the chair where he had kept his pointless watch, on top of his cigarettes. Louis took them. At the front door, he tossed a lit match back over his shoulder and stepped out. The heat was instant and fierce, making the skin on his neck feel too tight. He shut the door carefully and only stood on the porch for a moment, watching the orange glow behind Norma’s curtains. Then he crossed the porch, pausing for a moment, remembering the beers he and Jud had drunk here long ago, listening to the soft, growing roar of fire inside the house.

Then he stepped out.

Chapter 62

Steve Masterton rounded the curve just before Louis’s house and immediately saw the smoke—not from Louis’s, but from the old woman’s house across the street.

He had come out this morning because he was worried about Louis—very worried. Charlton had told him about Rachel’s call the day before, and that had made him wonder where Louis was… and what he was doing.

His worry was vague, but it bothered him—he wouldn’t feel right until he had gone out there and checked to see if things were okay… or as okay as they could be under the circumstances.

The spring weather had emptied the infirmary quickly, and Surrendra had told him to go ahead; he could handle anything that came up. So Steve had jumped on his Honda, which he had taken out of the garage just last weekend, and headed for Ludlow. Maybe he rode a little faster than he needed to, but the worry was there; it bothered him. And with it came the strange feeling that he was already too late. Stupid, of course, but in his stomach he felt like he had last fall when that Pascow thing happened—a feeling of miserable surprise and disappointment. He wasn’t a religious man (in college Steve had been in the Atheist’s Society for two semesters and had quit only when his advisor told him—privately and unofficially—that it might hurt his chances of getting a medical school scholarship later), but he guessed he was as likely as anyone else to have whatever biological or rhythmic conditions caused premonitions, and Pascow’s death seemed to set a tone for the year that followed. It wasn’t a good year. Two of Surrendra’s relatives had been jailed back home for some political thing, and Surrendra had told him that he thought one of them—an uncle he loved very much—might be dead. Surrendra had cried, and the tears from the usually calm Indian had scared Steve. And Charlton’s mother had had a radical mastectomy. The strong nurse wasn’t optimistic about her mother living another five years. Steve himself had been to four funerals since Victor Pascow’s death—his wife’s sister, killed in a car crash; a cousin, killed in a weird accident because of a bar bet (he had been electrocuted while trying to climb to the top of a power pole); a grandparent; and Louis’s little boy.

He liked Louis a lot, and he wanted to make sure Louis was okay. Louis had been through hell lately.

When he saw the smoke, his first thought was that this was another thing caused by Victor Pascow, who seemed, in dying, to have removed some kind of protection between these ordinary people and a string of bad luck. But that was stupid, and Louis’s house proved it. It stood calm and white, a nice piece of New England architecture in the morning sun.

People were running toward the old woman’s house, and as Steve turned his bike across the road and into Louis’s driveway, he saw a man run up onto the old woman’s porch, go to the front door, and then back away. It was lucky he did; a moment later the glass in the center of the door blew out, and flames poured through the opening. If the fool had opened the door, the blast would have burned him badly.

Steve got off his bike and put it on its stand, Louis forgotten for a moment. He was drawn by the mystery of fire. Maybe half a dozen people had gathered; except for the would-be hero, who stayed on the Crandalls’ lawn, they kept a safe distance. Now the windows between the porch and the house blew out. Glass flew in the air. The would-be hero ducked and ran. Flames ran up the inner wall of the porch like reaching hands, blistering the white paint. As Steve watched, one of the rattan chairs smoldered and then burst into flame.

Over the crackling sounds, he heard the would-be hero shout with a strange and silly optimism: “She’s gonna burn! Gonna burn for sure! If Jud’s in there, he’s done for! I told him about the creosote in that chimney a hundred times!”

Steve started to shout across the street to ask if anyone had called the fire department, but then he heard sirens in the distance, getting closer. Lots of them. Someone had called, but the man was right: the house was lost. Flames reached through several broken windows, and the front of the roof now had a see-through layer of fire over the bright green shingles.

He turned back, remembering Louis—but if Louis was here, wouldn’t he be with the others across the street?

Then Steve noticed something, just barely, out of the corner of his eye.

Beyond the top of Louis’s driveway was a field that went up a long, gentle hill. The tall grass, still green, had grown high this May. Steve saw a path, cut as neatly as a golf course. It twisted up the hill, toward the woods that started, thick and green, just below the horizon. There, where the light green grass met the darker green woods, Steve saw movement—a quick flash of bright white. It disappeared almost as soon as he saw it, but it seemed like he had seen a man carrying a white bundle.

That was Louis, his mind told him with sudden certainty. That was Louis, and you need to get to him fast because something bad has happened, and something even worse will happen if you don’t stop him.

He stood at the top of the driveway, unsure, shifting his weight from one foot to the other.

Steve, you’re scared to death right now, aren’t you?

Yes. He was. He was scared to death for no reason at all. But there was also something… something

(drawing him in)

yes, something drawing him in, something about that path, that path leading up the hill and into the woods—surely that path had to go somewhere, didn’t it? Yes, of course it did. All paths eventually went somewhere.

Louis. Don’t forget Louis, you idiot! Louis was the man you came to see, remember? You didn’t come to Ludlow to explore the damn woods.

“What do you have there, Randy?” the wannabe hero shouted. His voice, still high and somehow hopeful, carried easily.

Randy’s reply was almost lost in the growing sound of the fire sirens. “Dead cat.”

“Burnt?”

“Doesn’t look burnt,” Randy said. “Just looks dead.”

And Steve's mind returned stubbornly, as if the conversation across the street had something to do with what he had seen—or what he thought he had seen: That was Louis.

He started to move then, jogging up the path toward the woods, leaving the fire behind him. He was sweating by the time he reached the edge of the woods, and the shade felt cool and good. There was the sweet smell of pine and spruce, bark and sap.

Once in the woods, he broke into a run, not sure why he was running, not sure why his heart was beating so fast. His breath came in and out quickly. He was able to run faster going downhill—the path was surprisingly clear—but he reached the arch that marked the entrance to the Pet Sematary at just a fast walk. There was a sharp pain high in his right side, just under his armpit.

His eyes barely noticed the circles of graves—the bent tin squares, the bits of board and slate. His gaze was fixed on the strange sight at the far side of the circular clearing. It was fixed on Louis, who was climbing a fallen tree, seemingly defying gravity. He climbed the steep tree step by step, his eyes straight ahead, like a man who was hypnotized or sleepwalking. In his arms was the white thing that Steve had glimpsed. Up close, it was clear—it was a body. One foot, in a black shoe with a low heel, stuck out. And Steve knew with a sudden, sickening feeling that Louis was carrying Rachel’s body.

Louis’s hair had turned white.

“Louis!” Steve screamed.

Louis didn’t hesitate, didn’t pause. He reached the top of the fallen trees and started down the other side.

He’ll fall, Steve thought, his thoughts jumbled. He’s been so lucky, unbelievably lucky, but soon he’s going to fall, and if his leg is the only thing he breaks—

But Louis did not fall. He reached the other side of the fallen trees, disappeared for a moment, and then came back into view as he walked toward the woods again.

“Louis!” Steve yelled again.

This time Louis stopped and turned around.

Steve was stunned by what he saw. Besides the white hair, Louis’s face looked ancient.

At first, Louis didn't seem to recognize him at all. Then, little by little, recognition grew, as if someone was turning up a light in his mind. Louis’s mouth was twitching. After a moment, Steve realized Louis was trying to smile.

“Steve,” he said, his voice raspy and unsure. “Hello, Steve. I’m going to bury her. I guess I have to do it with my bare hands. It might take until dark. The ground up there is very rocky. I don’t suppose you’d want to help me?”

Steve opened his mouth, but couldn’t speak. Despite his shock, despite his horror, he did want to help Louis. Somehow, up here in the woods, it felt very right, very… very natural.

“Louis,” he finally choked out, “what happened? God, what happened? Was she… was she in the fire?”

“I waited too long with Gage,” Louis said. “Something got to him because I waited too long. But it will be different with Rachel, Steve. I know it will.”

He swayed a bit, and Steve saw that Louis was insane—he saw it plainly. Louis was insane and terribly tired. But somehow only the tiredness mattered in his confused mind.

“I could use some help,” Louis said.

“Louis, even if I wanted to help you, I couldn’t climb over that pile of wood.”

“Oh yes,” Louis said. “You could. If you just move slowly and don’t look down. That’s the trick, Steve.”

He turned then, and though Steve called his name, Louis walked into the woods. For a few moments Steve could see the white sheet flash between the trees. Then it was gone.

He ran to the pile of wood and started to climb it without thinking, first feeling for good places to grab, trying to crawl up it, and then standing up. As he did, a wild, bold excitement rushed through him—like breathing pure oxygen. He believed he could do it—and he did. Moving quickly and steadily, he reached the top. He stood there for a moment, unsteady, watching Louis move along the path—the path that went on past the pile of wood.

Louis turned and looked back at Steve. He held his wife, wrapped in a bloody sheet, in his arms.

“You may hear sounds,” Louis said. “Sounds like voices. But they are just the loons, down south toward Prospect. The sound travels. It’s strange.”

“Louis—”

But Louis had turned away.

For a moment Steve almost followed him—it was a very close thing.

I could help him, if that’s what he wants… and I want to help him, yes. That’s the truth because there’s more to this than I know, and I want to find out what it is. It seems very… well… very important. It seems like a secret. Like a mystery.

Then a branch snapped under his tilted foot. It made a dry, dusty sound like a starting gun. It brought him back to exactly where he was and what he was doing. Terror jumped into him, and he turned around awkwardly, arms out for balance, his tongue and throat thick with fear, his face showing the shocked look of a man who wakes up to find he has sleepwalked onto a high ledge.

She’s dead and I think that maybe Louis killed her, Louis has gone mad, completely mad, but—

But there was something worse than madness here—something much, much worse. It was as if there was a magnet somewhere in those woods, and he could feel it pulling at something in his brain. Pulling him toward that place where Louis was taking Rachel.

Come on, walk the path… walk the path and see where it goes. We got stuff to show you out here, Steverino, stuff they never told you about in the Atheists’ Society back in Lake Forest.

And then, perhaps simply because it had enough to feed on for one day and lost interest in him, the call of the place in his mind simply stopped. Steve took two stumbling, unsteady steps back down the side of the deadfall. Then more branches broke with a grinding sound, and his left foot plunged into the tangled deadwood; rough sharp splinters tore off his sneaker and then cut into his flesh as he yanked free. He fell forward into the Pet Sematary, barely missing a piece of orange crate that could have easily stabbed into his stomach.

He got to his feet, staring around, confused, wondering what had happened to him… or if anything had happened to him. Already it had begun to seem like a dream.

Then, from the deep woods behind the deadfall, woods so deep the light looked green and dull even on the brightest days, a low, chuckling laugh arose. The sound was huge. Steve couldn’t imagine what creature could make such a sound.

He ran, one shoe off and one shoe on, trying to scream but unable to. He was still running when he reached Louis’s house, and still trying to scream when he finally got his bike started and swerved onto Route 15. He almost hit a fire engine from Brewer. Inside his helmet, his hair stood on end.

By the time he got back to his apartment in Orono, he couldn't quite remember going to Ludlow at all. He called in sick at the infirmary, took a pill, and went to bed.
